There was an abandon-ship order that Mack barely heard and refused to obey. He had to stop those missiles. Struggling to right himself as the CIC took on ice-cold water, he grabbed onto the console, praying he wasn’t too late, certain he already was.
The display screen was locked, blank except for a single word: 
Leviathan.
A violent shudder that could have been the ship or his own panic. A scream from deep within the chasms of his very soul.
And then nothing but the cold, relentless sea.
 


8. THE HUM OF TRUTH 

11 hours before the Pruning
Exhausted after a long week of teaching and a Friday-afternoon staff meeting, Caleb braced himself against the biting wind as he hurried to his truck. He’d have to pick up Tic from basketball practice before long, but his afternoon visits to the Christian radio station had become something of a weekly, if not quite daily, occurrence.
Pulling up in front of the KWJD building, Caleb already felt the stress of his day lifting off his shoulders like mist rising from a frozen creek. When he stepped into the office, the sound of his best friend’s broadcast over the sound system greeted him warmly as Dev soliloquized from the sound booth.
“In John chapter 8, Jesus tells us plainly, ‘I am the light of the world. Whoever follows me will not walk in darkness, but will have the light of life.’” 
Dev spotted Caleb walking in and waved. Caleb poured himself a cup of coffee from the office pot, refusing to think of how old it might be, and sat down outside the recording studio and watched Dev gesticulating while he preached to an empty, soundproof room.
“This passage reminds us that no matter how dark the world gets, no matter how evil things appear, that Jesus Christ himself, the risen Son of God, is the one true light of the world, and if we walk with him, we will never walk in darkness but will have the light of life. I don’t know about you, but I want to walk in the light of life. I want to walk with my Savior each and every day he gives me on this beautiful, glorious earth he’s created.”
Caleb would never tire of listening to Dev’s Bible teachings. His friend’s slight Indian accent was always a comforting balm at the end of a hectic workday. 
Dev cut to a commercial break and stepped out of the booth, a smile brightening his entire countenance.
“Hey, brother. Great to see you.” He slapped Caleb on the back and clasped his forearm. 
“It’s a little early for Dev’s Devos, isn’t it? I thought you always aired at five.” Caleb glanced at the time, wondering if the radio station’s clock was faulty like so many others around town.
Dev chuckled. “No, my friend. I just got so excited I had to share that verse when I read it. I hope somebody who needed to hear it today was blessed.”
“I’m sure they were. Did you guys stay on air with the power out earlier?”
“We went dark for exactly four seconds before the backup system kicked in,” Dev answered with a proud gleam in his eye. 
“So, I guess all that new equipment from the faith drive was a good investment?”
“Sure was. We’re prepared for winds, fire, power outages, you name it. The Valdez earthquake in ’64? It wouldn’t have touched us. I’m telling you, when God promised to provide for us, he really provided.”
“Amen, brother.” 
“How’s the family?” Dev asked. 
“Just trying to avoid this flu going around. We had a dozen kids out sick today.”
“That’s why we homeschool,” Dev said with a grin.
“I’ll keep that in mind if we ever have any more children,” Caleb joked. The two men chatted for a bit longer until it was time for Dev to go back on air. With a promise to get together for a family dinner soon, Caleb was halfway to the exit when the lights flickered and shut off. In the office, the worship song that had been playing skipped for a half a beat then picked up right where it was.
“See what I’m talking about?” Dev called out as the lights flashed back on. “That backup system is a gift from the Lord God Almighty himself. The devil can try with all his might, but nothing will stop this station from singing God’s praise and declaring his glory.”

10 hours before the Pruning
“No matter how lonely or discouraged you might feel today, the Lord is with you and has promised that he will never leave you nor forsake you.”
Dev’s five o’clock Bible study had been a staple of Rachel’s spiritual life ever since the Singhs moved into the Copper River Valley and transformed the local station from one of random Christian songs and community updates to a worshipful, God-honoring 24-hour-a-day broadcast that blessed the people in and around Glennallen and soothed Rachel’s aching soul.
“God knows your worries, and he wants you to take those worries to him and lay them at his feet,” Dev said. “That means you, whether you’re anxious about your friends or your family or your loved one, or you’re anxious about that prodigal who won’t come home.” 
As Rachel stirred her pot of chicken soup, she wondered how it was that Dev seemed to always know exactly what word of encouragement she needed to hear.
Or maybe it wasn’t Dev at all.
The front door opened, and Cammy let out a happy yip as Caleb and Tic came bursting through the arctic entrance, bringing in a blast of cold air with them. The wind was ominous tonight, rattling the entire house like a wolf shaking the life out of its prey.
“Hey, you’re back.” Rachel greeted her husband with a kiss and forced Tic into one of those side hugs that were getting more and more awkward the taller he grew. “How was school?” she asked.
She couldn’t hear their answers over Cammy’s exultant barking at the KWJD broadcast, so she turned the radio down and asked Tic to get Cammy her dinner. As soon as she said the word, Cammy spun in two happy circles then raced to her spot in the living room to wait for her meal.
“How was work?” Rachel asked, giving her husband a quick hug.
“Good,” Caleb answered. “Power went out here too, I assume?”
“Yeah, a couple hours this morning then it’s been flickering on and off ever since. I made soup with that leftover chicken in case I ended up having to cook over the wood stove.”
“You could have always turned on the generator,” Caleb responded wryly. He could never understand how Rachel would literally sit in the dark for hours, waiting to see if the power would come back on, when they had a generator just outside that took all of two minutes to turn on.
“I was going to,” Rachel told him, “but the snow wasn’t cleared.”
Caleb glared at his son, but there was a softness in his tone when he said, “How many times do I have to tell you not to forget to make a pathway to the generator when you go out to shovel?”
“I’m sorry.” Tic plopped himself down at the table.
“Wash your hands,” Rachel snapped, surprised her sixteen-year-old still needed reminding. What kind of barbarian sits at the table with public-school hands?
Caleb shook his head. “That kid. If I ever find out who his parents are …” He kissed the top of Rachel’s head then sat down at the table. She watched him with a love that felt as cozy as chocolate milk on Christmas morning, with a love that felt solid and familiar. Just like their home.
“How was it at school with the power off?” she asked. Cammy, now fed, was running from one family member to another demanding love and attention before she made her way to the dining room table to beg.
“The kids were antsy. We ended up taking everybody into the gym for an extra-long PE.”
“The whole middle school?”
“Yeah, and halfway through the fifth grade came and joined us, too.”
“Wow, sounds …” Rachel paused, searching for the right word, but all she could settle on was “loud.”
Caleb chuckled. “You can say that again.”
After Rachel brought the soup to the table, Caleb prayed for the food and they all dished up.
“Did you hear Dev’s Devos tonight on the way home?” Rachel asked. “It was really good.”
Caleb wiped his mouth with his napkin, which Cammy tried to eat as soon as he placed it back down on his lap. “No, we missed it. What was it about?”
Rachel tried to remember. She could tell her husband how Dev’s teaching made her feel, how it brought comfort to her heart, but she couldn’t tell him what Dev actually said or even what passage from the Bible he’d been reading.
During dinner, Tic told the family about the chess board he finished in woodshop, the five-on-five basketball tournament the village hall was hosting next week, and the plans he and some friends from youth group were making for a camping trip over spring break.
“It’s going to be so cold, though.” Rachel knew she wasn’t supposed to worry about her kids, but telling her to stop would be like telling Cammy to stop begging at the table, an activity that was always rewarded with scraps from Tic and often from Caleb as well.
“It’s not until the end of March,” Caleb assured her. “And they’ve all got good gear, right son? Everything’s got to be rated for zero degrees or less.”
“Yeah, we’re set.”
Rachel let out her breath, and for the briefest moment, they were in this same house, except there was no Cammy, and Caleb was a good forty pounds lighter. She was wearing a summer sundress and little Tic was finger-painting with some spaghetti in his highchair and Yaya was daydreaming out loud about becoming an astronaut.
Where had those days gone?
She blinked, and the memory vanished, much like the carrots Tic had dropped into Cammy’s mouth when he thought Rachel wasn’t looking. She loved her family. So much. But everything felt so different now. Yaya was two hundred miles away in one of the coldest cities in the state. Tic was nearly grown, talking about leaving their warm, comforting home and living for a week in a tent.
Caleb took her hand and gave it a squeeze. She looked at him, this man she’d married over half a lifetime ago. Sometimes she marveled at the way God had brought them together. Sometimes she wondered if she really deserved to feel so happy and content when there were so many others in the world stuck in terrible marriages or trapped in dire poverty or suffering extreme illnesses.
She didn’t know why, when her life was so full and rich and fulfilling, she would give in to these spells of almost crippling melancholy. It was almost as if her heart weren’t designed to contain so much love and happiness and sometimes it had to wrench itself out in the center of her chest like a sponge squeezing out excess moisture.
Realizing her husband had spoken to her, Rachel blinked herself back to the dinner table and shook her head. “Sorry, what did you say?”
Caleb simply smiled at her. “I said this dinner is delicious. You never cease to amaze me, you know that?”
“It’s just chicken soup,” she said, feeling a slight flush on her cheeks, but she and her husband both knew he was talking about so much more than that.
The wind howled, and a shiver crept from the base of Rachel’s spine up her entire body. 
Outside, the dark night hummed.

Remote Broadcast Studio, KABC News Affiliate, Los Angeles, California
Pruning Day
There was nothing about the routine that should be unfamiliar to Maria Chen. How many breaking stories had she prepared for just like this, down to the 2:30 a.m. wakeup call and the uncomfortable closeness of the makeup artist exhaling coffee breath on her face?
She’d sat in this exact chair hundreds of times. But never for a scoop like this.
“We need her ready in five,” an assistant called out, her entire demeanor far too perky for the early hour and the scope of this story. Maybe she was like everybody else in America, everybody else in the world, really — enviably naïve, blissfully asleep. 
But in a few minutes Maria was going to wake the world up.
And then they would all have to face the consequences. 
She regarded her reflection in the mirror while the stylist spritzed and shaped her sleek bob.
“There she is.” That familiar voice of Paul Reeds, her old producer and closest thing she had to a friend in this business. He was notorious for being boisterous, even at this ungodly hour. But today he looked serious. Subdued. Like the weight of the entire universe had been hoisted onto his shoulders the second Maria divulged what she had unearthed.
She knew exactly how he felt.
When that dad reached out to her and told her how his son had joined some Alaska-based cult, Maria followed a few leads, thinking she’d have a simple weekend story. Cults were never breaking news, not until people got themselves killed in some kind of shocking massacre, but this was a new take on an old story. A group of techies out in the middle of nowhere Alaska, most of them extremely educated, all of them professing atheists, but undeniably a cult nonetheless. Instead of God, they worshiped some vague notion of a supreme AI entity, and instead of a biblical apocalypse they preached a technological doomsday.
It was supposed to be a moderately interesting cautionary tale: How to react if your child gets caught up in a tech cult, early warning signs to look out for, the usual sort of clickbait drivel. The only reason she followed up with that distraught father in the first place was because Paul had called her off the Project Black story. Said she was too close to it since the senator accused of embezzlement had belonged to her ex-husband’s college fraternity. 
At the time, she’d rolled her eyes. Pretty soon you wouldn’t be able to cover a story if it took place at a destination your aesthetician had once driven through on a first-grade field trip.
But as Maria dove into her research on this Alaska-based cult, she realized there was more behind the Copper Serpents than just some tech-focused doomsday weirdos. And when she followed the vine to find the melon, she was shocked to discover how rotten and poisonous the fruit actually was.
“You good?” Paul asked, snapping her back into right now, just moments before she broke her story on live morning TV. 
“Define good.” She chuckled mirthlessly. 
Paul let out his breath. “I just wanted to make sure you were ready.”
Ready? She thought about everything she’d gone through in the past three months, the looking over her shoulder at every crosswalk and red light, the multiple encryptions on every device she owned, the way she’d aged ten years in so many weeks.
And of course, the threats.
“You know how it’s gonna go,” Paul told her. “Just remember our signal, and the second you say so, we pull the plug. It’s as easy as that.”
Maria pushed aside the mascara brush that was blocking her view, and she leveled her gaze directly at him. “Is it, though? Is it really that easy?” 
Paul had the good sense to look away.
“We need Maria in place,” someone called in. 
Paul reached his arm out as if he were escorting her on a date. “Come on, let me walk you down the hallway.”
She was about to protest, but the sturdiness of his body next to hers gave her a small degree of comfort, and she let him lead her to the studio, the clicking of her heels on the linoleum echoing in her ears like the methodical banging of a judge’s gavel.
Paul handed her a small earpiece as she seated herself in a high-backed chair. A technician lifted his finger. “One minute.”
Her producer held her gaze for a moment longer, and Maria thought about all the other sensational stories they’d broken together. Fentanyl-laced drugs deliberately smuggled into minority neighborhoods with the DEA’s tacit approval … perhaps even direct oversight. Social media conglomerates selling data to foreign governments, including health information gleaned from biometric scans. The rise in youth suicides linked to manipulative algorithms that deliberately destroyed their users’ self-image in order to sell cosmetics or athletic shoes.
At the time, those stories had felt important. Monumental.
Until today.
On the screen in front of her, the video feed from the East Coast went live. She stared straight ahead while a voice in her earpiece counted down.
“Five … four … three …”
“Welcome back.” The New York anchor’s voice was steady and unwavering. “This morning, we’re joined by Maria Chen, award-winning investigative journalist, coming to us live from Los Angeles. She’s here to talk to us about an obscure Alaskan cult, a dire doomsday prediction, and a sweeping scandal reaching from the highest levels of world governments to the most powerful figures in global tech. Maria, thank you for being with us here today.”
Maria’s resolve was strong, and a surprising calm washed over her. This was, after all, her job. Not only that, it was her ethical duty to tell the public how horrifically they’d been deceived. 
She took a deep, steadying breath. But before she could say anything, the screen went black. She glanced at the crew, who all simultaneously began shouting and running and gesturing to each other en masse. 
Her heart started to race. Was the Copper Serpent behind this? Had someone realized what she was about to do and found a way to cut the power to the entire building? 
A deep, growling roar sounded in Maria’s headpiece, soon growing so loud she threw it off with a start. At the same time, a series of explosions rang out through the studio. Instinctively, she jumped off her chair and crouched behind a desk. 
Gunfire?
Peering out, she saw several studio workers on the ground nursing gruesome, bloody wounds.
What in the world?
The droning sound was so loud it shook the entire studio, except this time it wasn’t coming from her earpiece. A horrible, whooshing drone, loud and violent like the roar of an airplane at takeoff, a noise so strong it sounded as if it could rip the building apart.
A bright light. A shrill shriek. A searing pain.
Then nothing. Absolutely nothing.
 


9. A NEW DAWN

4 hours after the Pruning
Rachel woke up to the sound of Cammy whimpering quietly at her feet. “What is it, baby?” she whispered. “Is it your ears again, sweet thing?”
Cammy lifted her head, sauntered over to Rachel’s chest, then lay down, still whining slightly.
Rachel reached out to rub her dog’s ears but paused. Why was the bedroom so cold?
And so quiet?
The clock on the nightstand was blank. Great. Another power outage. Based on how dark it was, it could be midnight, or it could be eight in the morning. The only thing she knew for certain was that it was cold.
Too cold.
She nudged Caleb who was snoring beside her. She hated to wake him up, but with these kinds of temperatures, they couldn’t go very long without heat.
“Hun,” she whispered, “the power’s out.”
Caleb rolled over to look at her. “So?” 
Rachel could never get over how sexy her husband sounded when he first woke up, even when he was obviously annoyed. 
“It’s freezing in here.”
“What time is it?” Caleb reached for his cell phone and moaned.
Cammy looked over at him, one ear perked up in concern.
“What’s wrong?” Rachel asked.
“I plugged my phone in to charge last night, but it’s completely dead.”
“Here, I’ll check mine.” She reached over to her nightstand. Same thing.
“I hate these stupid power outages!” Caleb spat.
Rachel tried to ignore her husband’s outburst. Yes, it was inconvenient. Yes, it could even get dangerous when the temperatures were this far below zero in the middle of winter. But getting angry wasn’t going to warm their home.
“I guess it’s a good thing Dev helped you get the generator working,” she said, hoping her husband would read between the lines of her suggestion. 
“You want me to go out and turn it on now?” There was a hint of incredulity in Caleb’s voice.
“It’s really cold,” she reminded him.
Caleb groaned. “All right. Have I told you I hate this power system?”
Rachel reached her arm out to him. “Once or twice. Now hurry up, and when you get back, I’ll warm you up again.”
Her subtle teasing seemed to soften him. “All right. Do you have a flashlight or something? I’d use my phone, but …”
She reached into her drawer. “Here,” she said. “Take this.”
He flipped on the flashlight, and she gazed at his salt and pepper beard. He’d been clean-shaven when they met. Clean-shaven and so young. They both were. Rachel hadn’t even turned twenty before she knew this was the man she wanted to spend the rest of her life with. 
Power outages and everything.
“I’ll be back,” he told her. “Don’t let Cammy steal my spot.”
Rachel was glad to hear some playfulness back in her husband’s voice. “If anything, she’ll just warm it up for you.”
He flashed her a smile as he threw on some Carhartts. “That’s your job, babe.”

***

“Remind me again why we live in Glennallen?” 
Caleb wasn’t normally in the habit of talking to himself, not unless he was sleep-deprived, freezing cold, and perturbed. What made everything a hundred times worse was that Tic hadn’t shoveled a path out here like he was supposed to, so with each step Caleb took, ice-cold snow sank over the tops of his boots.
He was really going to have to have a talk with that boy.
Of course, he wouldn’t have to yell at Tic if the stupid electrical grid out here did what it was supposed to do. This was the first time his cell acted up, but if a power surge had fried his phone, the electrical company was going to buy him a new one.
In spite of the cold, Caleb was sweating under his coat when he yanked the pull cord on the generator. Instead of the expected whir, there was only silence. 
No, not exactly silence. That ridiculous humming was back again, surrounding him on every side. Was it louder than it had been yesterday, or was he just more easily annoyed right now?
He stared at the sky, trying to determine where the cursed noise was coming from. That’s when he saw it, the green eerie glow in the distance. It was way too low on the horizon to be an aurora. 
What in the world was going on?
He jerked the pull cord again, expecting that extra bit of exertion his foul mood gave him would get the generator working. In a few minutes, he’d be back in his room, the house would be warmed up in no time, and he and Rachel could enjoy a nice lazy Saturday morning. This was the first weekend all semester when Tic wasn’t traveling for basketball. The entire family had earned themselves a quiet weekend.
Except the generator still wouldn’t start. It wouldn’t even sputter.
“What is going on?” Caleb was aware that his voice was rising, but it didn’t matter. It wasn’t as if anybody out here could hear him arguing with this defunct piece of machinery. With all the time Dev spent last summer helping them get it up and running, Caleb couldn’t believe the stupid thing refused to turn over.
“Come on,” he urged and resisted the temptation to kick the entire unit.
He checked the gas, even though he knew the tank was full. That wasn’t the problem. So what was?
After several more futile attempts, each one making him angrier than the last, his torso was drenched in sweat, his feet and face ached with cold, and he could see the frost forming on his beard. Desperate, he pounded his fist on top of the petulant machine, half hoping to hear that beautiful mechanical whir, but to no avail.
Well, he couldn’t stay out here all night fighting with a stupid piece of equipment. Or maybe it was morning by now. With the power out and his cell phone dead, who knew? 
He reached into his pocket. At least he’d remembered his phone. If it was just the battery, he could plug it into the truck. On cold mornings like this, he liked to let the truck run a little anyway so the oil could still flow. The keys were in the ignition, but when he turned them, nothing happened.
What was going on? He jumped out of the truck and slammed the door shut. That horrid droning rattled inside his ears, but he still couldn’t tell where it was coming from. He lingered for a moment longer, frowning at the horizon, before heading defeated back into his cold, dark house.

***

Rachel was shivering under the blankets when Caleb returned. “Everything okay?” she asked, hoping the generator hadn’t given him too much hassle.
“No,” he replied dryly. “It’s totally dead.”
“Dead?” Her stomach sank. “Did you check the gas tank?”
“Of course I checked the gas tank.” She tried not to bristle at his tone. She knew how upset he got when things in the house stopped working. Repairs were always expensive, but even if you had extra money lying around, it was impossible to get skilled contractors to come all the way out here to fix anything. In order to survive in a remote place like Glennallen, you needed to be a genius with home repairs like Dev, or you needed a skilled best friend who was willing to help you out. 
Like Dev.
“The truck wouldn’t start either,” he grumbled.
Rachel sighed. Sometimes the temperatures would get so cold at night that if the vehicles weren’t plugged in they wouldn’t run. Good news was there wasn’t anywhere they needed to go today.
“Did you check the wall clock?” Rachel asked, hoping to get Caleb’s mind off the immediate problem of having no heat, no phone, no truck, and no generator in the middle of winter.
“Just after six,” he grumbled despondently.
“Wanna try going back to sleep?” She already knew his answer.
“No. We need to call the electric company and see how long this is going to last. They can’t expect us to go days like this with no heat.”
“We’ve got the wood stove,” she reminded him.
“That’s not the point,” Caleb spat. He looked at her face, and his expression softened. “I’m sorry.” He sat on the edge of their bed, his shoulders slumped. “I’m not mad at you, babe. I’m just frustrated and venting. That’s all.”
“I know.” Still, it was hard for her to see her husband so upset, hard for her not to immediately shut down when his anger flared up. After two decades of marriage, she still wasn’t used to it.
“What happened when you restarted your phone?” 
His question caught her off guard. Was she supposed to have read his mind and magically gotten her cell working again? 
“I didn’t try it yet.”
He let out a disgruntled sigh, visibly irritated. This was a common source of strife between them. If something was lost or broken, Rachel was content to wait and see if the problem would work out on its own. Keys would find themselves. Power would turn back on. You didn’t have to stress over small annoyances that in time would correct themselves without your help. 
But sometimes she felt that if she didn’t get anxious or angry at every inconvenience, Caleb assumed she didn’t care. Instead, she simply failed to see the point of getting stressed. Why blindly push buttons on a dead phone? Why call the electric company when the workers there obviously knew the power was out?
“I’ll try restarting it now,” she offered, but he was already fidgeting with his own cell. Cammy, as if trying to make him feel better, gave his hand two gentle, deliberate licks. He gave her a half-hearted push away but didn’t say anything.
There was nothing Rachel could do to ease her husband’s anger, but still she wracked her brain, trying to come up with something.
“You know,” she suggested, “even with the power off, once we get the wood stove up, I can make some of that oatmeal bake for breakfast. I think I can tweak the recipe to make it in a pot instead of a casserole dish.”
“Yay,” Caleb remarked dryly. “Oatmeal.”
Okay, Rachel was done trying to cheer him up or get his mind off their problems. If she knew anything about her husband, she knew that after a few minutes and a mental reset, Caleb would be back to the jolly, joking man she adored. Maybe the power would magically flick back on, and they’d find their phones had just run out of charge somehow in the middle of the night.
Everything would be easier once the sun came up. Tic would bring in more firewood, they’d keep the wood stove burning, and everything would be fine. With all the power fluctuations yesterday, she’d even had the foresight to fill the bathtub, so they’d have water to flush the toilets and wouldn’t literally freeze their butts using the outhouse in the cold.
It was going to be all right. They’d experienced dozens of power outages before. 
Today would be no different.

***

If ever Caleb needed a workout to seize control of his anxious thoughts, it was today.
“Come on, baldy,” Tic teased as Caleb locked out his hips while deadlifting 335 pounds. “Is that the best you got? I could do ten of those for my warmup.”
Caleb groaned as he let the weights crash to the ground. The iron plates hitting the rubber mat gave a satisfying metallic clamor. Caleb sank down on the bench to catch his breath. The basement was cold, far colder than normal, and he liked it.
“Tired already, you puny wuss?” Tic asked.
After the morning he’d already had, there was something comforting about his son’s playful insults. “All right,” Caleb replied, “if you think you’re so big and tough, let’s see what you can do.”
Candlelight flickered across Tic’s smiling face as he added fifty additional pounds and set himself up behind the barbell.
“Okay, keep the bar in contact with your legs, keep your spine neutral and lock out your hips,” Caleb told him, out of habit rather than necessity. In spite of all Tic’s trash talk, when it came time to actually lift, he was extremely careful. There was no way Rachel would trust either of them to push and pull thousands of pounds every day in the basement otherwise.
With a deep, controlled inhale followed by a loud roar, Tic raised 385 pounds of dead weight off the ground and locked it out.
“Nice work, weakling.” Caleb tried to hide his proud beam behind a stoic face as Tic dropped the bar with a grunt.
“You’re just jealous because I’m outlifting you so bad today.”
“You think I can’t match you?” Caleb glanced at the bar and its fifty extra pounds in addition to what he had just repped. “Watch this.” He stood up, knowing his back would regret his decision tomorrow, but the pain would be worth it. 
A day would come, far sooner than Caleb was ready for, when Tic would prove stronger than his father in every measurable way.
But today was not that day.
He’d just gotten himself into position when Rachel’s voice called to them from upstairs. “Breakfast’s ready.”
Caleb stared at the barbell and shrugged. “Guess I’ll have to prove myself another day.”
“Sure, old man, whatever.” Tic, possessed with the boundless energy of a healthy, athletic teen, was already halfway up the stairs.
The main level was toasty from the blazing heat radiating from the wood stove. Rachel had lit several candles around the room, far more than Caleb would have wasted, but the atmosphere was inviting.
“I made us some oatmeal bake,” Rachel said as they found their seats around the table. “Actually, I guess it’s more like oatmeal boil,” she added with a smile. “And we’ve got some canned peaches to go with it, and I heated up some water for pour-over coffee.”
Both Caleb and Tic made simultaneous gagging noises, and Rachel rolled her eyes. “Okay,” she teased, “well, in that case, I’m happy to share some of my tea.”
“No, no, no,” Tic responded jovially. “Pour-over coffee is definitely better than no coffee.”
Caleb wasn’t certain that he shared his son’s sentiment, but he was thankful that his workout in the basement had reset his brain. He still wasn’t happy about the power failing, but he wasn’t ready to kill the world or burn down the electric company. 
“That oatmeal smells good,” he lied to his wife. Caleb never fully understood the point of a breakfast that didn’t consist of eggs, bacon, and preferably another type of meat or three. But he knew he’d been an ogre when Rachel woke him up, and he wanted to make it up to her. 
“All right.” Rachel reached out her hands to her family. “Anything in particular we want to thank God for today?”
“This son of ours managed to hit 385 on his deadlift.” Caleb beamed at his boy. “As much as I like to tease that kid for being a puny little baby, I’m actually quite proud of him.”
Tic rolled his eyes, but Caleb caught the pride shining in his expression.
Rachel gave his hand a squeeze. “I’m thankful that we had some warning yesterday that the power might stay off so I filled up the bathtub. It’ll be nice to have that extra water to …”
Tic interrupted her with a groan. 
“What is it?” she asked.
Tic squeezed his eyes shut, as if he couldn’t bear to watch the words coming out of his mouth. Or maybe he didn’t want to see his parents’ faces as he spoke them. “I drained the tub last night to take a shower and forgot to fill it up again.”
Immediately, Caleb’s stress level, which had never gotten far below a four out of ten, shot up to twenty. Or maybe two hundred. “You did what?” He pulled his hands away from his family.
“I’m sorry.” This time, there was no teasing tone or fake insults when Tic spoke. “I was tired and gross and sweaty after basketball, and I just forgot.”
There wasn’t a single word Caleb could speak right now that he wouldn’t regret. Thankfully, Rachel was ready to jump in. “Well, good news is there’s lots of snow.” She leveled her gaze at their son. “After breakfast, I want you to take one of the big bins from the garage and use that to fill the tub. And in the meantime, I guess we can be thankful for our outhouse.”
“Sorry, Dad,” Tic mumbled, and Rachel put a calming hand on Caleb’s leg.
“That’s all right,” he responded. “It’s not your fault the power’s off.” 
Caleb took his wife’s hand back in his and reached for his son’s with the other. “Well,” he said, “speaking of what we’re thankful for, I’m thankful for my family, and I’m thankful for our wood stove, and I’m thankful for this oatmeal. Now let’s dish up before it gets cold.”

***

While Rachel watched her family seated around the breakfast table, she tried to suppress her uncomfortable sense of foreboding. Something was wrong, and not just with the electricity. 
While Tic and Caleb had been working out downstairs, she’d assessed several things around the house. With every other power outage, she’d been able to get at least a few hours of work done using her laptop’s battery reserve and her mobile hotspot. This morning, neither device turned on at all, even though she always kept both charged. 
The damage seemed to impact other electronics, too. Caleb always insisted their home security system wasn’t necessary in a community like Glennallen, but Rachel felt safer having it. Every other time the electricity turned off, it ran on backup battery power, except this time she couldn’t even get the home screen to start up. 
She kept waiting with held breath. Any minute, the power could turn on, and just like that, their cell phones and electronics might all start working again. Just one of those strange things that happened out here, like the way digital clocks all ran a little slower than they were supposed to, or the way the ceiling fan in the living room would occasionally turn itself on in the middle of the night for no apparent reason. Just a symptom of their quirky power grid out in the middle of nowhere.
She sipped her tepid tea and tried not to think about how cut off they were with no phones or internet. It was like back in the homesteading days when people had to rely on telegrams or radio.
That was it. Rachel jumped up out of her seat, startling Cammy, who was licking Tic’s pants leg under the table.
“What’s wrong, babe?” her husband asked.
“I’m trying to remember where that old radio is.” She grabbed a flashlight and hurried to the basement, returning a few minutes later with the small battery-operated machine, praying it would work.
“I need two double-A batteries,” she announced. 
Caleb stepped into the living room and pulled their clock off the wall. 
Rachel shook her head. “Not from there.” That analog clock was the only thing keeping time in their entire house. And until everything started working again, she was going to make sure it stayed ticking.
She pulled the batteries out from the TV remote then clicked the radio on. A rush of relief surged through her when she heard the familiar sound of static. 
“It’s 87.9,” Caleb told her.
She slowly raised her eyes to meet his. “I know,” she said and held up the radio so he could see the dial. It was already tuned into Glennallen’s only station. 
But there was nothing but static.
Caleb and Rachel stood staring at each other, and an entire wordless conversation flashed between them.
It’s probably nothing, Rachel thought. Just a really bad power outage that took out all the cell towers and fried all the electronics. But deep in her heart, she feared there was more to it. And by the look on her husband’s face, she could tell that he feared it, too.
“Mom? Dad?” Tic’s voice sliced into their silent discourse like a surgeon’s scalpel. 
Rachel forced a smile onto her face. “Guess all that fancy equipment Dev bragged so much about wasn’t as all-powerful as he wanted to think.” With deliberate cheer, she headed back to the table. “Come on. I don’t think the oatmeal bake will keep very well, so we better eat what’s left over.”
“Dad?” Tic was looking at his father with an expression that pierced Rachel’s soul like a poisoned arrow.
Caleb put his arm on Tic’s shoulder. “It’s okay, son. Your mom’s right. We may as well eat since there’s not a whole lot we can do until we figure out what’s going on.”
“But what is going on?”
Rachel longed for the days when her little boy would believe each and every word that came out of her mouth. She could tell him Santa Claus wouldn’t bring presents unless he was asleep, and he wouldn’t question her. She could tell him there was a man who lived on the moon and fished for shooting stars and disappeared once a month to go to Saturn to buy bleu cheese, and he’d ask her what his name was.
More than anything, she wanted to believe this was another normal power outage. After breakfast, they could clear the table and bring in a few more candles and play Scrabble or Risk as if nothing was out of the ordinary. She wanted to find some honest way to communicate this hope to her family, but before she could respond, Cammy yipped and raced to the door.
Someone pounded frantically on their front door. “Caleb? Rachel? Are you in there?” 


10. VISITED

Tic wasn’t a baby, and in spite of all his father’s good-natured nicknames, he wasn’t an idiot either.
When he finally demanded answers from his parents, he was half afraid they’d assure him everything would be okay and then go back to worrying like they had all morning. But instead, their conversation was cut short by the arrival of Tic’s best friend Benji and his family.
“Dev’s been out all morning,” Mrs. Singh explained. “He’s trying to get the station up and running again. We’re out of heat at home, and he thought it best we come over here.” 
“Of course.” Mom gestured to the living room, where the wood stove crackled in the corner. “Here, let me get some water heated up, and we can make you some tea.”
Benji’s little sister scurried to the couch.
“Hi, Priyah,” Tic called out. She didn’t answer but buried her bright-red face in a backpack full of gadgets.
Next to enter was Tic’s best friend, Benji. They greeted each other with their usual multi-step handshake, but their movements were stilted and habitual. Even their goofy hip bump at the end felt forced. “Wanna go upstairs?” Tic asked.
“It’s freezing up there,” Mom protested before Benji could answer. “Don’t you want to stay here by the fire?”
“It’s way too hot,” Tic complained. “Look, I’m dripping in sweat.” He held up his arm to show his mom the pit stain on his T-shirt. Priyah giggled quietly in the corner, and Mom feigned disgust.
“What are you gonna do upstairs, anyway?” she prodded.
Tic shrugged. “I dunno. Play guitar or something.”
“Isn’t it electric?” 
“It works without the amp,” he told her, trying not to roll his eyes. “It’s just quieter.” He turned back to Benji. “Come on.”
“Cool. Where should I put my helmet?”
Mom reached out her hand. “Give it to me and I’ll hang it up in the arctic entrance. Did you guys snow machine over here?” she asked Benji’s mom.
“Yeah,” Mrs. Singh answered. “We couldn’t get the van running.”
That got Tic’s attention, and he held off from racing upstairs long enough to listen in.
Mom seemed just as fixated on the news. “Caleb’s truck wouldn’t start either, but it’s not that cold out, is it?” 
Mrs. Singh shook her head then lowered her voice. “I don’t think it’s the cold.”
“Little Miss Genius is convinced it’s an EMP.” Benji’s voice was taunting.
Priyah looked up and glared at her brother. “I said it might be.”
Mrs. Singh looked sharply at both of her children. “Both of you cut that out,” she warned. “We don’t know what’s going on, and there’s no use speculating.”
Benji shrugged. “If you ask Priyah, it’s either a cyberterrorist attack or a solar storm.” 
Tic was actually relieved that someone — even Benji’s pathologically shy little sister — was actually trying to explain what was happening. He moved toward the couch where Priyah was tinkering with her bag of electronics. 
“A solar storm,” he prodded. “If that’s what’s doing all this, will it all go back to normal once it ends?” He felt stupid that a middle-school student could understand the science better than he did, but Priyah was basically a homeschooled genius. If she was the only one willing to talk about what might be causing all the electronics everywhere to crash, he was determined to listen.
Her hands were shaking a little as she tried to connect two exposed wires. She bit her lip but didn’t respond.
“Eh, don’t bother her,” Benji said. “She’s crazy.” He circled his ear with his finger to drive home the point.
“All right.” In all the time he’d known her, Mrs. Singh had never raised her voice, but right now she sounded tired and exasperated. “Benji, if you’re going to insist on taunting your sister, then go upstairs with Tychicus. It’s either that or you can apologize to her and to our hosts for being so rude.”
Benji’s shoulders slumped. “Sorry,” he muttered then turned to Tic. “Play some guitar?”
Tic lingered at the bottom of the stairs. He wanted to find a way to say something kind to Priyah to make up for her brother’s behavior. 
“Uh, what are you working on there?” 
She either didn’t hear him or pretended not to.
“Priyah found the parts to an old ham radio,” Mrs. Singh explained. “She’s going to put it together and see if we can find out what’s going on out there.”
Tic smiled in her direction, even though she wasn’t looking at him. “Cool, well, good luck with that,” he said then followed Benji upstairs. He’d find another chance to ask Priyah about solar storms. In the meantime, he didn’t really feel like fooling around anymore on his guitar.
“Why are you such a jerk to your sister?” he asked once he and Benji were alone in his room.
Benji shrugged. “I’m not a jerk. But you know what a nerd she can be. Honestly, I think she wants it to be an EMP or something so she can prove that she was right.” Beneath his friend’s blustering surface, Tic detected the same worry and unease that had been swirling around in his gut all morning.
He sprawled out onto his bed. “You really think it might be something serious like that?”
Benji plopped into the oversized recliner. “I dunno. Maybe it’s one of those things where everybody goes crazy and tells you it’s the end of the world and it ends up being nothing?”
Tic chuckled. “Yeah, I think my grandparents were way into Y2K back at the turn of the century. Stockpiled a year’s worth of food and everything.”
Benji shook his head. “That’s what I mean. Everyone was all worked up and nothing came of it.”
Tic leveled his gaze. “So you really think that’s what all this is?”
Benji didn’t answer right away and averted his gaze before mumbling, “Yeah. Of course.”
“Well, that still doesn’t give you an excuse to be so rude to your sister.”
Benji rolled his eyes. “Oh, please. The last person I want to hear you talking about is Priyah.”
“I’m just saying, she’s smarter than you give her credit for.” Tic wasn’t sure why he was defending her all of a sudden. Maybe he was itching to do something, anything, even if that was to pick a stupid fight with his best friend.
“You wanna go on about each other’s sisters? Fine.” Benji’s face was set, and his usual good-natured tone had vanished. “Let’s do it. Let’s talk about Yaya and you see how much you like it when I tell you that she —”
The pillow was in Tic’s hand one second and smacking Benji’s smug face into silence the next.
“What the …” Benji jumped up and glowered down at Tic. He was taller, but Tic was stronger by far.
Slowly and deliberately, Tic stood up and faced him, his muscles ready for action.
For a brief moment, everything was perfectly still, as if the world was frozen in place except for the two of them. Then Benji let out a forced laugh and his face broke into a lopsided grin. “Come on, man. I was just messing with you.” He tossed the pillow at Tic’s chest. Tic rolled his eyes and threw it back on the bed.
They both sat back down, and Benji sighed. “You know I didn’t mean anything about Yaya.”
“Yeah, I know.” 
Benji shook his head. “It’s just so weird with this thing between you and Priyah.”
Tic stared at him, as surprised as if he’d just confessed that he was going to ask the ancient Miss Cecelia to the prom. “What?”
“You know, that big unspoken crush between the two of you.”
Tic couldn’t contain his laughter. “Bro, it’s definitely not like that. I’ve known Priyah since she was in diapers.”
Benji chortled. “She was five when we moved here, moron.”
Tic shrugged. “So? How old are little kids when they stop wearing diapers?”
“You’re such a baby.”
Benji picked up Tic’s guitar and started strumming, and soon the tension faded into the sound of melodies that carried over the hum and the stress of the outside world.

***

Caleb stewed in his recliner in the living room, growing hotter every moment. Whether that was from the wood stove or the stress was anybody’s guess.
“Do you guys have a landline at your place?” Rachel asked Angel Singh, who’d stopped by earlier with her kids to get out of their cold house. 
She smiled sadly. “Yes, but it’s not working.” She lowered her voice. “We’ve had lots of power outages, but I’ve never experienced anything like this. Have you?”
“It is weird,” Rachel agreed, “but you never know. Might just be one of those strange flukes.”
Caleb had heard enough. He wasn’t going to sit around and listen to his wife’s typical “let’s wait and see if things get better on their own” drivel. The last thing he wanted was to sit around here doing nothing. Not with their entire town completely cut off like this. And who knew how bad it was in the rest of the world? Anchorage, Juneau … Fairbanks.
He jumped out of his seat and strode toward the basement door.
“Where are you going?” Rachel demanded. 
“Downstairs,” he answered, slamming the door shut behind him.
In the basement, he cursed himself for forgetting a flashlight. Thankfully, there was enough sunlight coming in from the upper window that he managed to find a battery-operated torch. He stubbed his toe on one of the weights and wanted to scream. Why did Tic always forget to rack his weights after a workout?
That was it. He couldn’t take one more stressor. He needed to do something to put his mind at ease.
He threw open the door to the freezer. They still had several dozen packages of caribou. Even without electricity, it was cool enough down here the meat would last quite a while before thawing. And when that happened, they could always take some of it outside and hope the bears didn’t notice.
One entire freezer shelf contained nothing but Ziploc bags filled with Rachel’s soups and stews. He could think of a hundred other meals he’d prefer to eat, but at least nobody would go hungry.
The pantries were stocked with staples: pasta, spaghetti sauce, those silly granola bars nobody ate but his wife kept buying. They had canned beans, canned corn, and several types of canned fruit. As long as the handheld can opener wasn’t lost or broken, they’d be okay.
Stacked in the little crawl space beneath the basement stairs were boxes of shelf-stable foods Caleb hoped they’d never have to use — freeze-dried camping meals and old MREs as well as bulk bags of rice and dried beans.
What about water? The Winters had a well on their property, but it ran on electricity. A couple years ago, after the last prolonged power outage, Caleb invested in a hand-operated pump he could install outside. They’d have to carry water inside with buckets, but that was certainly better than nothing.
It’d be a pain to operate in this cold, but then again, it’d be a pain to die of dehydration.
Was he overreacting? Caleb had no idea. For all he knew, the entire world may have been obliterated by nukes and only individuals in rural pockets like Glennallen had even survived, in which case he was grossly underreacting.
What he needed was more information. He couldn’t wait around for Priyah to fiddle with that little amateur radio. He stomped upstairs and grabbed his coat and boots. 
“I’m going to find Dev,” he called out to his wife then braced himself against the bitter cold. 

***

“Is he okay?” Angel asked after Caleb slammed the door shut. 
Rachel let out a sigh. The last thing she wanted was for Mrs. Proverbs 31 herself to start judging her husband or her marriage. “Yeah. You know Caleb. He just gets stressed.” 
She didn’t want to talk about her husband’s anxiety. Angel, with her picture-perfect family that might have walked off the cover of a Focus on the Family magazine, could never understand. Besides, there was so much more on Rachel’s mind right now than Caleb’s blood pressure. 
“I think it’s ready.” Priyah had been sitting so quietly on the couch in the corner that it was easy to forget she was there. The young girl glanced up from a clunky metal box with wires trailing from it like vines. Rachel had no doubt that if anybody could get a radio operating under these conditions, it was Priyah. Last year, she and two other local homeschoolers had placed third in a statewide robotics tournament, competing against huge schools from large cities like Anchorage and Fairbanks.
“Did you get it working, sweetie?” Angel asked.
“I think so.” She flipped a switch, and the radio came to life with a burst of static. 
Priyah smiled sheepishly. Rachel held her breath. Even Cammy had stopped dozing by the stove and was staring at them attentively, her head cocked to the side.
Priyah bit her lip and with steady fingers turned one of the dials. A series of tones warbled in and out of focus. Then a succession of quick beeps, followed by more static. 
The speaker sputtered again, and a voice crackled over the waves. A bit more static as Priyah fine-tuned the dial.
“Station … south or west. We’ve lost … Copy?”
Rachel leaned forward, as if she could turn her body into an antenna and improve the signal. “Can you bring him back?”
Priyah continued making adjustments until the voice was crisp and clear.
“This is Buster, station Kilo November Six Mike Alpha, calling from Delta Junction, Alaska. We’ve got complete power failure, no cell, no internet. Cars stopped running. Can anyone copy? Please respond if you’ve got ears on.”
They all waited, staring at the device in Priyah’s shaking hands. 
“You should say something to him,” Angel prodded. Priyah’s eyes grew twice their size and she tossed the transmitter into Angel’s lap like a hot potato. 
“Tok here,” another voice answered back. “This is Kenny Parks, Whiskey Seven Delta Charlie Foxtrot. We’re seeing the same thing here. Whole town’s blacked out, nothing on the grid, vehicles are deadweight. You thinking might be a solar flare?”
“Could be. Or something bigger,” Buster added, his voice lowered. 
“You heard from anybody in Anchorage or Fairbanks?”
At the mention of her daughter’s city, Rachel sucked in her breath as if she’d been punched in the solar plexus. Until now, she’d done her best to stay positive, to think and hope and pray that what they were experiencing in Glennallen would prove to be a short-lived, isolated event, a blip on the radar of their calm and peaceful lives.
There was a pause before Buster spoke again. “Delta Junction calling. Any stations in range? Fairbanks, Anchorage, Juneau … Please respond. We’re trying to find out how far this goes.”
“Should we tell them we’re here?” This time it was Angel asking. Rachel nodded, even though her thoughts were solely on her daughter.
Priyah showed her mother how to hold down the button to talk, and in a shaking voice Angel said, “Hi there, this is Angel.” She cleared her throat. “My daughter got this radio up and working. We’re in Glennallen. We can hear you.”
“Glennallen? You doing okay? Power out there, too?”
“Yeah,” Angel answered, appearing to grow more confident as she spoke. “It’s just like you said. We can’t use the landlines or cells or anything.”
“You mentioned you’re with your daughter. Are you both okay? Is anyone else there?”
Rachel grasped the transmitter without thinking. “Hi, I’m here too. This is Rachel. From Glennallen. My daughter …” Her words came out in frantic, breathless spasms. “My daughter’s in Fairbanks. At the university. She’s staying in the dorms. I don’t know … I haven’t heard …” 
She couldn’t finish. Cammy scurried over and started licking her anywhere she could reach.
There was a pause on the other end. Then the same voice returned, softer this time. “Copy, Rachel. We’re hoping someone from Fairbanks comes on soon.”
“It’s her first year of college. She’s never been on her own before.” Rachel didn’t know why she was explaining Yaya’s life story to a stranger, but she couldn’t stop herself. With a soft whine, Cammy laid her head in her lap.
This time the operator’s voice was more official. “This is Buster in Delta. If there’s anyone in Fairbanks monitoring this frequency, please respond. We’ve got a mother here trying to reach her daughter.”
The radio crackled, then went quiet again.
“What was that?” Rachel asked. Cammy’s ears perked up, and she tilted her head to the side.
“… dark… copy … repeat, if you …”
Rachel froze, not even daring to breathe.
The voice crackled in again. “Repeat, Fairbanks … smoke … unknown …”
Rachel turned to Angel. “Did they say Fairbanks?” Just as quickly, she pounded the talk button with her free hand. “Buster? Did you get that? Did they say Fairbanks? Buster? I couldn’t hear the whole thing.”
Buster’s voice remained calm. “Rachel, we heard something too, but it wasn’t clear enough. Could’ve been Fairbanks, but it was too weak to lock on. Best thing you can do right now is wait.”
She clasped the microphone so tightly she was afraid it might break in her grasp. “What does that mean? Do I just stay here? I don’t know how this works. I just need to know my daughter’s okay.”
“What’s her name, ma’am?”
“Yaya, I mean her full name is Jael. Jael Winters.”
Buster’s voice was calm and stable. “Okay. Jael Winters. And you’re Rachel Winters? From Glennallen?”
“Right.” She could barely talk through the lump in her throat, the flutter in her chest.
“All right, Rachel. Tell me something about your radio. How are you keeping that thing powered right now?”
“It’s a car battery, right?” She looked to Priyah for confirmation. “Yeah, it’s a car battery.”
“Okay, good,” Buster replied. “That should buy you a good amount of transmission time, but you don’t want to waste it either. I’d say power down for now and come back every couple of hours. We’re logging all calls. If we find out what’s happening in Fairbanks or get a message from Jael, we’ll pass that on to you when you check back in.”
Rachel couldn’t answer him but only nodded her head. While Angel stared at her sympathetically and Cammy nuzzled her head into her lap, Rachel clutched the radio as if it were the last strand of hope connecting her mother’s heart to Yaya’s lost soul.
 


11. AFTERMATH

The fact that the sun was rising over Fairbanks seemed like the universe’s biggest insult.
Yaya wasn’t sure if she was in shock or just cold, but she couldn’t stop shaking.
“And I looked over, and bits of her phone were over here, and bits of her were over there.” Lilly hadn’t stopped talking about the exploding cell phone that killed her roommate. But whereas her friend apparently wanted to rehash the incident twenty times over, as if that might purge it from her memory, Yaya needed the exact opposite.
She needed silence, and she needed to forget. Forget the screams, the smell of smoke, the chaos. 
In reality, Lilly was the lucky one. Her roommate had died instantly, not in Yaya’s arms while she tried screaming for help with a voice that couldn’t carry over the shrieks of other students. Eventually, Yaya’s RA ran into her room and told her to evacuate. The cell phone detonations had started a series of fires in the dorms. But how could she leave? Even once the RA confirmed that her roommate wasn’t breathing, he had to pry Yaya away and practically throw her coat and boots over her pajamas.
She didn’t have anything else. No backpack, no purse, no identification. 
And of course, no phone.
All year, students in the freshmen dorm complained that the outlets were too far away from the beds. Yaya kept her phone charging on her desk at night. Her roommate bought an extra-long cable from the campus store so she could scroll herself to sleep. And now look what had happened to her. 
“And I haven’t heard from Kai, either,” Lilly rambled. “I don’t know where he ended up in this mess.”
Yaya cared for her friend too much to speak what was truly on her mind. Kai was never more than an inch away from his cell. With everybody’s phones inexplicably exploding at the same time, the odds certainly weren’t in Kai’s favor.
“Maybe he went with the first group to the library,” Lilly said, more to herself than anyone else.
At first, all the students had been told to congregate in the library, but when it looked as though that was going to have to turn into a triage center, those without any serious injuries were urged to seek shelter in other buildings. The problem was finding one that was unlocked. Yaya had been working so many late nights with Boone in the GI building that her advisor had finally given her a key code, so that’s where she and several others from her floor headed. With the power off, the key code hadn’t worked, but a few students managed to break a window to let them in. 
Now, there were about a dozen others besides Yaya and Lilly huddled in the middle of a lab, and it seemed as if Yaya was the only one who didn’t want to speculate and rehash the terror of the previous night.
“Did anybody manage to call 911?”
“No, none of the phones are working, even the landline in the RA’s office.”
“The fire department’s gotta know the campus is burning, though. Why haven’t they come?
“Fire trucks can’t get here.”
“Why not?”
“You didn’t hear? My buddy was going to drive our friend to the hospital, but nobody’s cars will start. It’s an EMP or something.”
“I heard there might have been a bombing. Do you think that’s what that big boom was?”
“I didn’t hear it. I was asleep till my cell phone blew up.”
“No, the big one came after. The phones all went off, then a few seconds later there was this huge explosion.”
“I didn’t see it.”
“You couldn’t see anything, but I felt it. Thought it was an earthquake.”
“No, earthquakes don’t make noises like that, right?”
“Someone said it might be the base getting attacked.”
“Which one?”
“I dunno. I can never keep them straight anyway.”
“You can see the smoke still. Which direction is that? Is that where Wainwright is?”
“You should stay away from the windows,” Yaya told them.
“She’s right,” Lilly jumped in. “I mean, if cell phones are going off and bombs are exploding, that’s probably not the safest place.”
“If bombs are exploding, then nowhere’s safe.” 
There wasn’t much arguing with that logic. Nevertheless, standing by an open window seemed stupid. So did staying in this lab when there were other structures just down the hallway that would be better suited.
“I think we should go to one of the other rooms,” she told Lilly.
“What? Why? Even if there was some kind of attack, they’d bomb the bases, not here, right?” Fear flashed in Lilly’s eyes.
“What if we check out the seismic lab?” Yaya suggested. “Or even just something more interior without all this glass? Want to come look with me?”
“I don’t know. I think we should probably all stick together, don’t you?”
Yaya glanced around the room at the small cluster of students. No RAs, no security officers, no staff. They were all stragglers from the first-year dorm. Even the oldest among them couldn’t legally buy a beer. Everybody looked shocked, and everybody looked as if they were just waiting for someone else to take charge. 
Certain of her plan, if not of her own confidence, Yaya stood up. “We should see if the seismic lab is unlocked. It’s got insulation and extra supports.” Yaya was slightly surprised when everyone turned to look at her. What didn’t surprise her were the looks of doubt on their faces.
“It’s literally designed to withstand earthquakes and stuff,” she urged. “We’d be a lot safer there.” She turned to Lilly, who simply shrugged and grabbed her coat. Yaya expected maybe two or three at most to follow, but to her surprise every last student filed into the hall.
“So where is this earthquake lab or whatever?” Lilly asked.
Yaya knew the area well. Sometimes if Boone was manning an experiment, he’d move his office hours there. “Down this hall,” she answered, grateful to have Lilly standing beside her.
She wasn’t sure what they’d do if the room was locked, but thankfully the door opened easily.
Eyeing the lab, she immediately felt more convinced of the wisdom behind her plan. The walls were lined with thick acoustic panels, and heavy steel framing cut through the ceiling. Maybe it was just her mind playing tricks on her, but even the floor felt sturdier.
Then she saw the body.
And recognized who it was.
She ran toward the crumpled form of her advisor and knelt by his side, her eyes sweeping over his unconscious body. His shirt was bunched up to reveal a bloody gash on his abdomen.
“Boone?” She was so shocked she couldn’t manage to make her voice louder than a whisper. “Can you hear me? What happened?”
A second later, Lilly was on the ground beside her. “Is that your professor?”
“Yeah,” Yaya answered. “He must have been down here when the phones exploded.”
“What would he have been doing here so late?” Lilly asked.
Yaya didn’t bother explaining about her advisor’s work, the experiments that sometimes required round-the-clock monitoring. None of that mattered. What mattered was tending to Boone’s injuries. His sharp, jagged breaths fueled her resolve. 
She turned to the group of students. “Does anyone have first aid experience?”
“I used to be a lifeguard,” someone said, but his face was so pale Yaya wasn’t sure he could keep himself from fainting, let alone prove to be any help for Boone.
“What do we need to do?” she asked.
“Uh … um … first thing is …”
Lilly moved toward the boy and placed herself between him and the injured professor. “Hey, it’s all right,” she told him. “What’s your name?”
“Zeke.”
“Okay, Zeke,” Yaya called out, thankful that her friend was there keeping his focus away from the blood and mess. “So he’s bleeding right above his hip. If this was your basic first aid class, what would we need to do?”
“Call for help.” Zeke’s answer was met with silence, but Yaya didn’t want to give him too much time to get more flustered.
“Okay, and then what’s next?”
“Uh, we stop the bleeding. If we can. Is it …” He leaned to the side to get a better view then started to sway. Lilly barely caught him and turned him around. 
“Don’t look at him,” Lilly ordered. “Just tell Yaya what he needs.”
“Okay.” Zeke’s voice was starting to sound calmer now. “So, first you need to see if he’s breathing.”
“Yeah, he’s breathing,” Yaya answered. 
“And, um, do you feel his pulse?” 
Yaya put her fingers on Boone’s neck. The skin was cold and clammy, but there was definitely a heartbeat. “Yeah, it’s there, but I don’t really know what I’m looking for.”
“I don’t really either,” Zeke admitted with an embarrassed chuckle. “So, um, if he’s breathing and has a pulse, then we want to see if there’s anything dangerous around him. Like any glass or anything we need to move out of the way.”
“I’ll get these pieces of his phone picked up,” someone volunteered.
“You’re doing great, Zeke. Now what do we do?” Yaya didn’t want to mention the bleeding and make the poor kid pass out, but she had a feeling that tending to Boone’s gaping abdomen was a slightly higher priority than sweeping floors.
Zeke cleared his throat. “So, for his wound, is it like … the blood … does it like … is it …”
Yaya cut him off before he passed out. “It’s not spurting or anything. It looks almost more like bad road rash or something.” She wondered if that was the wrong term to use given Zeke’s present constitution. “It doesn’t look like he’s actively losing a lot of blood right now.” She hoped that was a good sign. 
“Right, next up. So, the thing to do …” His voice faded out, and for a moment Yaya worried he’d faint and hit his head. Then she’d have two patients on her hands, not just one.
“Come here,” Lilly told him. “You better sit down.” She led him to one of the lab stools.
“I found a first-aid kit on the wall,” someone said and handed the large plastic box to Yaya.
Finally. Now they were getting somewhere. “Okay,” she said. “They’ve got some sterile gauze. Should I just put it on the wound?” She addressed the room, assuming Zeke was out of commission for the moment. 
“Do I need to clean it or anything?” She dug through the medical supplies, looking for any type of antiseptic. She found a small single-use pack of antibiotic ointment, but there was no way it’d cover a wound that large. Besides, she couldn’t picture it being healthy to just goop Neosporin on top of an open gash like that.
“You should put a bandage on the area then hold it down with pressure,” a kid with dyed hair said.
“Do you have first-aid experience?” Yaya asked him in relief.
“No,” he answered, “I just watch a lot of YouTube.” 
Yaya had no idea if he was joking or not. Either way, she was more than happy to abdicate her position as Boone’s paramedic if somebody else was more qualified. And by the sound of heaving coming from the other side of the lab, Zeke was done giving medical advice.
The blue-haired student knelt by Yaya and rummaged through the first-aid kit. “So, we should probably use a little of this saline rinse on the area just to make sure it’s clean. Then we’ll put the gauze on, and add some pressure to stop any internal bleeding. You can use these gloves,” he said, handing her a sterile package.
“Do you want to do it?” she asked hopefully.
“I don’t touch blood,” he stated without a hint of irony.
Yaya glanced up at the other students, but none of them stepped forward to volunteer. She took a deep sigh. “Okay. I guess maybe I should wash my hands first?”
The boy shrugged. “Couldn’t hurt.”
Yaya stepped toward the sink in the lab, but of course there was no running water. She wiped her hands with antiseptic from the first-aid kit then wrestled her fingers into the gloves. She wasn’t extremely squeamish, but she didn’t want to do anything that might make Boone worse. Covering his wound with a bandage was one thing, but putting her palms over it and pressing down and … 
She couldn’t think this way anymore. She had to dive in.
She looked down at Boone, remembered how kind he had always been to her, and took a deep breath.
“All right,” she said, “let’s do this.”


***

“All we can do now is pray for Yaya and everybody up in Fairbanks with her.” Some part of Rachel’s brain wanted to respond to Angel’s words, but all her energy was focused on keeping her heart beating, her lungs working, and her entire body from collapsing.
All we can do is pray. Angel’s platitudes, well-meaning though they were, sounded hollow and empty as they rattled around and echoed through Rachel’s soul. All we can do is pray? Had Rachel stopped praying for even a second since her daughter left for school last fall? If a mother’s prayers could protect a child from harm, Yaya was destined to be the most blessed, most protected, most covered young woman on the planet.
Unless everything the Bible said about prayer and faith was a complete lie?
“Priyah, honey,” Angel cooed, “Why don’t you go find something for Mrs. Winters to drink. Maybe check the kettle for some tea or see if there’s something in the fridge.”
If Caleb were here, he wouldn’t want anyone opening the fridge. He’d want to keep it sealed and shut and cool for as long as possible. But Rachel didn’t have the mental capacity to care or the physical energy to tell Priyah she wasn’t thirsty.
A short moment later, Rachel was sipping a mug of weak, tepid tea, not because she was thirsty but because she didn’t want Angel to keep pestering her.
How long since they checked the radio? She needed to learn more about the situation in Fairbanks. But according to the wall clock, only ten minutes had passed since receiving that terrible, cryptic message. It didn’t make sense. She’d been sitting here for hours in her fear and in her worry. 
No, not hours. Days. 
Weeks.
She’d been stewing in her worry since the moment Yaya was born. And now, she had no idea where her daughter was or if she was even …
Cammy’s bark interrupted her thoughts and startled Rachel out of the dark places where her soul had begun to teeter.
“Is that someone outside?” Angel asked.
Rachel couldn’t find the strength to respond but simply shrugged. She knew Angel was trying to be a good friend, but her attention was smothering. Not only that, but it was distracting Rachel from focusing on all the terrible things that might be happening in Fairbanks if the power wasn’t restored. How would the college feed all those hungry college kids? How would they keep them warm and safe? The campus must have some kind of generator, right? But if the generators weren’t working …
And what if nobody’s cars would start up there? All the staff would be at their homes. Who was going to take care of all those kids?
“I’ll go see what’s out there.” Angel stood up.
Cammy started barking even more frantically and let out a high-pitched whine. Rachel turned and saw Angel staring out the window as if a beheaded John the Baptist were on the doorstep.
“Who is it?” Rachel asked, certain that if she stood up she would spot a trooper at her front door, coming to tell her that Yaya was dead.
“It’s that man,” Angel whispered. “The one with the scar. He’s got …”
A blast of cold air chilled the entire house when Griz burst through the front door, carrying a limp body in his arms.
“Dani?” Rachel jumped up.
“I saw her a little over a mile from here,” Griz said, stooping as he came in from the arctic entrance. Rachel and Angel followed him as he carried Dani into the living room, tracking snow and gravel behind him. “Found her in the snow. Lost a lot of heat.” Without waiting for any instruction, he laid her on the couch closest to the fire. He grabbed a throw blanket and wrapped it over her. 
Angel helped him straighten it out, and Rachel immediately examined the unconscious woman. Dani’s face was a pale, bluish gray. Her lips were split and cracked open, with flecks of dried blood around her mouth. 
But that’s not what made her gasp. 
“The baby!” she exclaimed. “Where’s Leo?” 
That’s when she heard the faint cry coming from within Griz’s coat. He unzipped it, revealing Dani’s six-month-old foster baby fastened to his chest with Griz’s suspenders criss-crossed to hold him in place.
Rachel’s entire body flooded with relief. Angel, who possessed more maternal instinct than anybody else Rachel had met in her entire life, rushed forward and took the child from him, feeling his hands and forehead.
“He’s still warm,” she breathed gratefully.
Angel grabbed an old flannel Tic had forgotten on the couch and wrapped him up. 
“Might have got himself a bit jostled when she fell,” Griz said, “but I checked his pupils. They’re dilating just fine.”
“I told her to come here,” Rachel gasped. With the realization came a growing sense of horror. “She was worried about the heat, so I told her the next time the power went out to bring Leo over here …” 
Was Dani’s car amongst the vehicles in town that wouldn’t start? Had she tried to walk and gotten injured on the way? 
The entire scenario was too horrific to fathom. If Dani had started off for Rachel’s first thing in the morning and somehow slipped or gotten injured, she could have been exposed to the elements for hours before Griz found her. How long had she been lying in the snow before she passed out? How long was she calling for help, but with no neighbors nearby and all the cell phones down … 
Rachel bristled when she touched Dani’s cold cheek with the back of her hand. Just yesterday, the two women had been busy working at the pantry, stocking shelves, talking about Leo, and now …
“We need to get her coat off,” Griz ordered. “All that snow and ice is sucking the heat straight out of her core.”
Rachel tried to work Dani’s arm out of its sleeve, but her entire limb was stiff and unyielding, almost as if rigor mortis had set in early. For a second, she had to double-check that her young friend was, in fact, still alive. Dani’s breathing was faint and shallow, but at least it hadn’t stopped.
“Not that way,” Griz snapped, stepping forward. “We’ve got to keep her as still as we can. She’s got a nasty cut on the back of her head. I don’t know what else she hurt.”
“But her coat’s too stiff to move,” Rachel observed, not wanting to mention that Dani herself was, too.
“I know,” Griz said. “We need to cut it off.”
Rachel stared at Dani’s thick North Face coat. Scissors wouldn’t even scratch a seam. She felt completely lost until an idea set in. “Priyah,” she said, turning to the young girl, “go knock on Tic’s door and tell him to bring me up the garden shears from the basement.” 
“What in the world happened?” Angel whispered as she watched her daughter race timidly up the stairs.
“From the looks of it,” Griz explained, “she fell and hit the back of her head on the ice.” 
That’s when Rachel first noticed the blood. She tried not to show her shock. She had to stay composed. Had to help Dani.
“Did something to her ankle too,” Griz continued. “See here? Whatever it was, she couldn’t get up. Based on the marks in the snow, she focused all her attention on keeping the baby warm before she passed out. Made sure he was completely zipped inside her coat. Good quality coat, too,” Griz observed. “Probably saved the little guy’s life.”
Nobody commented on what Griz didn’t say. 
That coat may have saved baby Leo from dying from exposure, but what about Dani?
 


12. CRUMBLING

“Okay, brother, we’ve reached the moment of truth.”
Caleb clenched and unclenched his fists nervously as Dev clamped a wire to the 12-volt battery. 
“It’s not sacrilegious to lay hands on a piece of machinery, is it?” Dev asked. His smile did little to cover up the intensity of the moment. 
For the past half a day, Caleb had been in the KWJD studio helping Dev gather tools to assemble a makeshift shortwave receiver. That way, they could at least tap into some of the emergency frequencies. The transmission system was completely toast in this blackout, meaning the Christian station was off the air indefinitely, at least until some semblance of power and normalcy returned. But if this rigged-up contraption worked, they’d be able to listen in on emergency chatter and hopefully get an idea of what was happening around the state.
Caleb knew his wife would be at home worrying herself sick about Yaya. Caleb had been anxious too, of course, but at least he had this project to keep his mind occupied for the last several hours.
His jaw was clenched as he watched Dev connect the last lead to the 12-volt battery and flip the switch. He held his breath.
Nothing.
“Please, Jesus,” Dev whispered.
He fidgeted once with the antenna tuner, and the dials lit up with that beautiful glow of real, actual electric power. The room was filled with a low persistent hum, as welcome to Caleb’s ears as the very last school bell on a Friday afternoon.
And then, just when Caleb thought there wasn’t a sweeter sound on this side of heaven, came the beautiful, garbled crackle of static. The rig was working.
Caleb was so elated he may have started whooping and hollering, or perhaps stripping down to his underwear and dancing like David before the Ark of the Lord. But Dev was still complexly absorbed in his work, his back hunched over and face set as he tuned the dials with a steady hand. 
“Okay,” he announced casually, as if completely unaware of this miraculous technological achievement. “I’m gonna look for one of the HF bands they use for emergencies.”
The needle on the signal strength meter warbled for just a moment, then shifted as if clicking into place. Caleb was certain his blood pressure was skyrocketing with anticipation, then a faint voice came through the speakers.
“This is K7AJS in Palmer.” The voice crackled with static, and both Caleb and Dev leaned forward toward the receiver. “We have relay from FEMA Alaska and scattered contact from the Lower 48. Widespread infrastructure failure confirmed.”
Caleb’s heart stopped in his chest. Time held still.
“Statewide power grid is down. Cellular networks are offline. Vehicles impaired. Assume full systems collapse. Repeat: No cell service. No Wi-Fi. No functional grid power.”
For the first time, Dev was without anything to occupy his hands, and the color drained from his face in degrees.
The transmission crackled, and Caleb worried they’d lost it, but then the voice returned. “Fairbanks reports widespread instances of phones and portable devices exploding. Casualties reported.”
Caleb felt Dev’s gaze turn to him, but he couldn’t tear his eyes away from the receiver.
“Explosions confirmed at Fort Wainwright and Eielson Air Force Base. Origin of the attack unknown. Multiple fires reported throughout the city. Emergency vehicles are unable to respond. Extent of damage and number of survivors unknown.”
“Are you okay, brother?” Caleb could barely register Dev’s words.
“Is this the same frequency Priyah’s trying to connect to with her ham radio?” He spat out the question but rushed out the door before waiting for an answer. 
He had to get home. 
He had to reach Rachel before she heard this transmission.

***

Caleb jogged the entire way home, even as his lungs burst from the cold and exertion and his huge, clunky bunny boots might as well have been two cement flippers. 
He burst into his front door, his only thought that the news from Fairbanks would destroy Rachel, and he had to be the one to tell her.
But when he saw her silently weeping next to Angel on the couch, with Tic and the Singh kids looking shell-shocked and forlorn beside them, he knew he was too late.
“Rachel,” he breathed, and she looked up at him with an expression more pained than any painting of any Madonna in the entirety of art history.
He rushed to her. “Babe, I’m so sorry. We’ll do everything we can to get her help. We’ll figure it out. You’ll see.”
“It’s too late,” Rachel sniffed. “She’s already gone.”
The words hit Caleb with the force of an avalanche. He always trusted in his wife’s intuition, and had seen it proven right on multiple occasions, but this …
No, he refused to accept that. 
“She’s not,” he coaxed, begging with all his soul to get through to his wife, pleading with all his might that this was the one time in their entire marriage that her gift of discernment was wrong. “She’s not,” he repeated, trying to make his voice sound stronger. More convincing. 
Rachel had to be mistaken. Surely God wouldn’t …
“She died just a few minutes ago.” Rachel let out a stifled sob, and that’s when Caleb’s tunnel vision began to clear up enough that he noticed others in the room as well. 
A baby in Angel’s arms. Griz standing in the center of the room. And a woman on the couch.
A woman he knew.
Oh, no.
“Is that …” He didn’t want to complete the sentence.
Griz took a step forward. “She fell in the snow. We did what we could.” He lowered his gaze and shook his head.
