Caleb had never experienced the type of emotional whiplash he was experiencing right now. First Fairbanks, then rushing home to Rachel. 
And now Dani? A young mother on his couch … Dead? And her foster son …
“The baby’s okay?” Caleb asked, reaching out to the infant in Angel’s arms. 
Leo squirmed in his hands and reached to grab his beard. Babies were always going for Caleb’s beard, but this time there was no joy or laughter in the gesture.
He glanced down at Leo’s mom. “You’re sure that she’s …”
Griz nodded gravely. “A few minutes ago.”
So that’s why the children looked so horrified. That’s why Rachel was crying, and Angel too, now that he could see more clearly.
But did that mean … Uh-oh.
He’d prayed to reach home before Rachel heard the news from Fairbanks. Maybe he’d been wrong to wish so hard.
He glanced at Angel, who right now seemed the less broken of the two women. “Did you end up getting the radio to work?”
Angel nodded her head. “We got through this morning. They didn’t tell us much, just to conserve the battery and check in every few hours. We would have tried again, but then …” She gestured to the entire room and shrugged. 
So, they didn’t know. And now Caleb was here, and now with a friend of theirs dead on the couch and her tiny baby here in his arms, completely oblivious, he was going to have to tell them all.
He thought for a moment about sending the kids away. He thought for a moment about talking to Rachel alone. But he couldn’t. As of today, the children were adults. And everyone here was in this tragedy together.
“I was at the station with Dev,” he began, but before he could continue, there was a knock at the door. Without waiting for an answer, Dev let himself in. He was a few years younger than Caleb but was panting hard from their sprint. 
“I’m here,” he said but didn’t seem to be phased by the infant in Caleb’s arms or the tears on his wife’s face. 
“I was just going to tell them,” Caleb explained.
Rachel jerked her head up, and he could see the way the little indent of her throat quivered. “Tell us what?” she demanded. 
Her voice didn’t sound like hers at all. Even the dog seemed to notice, and Cammy took a few timid steps forward and sniffed her.
He couldn’t stand here and make them wait. This wasn’t like tearing off a Band-Aid, though. This was like dropping an atomic bomb right in the center of their living room.
“Dev and I got the radio rig working,” he told them. “We picked up an emergency broadcast from FEMA. Both bases in Fairbanks were bombed. Cell phones and devices there detonated, there are fires all over the city, and no emergency vehicles are working.”

***

Tic had never heard anyone, let alone his own mother, cry that way. No, it wasn’t a cry. It was like a wail, but more intense. For a moment, it sounded as if the devil himself had wrapped himself in her lungs and she was trying to exorcise him and an army of demons with nothing but her voice.
Instinctively, he turned to his dad, who seemed to note how worried he was and gave him a grim nod. 
Tic went to his mom, knelt down by her feet, and threw his arms around her waist. It was a strange and intimate posture. Benji was watching, and Tic knew he was way too old for this type of display, but it was all he could think to do. 
Besides, the gesture wasn’t for him.
It was for his mom.
She leaned her head over him as if her body would collapse if he didn’t hold her up. In no time at all, his shoulder was wet from her tears. Loud, shaking breaths racked her whole body, and Tic wondered if she was struggling to breathe. Did they need to do anything to help her? Was this some kind of asthma flare-up? A heart attack?
Again he looked to his father. Dad’s expression was deadly serious, but not afraid.
For several moments, time didn’t exist. The Singhs didn’t exist. Neither did Griz or the baby or — most surprisingly of all — the dead woman lying just a few feet away from all of them on the couch. In that moment, Tic was aware of a permanent shift. He wasn’t a child needing comfort from his mother anymore.
He never would be again.
For sixteen years, she’d fed him and loved him and fussed over him. She’d bandaged his scraped knees and kissed his cuts and watched cartoons with him while he recovered from his tonsillectomy and rushed onto the basketball court in the middle of a game the night his knee decided to pop completely out of its socket.
It would never be like that again. Now, it was his turn to take care of her.
“I know, Mom. I know.” He didn’t really understand what he was saying or why. He just knew it was what she needed to hear. 
“I know,” he repeated. “I’m here. It’s okay.”
Except it wasn’t okay. Nothing was okay. A woman had frozen to death out in the cold, unable to call for help because all the cell phones were dead. Tic’s sister was trapped in Fairbanks, the city was on fire, but the scariest part wasn’t Yaya out there on her own but the way his mother had simply crumpled under the news. 
When he glanced up long enough to look into her face, she looked as old as Grandma. Or even worse.
“Dad?” His voice quivered, but he didn’t know exactly what he was asking. 
The next moment, Dad was there, next to Tic on the floor by Mom’s feet. He held her hand in his. He didn’t say anything, but Tic thought he heard the smallest of whines.
It might have come from the dog. Cammy was as stressed and anxious as the rest of them. But Tic was pretty sure the sound came from his father, whose lips were pressed together so tightly his entire jaw trembled.
“Dad?” he asked again, but Dad just shook his head.
There were no words. Not now.
Soon they would have to speak. Tic would have to ask him what it all meant, what was going to happen, what would become of their family. But for right now, his only job was to sit here and let his mom lean her entire body against him. He was strong, and he would hold her up. Even if the entire house — the entire world — crumbled down around them, he would be here with her.
So help him God.  


13. FIRE AND FLAMES

The world hung suspended in gloomy twilight. Candlelight flickered on the walls, illuminating their living room with an eerie orange glow, reminding Rachel of some macabre mixture of Halloween decorations and what she’d expect to see inside a Gothic cathedral.
According to the wall clock, it wasn’t even 5:00 yet, not even time for Dev’s radio devotions on KJWD to start. But of course, there’d be no broadcast tonight.
Would there ever be again?
It didn’t make sense. None of this made sense. Twenty-four hours ago, Rachel was worrying about her daughter, praying over her midterms, asking God to keep Yaya healthy and sustain her through her second semester of college. Tic was at basketball practice, giving Rachel even more things to worry about, more things to add to her prayer list: that he wouldn’t reinjure his knee, that his season wouldn’t get cut short like it had last year after two of their starting players got expelled.
And now this. A cold, dark house. The world in chaos. A crying baby Angel was trying to feed with a spoon since they had formula in their home but no bottles.
Neither her dog nor her son had left Rachel’s side all afternoon, and she worried that the stress was making Cammy even more neurotic and forcing Tic to assume far more emotional responsibility than a boy his age should have to endure.
On the one hand, millions of individuals have lived through wars or bombings or plagues or devastating natural disasters. On the other hand, nobody in all of human history, at least until today, had ever lived through a technological failure of this magnitude.
They still didn’t know what was behind any of it, or if the scope was national or global. Dev had checked in on Priyah’s ham radio a few times, but he didn’t want to waste battery power, and none of their calls had revealed any significant news. The power was still out around the entire nation. Fairbanks was still burning, with no one to tell them or even estimate the extent of the damage, the number of dead, or the desperate condition of the survivors. Even something as simple as tracking the Fairbanks weather, a habit Rachel had picked up during Yaya’s very first week of school, was now impossible. With no phones, no internet, no public broadcasting services, they were completely isolated and cut off from everybody.
While Angel fed the baby, or at least tried to, Dev and Priyah were tinkering with the radio. Benji was trekking in and out, carrying in stacks of wood to keep their home heated.
And Caleb, her beloved husband of twenty-one years, was completely delusional.
“I’m going to get Yaya,” he stated with manic energy as he paced the entire length of the house. “I’m going to get her, and I am going to bring her back home.”
Tic looked up at Mom with heartbreaking hopefulness in his expression. Tic, her baby boy, who had always idolized Caleb, who loved and fiercely protected his sister, whose life and childhood would never be the same after today. 
Tic, who shouldn’t have to witness his father go completely crazy before his very eyes.
“You’re not going to Fairbanks,” Rachel stated. It had always been their policy not to fight in front of the kids, let alone a house full of guests. But that rule didn’t seem to make particular sense at the moment, especially given the fact that Caleb was completely delusional. 
“The city is burning,” Caleb argued, as if Rachel might have forgotten. “We have no idea what’s going on at the campus. Once they run out of basic resources, it’s going to be a bloodbath. We’re not leaving our daughter stranded in a big city in the middle of the apocalypse.”
Rachel didn’t even know where to begin. First of all, this wasn’t an apocalypse, at least not in the biblical sense. Nowhere in the Book of Revelation did it even come close to mentioning exploding cell phones. 
But more importantly, there was no way for Caleb to get to Fairbanks. They had confirmed with Griz, the Singhs, and neighbors on both sides of their property. All the vehicles were completely useless, heavy lawn ornaments. Nobody could drive anywhere, and nobody could walk from here to Fairbanks and back in the middle of winter. Especially not someone in Caleb’s condition.
Sure, he lifted weights and a couple years ago trained to complete a 5K fun run. But he fell into that category most doctors would euphemistically describe as overweight. And let’s be honest. He might look like an Alaskan mountain man with his flannels and Carhartts and long, unkempt beard, but he was a middle-school teacher, not a forest ranger or a search and rescue professional or some superhero father like you’d see in a movie. Aside from going hunting with a few guys from church to bring home a caribou or two, he wasn’t even really all that outdoorsy.
She wished that Dev would chime in and talk some sense into his best friend, but he was so fixated on his daughter’s radio he probably couldn’t hear a word they were saying.
Caleb stopped pacing long enough to stare at Angel in the kitchen. She was still trying to spoon milk into the baby’s mouth while Leo fussed and wiggled. By the looks of it, the pair were getting more formula on Angel’s shirt than in his belly.
“Don’t we have a baby bottle or two left over from that pregnancy center fundraiser?” Caleb asked.
Rachel was relieved to find her husband talking about something other than an impossible 250-mile trek across the state in the dead of winter. It was almost refreshing to think of something as mundane as misplaced clutter.
“I might have put them in one of the drawers in the closet, or they could be in that pile of stuff in the basement I’ve been meaning to take to the church.”
“Do you want me to go look?” Tic asked.
At the sound of his voice, Priyah looked up from her electronics to cast him a furtive glance, while a flush of red crept up her cheeks. Rachel’s heart wanted to split in two when she tried to imagine the type of earth that children like Tic and Priyah were now destined to come of age in. 
Slowly, she shook her head. “No,” she told Tic. “I wouldn’t even know where to have you start looking.” What she didn’t want to tell him was that letting him out of her sight might destroy her.
“I’ll go check the closet,” Caleb offered. “I’ve got some things to put together upstairs anyway.”
Rachel stared at him blankly. “What things?”
When she saw the resolve in her husband’s eyes, she wondered why God didn’t just suck out the remaining breath from her lungs right now. It would certainly be easier and far more merciful than this horrible, prolonged torture of an existence.
“I need to pack,” Caleb told her, halfway up the stairs. “I already told you. I’m going to Fairbanks, and I’m going to bring our daughter home.”

***

“So, a bunch of us were talking,” Lilly said. “They want to go back to the dorms and find blankets and things before it gets dark.”
Yaya could barely process what her friend was blabbering on about. “Yeah, okay.”
“Do you wanna come with us, or do you wanna stay here and I’ll bring you back some stuff?”
“That sounds good.” Yaya looked up when she realized Lilly didn’t respond. “What?” 
Lilly looked down at Boone, asleep on the floor where they’d found him. His wound was bandaged, and there weren’t any more signs of visible bleeding. He’d woken up several hours earlier and assured her his injuries weren’t as bad as they looked. Still, she wasn’t going to leave his side.
“Are you sure you’re okay?” Lilly asked. “I mean, you haven’t gotten up from this spot since we found him. You haven’t moved, you haven’t eaten …”
“What’s there to eat?” Yaya didn’t mean to snap, but it was as if her friend was telling her to simply click her heels together three times and wish that today had never happened.
“I told you, there are people in the cafeteria giving out bag lunches. I brought you one, and you haven’t touched it.”
Yaya eyed the paper sack on the floor, only vaguely remembering when and how it appeared at her side. “I’m not hungry,” she murmured.
Lilly lowered her voice. “Look, I know this Boone guy’s important to you. And I never wanted to outright ask you, but I know you were meeting him late at night and maybe there was more to it than …”
“There wasn’t anything like that,” Yaya interrupted impatiently. What was Lilly thinking? Had PTSD made her go crazy?
“Okay.” Lilly raised her hand conciliatorily. “I’m just saying, it’s great you’re looking after him, but you need to look after yourself too. Have you even used the bathroom?”
Yaya shook her head. “I haven’t had anything to drink.”
“Well, that’s an even bigger problem. Come on, we’ve been melting snow all day.” She stood up and a moment later returned with a lab flask filled with something that looked similar to water.
“Is it clean?” Yaya asked suspiciously.
Lilly shrugged. “Couple people built a small fire outside to try to boil it, but it’s too cold. We’ve been drinking it all day and no one’s gotten sick yet.”
The water was lukewarm and reeked of ash. Normally, the first sip would have been enough to make Yaya spit it out, but she was thirstier than she realized, and thankful that she had a friend watching out for her.
“Good.” Lilly rewarded her with a tired smile. “Okay, so when you need it, here’s the bathroom situation. Guys are peeing outside. Girls are using the girls’ room for number one. The boys’ room is for anybody to go number two. The people at the cafeteria will be doing bag lunches again in the morning, and they’ll try to give everyone enough to last the day. Someone mentioned the Red Cross, so by this time tomorrow if we’re still here there will be cots and real water and that kind of stuff. Just pretend we were duped into buying tickets to one of those horrible music festivals and ended up having to sleep in FEMA tents.”
Yaya loved her friend for trying so hard to keep a positive attitude. She knew none of this was easy on Lilly either, especially since she must still be worried sick about her boyfriend. “No word yet from Kai?” she asked.
Lilly’s smile vanished. “No.”
“Everything’s crazy around here.” Yaya tried to come up with a comforting lie. “Just wait, you’ll go to the dorms to pick up some blankets and he’ll be there scrolling his phone like nothing happened.”
The girls shared an exhausted, pitiful chuckle, then Lilly turned toward the exit. “Okay, I’ll go grab us some blankets and pillows. And when I come back, I want the food in that brown bag to be in your belly.”
“Yes, Mother.” Yaya had been trying to make a joke, but her throat seized up at the word. She turned her gaze back toward Boone, choking back her tears, finding herself praying to a God she didn’t think she still believed in.

***

Caleb had never seen his wife this angry or this anxious, not even that time when a preteen Tic sneaked onto a friend’s four-wheeler without parental permission (or a helmet), crashed into a tree, and gave himself a concussion.
She should understand. This was the woman who had raced onto the court in the middle of a basketball game when Tic’s knee popped out of place. Caleb hadn’t even noticed it happen. He was sitting there in the bleachers watching Tic’s team lose terribly. Next thing he knew, his wife was on the court, bent over their injured son.
This was the same woman who had driven 250 miles to take their daughter to the clinic when she got strep throat last semester. Yaya could have literally walked to the campus health center in five minutes, but Rachel — unable to stand the idea of her daughter being sick away from home — had packed up five freezer bags of homemade chicken soup, called to set up Yaya’s appointment for her, and made the drive to Fairbanks.
If anyone could understand Caleb’s need to find their daughter, it should be her.
“Population centers are going to be deadly,” he tried to explain. “Riots and looting, and that’s just the beginning.” That’s why he had to go rescue their daughter.
But Rachel was adamant, more stubborn and upset than he’d ever seen her before, without a trace remaining of his teasing, joking wife. “If you mention walking to Fairbanks one more time, I’m burning your bunny boots in the fire.”
When he mentioned traveling barefoot, she didn’t even realize he was joking.
“Don’t forget,” she glowered, “I know the code to the gun safe.”
“So what are you gonna do?” he asked, wanting to laugh at the absurdity of this conversation. “Shoot me in the knee? You don’t even know how to fire that thing.”
“I’ll figure it out,” she answered with deadly seriousness.
 They argued for over an hour until Griz said he needed help outside. At first, Caleb assumed this was a pity ploy designed to give him a break from the yelling, except it was anything but.
They needed to find a way to bury Dani. 
Who would have thought that pounding nails while building a makeshift coffin could be so cathartic? 
While Caleb hammered away, his breath freezing his entire beard, Griz set to work building a large fire at the farthest corner of the Winters’ back yard. 
“We’ll need to keep it going all night to give the ground a chance to thaw,” Griz explained. “The five of us men will take turns tending it.”
Caleb was going to remind him there were only three men here. Then he realized Griz had included both Tic and Benji in his counting. 
Without ever having had the actual discussion or extending an official invitation, it was understood that the Singhs would stay here. People could start in the rooms upstairs, but if those got too cold they could move their mattresses and blankets into the living room.
What still hadn’t been decided, or at least not agreed upon, was whether Caleb was going to Fairbanks to get Yaya. Dev had picked up a few more FEMA reports on the radio. All the major military bases in Alaska were destroyed, although it was still unclear who had fired on them. Russia was a likely culprit, but there were still no official reports. Canada and the Lower 48 were off-grid, and cell phones had exploded in all their big cities just like in Alaska.
An even more alarming layer to the terror was reports of planes everywhere – dozens of them – crashing into population centers. As hard as it was being cut off in the middle of nowhere, Caleb had to assume it was safer than being almost anywhere else.
Which was exactly why he needed to get Yaya. His argument with Rachel had strung along late enough that nothing could happen until morning, but when the sun rose, he was going to be on his way. 
If he had suspected that Rachel could even figure out how to use his gun, let alone have the fortitude to fire it, he might have paused given the intensity in her expression when she made her threat. But he knew his wife. She didn’t even watch gunfights in movies but shut her eyes and begged Caleb to turn down the volume.
Unfortunately, that led Caleb to an entirely new set of worries. If he headed to Fairbanks, that left Rachel and Tic alone. The Singhs would be here, but Dev was definitely more of the turn-the-other-cheek kind of Christian than the don’t-tread-on-me-or-I’ll-shoot-you-in-the-face type that were so common out here in rural Alaska. Angel was as small and unassuming as Rachel, and now they also had Dani’s baby to care for, leaving everyone even more vulnerable.
He took a pause from his work and walked toward Griz, who sat on an overturned bucket staring at the blazing fire he’d built.
“Hey Griz, let me ask you a question.”
Griz turned to him silently.
“I want you to do me a favor. A big one.”
Griz simply lifted his chin in an approximation of a nod.
“I’m leaving for Fairbanks tomorrow to bring Yaya home. While I’m gone, I want you to stay here and help take care of my wife and house.”
Griz was silent. His working eye turned back to gaze at the fire that was thawing the earth so they could bury their neighbor. 
If ever there was a time for friends to help each other out, wasn’t this it?
“We’ve got food and heat,” Caleb told him, thinking of every positive argument that might convince him to stay. “And I have a hand pump so we can get well water.” He continued to speak, if for no other reason than to fill the silence.
When Griz finally responded, his voice was as low as the crackling of the wood on the flame. “My uncle used to say something.” 
He paused for so long Caleb wondered if more was coming. 
Finally, Griz stated, “It takes only one to shoot the moose, but two to carry it home.”
Usually, Caleb was riveted on those rare times when Griz spoke about his Alaska native heritage or mentioned anything about his past. But today he didn’t have the patience for riddles. 
“So … you’ll stay here while I’m gone?” he pressed.
Griz shook his head gravely. “You won’t make it. It’s a fool’s mission.”
Caleb was taken aback, as shocked as if the sparks flying around them had set his beard ablaze. “I’m going to get my daughter. No matter what it takes, I have to bring her home.” 
Why did he have to keep on repeating himself like this to everyone around him? Maybe Rachel could never understand, but he was certain that Griz …
His old friend held up his hand to interrupt Caleb’s mounting monologue. 
“I won’t stay here.” He continued to stare straight into the fire, the flames casting shadows that danced across his scarred face. “But I will help you.”
Caleb was so frustrated by Griz’s inability to answer a single question that he wanted to scream.
Then Griz turned to him with a warm expression. “I’m going with you.”
Caleb could only stare back at him, wondering if he’d heard correctly.
“I know the road,” Griz continued. “I can hunt. I’ve got cold-weather equipment. You can’t make it alone. But together, we can bring your daughter home.”

***

“No. Absolutely not. Positively not. No way on God’s green earth. The answer is no.” Rachel was pacing the room while her husband sat dejected on their bed.
What was Caleb thinking? He’d truly and actually lost his mind. It was the trauma of the day. Call it PTSD or something. Caleb was genuinely, completely, and in all other ways insane.
“I told you, if Griz and I go together …”
“Griz?” She wanted to spit his name. “You know I respect him. We owe Tic’s life to that man, for crying out loud. But there are dozens of reasons a man like that becomes a hermit who shows himself in public once a season or less, and none of them are good.”
“You know he’s retired from the Marines.”
“I know he said he’s retired from the Marines,” Rachel corrected. “But seriously. The man is a complete recluse, and not in the normal rural-Alaska kind of way. It’s not right. There’s something wrong …”
“He saved Tic’s life,” Caleb interrupted, as if Rachel ever needed to be reminded. There was no way she could look at that scar on Griz’s face, the spot where his eye should be, and not remember what happened.
“Yes, he saved Tic’s life,” she conceded. “I thank God every single day for that. But that doesn’t mean we know him. What’s next? He saved Tic all those years ago, so now we name him the executor of our estate? We make him an authorized user on our credit cards? We let him adopt our son?”
Caleb stared at her as if she had truly lost the plot. Perhaps she had.
Rachel took a deep breath and tried again. “Listen, there is no one in the world more worried for Yaya right now than me.” 
Seeing Caleb raise his hand in protest, she preemptively interrupted his arguments. “Call me narrow-minded if you want. But there is nobody, literally nobody in the entire world, who will worry more than a mother. I get that you love Yaya. I get that you want to do anything in your power to protect her. I understand that. But what you don’t understand is that this child was in my womb. Our bloodstreams were connected. Any emotion I experienced, any bite of food I ate, any molecule of oxygen I breathed, it was all shared with her. My life, my cells, my being literally went into that little baby. And the day she left home …” Here Rachel’s voice caught, and she had to recenter herself.
“The day she left home, I felt like this invisible umbilical cord that had kept us connected for the first eighteen years of her life was yanked out of my uterus so brutally and so violently it’s a wonder I didn’t hemorrhage to death right then.”
Caleb tried to open his mouth, but she wasn’t finished.
“I know you love Yaya. We both do. But I have absolutely no way to say this any clearer than I am right now. You will not survive a trip to Fairbanks in these conditions. If you try it, you’ll die from the cold. And then Tic and I will be left here alone, and Yaya will still …”
This time, she didn’t try to stop the sob that welled its way up from the core of her being.
Caleb was there, wrapping his arms around her, and she wondered at the absurdity of marriage. Here he was, this monster who was about to kill her by making plans to leave, a man she only half-jokingly threatened to shoot if he tried to walk away. And yet his arms were the only possible comfort in the entire universe.
“I don’t know what else to do,” Caleb whispered into her hair. He pulled back and stared into her eyes. “Our daughter needs me. She needs us, her family. What are we supposed to do? Just sit around worrying about her until the stress literally kills us both?”
Yes, that had basically been Rachel’s plan. Either that or wait for the power to be miraculously restored, for life to go back to how it was meant to be.
“Listen.” Caleb rubbed her arms, as if he wanted to physically transfer his love and comfort and strength into her body through the movement. “I will never forget the day you gave birth to our beautiful baby girl. You were covered in sweat and your voice was raw from screaming, and I will always remember the look on your face when I told you what you’d shouted at that nurse.”
Rachel couldn’t help but return her husband’s smile, even though her throat ached from suppressed sobs and her entire soul exhausted from fear.
“And when she came, she was so perfect, and she was so beautiful, and I was so proud of you, and I was so scared I was going to do something wrong. Remember how freaked out I was to even hold her? I was convinced I’d drop her.”
Rachel could only nod.
“But then the nurse showed me what to do and put this little unblemished thing in my arms. And this perfect little child that we created together hadn’t breathed in a single speck of pollution or encountered a single germ in her entire life, and my heart was so full even though I was still so scared, and I told God I would do anything to protect her.” He lowered his voice and leveled his gaze. “Anything.”
Rachel rested her head against Caleb’s chest, resisting her body’s urge to let go, to give in. He couldn’t do this. He couldn’t leave. 
On the one hand, she understood what he was saying completely. On the other hand, Caleb’s plan was as ridiculous as if their daughter had gotten herself stranded on Mars and he’d announced he was going to charter a private jet to bring her back.
“You can’t go,” she whispered faintly.
“Well, nothing is happening tonight.”
Rachel’s body relaxed just a bit, and relief flooded her with warmth in spite of how cold their bedroom had grown.
“The guys and I are going to take shifts watching the fire,” Caleb said. “Tic and Benji are out there now, then they’ll come switch with me. I doubt I’m going to get any sleep, but I may as well try. Tomorrow, we’ll figure out what’s happening. Maybe we’ll get more news on the radio.”
Rachel clung to his words the same way Cammy would hang onto her favorite chew toy during a raucous game of tug. Caleb realized it was pointless to leave this evening, which meant that tomorrow he’d have time to come to his senses and give up this ridiculous plan altogether.
Except something was shifting inside Rachel’s chest. She didn’t know how or why, but she knew the gentle tug of intuition when she felt it. Was God telling her to be ready to surrender Caleb to the unknown road ahead?
While her husband changed into his pajamas, she wondered if she was going to have to go through their entire argument all over again, only this time with God Almighty himself. And in that case, it was even less likely for Rachel to end up getting her way.
Downstairs, the house was silent. On their bed, Cammy was curled up by Rachel’s pillow.
Rachel slid under the covers, tucking herself and Cammy under the extra-thick blankets they’d pulled up from storage. 
God, she prayed, you know I want to follow and obey you. But please, Lord, either stop Caleb from trying to get to Fairbanks, or else part the waters and send some kind of miracle so he’s not trying to make the trip on foot.
Rachel didn’t know exactly what she was praying for. A hitched-up dogsled team just appearing in their driveway in the morning? Dev working his tinkering magic to make two snow machines run and have enough gas to get Caleb and Griz to Fairbanks and back? A magic carpet sounded just as plausible.
But at least they had time to figure it out. Best-case scenario: Caleb would wake up tomorrow, lean over and give her a kiss, and tell her he realized a trip to Fairbanks was impossible. 
No, scratch that. Best-case scenario was waking up in the morning, hearing on the radio that the power grid had been restored back to normal, and all vehicles and cell phones were now completely operable.
The image fueled her prayers as she did her best to drift off to sleep.

***

“You okay, bro?” Benji asked as the two boys stared at the dancing flames. 
Usually, Tic loved a good campfire. He couldn’t count the number of times he and Benji had been out here in the back yard, strumming their guitars or goofing off around the fire pit just like this.
Except not just like this, because this wasn’t a campfire. This was a funeral fire, designed solely to warm the ground enough that they could try to bury some pieced-together coffin his dad built for a woman young enough she could have been Tic’s older sister. 
He hadn’t known Dani well, but he had known her. Other than random relatives in the Lower 48 he barely remembered, nobody else in Tic’s life had ever died, certainly not like this, and certainly not in his own house. 
He poked at the firewood and shrugged. That was one thing he liked about Benji. They didn’t have to talk if they didn’t feel like it. Except tonight, Benji didn’t seem to be in the mood to shut up.
“So what do you think it was?” he asked.
“What do you mean?” was all Tic could think to say, even though he knew quite well what Benji meant.
“The attacks and stuff. You know, the bombs and cell phones.”
Tic shrugged. “Some kind of cyberattack.”
“Yeah, I know, but who was behind it? Think it was Russia? I heard they wanted to try to annex Alaska.”
“This stuff all happened in the Lower 48, too,” Tic replied. 
As much as he didn’t want to have this conversation, or any conversation, right now, he’d been wondering the same thing.
“It couldn’t just be like a solar flare though, right?” Benji went on. “Not with all the bases getting bombed or planes being flown into buildings. So there must be someone behind it.”
“I dunno, man.” Tic poked at the fire again, feeling a tiny hit of satisfaction when a log moved and crackled and sent sparks flying into the winter air.
“You think your sister’s all right?” Benji asked after a pause.
Tic shrugged again. “Yaya? Yeah. She’s smart. She’s scrappy. And she’s pretty good at keeping her cool.” He thought of the way his sister acted on the opening night of a play. She didn’t get nervous, she said, just excited, like the adrenaline in her system made her extra attentive. Wherever she was, whatever she was doing, Tic imagined her using that same degree of calm and focus to seek shelter, find warmth, and stay safe.
At least he prayed she could, as best as he was able. Prayer had always been his mother’s spiritual gift, not his. He wasn’t long-winded or eloquent, and he couldn’t remember the last time he even prayed for a full minute without getting distracted.
But still he tried his hardest, and he hoped to heaven that God would hear him now.  


14. THE DECISION

Caleb was surprised he managed any sleep at all after keeping a four-hour vigil around the fire in the backyard. Even more surprising was that the sun was already rising when he woke up.
Rachel was out of bed, and he hoped she wasn’t still mad at him over yesterday’s argument. He understood she was worried about him going to Fairbanks, but his mind was made up. Last night beside the flames, he’d prayed long and hard and asked God to show him if there was another way, if going to rescue his daughter was the suicide mission Rachel seemed to think it was.
And while he never got an assurance of success, he knew he had to try. Protecting his family was in his DNA, just like worrying about them was in Rachel’s. That was how God designed them both, and it was pointless for either of them to fight against their nature.
He’d spent some time last night talking to Griz and figuring out a plan. First things first: as soon as the ground was even remotely thawed, they’d bury Dani. Whatever they couldn’t get completely underground they’d cover with logs and twigs and gravel and dirt. The unsightly mound would remain until spring, when the grass would grow over it and the landmark would remain as a permanent scar, a permanent reminder in their yard of what they’d gone through.
Next, Caleb had a mental checklist of things to gather. Griz had high-quality cold-weather gear from his years spent as an arctic warfare specialist, so he’d be in charge of tents and sleeping bags and survival equipment. Caleb had to find the lightest clothes he could, lots of extra socks, flashlights, food, and of course both his .44 Magnum revolver and AR-10. Between both of these, he’d be prepared for any threat — the four-footed or the two-footed kind. He was leaving the AR-15 here since it was a small enough caliber that Rachel could theoretically manage the gun if necessary. Not that she’d ever been willing to touch the thing in the past, even when he begged her to learn. But God alone knew what the future held in store for her and Tic. 
God alone knew how someone like his peace-loving wife might have to harden and change in the days and weeks to come. 
Before he got out of bed, he reached for his pen and old abandoned journal in his nightstand drawer. He’d composed a good-bye letter to Rachel in his mind last night by the fire, and today he had to jot it all down. Actually, he’d composed two. One to give her before he left. 
And one to hide somewhere in the house in case the journey didn’t go as planned.
The first one was easy to write. He told her how much he loved her, how much the past 21 years had meant to him, how she’d grown ever more beautiful with each passing day. He added some of her favorite Bible verses from the Sermon on the Mount, hoping their lessons about faith and worry would carry her through whatever came next.
The second letter was infinitely harder. What do you put in a letter for your wife to read in case you never come home? I’m sorry I couldn’t save our daughter. Caleb wasn’t even sure she could survive such a devastating blow. 
In the end, he turned to the last page of his journal, then simply wrote the day’s date and Dear Rachel, I love you so much and always will. 
He dressed in layers like Griz instructed him. A thin underlayer, nothing that would get too wet with sweat. Something heavy on top but not too bulky since they’d be carrying rucksacks and tents and all their gear on their backs the entire trip.
He was about to head downstairs when Rachel cautiously opened their door. “I heard you up,” she said, her voice weak and low.
He took her into his arms, and she sank against his chest. “You’re going?” It wasn’t a question so much as a declaration. The fact that she was resigned to his leaving didn’t bring him any relief, only sorrow.
He laid his chin against the top of her head, urging his body to remember everything about this moment. The feel of her cotton hoodie. The way her legs pressed up against his. 
“Sometimes I forget just how short you are,” he said, and they both laughed sadly.
“Dev and Griz are out back,” she told him. “They’re going to dig up what they can, then start another fire before …” She didn’t finish the thought. She didn’t have to.
“Is Tic awake?” Caleb asked. 
“No, I thought I’d let him and Benji try to sleep in as long as they could.”
“Good idea.”
These were the words that were spoken between them this morning. These were the words that would ring in Caleb’s ears on the miles and miles of road ahead of him.
But these weren’t the words they meant to say.
Thankfully, Caleb knew his wife well enough that he could translate the silence, and he was convinced she could too. When Caleb asked about Tic, he was telling his wife that she had to let her son grow up now. Tic would be the man of the house, and instead of always rushing to be the one to protect him, she had to learn to let him take care of her.
When Rachel talked about the funeral they would hold for Dani, she wasn’t just mourning a friend she’d lost. She was telling Caleb how terrified she was that he would walk away today and never come home, and they wouldn’t even have a mound of dirt in the backyard to remember him by.
“I love you,” he said, and in those three words were years of memories: The first time they met when she was that cute little assistant working at the Christian bookstore in Anchorage and he was a young, impassioned Bible college student trying to track down a hard-to-find title. The next Sunday he happened to show up at the same church she happened to mention she attended, and they happened to recognize each other and smile.
That smile had warmed Caleb’s soul more than the twenty-four-hour sunlight on the summer solstice ever could.
“I love you, too,” she whispered, her words adding onto the memories: Their Bible debates in the singles Sunday school class they both happened to join. Caleb’s painfully awkward declaration before they’d even gone on their first date. Rachel’s shy but enthusiastic response. Their wedding six months later, right here in her parents’ home in Glennallen. The joys and trials and hardships of their growing family. And every single moment of their lives they’d spent together since then. 
Thinking of the morning ahead — the burial, the packing, the preparations that still had to take place — Caleb wondered if this was the last private moment he’d ever share with his wife. He knew God was calling him to this mission, but he wasn’t naïve enough to believe that meant he was promised a safe return or Rachel a happy ending.
There was more he wanted to say. More he wanted to remember.
But the sun was rising in the pale pink winter sky, and the miles stretched out before him.
It was going to be a long day.

***

Caring for baby Leo was a welcome distraction for Rachel while Angel fussed in her kitchen, pulling together ingredients for breakfast. If Rachel had been cooking, she’d settle again on oatmeal, but if it made Angel feel useful she could at least try for something more creative. 
And seeing as how Angel was the perfect wife, homeschool mom, and homemaker, maybe Rachel shouldn’t underestimate her.
Leo had been happy all morning, ever since Dev had taken the snow machine to Dani’s house and come home with plenty of bottles and diapers. Rachel sat amazed at the way someone so small and tiny could hold up that big head of his and look around the room with those bright, wide eyes that didn’t miss anything.
She watched him with a melancholy sense of awe and nostalgia. Had Yaya and Tic truly ever been this small?
Where had the time gone?
“I can’t even picture my kids at this age,” Rachel confessed. 
“I know what you mean,” Angel agreed, then asked somewhat distractedly, “You have potatoes somewhere?”
“In the pantry.” Rachel was surprised when her friend started pulling out potatoes, onions, garlic … even a couple turnips.
“What are you making with that?” she asked.
“I think I’ll do a breakfast hash. Can you show me where you keep your cutting boards?”
Twenty minutes later, the smell of garlic and onion mingled with the scent of sizzling bacon and smoked salmon. The boys were still upstairs and the men were outside trying to get the ground as thawed as possible, so it made the most sense for everyone to eat in shifts.
“Think the scrambled egg is too chewy for a six-month-old?” Rachel asked when she saw the way Leo was trying to grab at her fork.
“If it were me, I’d mash up some of the potato and turnips,” Angel said.
“Was he already eating cereals or solids yet?” Rachel tried to remember her conversations with Dani while they worked together at the food bank. They’d talked about the foster agency and the social workers and the piles of adoption paperwork. They’d talked about the inconvenience of buying diapers and formula in bulk so you didn’t have to drive into town every week and waste hours of your life.
But she couldn’t remember them ever talking about food.
“Miss Dani showed me how to make him a little rice cereal last time I babysat.” Priyah had been so quiet all morning, Rachel had started to wonder if she was so scarred by yesterday she might never speak again.
“She did? Did he take to it okay? I mean did he like it?”
“Yeah.” A little grin spread across Priyah’s face. “He got real excited and started to babble really fast like he wanted more.”
Somehow this little tidbit about baby Leo’s diet made Rachel smile more than anything else had all morning long. She felt like she had missed something vitally important when her own children were this age.
Maybe Leo was God’s second-chance gift. She just hoped this time she wouldn’t mess it all up like she had in the past …
“Why don’t you grab me a little bowl and mash up a few pieces of potato,” Rachel suggested. “Maybe add a tiny bit of that distilled water to it. Do you remember about how runny the rice cereal was you gave him before? Try to make it about like that again.”
When the mashed potatoes were ready, Rachel tested to make sure they weren’t too hot and was about to give Leo his first bite when she got a better idea. She handed the spoon to Priyah. “Here,” she said. “Do you want to see if he likes it?”
Priyah looked to her mother, who gave her a warm smile. Priyah bit her lip as if trying to suppress a grin, but when Leo burst into enthusiastic wiggles at the sight of the food, none of them could stifle their laughter.
It took a couple tries before Priyah managed to get the spoon to Leo’s mouth before he could grab it and turn them both into a messy glob of potato goop, but when he tasted that first bite, he let out an exuberant squeal. The kitchen erupted in even more laughter, and suddenly there was nothing any of them wanted to do more than see if they could get Leo to make that same sound again.
The world might be ending. Cities might be burning. Humanity might be forever scarred by whatever was going on out there.
But right now, at this exact moment in time, two middle-aged moms and a twelve-year-old girl could still fuss and fawn over a little baby who was excited to eat his first bite of mashed potato and wanted the entire world to know it.

***

Tic hadn’t ever attended a funeral before, at least not any he could remember. And he definitely hadn’t attended one outside in the middle of winter.
Mrs. Singh had stayed inside so Dani’s baby wouldn’t have to go out in the cold. Tic wondered if Leo would miss his foster mom or if he was little enough he wouldn’t even notice her. He hoped that was the case.
Even though they’d already read the Bible and sung a few hymns and prayed in the living room, it felt like all the adults wanted to do the exact same thing now that they were out in the snow. On the one hand, Tic wanted it to be over. It was cold out here, and he hadn’t even known Dani all that well. It felt strange that they were the only ones here, not her real family. Last night Dev called in on Priyah’s radio to tell some operator that Dani had passed away. He wasn’t sure what the man was supposed to do with that information, but gathered this was some sort of prehistoric bulletin board system people used to share news about their loved ones.
They’d given Yaya’s name on the same channel last night, and every couple hours Mom would rush to the radio to see if there were any updates.
There never were.
Beside him, Benji was fidgeting, perhaps as impatient or uncomfortable as he was. Except there was part of Tic that wished for this funeral to stretch on forever. Because once Dani was completely buried, once they were done saying their prayers and covering her coffin over with all that dirt and the extra logs and twigs and leaves he and Benji had gathered up, Dad was leaving.
He’d heard his parents fighting about it last night. Everyone had heard his parents fighting about it last night. And then this morning when he’d woken up to the smell of bacon and potatoes and had gone downstairs to eat Mrs. Singh’s breakfast, Mom told him that Dad and Griz were leaving for Fairbanks as soon as Dani’s service ended. He was surprised the news didn’t shock him more. 
His dad was leaving on a dangerous journey in the middle of winter, and even though nobody would say it out loud, Tic was smart enough and old enough to recognize there wasn’t any guarantee Dad would find Yaya alive once he even got to Fairbanks.
If he even got to Fairbanks. 
Aside from a few summer camps or trips for basketball, Tic had never been away from his dad. Like ever. 
Shouldn’t he feel something? Worried? Sad? Anything?
Dad had given him the “you’re the man of the house now and I’m counting on you to look after your mom” speech after breakfast. But it was like Tic was hearing it underwater. Or listening to a podcast at half speed. The words were all clear and coherent, but they didn’t make any emotional connection in his brain.
He wondered if there was something wrong with him. He couldn’t ask Benji. They spent tons of time together, but it was always shooting hoops or playing guitar or building fires. He wasn’t the kind of friend you sat down and had hour-long conversations with about your feelings. Dad was the one Tic turned to at times like that, except now Dad was also the one who was leaving.
Last week, if he’d been confronted with this hypothetical scenario, Tic could have guessed at half a dozen conflicting emotions he might experience. Anger at his dad for ditching them when the world was collapsing. Jealousy that Dad was going to rescue Yaya with Griz instead of with him. Pride that Dad was entrusting him to look after Mom and the house. Terror that his sister might be dead, his dad might be about to die, and the world was completely and utterly screwed. 
What he didn’t expect was to feel so much … nothing. He was completely hollow. Empty. Unguarded. Like that possessed man Jesus talked about, where after the demons were cast out, even more came and took the first one’s place so that he was worse off than before.
Tic had never experienced anything remotely like this before, and he didn’t claim to have anywhere near the type of intuition Mom seemed to magically display at times. But he knew that this void of emotion couldn’t last for long, and this vacuum where he would have expected sadness or fear or grief to reside would soon give way to something else. 
Something dangerous and untamed. 
Like anger.
He clenched his fists and set his jaw while the mourners around him sang what felt like the twentieth verse of Amazing Grace. Let them sing. Let them grieve. Let Dad leave in search of his little darling daughter and leave Tic here alone while cell phones exploded and bombs ripped cities apart and planes crashed into buildings.
He didn’t care. Like he said, the world was screwed. 
And since he couldn’t fix it, he might as well learn to deal with it.
After the funeral finally ended, they all went inside. Tic expected Dad and Griz to leave right away. They’d already wasted almost half of their daylight. 
But instead of a hurried goodbye, Mom was upstairs (presumably crying) and Dad was showing Dev things around the property since now apparently the Singhs were moving in. So much for Dad trusting Tic to be the man of the house.
Griz was nowhere to be seen, but that was normal for him. He’d come and go and come back again like a ghost. Tic guessed that was probably why he’d done so well as a Force Recon officer in the Marines. 
He respected Griz. After all, the man had saved his life when he was just a baby. Griz’s heroics had lost him an eye and earned him a gruesome facial scar in the process. But still, there was something about him …
“You down here, idiot?” Dad asked, stepping into the basement where Tic had been half-heartedly lifting weights.
“Yeah.” 
Dad gave him a solemn once-over. “You doing okay?”
Tic couldn’t quite meet his gaze. “Yeah.”
“Griz and I are leaving soon.”
“I know.”
His dad sighed. Tic had the impression that he was supposed to say or do or feel more than he was. He just couldn’t.
“So, a few things I want to go over with you before I leave.” Dad started ticking things off on his fingers. “You know where the AR-15 is and all the extra ammunition?”
“Yeah.”
“And you remember the code to get to the handgun up in my safe, right?”
“I remember.”
“Now listen to me.” Dad leveled his gaze. “Those instruments are tools for you to use to defend your home and the ones you love. You never point them at something you’re not willing to destroy. Because the time might come when you have to step up in order to protect your mother. You know how she is with firearms. Even if she wanted to, she probably wouldn’t know where to find the trigger. So if worse comes to worst, the job of defending our home is going to come down to you.”
Even though a moment earlier Tic had been wishing he could feel something other than complete emptiness, he now took it all back and wished to God he could go back to that state of blissful numbness.
But Dad didn’t seem to notice or care about the impact his words were having on him. “As believers, Jesus calls us to feel compassion and grace for everyone,” he continued. “But this whole blackout is going to bring out the worst in people. It might get really, really ugly. And before I go, I need you to tell me that you’re ready to protect Mom and yourself if it comes down to it. Can you do that for me, son?”
Tic hated everything. Hated the tears he couldn’t stop from flowing down his cheeks, hated his voice for refusing to answer, hated his dad for leaving and the world for going to hell before his very eyes.
But he didn’t hate his mother. And he didn’t hate his sister. And the love and fear and protectiveness he felt for his family was the same love and fear and protectiveness he saw now mirrored back in his father’s gaze.
Dad opened his arms, and Tic awkwardly let himself lean into his hug.
“I believe in you, son. No matter what happens, no matter how much time passes, I want you to know that I couldn’t be more proud of you, and I love you with all my soul.”
Tic’s entire body was heaving with sobs, and he didn’t have the strength to answer.

***

There was so much Rachel should be doing. Comforting her friends after they had just buried their neighbor. Helping Angel and Priyah care for baby Leo, who was now everybody’s responsibility to watch over and nurture. Prepare herself for the most difficult good-bye she’d ever said in her life.
Instead, she was here in her room, burying her face in the box she kept tucked away in her closet, the one with all the love letters and pictures and trinkets she and Caleb had amassed over twenty-one years together.
Here were the pictures from that photobooth at the Alaska State Fair. Rachel had been pregnant with Yaya and exhausted from the heat and too nauseated to eat any of the fair food, but still they managed to make it one of the best days of their lives.
Further down were the love letters she’d written Caleb when he went on that two-month-long mission trip to Siberia the summer before they got married. Because he wouldn’t be able to receive any mail, she’d penned one letter for every day that he’d be gone. They were so sappy she was too embarrassed now to even read them, but they were a testimony to their love.
Next, her fingers landed on their wedding album. They both had been so young. Rachel had only been a couple years older than Yaya was now. So young and naïve, still starry-eyed and optimistic about the future, ready to face whatever God or fate threw their way as long as they stood side by side together.
She opened to the first page, and even the images of their rehearsal dinner were too much for her to bear. She was about to bury the album at the bottom of the box when something she hadn’t seen in years slipped out from between the pages.
Rachel couldn’t remember the last time she’d seen, let alone held, a CD. But here was that shiny silver disc in its bright white envelope. Wedding Mix Tape. Rachel and Caleb.
The memories came rushing back to her like an avalanche. The hours she’d spent pulling it together since they couldn’t afford a DJ at their reception. Swaying to the love song theme from Princess Bride for their first dance, even though her dress’s bustle was uneven and she felt like a lopsided fish on land. Laughing and shouting with their friends and family and Jon Bon Jovi himself to Living on a Prayer. Ballads and anthems and love songs, each selected for such a specific reason, each connected to their own torrent of memories. 
The way she’d asked her pastor if it was appropriate to play Heaven by Bryan Adams at a church wedding, since it implied that romantic love was equivalent to paradise. The way it was the first song running through her head when she woke up in her beloved Caleb’s arms the first morning of their honeymoon.
And now she was sobbing, not only from the torrent of memories, but the realization that if the power grid wasn’t fixed, all these songs might be lost forever.
Forever. 
In the early days of their relationship, Caleb had shared his love of music with her. When she was growing up, Rachel’s family listened to classical music and hymns, and when they weren’t home Rachel would sneak a song or two from the pop music station to try to fit in with the cool kids. But it was Caleb who introduced her to all the classic bands he loved, and as she fell in love with him, she fell in love with his soundtracks, too. And now, she was sitting here facing a future where ballerinas would never dance in Elton John’s hand and smalltown girls would never take another midnight train.
Ever again. 
It seemed ridiculous, so shortly after burying Dani and so shortly before saying goodbye to her husband that she would be weeping over a few songs she might not be able to hear again if phones stayed dead and electronics never started back up. But the bleakness and emptiness of the musicless future that now stretched before her and her children was all-consuming, a weight crushing her chest so oppressively she couldn’t breathe, couldn’t gasp, couldn’t even pray.
“Mom?” The timid knock on her door made her glad her sobs had been mostly silent. She wiped her face as dry as she could and got up off the floor.
“Yeah?”
Tic stepped in and placed a bare foot on the bed. “Can you look at my toenail? It’s got this weird thing on the side. I tried using the clippers, but …”
“It’s a hangnail,” she diagnosed. “Here, let me take a better look.”
She grabbed a flashlight from the nightstand. “Looks like it might be getting an infection,” she told him, relieved to have something as mundane as a hurt toe to pull her back to reality. “Let’s go melt some snow and put in some Epsom salt and give it a little soak. I’m guessing it’ll be fine, but I’ll add some Neosporin too, just in case.”
And just like that, she went from being a helpless, sobbing mess on the floor to Tic’s mother, a woman who knew how to take care of her children.
A woman who would put her family first and endure whatever future God had in store for her, just like women and mothers had for centuries and centuries past throughout all of human history.
Downstairs now, her son’s toenail soaked, clipped, and disinfected, Rachel sat at the table, listening to Griz and her husband hash out the last of their plans. Dev had put his engineer’s mind to good use and even made them a chart to keep track of their supplies and ration their food. They certainly weren’t carrying enough to get them to and from Fairbanks, but there were towns along the way as well as game Griz could hunt.
Rachel still couldn’t believe this was really happening. She thought back to last night, when she’d prayed so hard for God to change Caleb’s mind or magically fix the power grid or at least send them a dog sled team out of nowhere. He’d answered prayers like that for George Müller and countless missionaries. 
Why hadn’t he answered hers?
Cammy was restless, alternating between comforting Rachel and whining at Tic’s feet and going over to sniff baby Leo curiously. For the most part, Rachel either ignored her or patted her head mindlessly, but when Cammy growled at the window overlooking the driveway, Rachel grew nervous. All her husband’s warnings about guns and violence and catastrophes like this inviting the worst in mankind was making her paranoid. 
So paranoid she was certain she heard a car pulling up the driveway, which was impossible because all the cars had shut down with the electricity and the internet and the phones and everything else.
She was convinced that she was making things up until Tic glanced out the window and looked back at her, confused. “Mom?”
She jumped to her feet at the unmistakable sound of a car door shutting. What in the world?
Recognizing the station wagon, she rushed to the front door to greet Miss Cecelia, who was bundled up for winter and looked no worse for the catastrophe that had overtaken their world.
“Hello, Rachel.” Miss Cecelia’s voice was curt as usual. “I hate to bother you, but I was wondering if I might borrow Tychicus and Caleb. With the power out, I’m worried about the moose meat I’ve got in the freezer at the pantry. I want to move the whole thing outside and obviously can’t do that myself.”
Rachel blinked. And stared. Then realized she had the front door open and was letting all their heat out. “Um, yeah, of course. We’re right in the middle … why don’t you come in.”
“I don’t need to stay long,” Miss Cecelia declared. “I would have called, but my phone’s out. And did you know, I can’t get KWJD to come in either.”
Rachel’s mind was having trouble processing this conversation. 
“Um, we’ll worry about the meat in a second. But first, how are you? Are you all right?”
Miss Cecelia cocked her head to the side. “Oh, you mean my cough? It’s no worse, at least.”
Rachel couldn’t find a response.
“Or were you talking about the power?” Miss Cecelia went on. “I’ve got a fireplace, and my home is small, so I don’t have anything to worry about except for that meat I mentioned at the pantry. Oh, looks like everyone’s here.” She glanced into the living room. “Dev, did you know the radio station’s down?”
Before she could let Dev answer, Rachel finally managed to blurt out the question that was causing her brain to glitch so hard. “How did you get here?”
Miss Cecelia looked at her as if she’d asked where babies come from. “I drove myself, dear,” she answered dryly.
“I mean, how did you … why didn’t your …” Rachel’s voice was no use.
“She’s asking how your car is working,” Caleb cut in for her.
“Well, it’s not electric, obviously.” Miss Cecelia let out a mirthless chuckle then glanced around the room as if suddenly suspicious of everyone and everything in it.
Caleb looked at Rachel, now apparently just as lost as she was.
It was Dev who stepped up to intervene. “Miss Cecelia,” he said, “have you heard any of the news reports since the power went out?”
“Well, no,” she declared. “I told you, I haven’t been able to tune into KWJD. My phone line’s dead. I’ve been at home reading by the fireplace until now, when I started to worry about the moose meat. Caleb, I was wondering if you and Tychicus might be so kind as to …” 
“This isn’t just a power outage,” Dev interrupted. 
“Well, no, it’s quite longer than normal.”
Dev’s voice never wavered. “Miss Cecelia, I think you need to sit down. We’ll talk about the moose meat in a minute,” he added when she looked determined to butt in again. 
With the patience of an apostle, Dev explained how everything — phones, electricity, internet, even vehicles — were offline, presumably the result of some type of massive attack.
“That doesn’t make any sense,” Miss Cecelia replied, seemingly unperturbed. “As you can see, my station wagon’s operating just fine.”
“How old is it?” Dev asked.
“What?” Miss Cecelia was almost shouting. “I haven’t been able to charge my hearing aids, so you’ll have to speak up.”
“What year is your car, Miss Cecelia?”
She paused for a moment. “Well, when I bought it, that was either under Bush or Clinton, I think. But to be fair with you I’ve got better things to do than bother myself with dates that far back. She’s old, I’ll tell you that, but served me just perfectly, by the way. Never had a day of trouble with her.”
Dev was smiling. “Miss Cecelia, you know what this means?”
“It means I have an old car because I’m an old woman who’s stuck in my ways and doesn’t like change.”
Dev ignored her remark. “It means that you may have one of the only working vehicles left in Glennallen.”
Now Rachel understood how George Müller must have felt when that dairyman appeared at his doorstep with an entire delivery of milk for his orphanage just moments after he prayed. She clasped her hands together and beamed at her husband. “It also means that you don’t have to walk to Fairbanks!”
Half an hour later, Miss Cecelia was sufficiently brought up to speed on the state of the world, had mourned the news of Dani’s passing, and readily agreed to let Caleb and Griz take her station wagon. She also accepted the Winters’ invite to stay with them and promised she would help take care of Dani’s baby.
“It’s the least we could do for his mother, poor soul,” she declared.
Rachel’s heart was overwhelmed with gratitude at this amazing gift of providence. She raced up the stairs to make sure Caleb hadn’t forgotten anything, saw their boxes of love letters, and impulsively grabbed all the notes she’d written for him to read while he was in Siberia. If he was traveling by foot, she never could have justified even an extra ounce of non-essentials, but now that God had provided him with a vehicle, she wanted Caleb to have this gift to remember how much they had — and still did — love each other.
Her eyes fell on one more memento in the box, and smiling to herself she shoved it in with the love notes. Lifting up prayers of praise and thanksgiving to Jehovah Jireh, the God who really does provide, Rachel raced downstairs, a lightness in her soul she doubted she would ever experience again.
Today had started with a funeral and a good-bye she thought might rip her very soul into shards. But now that her husband’s journey could be measured in days and not weeks, now that she knew he wouldn’t be outside braving all the elements, she allowed her heart to hope that before anything else could go wrong, her entire family would be home safe and sound, together again.
Yes, it would be hard to watch Caleb and Griz pull out of that driveway. Yes, their journey was still long, and they still had no idea if Yaya was safe or how he would find her once he got to the campus.
But as long as Rachel had this hope to hold onto, she knew her feet were planted on a firm foundation, and her family could face whatever might come their way.
 


PART TWO

“When you pass through the waters, I will be with you;
and through the rivers, they shall not overwhelm you;
when you walk through fire you shall not be burned,
and the flame shall not consume you.
For I am the Lord your God,
the Holy One of Israel, your Savior.”

Isaiah 43:2-3
 


15. THE JOURNEY

Only a few precious hours of daylight remained by the time Caleb and Griz finished loading the station wagon and climbed in. Staring out the passenger window, Caleb watched all the familiar landmarks of home disappear, his mind racing as fast as their car as he considered everything they might have forgotten to pack.
If he kept his thoughts focused solely on the long journey ahead of them, he could try to squelch his fears for the family he was leaving behind.
Was it right for him to abandon Rachel and Tic to try to save his daughter? Caleb had read the parable of the lost sheep hundreds of times, but he never thought about the helpless flock that was left behind. Unguarded. Exposed to the elements.
Vulnerable to vicious and violent attacks.
True to his nature, Griz was completely silent, leaving Caleb alone with his doubts and regrets and anxiety.
The first stop would be Griz’s cabin so they could grab the cold-weather survival gear he’d accumulated during his career as an arctic warfare specialist. Caleb had never been on this secluded way before, and as Griz drove down what seemed to be a completely abandoned road, he hoped to God their stop wouldn’t add too much time to their journey.
Tucked up against his hip, Caleb stored treasures from home — a manila envelope full of love letters Rachel wrote while he was on a mission trip in Russia, the mixtape from their wedding playlist, and an old pocket New Testament Tic had won in fourth grade. He had memorized more verses than anyone else in Miss Cecelia’s Sunday school class, and as they were saying goodbye, Tic gave it to Caleb to take with him on the road. 
Caleb’s eyes burned with the heat of unshed tears, and he glanced at the dashboard clock, uncertain if it was correct or not. It seemed as if they had been bouncing along this driveway for the past twenty minutes, and every twist in the road without a cabin in sight made him even more anxious. Their plan was to meet the ham radio operator in Delta Junction before dark. Buster had offered the two men shelter for the night and, even more importantly, the chance to relay a message back home to Rachel to let her know they had completed the first stretch of their journey safely. 
But Fort Greely had suffered the same type of assault as Fairbanks, and the town had already erupted into bursts of violence. The stores were looted, with rumors of gunfights over snowmachines or old vehicles that were still running. Buster warned them in no uncertain terms that they were welcome to come in the day, but if he heard a vehicle pull up at night, he was coming out with his shotgun. Like just about any old-time Alaskan sourdough, Buster was a shoot-first, ask-questions-later kind of homesteader.
With night falling in a couple hours and treacherous winter roads ahead, Caleb and Griz couldn’t afford even the slightest delay.
Griz finally slowed the car down, but his cabin was surrounded by dense woods and so small Caleb almost had to squint to see it. Before he stepped outside, Griz glanced at the back seat. “Need more space in here.”
When packing, then men had left plenty of room in the trunk for Griz’s cold-weather gear, and Caleb wasn’t a fan of last-minute changes to well-established plans. “What for?” he asked.
“Arsenal.” 
Well, that was as good a reason as any. Caleb would be thankful to have more weapons than just his AR-10 and .44 Magnum all the journey with them, so if that required taking a few minutes to resituate the gear they had already packed, he wasn’t going to complain.
“You need a hand?” Caleb called out as Griz headed toward his home.
Griz didn’t even turn around. “Just more room.”
Caleb got out the station wagon, braced himself against the wind, and began moving things around in the back seat. He had gotten used to the heat on full blast and had to admit that Rachel was right. Trying to make this journey on foot would have been a grave mistake. Thank God for Miss Cecelia’s ancient station wagon and the old woman’s only slightly begrudging display of generosity.
After a few minutes, Griz returned and began hefting duffel bags and camping gear into the trunk. After all the fuss he’d made about Caleb clearing space in the back seat, he ended up not using any of it. Caleb wondered if their entire trip would be like this, with Griz effectively mute except for making the occasional demand that didn’t even make sense when all was said and done.
Even after he finished packing, Griz made no move to return to the vehicle. 
“Is that all?” Caleb finally asked, anxious to get back on the road and to Buster’s before night came.
“One minute.” Griz turned and stared at the woods, so stoic and reflective that Caleb wondered if he was reciting some kind of prayer. 
The two men never really talked about faith or spirituality. Once or twice a year, Griz would show up at church, usually right around Christmas and Easter. He was also connected to his Alaska Native heritage. Maybe this moment of silence was some sort of ritual or blessing before the men set out their journey. Caleb wasn’t sure if Griz was praying to the God of the Bible or his own ancestors, but he hoped it was the former.
After another moment of painful and awkward silence, Caleb heard trampling in the snow. A moment later, the largest malamute he had ever seen — so massive it could have been a wolf if not for the harness and collar he wore — charged straight toward them. Sensing the animal was ready to jump, Caleb steadied himself and tensed his muscles to prepare for a 150-pound impact. But the dog came to a full stop just before plowing into Griz, then sat down at his feet and looked up at him patiently, his majestic tail swishing behind him.
“Let’s go, Arsenal.” 
The malamute jumped into the back, and Griz lowered himself into the passenger seat, his only indication he was ready to trade driving duties.
A minute later, with Griz’s enormous panting dog drooling over his shoulder, Caleb was behind the wheel and making his way down Griz’s long and winding gravel road, toward the Richardson Highway and his daughter.

***

By the time Caleb and Griz crossed the Gulkana River, the sky was starting to turn that hazy shade of grayish-pink, an Alaskan twilight that would last for hours before the day turned to full night. When they passed the sign for the native village of Gulkana, Caleb thought about his students who lived here, the families he had known, many for a decade or longer. Arsenal whined once at the window as the village vanished in the rearview mirror.
On the Richardson Highway, two ravens were pecking at the snow, but Caleb couldn’t see what kind of trash or roadkill they were scavenging. His one focus was to get to Delta by nightfall. To reach Buster and his ham radio. To let Rachel know they’d reached the halfway point safely. Because Caleb knew she’d be waiting. 
On one hand, Rachel was the strongest woman he’d ever met. Serving at church, working with her clients, or caring for her family, Caleb’s wife was a steady force who led with a gentle grace, respected and admired by everyone in her orbit. What they didn’t know was the dark, the heaviness that could overcome her. With the world in chaos and their daughter unaccounted for, would she fall back into that bleak despair? For the thousandth time since they’d left home, Caleb wondered if he’d made the most foolish decision of his life.
“So how old is Arsenal?” Caleb asked, desperate to distract himself from the chaotic turmoil swirling around in his brain.
“Don’t know.”
If Caleb expected that committing to a long and dangerous journey together would make Griz more talkative, he was sorely mistaken.
“Where’d you get him?”
“Found his leg broke in one of my traps. Freed him, set his leg, and now the bastard won’t leave me alone.”
Caleb thought he might detect a hint of affection in Griz’s tone, but couldn’t be sure. “So is he a malamute or a wolf?”
Griz shrugged, and that was the end of their conversation. Arsenal, however, must have sensed he was being talked about, because he let out a loud yawn that turned into a halfhearted howl, then dropped his head on Caleb’s shoulder, where he proceeded to drool.
For a minute, Caleb wondered if the silence would get less awkward traveling with Arsenal up front and Griz in the back, but he wasn’t about to make the suggestion. He knew what a sacrifice this trip represented for Griz. What a true act of friendship it was. 
If only his traveling companion wasn’t so aloof …
“Hey, I have an idea.” Caleb gestured to the manila envelope Rachel had given him. “There’s a CD in here. An honest-to-God, real-life compact disc. Put it in. Let’s see if the sound system still works in this old beater.”
Griz had either never held a CD in his life or had completely forgotten how to use one. After Caleb showed him which side was up, Griz tried to slide Rachel’s mix tape into the slot, but something kept blocking it.
“Look for a button that says ‘eject’ or something like that,” Caleb said, not wanting to take his eyes off the road. The wind had picked up, driving horizontal sheets of snow across the highway.
There was a soft mechanical whir, and Griz pulled out the CD that had been jammed in Miss Cecelia’s player.
“Amy Grant?” Caleb let out a chuckle. “I haven’t heard her music in decades. Here.” Caleb picked up the CD Griz had unceremoniously laid face down on the dashboard and hoped it wasn’t scratched. “Slide this in, and we’ll have some music for the road.”
By the time they reached the historic Meier’s Lake Roadhouse, Caleb had relived dozens of memories with Rachel: their first dance, the jubilant celebration at their wedding reception, the long journeys they used to take back when gas was cheap and road trips were their favorite way to spend quality time together. This very same mixtape had accompanied them on their move from Anchorage to Glennallen after her father died and she inherited her childhood home, and later on all the doctor appointments to and from Glennallen and eventually the hospital where Yaya and then Tic were born.
As the Richardson began its gentle curve around Paxson Lake, Caleb was swallowing down a lump in his throat while listening to Faithfully. He didn’t even make it through the entire first verse before he had to turn the CD off. The blowing snow was blinding enough. 
He didn’t need teary eyes to make it worse.
Born and raised in Alaska, Caleb had driven through every type of hazardous condition the state had to offer. But the low visibility combined with the icy road and the stress of the last two days made his eyes strain and his shoulders ache as he clenched the steering wheel. He gritted his teeth so hard his jaw began to ache.
At a bend in the road, his back wheels spun out on some ice. For a moment, it looked as though the station wagon was going to lose control and dive headfirst into the frozen lake. He managed to right the steering wheel and get back in his lane, but the close call left him with more adrenaline in his system than his body knew how to handle.
Griz was stoic beside him, and even Arsenal in the back seat seemed so unfazed Caleb wondered if he had imagined the entire near miss.
“Well, I guess that woke me up,” he said with a nervous chuckle.
“I’ll drive when you’re tired,” was all Griz replied.
Needing a distraction that could speak more than five words at a time, Caleb turned Rachel’s mixtape back on, vowing to skip any of the sappy songs. A moment later, he was drumming happily on the steering wheel to Whitesnake: Here I go again on my own. An appropriate anthem for any road trip, but especially one of this magnitude. 
Caleb started to sing under his breath, even though Rachel always insisted he was off-key (whatever that meant). If Griz noticed or cared, it certainly didn’t show, and by the second chorus, Caleb felt his volume rising and his worries lifting.
Going down the only road I’ve ever known.
They were making good time. At this rate, there was no reason they shouldn’t make it to Buster’s by nightfall, where a hot meal, a bed, and a radio waited for them.
He wanted to tell Rachel everything about the past two hours. She would fall in love with Griz’s dog the moment she heard about Arsenal. Just the thought of talking to her again sent pangs of longing jabbing into his ribs, making his shoulders heavy, his neck tense. And there were so many things Caleb had forgotten to tell her before he left. How beautiful she was. How it was thoughts of her, and her alone, that gave him strength to make this perilous journey. 
The music she had burned onto this CD over twenty-one years ago was now ministering to his soul in a way no worship service ever had.
Like a drifter, I was born to walk alone...
A jolt. An uneven swish of the back tires. A cramp in Caleb’s leg as he pumped the brakes, resisting the urge to slam the pedal.
The way Arsenal started to bark, alerting them to danger. 
The drop-off looming in the front window. A desperate prayer: Please, Jesus, no.
Griz reached out and his strong hands yanked the steering wheel hard to the right.
And then the slam as their bodies lurched forward and the station wagon crashed into a mountain of snow and ice. 


16. UNSETTLED

Yaya hadn’t left her professor’s side since she found him injured yesterday in the seismic lab. Even though he assured her his wound wasn’t as bad as it appeared, Yaya could hardly bring herself to sleep more than a few fitful minutes at a time, terrified his health would take a turn for the worse. 
In addition to the burn on his abdomen, Boone had somehow smashed his shoulder when he fell. With his calm and patient instruction, he’d talked Yaya through the steps to make him a splint. She had also grabbed him some spare pillows Lilly brought back from the dorm, and when he wasn’t too tired, she helped him prop himself up and they sat on the floor and talked.
