She told Boone about everything going on in Fairbanks — the dorm fires, the bombing rumors, the exploding cell phones (with which he had unfortunate firsthand experience). Maybe it was because he was older and more mature, but he was the only other person holed up in the GI building who didn’t want to relive their collective trauma or spend every waking moment indulging in wild speculations about current events.
Her friend Lilly, when she wasn’t trying to shove bagged cafeteria food down Yaya’s throat, spent her time rehashing the moment of her roommate’s death. Others there seemed to think they were in a competition to determine who could come up with the most politically charged or terrifying reason for the attacks in the first place.
A couple of the students were convinced Russia had made good on their threat to take Alaska back. Any moment, they expected to see Soviet-era tanks rolling through their campus. A few science majors suggested a massive solar disturbance, which could explain the electronics and (with a dizzying amount of mental gymnastics) perhaps the exploding cell phones. But their theory certainly couldn’t account for the bases getting bombed, so they proposed some type of meteor strike coupled with a solar storm.
Ozzie, the boy with dyed hair, was the only one who mentioned aliens, though Yaya couldn’t even begin to guess if he was joking or not.
Boone wasn’t like the rest of them. Maybe it was because he had more life experience. Maybe he was doing it as a favor to Yaya because he knew her brain couldn’t cope with the pointlessness of vain conjecture. But when he sat up in the little makeshift hospital bed she’d provided for him, he’d look straight into her eyes and ask her things about home.
“You mentioned you have a little brother. How old is he?”
“Sixteen.” Yaya let her mind drift. If she just shut her eyes for a moment and listened hard enough, she could hear the sound of Tic clanging weights in their basement back home.
“Are you two close?” Boone asked.
“A little.” This type of questioning always got Yaya tongue-tied. “We don’t have much in common, you know, but he’s my brother. So…”
By the look in his eyes, Yaya knew Boone understood. She liked talking to her professor in this way, and liked that they were getting to know each other outside of labs and design projects and office hours.
“What about you? Any siblings?”
“No,” he answered with a sad shake of his head that made her want to reach out and heal all his wounds, both the seen and unseen. “No brothers or sisters, but I did have over a dozen aunts and uncles.” He grinned and his blue eyes twinkled. “That’s why I grew up so spoiled.”
Yaya had to chuckle. “I have a hard time picturing you as spoiled,” she admitted, then felt an unwelcome flush creep up her cheeks. She hoped Boone didn’t notice, but he caught her eye and held her gaze steadily for just a moment.
The world might be ending. The Russians might be about to invade any moment. Or maybe the devastation was caused by a solar flare or even aliens. 
The only thing Yaya knew for certain was that since she couldn’t be home with her family right now, there was no one she would rather be spending her time with than this sensitive, charming man right here.

***

Tic’s day had been so weird with the funeral and then Dad’s departure, he was surprised when Dev reminded everyone it was Sunday. It felt strange, like days of the week were things that normal people kept track of and cared about. But with a dead woman buried in their backyard and his father gone on to brave the winter elements and bring Yaya home, what did it matter if it was Sunday or Monday or the Fourth of July or February 31?
“Maybe we should hold a church service,” Mom suggested. “Tic and Benji can play the guitar.” 
It wasn’t exactly Tic’s idea of fun, but when he saw mom’s eyes light up for the first time since Dad left, he readily agreed.
A few minutes later, seated next to a stove so hot Tic had to strip down to his tank top, he and Benji were leading their families in singing. It was hard to find songs everybody knew. Miss Cecelia kept asking for old hymns, and even though Benji could kind of figure out the tune, Tic knew fewer than half the lyrics and stumbled through the chords. And a lot of the more contemporary songs he and Benji knew best were hard for everyone to join in on without lyrics projected onto a screen like at church.
All in all, their impromptu singing session would have been awkward enough without Priyah’s face turning beet red whenever she looked at Tic or without Miss Cecelia interrupting their music with a loud coughing fit any time they forgot a line to one of her favorite hymns. Dani’s baby seemed to be the only one happy no matter what was they were singing, and as Leo sat in Mrs. Singh’s lap, he waved his arms excitedly like he was trying to beat an invisible air drum. 
The mandatory singing session came to a merciful close, and Dev opened his Bible. “Well, friends.” He began in a booming voice, as if he was preaching to a church filled with hundreds instead of a living room with exactly four adults and a few kids and teens. “With the power out and God alone knowing when it’ll come back, I couldn’t think of a more appropriate passage to share with you today than Matthew 5. Taken from the Sermon on the Mount, Jesus tells us, ‘You are the light of the world. A city set on a hill cannot be hidden. Nor do people light a lamp and put it under a basket, but on a stand, and it gives light to all in the house. In the same way, let your light shine before others, so that they may see your good works and give glory to your Father who is in heaven.’”
Dev shut his Bible and stared at each of them in turn. “God has called us to be lights in this dark time, to be cities on a hill, shining bright for all to see. Now, if the good Lord wills it, this whole ordeal might blow over soon. The power will come back on, the grid will get back up and running, and life will resume as it was before. But if it doesn’t …”
Here Dev leveled his gaze, and Tic stared at his lap, both thankful and afraid that another adult was giving voice to the fears that had been swirling around in his head all evening.
“We need to be ready for anything. Jesus told his disciples to be shrewd as serpents and innocent as doves. Later on in Matthew, he tells us that neighbor will turn against neighbor and brother against brother. So before it comes down to that,” Dev continued, “we need to ask ourselves if we’re ready. Are we ready to turn the other cheek? Are we ready to hand over our cloak when someone demands our tunic?”
Tic’s mind always wandered when anybody but his dad was teaching about the Bible, but now he was absorbed in Dev’s words — not because he believed them, but because he doubted.
“If God has given us food,” Dev went on, “we must be willing to share it with anyone who asks. If God has given us warmth and shelter, we must be ready to open up the doors to our home and let the strangers in.”
Tic shot a glance at his mother while their friend spoke about their house as if it were his to offer up to any passerby. Tic understood charity and hospitality. He understood neighbors helping neighbors. That’s why Miss Cecelia was here. That’s why the Singhs were here. That’s why Griz had agreed to go with Dad on a trip he had no obligation to take to bring home Tic’s sister whom he barely knew.
But when crap hit the fan, as his dad would euphemistically put it, who was going to defend their home from attack? Who was going to protect their food supply and their shelter and their safety? The way Dev was talking, if an armed man showed up right now on the porch and demanded room and board, the Winters were biblically bound to take him in. Not only that, but while some random stranger stole their food they were also supposed to offer him their water and their blankets and their ammo as well.
That’s not how Tic had been raised. And that’s certainly not how he planned to react if the time came that people in Glennallen really did start turning against each other.
Before he left, Dad had entrusted Tic with the solemn obligation to protect his mom and protect their home. And — Golden Rule or not — Tic didn’t plan to let anything stop him.

***

Rachel couldn’t adequately express how much Dev’s sermon had meant to her. For one thing, it got her away from the radio, if only for a few minutes. Even though it was still too early for Caleb to have arrived in Delta, she still checked in with Buster on the hour like clockwork and desperately needed a distraction.
As Dev led the closing prayer for their impromptu church service, she felt the burden of the day lift off her shoulders. Dev’s words had reminded her more than anything else to count her blessings. They had food. They had heat. They had each other.
And even with Caleb gone, she knew there were still plenty of reasons to praise the Lord. Her husband was heading to Fairbanks by car, thanks to Miss Cecelia’s station wagon, so old-fashioned it was one of the only pieces of technology that hadn’t crashed along with the rest of the world. He and Griz had made plans to spend the night with Buster, the radio operator outside of Delta, which meant that they wouldn’t be cold and also meant that Rachel would hear from her husband tonight before she went to bed.
Of course, she wished that none of this had happened in the first place, that Dani hadn’t died, that Caleb hadn’t left, that the world hadn’t collapsed into chaos. There were so many things that could be better than they were. 
But then again, they could be so much worse.
Rachel thought about all those in Glennallen who relied solely on heating fuel to make it through the winter. What would happen to them if the power wasn’t restored? Dev’s sermon tonight had made her both pensive and convicted. She had told God multiple times today how thankful she was for their wood-burning stove. Did that mean she was ready to invite dozens of acquaintances — even strangers — into her home to share their heat?
And what about food? The people in this home could ward off hunger for several months given what they currently had in their freezers and pantries. They would have to get creative with their meal-prepping (Rachel had a feeling that Angel could teach her a thing or two about that), but even if they got bored with beans and lentils, their food would last them through the winter and into spring when they could start thinking about gardening and foraging, not to mention hunting and fishing.
But Dev’s words — no, God’s words, really — continued to bounce around in Rachel’s head long after their closing prayer. What if another young mom came begging for baby formula? Should Rachel give her half of their supply even if that meant baby Leo might go hungry? What if several families showed up on their porch, asking for food, even shelter? 
Should they take them all in, even if that meant they’d run out of supplies before spring?
No matter what Dev said, Rachel knew her primary responsibility was to her family and to the individuals gathered right now in her home. Taking in friends and neighbors was one thing. Complete strangers off the streets? She didn’t want to say never and find herself arguing with God, but hadn’t Dev’s sermon started with a warning to be shrewd as serpents?
Best-case scenario, something magical would happen and the power would come back on. Caleb and Griz would bring Yaya home, and she’d whine and complain about them being overprotective and beg to go back to school. Nobody would have to worry about running out of food or taking in random vagabonds, because life might go back to normal.
And if it didn’t… well, those were questions Rachel would have to face another day.

***

Caleb stepped out of the car, thankful to no longer breathe in nothing but airbag dust. His body was so flooded with adrenaline and his nerves so consumed by fear and frustration that he scarcely noticed the cold. After brushing himself off, he leaned in to check more closely on his friend.
Griz’s airbag (if it even existed in a car this old) hadn’t deployed, and there was a large cut on his forehead. “I’m fine,” was all he said. Caleb was too dazed to jump into an argument about first aid with him and tried not to wince when Arsenal started licking the blood off his owner’s face.
Griz’s door was smashed up against a mountain of snow, so he had to get himself out through the driver side. “Stay,” he told Arsenal, who looked dejected and abandoned but still obeyed.
 The men stood shoulder to shoulder, and for several moments neither spoke. The front passenger side of the car was buried under the snowbank that had broken their spin. They’d have to clear a way to get to the hood and measure the extent of the damage.
Griz put on his winter gear, picked up the snow shovel he’d packed, and immediately, silently, set to work. Caleb rummaged around in the back seat, trying his best to avoid Arsenal’s drool and hot breath, until he found a small collapsible shovel Miss Cecelia kept. He began digging out the wheels while Griz focused on clearing the snow that had collapsed around the hood.
“You need gloves,” Griz stated.
Caleb knew he was right, but he refused to slow down. Rachel would already be waiting by the radio right now, frantic to hear from them. Delta was still an hour and a half away — if you were lucky enough to own a working vehicle — and the daylight wouldn’t last.
Thoughts of his wife and son back home, his daughter so many hundreds of miles ahead of him, fueled Caleb’s resolve. He was tossing snow behind him twice as fast as Griz, but since his collapsible shovel was so comically small, he had very little to show for his exertion other than sweat that immediately turned to steam as it rose from his exposed skin.
“Gloves,” Griz repeated.
“I will in a minute.” Caleb couldn’t slow down, couldn’t stop working. His family depended on him.
“I’m not treating you for frostbite.” There was no hint of teasing or irony in Griz’s tone.
Caleb let the little plastic shovel fall, climbed back into the station wagon, and reluctantly pulled on his hat and gloves. Arsenal took the opportunity to jump out of the car, sniff both back tires, and let out a long stream of steaming urine right where Caleb had been working.
“Look at that. If we give him enough to drink, he can melt all the snow away and we’ll be out of here in no time.”
Griz didn’t respond to Caleb’s attempt at a joke. Instead, the man continued working in silence. 
Caleb’s breath frosted onto his beard, but he didn’t feel the cold. Right now would be a perfect time to pray. A perfect time to ask God for favor, to help them get the station wagon free, to ensure the engine hadn’t been irreparably damaged in the crash. But he was too riddled with anxiety, his body still flooded with cortisol, his brain too jostled to form any cohesive thought, let alone an actual prayer. All he could hope was that God would look down and see his struggle and somehow interpret his beads of sweat, his stinging face, and his pants of exertion as a cry for help.
As the pink and gray twilight turned to the almost colorless violet that appears just before complete darkness, Griz shoveled snow off the crumpled hood of the station wagon, then popped it open. Caleb paused shoveling long enough to hear his assessment.
“Cracked coolant hose.”
Caleb could barely register the words. There was only one question on his mind. “Will it drive?”
Griz was silent for far too long before finally tramping through the snow and popping open the trunk. “Need duct tape.”
If duct tape could fix whatever damage the station wagon suffered in the crash, maybe they weren’t in such bad shape after all. 
Griz tinkered under the hood. Caleb kept shoveling around the tires so once they got the car running they could pull it out of the snowbank. From time to time, Arsenal would get bored staring out at the vast winter landscape. Then he would join Caleb in digging for a few minutes before sitting back on his haunches to keep solemn vigil over the setting sun yet again.
“Try the engine.”
Griz’s words were like music to Caleb’s ears. The only sweeter sound would be that of the engine turning over and roaring to life when he hopped into the driver side and turned the key in the ignition.
Nothing.
“Need paracord,” Griz announced, and started digging around the trunk.
Caleb couldn’t guess how long they’d been working, but there was no way the remaining sunlight would last them all the way to Delta. Even if they miraculously got back on the road in the next five minutes, there was no way they’d make it to Buster’s home tonight.
And that was only if they got the car working. He couldn’t even guess what would become of them if they didn’t. So he stepped back out into the chilly evening and fell into a solemn rhythm freeing the tires.
Caleb hadn’t shoveled snow in years, not since he got the plow extension for the truck and Tick got strong enough to take over clearing the porch and the walkways. The physical exertion gave his mind a tangible goal to focus on, but the stinging in his lungs and the exhaustion already setting into his muscles did nothing but remind him that Rachel was right. This was a journey he could not make on foot. They had to get the car running.
Please, God, was the only prayer he could form.
“Again,” Griz ordered, but as hard as Caleb prayed before turning the key, nothing happened.
It didn’t make sense. When Miss Cecelia showed up that morning, it felt as if God himself had created a station wagon out of ice and snow and miraculously plunked it down onto the Winters’ driveway. If it hadn’t been for that gift of providence, the men would have taken a full day walking to Griz’s to gather the rest of their supplies. Day two would have gotten them no farther than Gulkana. The station wagon had already saved them four or five days of walking just to get them this far.
God wouldn’t have provided this gift so miraculously just to have it fail them so early in their journey.
Would he?
The two men continued their dance of insanity, Griz tinkering under the hood for a few minutes, Caleb popping behind the wheel and holding his breath and wishing to heaven for the engine to work.
It never did. 
Arsenal let out a loud, mournful howl as the last vestiges of light disappeared, and Caleb tried hard not to think about his wife back home, sitting desperately by the radio, wondering where her husband was. Knowing her propensity to worry, he explained to her that if they didn’t reach Buster’s before dark they’d end up spending the night in the station wagon. But that wouldn’t be enough to calm Rachel’s nerves, not on a night like this.
And so he turned his thoughts toward Yaya, reminding himself why he and Griz had started out on this hazardous journey in the first place. If God hadn’t provided Miss Cecelia’s station wagon, they’d still be just a few miles away from home and spending the night in one of Griz’s cold-climate tents.
“Again.” Griz’s voice was a welcome interruption to his spiraling thoughts.
Caleb’s hand was shaking, maybe from cold or maybe from nerves, when on the third attempt the engine sputtered. The roar of the engine was a glorious ruckus that only a gas guzzler this old could make, as welcome to Caleb’s ears as oxygen to a drowning man’s lungs.
Caleb let out a triumphant hoot and exultantly stepped out of the car, leaving the engine running. “You did it!”
The barest trace of a smile spread over Griz’s lips.

***

The station wagon’s clock, so much as it could be trusted, read quarter after seven by the time Griz and Caleb got the engine running. As soon as they were both out of the cold, Griz insisted they change their socks. 
After a few minutes, once he could feel some circulation again in his hands, Caleb took off his gloves and rummaged around in his backpack for the jerky Rachel had packed for them. He and Griz split the small package, both sharing a few pieces with an enthusiastically demanding Arsenal. In spite of how little he’d eaten that day, Caleb barely had an appetite.
The engine worked, thanks to some duct tape holding the cracked coolant hose together, but the tires were still stuck in a five-foot snow drift. With night on them, it was pointless trying to shovel out more snow. They were stuck here for the night. They couldn’t run the heater indefinitely, but if they turned it on at intervals they could hopefully keep themselves and the engine from completely freezing.
Caleb hated the idea of even a moment’s delay. He tried to think of anything else besides Rachel waiting desperately at home. This was the normal time on a Sunday evening when the two of them would be wrapping up youth group. He’d been so worried about his own immediate family today that he had scarcely spared energy to think about the extended members of his community — Pastor Tom and other elders at the church, his colleagues at the school, all the students in his classroom and youth group he had devoted so much of his life to.
Everybody must be just as lost and as worried as he was, but instead of that bringing him any sense of connectedness with the world, he felt even more overwhelmed. What right did he have to complain that he couldn’t reach Rachel tonight on the radio when they had buried their friend Dani just this morning? 
What about his students without a way to heat their homes or any extra food? 
At least he and Griz had a source of heat and a trunk full of cold weather supplies. What did Yaya have, and those with her up in Fairbanks? Coats and a blanket or two, hopefully shelter of some sort. And then what?
In any other emergency of this magnitude, victims could expect FEMA and the Red Cross to show up, maybe not instantaneously all the way out in rural Alaska, but pretty darn close if you were in a city like Fairbanks. But what good was FEMA when the entire federal government was crippled — perhaps obliterated? Rumors abounded on the radio channels about multiple bases in the Lower 48 attacked just like the ones in Fairbanks and Delta. And how could the Red Cross mobilize without working phones or running vehicles?
Dreadful as it was to admit, Yaya and the other young students she was with were very much on their own. Unless help arrived, they would have to fend for themselves against the elements, the fear, and the urban dangers Caleb refused to let his mind even contemplate.
The frost on Caleb’s beard had scarcely started melting when Griz reached out and killed the engine. “Can’t risk overheating.”
Caleb was too mentally exhausted to argue.
A few minutes later, Griz had stretched his seat as far back as it would go. Arsenal, seizing the opportunity, climbed on top of his chest and began play-nibbling his ear then tucked his head into Griz’s armpit in a gesture that seemed quite familiar to them both.
Caleb’s arms ached to hold his wife. Heck, he’d even enjoy some cuddles from Cammy right now, as much as the spaniel could annoy him with her vibrating tail stump and propensity to whine whenever she felt any extreme emotion.
“I’ll take the first shift to make sure it doesn’t get too cold in here,” Caleb offered. To keep the oil from seizing up, one of them would have to stay awake, ready to run the engine.
Griz grunted, and judging by the sound of the calm regular breathing that followed, he was fast asleep. Arsenal, considering his job complete, returned to the back seat and curled up like a donut.
Caleb pulled a small flashlight out of his coat pocket. If he were being as cautious as he should, he wouldn’t waste even a few seconds of its battery power, but he was miles away from home and hours away from sleep. 
He opened the manila envelope Rachel had given him when they said their last goodbyes, pulled out the first page, and took in his wife’s words.
Dearest, darlingest Caleb,
Right now when you are reading this, you are probably on a plane flying to Russia on a trip to serve God and encourage those you come across. I just want you to know that I love you so much, that I am so thankful to be engaged to a man as strong and godly and selfless as you, a man who has devoted his life to spreading the gospel to the farthest corners of the earth and serving others with the love of Christ.
The words sent pangs of ice and regret shooting through Caleb’s heart. When he and Rachel first met and fell in love over their shared passion for the gospel, they had talked about all the ways God might use them in their future. And in many ways, he had. There was the mission trip they both took to Kobuk in the Alaskan bush to run summer youth camps and VBS. Then years of parenting and youth ministry, serving the Lord together side by side, ministering in their church …
But was this what Rachel and he pictured when they committed their lives to God and to each other? It seemed like there was supposed to be so much more in store for them …
Darling, this will be the first time since you told me that you loved me that we will have gone a single day without seeing each other, gazing into each other’s eyes, praying together and worshiping our wonderful Lord and Savior side by side. But I don’t want to dwell right now on how hard it is going to be, because I don’t want you to regret your decision to follow the Lord wherever he may lead you. I don’t want to tell you how desperately I am going to miss you, because I don’t want you to be distracted from the work he set before you. The next two weeks will be excruciating to be separated from the one true love of my entire life, my heart and my soul, but I am so proud to be loved by and pledged to a man who will follow God no matter what, a man whose heart is so selfless and so devoted to spreading God’s word.
I know that this journey, yes, even this separation we are facing, is part of God’s plan for both you and me. In the same way, I hope you know just as confidently and faithfully how much I love and adore you, and I will be praying for you nonstop from this moment until you return back home and I am back in your arms once more, right where I belong.
Caleb let out a sound that could only be described as a moan. 
Arsenal glanced up, then stretched out to tentatively lick Caleb’s trembling hand. His beloved had written this letter over two decades ago, but she may as well have penned it last night.  


17. TINY DANCER

“This is Rachel Winters from Glennallen.” Her hand trembled as she held down the talk button.
A burst of static, and then the strong voice she had grown accustomed to hearing on the other end.
“Buster here, Rachel. Still no sign of your husband or his friend. I’m sorry.”
Rachel glanced at the clock. Caleb and Griz should have arrived in Delta hours ago. Something must have happened…
“Do you think they made it to Delta and maybe got lost?” she asked.
Seated on the couch across from her, Miss Cecelia alternated between bouncing baby Leo on her knee and casting furtive, sympathetic glances in Rachel’s direction. Why couldn’t the old woman give her some privacy? Everyone else had. Tic and Benji were in the basement lifting weights. Dev had taken Priya into the garage, something to do with an old Canon camera she’d found in a box of candles, but Rachel had been too mentally exhausted to care and simply nodded when they asked for permission to dig around more to hunt for film. Angel and Priyah were in the office, their quiet voices occasionally drifting down the hallway. 
Everybody else had found something else to do. Why couldn’t Miss Cecelia? 
“I gave them pretty good instructions,” Buster said. “Doubt they’re lost. Best thing you can do is try to get yourself some sleep. Call back in the morning when it’s light.”
“But why haven’t they gotten there yet?” Rachel had to know. Had to get answers. Had to get herself away from Miss Cecelia’s smothering attention.
Buster paused before replying. “Coulda been anything, really. Try not to worry. I been near this radio all day. Nobody’s called in to mention any type of accident or nothing like that. Most likely, they just got a little delayed, that’s all. And I told them they couldn’t come poking around after dark, so I’d do your best to save your battery power and we’ll talk in the morning when the sun’s up.”
It would almost be easier for her if Buster weren’t so kind and patient. If Rachel sensed he was annoyed by her frantic worry, it might not hurt so much. 
“But if you see him before them, you’ll tell him I called?” 
She wasn’t ready to turn off the radio yet. Wasn’t ready to face a restless night without any idea where her husband was. Was he hurt? What if there was an accident? What if something went wrong with the car? 
What if Caleb was already dead?
“I’ll tell him,” Buster answered gently. “Now I gotta leave this channel open for others, Rachel. You know that.”
“I know. I know.” Her face felt hot.
“I’ll tell him you called,” Buster repeated.
“And you’ll tell him I love him?” Her voice came out like a squeak.
From the speaker, she could hear Buster’s small sigh. “Of course. I’ll tell him that, too.”

***

“Dude, I think you got some kind of zombie toe,” Benji exclaimed when Tic took off his socks to practice his deadlifts.
Tic glanced down. It had been hurting earlier, but he hadn’t paid it any attention in hours. He shrugged. “Just a hangnail.”
“Janky.”
“Yeah, Mom said it’s getting infected.”
Even Cammy seemed interested in giving Tic’s foot a few perfunctory sniffs before curling up in the corner and beginning the process of tearing the fabric off a tennis ball.
Tic had more important things to worry about right now than what was going on with his foot. Like where Dad was. He should have radioed in from Delta hours ago. But since nobody had any clue what happened to him, Tic was going to overload his brain (and his muscles) by attempting a personal record on his deadlift. Benji stood to the side doing lateral raises with some fifteen-pound dumbbells, but Tic was going for the heavy weights.
Anything to keep his mind distracted.
Tic walked up to the loaded barbell, toes angled, heels planted firmly on his mat. Hitting 385 on deadlift would give him his second PR of the week. 
Getting himself in place, he ran himself through the form and safety checklist Dad had ingrained in him from their earliest days lifting together. Big belly breath, straight back, bar touching the shins, neutral spine. 
He was ready. 
This lift is for you, Dad. A strong inhale, then Tic pulled with all his might. Slowly, painfully, fighting for each centimeter, he lifted the bar off the floor. His muscles locked into place, but a tremor ran through his legs as they strained to hold both his body and the weights steady. Then that brutal moment when the bar hovered mid-thigh and his muscle fibers twitched and screamed for release. That brutal moment when he had to decide: 
Drop it all now, or keep going?
He pictured his father, who up until today had been present for every single one of Tic’s PRs. You can do it, son, he seemed to say.
And Tic knew his dad was right. He could do it. His muscles were poised, eager, ready to make those last few inches. Tic could visualize the movement. Accomplish the flow in his sleep. One last burst of exertion to drive his hips forward. He felt the pull in every cell, every muscle fiber, every ounce of willpower. 
He locked out the bar and let the breath he’d been holding explode out his lungs. The bar clanged to the ground, as competing surges of both dopamine and fatigue flooded Tic’s body.
It was a huge accomplishment. But he couldn’t enjoy it. 
Not without Dad here. That lift hadn’t meant anything. He slumped onto the bench, his head light, his quads and thigh muscles twitching.
“Dude, you wimp out?” Benji asked. 
Tic just shrugged. He’d rather have his friend believe he’d exhausted himself on the mat than confess the truth. Hitting a PR without his dad here felt like a betrayal, one he regretted the instant he locked his hips in place.
Benji was staring at him. “You straight, bro?” he asked.
Tic nodded. “Yeah, I’m good.”
“If you say so.”
There was a soft knock on the basement door, and Tic was surprised to see Mrs. Singh there, holding a candle and looking worried.
“Tic, can I talk to you for a moment?”
“Sure.” He reached down and began to pull his socks back on.
“I’m a little worried about your mom,” Mrs. Singh admitted.
Tic’s heart sank. Aside from news that his father was dead or that Fairbanks had been completely obliterated by nukes, it was the last thing he wanted to hear. All evening, Tic had watched his mom shrink and wilt with each passing minute until he was afraid the clock would eventually strike midnight and she’d turn to dust and ash. Coming down here to lift was his last-ditch effort to get his mind on something else. But if Mrs. Singh was worried, that meant Mom was not okay. 
And he had promised Dad he’d make sure she was okay.
Tic didn’t know how to respond, but sensing he was expected to come up with some sort of reply, he asked, “Is she sick?”
“She’s just really worried about your dad. I’ve told her she should try to get some rest. The radio man said nobody was coming in after dark, so there’s nothing she can do tonight. I thought maybe you could …” 
Mrs. Singh didn’t have to finish her thought. Tic was already halfway up the basement stairs, Cammy at his heels.
In the living room, Mom was in the same spot she’d sat in for hours, staring out the window at a whole lot of nothing and clutching the radio in her lap. 
It wasn’t even turned on.
“Mom?”
She turned around with a start, her eyes vacant.
“Mom?” he repeated. She couldn’t have aged this much in a day, could she? Then why hadn’t Tic noticed all those wrinkles before? Even her long hair looked more gray than black. It must be a trick from the candlelight. 
“Hey, honey.” Mom stretched out her arms toward him, and even though it made him feel like a baby, he sat down beside her and leaned into her chest while she rested her cheek on the top of his head. Cammy joined her on the other side, nestling her head beneath Mom’s free arm.
“How’s my guy?” Mom asked.
“I’m fine.” He resisted the urge to pull away when he heard Benji coming up the stairs. “But I’m worried about you. It’s getting late, you know.”
“Oh, I know, sweetie. I was just waiting to hear if there’s any news.”
“Your radio’s not even on.”
Mom looked down at the machine. “Oh.”
“Come on.” Tic held her by the elbow and tried to raise her to her feet. “It’s late. You should go to sleep, unless you’re gonna stay here by the fire.”
Mom’s voice was like that of a small child waiting to be tucked in. “No, I guess I’ll go to bed.”
Hearing the word, Cammy ran to the foot of the stairs then glanced back to wait, half a tennis ball in her mouth, tail stump wagging.
“Mom, leave that down here.” Tic tried to take her hands off the clunky ham radio, but she held onto it with an iron grip. “Your father might call.”
“He can’t, Mom.” He gently pried her fingers away. “He probably just got to Delta later than he expected. Tomorrow he’ll give you a call.”
Mom let out her breath and nodded. “Yeah, that’s probably right. But maybe I should stay down here with it, just in case...” She reached out again for the radio, but Tic took her arm and linked it in his. 
Leading her toward the stairs, he just said, “I think it’s time to try to sleep, don’t you?”
Mom turned to look at him, staring directly into his soul. Tic had no idea that two human eyes could contain so much desperation. “He’ll be all right, won’t he?” Mom leaned her weight on him, as if the wrong answer might drop her to the floor.
Tic cleared his throat and forced confidence into his voice. “Yeah, Mom. Remember,” he repeated, “he’s with Griz. What can go wrong?”

***

Rachel jumped out of bed with a start. What was that noise?
She had a vague recollection of Tic helping her into bed earlier as if she were in a drug-induced stupor, but now her senses were sharp.
The low hum, a sound she’d grown accustomed to in the past couple days, was now joined by an ominous growl. But it wasn’t coming from outside.
Cammy stood with her paws on the windowsill, her tail stump twitching, an uncharacteristic snarl vibrating her entire body. The dog had only made that sound a few times in her entire life. Rachel knew exactly what it meant. It meant that someone — or something — was outside.
“What is it, girl?”
She peered out from her second-story window. The moon cast an eerie glow over the snow. She looked in the direction Cammy was staring. Was that a figure in the woods?
It wasn’t until Cammy let out an even lower, throaty growl that Rachel felt fear completely seize her body. Outside, a shadowy form was moving closer to their house.
But then she squinted to see better, and the vision completely vanished. Had she made the entire thing up?
She glanced at her husband’s gun safe, recalled his warning to protect their home while he was away. But she also remembered Dev’s sermon about giving anything to those who asked.
Which of the two men was correct?
She pried her eyes away from the safe. She was just on edge, worried for her husband, unaccustomed to sleeping alone. Perhaps Cammy had picked up on her anxiety and was growling at phantoms.
With the pounding of her pulse in her ears, Rachel thought she heard voices. Was someone awake downstairs, or was it coming from outside? Maybe she should take the gun out, just in case.
Caleb, why can’t you be here?
Rachel peered back out the window. If the power worked, the motion detectors would flood the area with light and she could tell at a glance if there was something in the woods. But now, she was almost as bad as blind. 
Blind, and scared, and entirely uncertain what to do.
Cammy jumped from her vigil at the window and whined at the door. Should Rachel let her go outside and investigate? If it was an animal out there, Cammy could scare it off. But if it was a person ...
Rachel thought she heard a noise but couldn’t tell where it was coming from. She wrapped her bathrobe around her and opened the bedroom door. She told Cammy to stay beside her while she assessed where everybody should be. 
She strained her ears. Unless baby Leo was up and fussy, nobody should be awake.
Silence from Tic’s room, the one he was sharing with Benji. She would have been thrilled to simply find the two boys up late talking like they had on so many sleepovers for so many years. But there was nothing.
Yaya’s was the only other room upstairs, but it was empty. Everybody else was sleeping downstairs closer to the heat. The Singhs were staying in Rachel’s office with a closed door and some privacy, but Miss Cecelia chose to stay in the living room with baby Leo so he could be nearest to the stove.
At the top of the stairs, Rachel wavered for another moment, listening for any noise. Cammy looked up at her, waiting. If everyone was asleep, there was no reason to bother them. 
She was about to return to her room and chastised herself for her bad case of nerves. The entire last half of Dev’s sermon that evening had been about trusting God and not giving way to anxiety, and yet here she was, worrying so hard she was surprised the sound of her pounding heart hadn’t woken everyone in the house up by now. 
Then again, Cammy had most definitely been growling at something. Maybe Rachel should go down and check.
She heard the unmistakable sound of a door opening, which sealed her resolve. As quietly but as quickly as she could, she hurried downstairs.
Miss Cecelia was fully dressed, snow boots and coat included. She had Caleb’s shotgun slung over her shoulder. She turned to face Rachel and held a finger to her lips. “I think there’s something outside,” she whispered.
Rachel couldn’t tell which she felt more — relief that she hadn’t been crazy or terror that she had been right.
“I need the strongest torch or flashlight you got,” Miss Cecelia said in her matter-of-fact way. She eyed Rachel’s bathrobe and stockinged feet. “Better bundle up, too.”
Rachel didn’t move. “Should we wake up Dev?”
Miss Cecelia snorted, coughed, then shook her head. “No. We’ll take care of this.”
Rachel eyed the shotgun. “You know how to use that?”
Miss Cecelia made another sound — halfway between a laugh and a stifled cough. “My parents homesteaded on this land before there was a pipeline, before Alaska was even a state. I can hold my own. I just need you to grab us a flashlight.”
Rachel clutched Miss Cecelia’s arm. “What if it’s a person out there?”
Miss Cecelia’s expression never changed once. “Then I hope they’ve made peace with God, ’cause even at my age, I’m a dead shot.”
“I don’t know if this is a good idea,” Rachel whispered. She felt sick to her stomach, like nothing she had eaten in the past 24 hours had been even remotely digested.
Miss Cecelia had one hand on the doorknob, the other on the shotgun. “Honey, you and the Singhs are good people, and I’m mighty thankful you’ve taken me in. Least I can do is repay the favor in this one small way. Now I’m stepping out onto the porch whether you come with me or not. And I’ll understand if you don’t have the stomach for it. I’m telling you I know how to defend my own, but I could do it a lot better if I had some light.”
Before Rachel could force herself to move or even speak, the front door burst open. Rachel let out a scream then saw it was Tic standing in the arctic entrance, his chest just inches away from the shotgun barrel.
Rachel pushed him against the door and jumped between Miss Cecelia and her son.
“Mom?”
Rachel couldn’t help it. At the sound of his voice, she started to sob. She had always hated guns, never understood that ridiculous Alaskan fixation with firearms. When the kids were little, Caleb grumbled at the way she insisted everything was locked up and stored out of reach, how the ammo had to be kept in a separate location (also locked up). 
And now, just two days into a prolonged power outage, she and ancient Miss Cecelia had almost … They could have … 
“What in the world were you doing out there at this hour?” Rachel demanded, angrily swatting away her tears. She’d much rather be furious than scared. Scared at what could have happened to her only son. The one Caleb had left her home to protect.
“I had to get something from the garage,” Tic answered defensively, eyeing the shotgun in Miss Cecelia’s hands. “Is everything okay?”
“We heard noises. And you’re lucky you came in when you did, because we were about to go out there and …” Again, tears sprang to her eyes, and she furiously worked to compose herself. Tic wouldn’t take her seriously at all if she were a blubbering mess.
She fixed her expression and demanded once more, “What were you doing out there?”
“I just …” Tic couldn’t meet her gaze. “It was nothing. I couldn’t sleep. Just forget about it.”
Forget about it? Had those words seriously just escaped her sixteen-year-old boy’s lips? He goes outside, terrifies her into thinking there’s an intruder, could have gotten himself shot and killed, and she’s supposed to forget about it? She gave him an icy glare and refused to look away.
“I’ll just put this back in its place.” Miss Cecelia tucked the shotgun back on the top shelf of the closet and excused herself. 
Rachel and Tic stood squared off in the arctic entrance. “What were you doing out there?” 
Her mind raced through all the possibilities. She and Caleb didn’t drink or smoke. As far as she knew, there wasn’t anything in the garage he could have gotten into to get in trouble. Could he have been meeting somebody? She knew he had a crush on AJ at school, but surely she wouldn’t walk three miles here in the dark and the cold to see him.
Then again …
She stared at her son’s face, looking for tell-tale signs of guilt or embarrassment or even a smudge of lipstick. Anything that could help her figure out what he had gotten into.
He was still staring at his feet when he mumbled, “I’m sorry I scared you.”
“Scared me? You could have gotten yourself …” She realized her voice had risen to a slightly hysterical level and forced herself to whisper. “You could have gotten yourself shot.”
“I was just messing around in the garage.”
“Doing what, exactly?”
Again, he couldn’t meet her gaze. “It was nothing.”
“Nothing doesn’t get a teenage boy out of bed in the middle of the night in the middle of winter in the middle of a power outage. Nothing doesn’t make so much ruckus that Miss Cecelia even without her hearing aids came out with a loaded weapon to see if someone was trying to break in.”
“I’m sorry,” he repeated, as if that were enough to make Rachel forget all her worries and go straight back to bed.
“Everything all right out here?” Dev called out from the back room where he and his wife and daughter were supposed to be sleeping.
“We’re fine,” Rachel answered. 
Great, now the Singhs would see firsthand that she couldn’t control her son.
“I’m going upstairs,” she hissed. “If you want to come up and tell me the truth and let me know what you were really doing out there tonight, you know where to find me.”
Without giving Tic another chance to obfuscate anymore or time to come up with some lie or excuse, she stormed up the stairs, furious at the ridiculousness of this entire situation. No sixteen-year-old goes out to the garage in secret at this time of night unless he’s up to no good. 
And what was she supposed to do? Ground him? From what? Playing his guitar — the only thing he possessed that hadn’t been destroyed in the outage? She flopped on her bed in a huff. She hated that she’d cried in front of him. It made her feel weak. She wasn’t weak. She was outraged. If he did anything stupid and got himself hurt …
A timid knock at the door. “Mom?”
She let out her breath, feeling her mother’s heart melt just for a moment at the sound of his contrite voice.
“Can I come in?”
“Yeah.” She let out a heavy sigh.
Tic stood at the foot of her bed, his eyes still downcast. Whatever he had to say, she needed to be ready. When the kids were little, she and Caleb had always tried to reward them for telling the truth. There may still be consequences for wrong behavior, but if they confessed right away, their punishment might not be as harsh.
No matter what happened, she needed to remember that the past two days had been just as hard on Tic as on her. The world had changed literally overnight. They had buried a friend, and their home was filled with extra bodies. They had no word from Yaya and were both worried about Caleb, both trying to keep from wondering why he hadn’t called in on the radio …
“I, um, I didn’t mean to scare you. I hope you know that.” Tic shifted his weight from one foot to another.
“Yeah, I know.”
He let out a small, nervous chuckle. “Was Miss Cecelia really going to come outside with the shotgun?”
Rachel didn’t return his laugh. “Yes.”
“Oh.” He continued to stare at the floor. “I guess that makes sense then why you were so worried.”
“I was,” she admitted. Worried. Furious. Terrified …
“Well, I know now it was a stupid idea, but I couldn’t sleep. You said something tonight at church, and I just kept thinking about it. Dev asked what each of us would miss about the old life if things don’t go back to normal soon, and you talked about not being able to listen to music whenever you wanted?”
Rachel hadn’t known what type of confession or excuse to expect when Tic came upstairs to her room, but it certainly wasn’t this.
He reached into the pocket of his hoodie and pulled out a small box. “It got me thinking,” he went on. “What you said about music? And how we can’t stream anymore and we don’t have tape players or CDs and stuff? And then I remembered seeing this in the garage, so I went out to look, and …”
He handed her a small box. “I found this for you. I thought you might like it.”
She knew exactly what it was, and if it wasn’t for how embarrassed she’d made herself by crying earlier, she might have given way to the lump in her throat and gladly released the tears that threatened to spill down her cheeks. 
She cracked the box open, and a little ballerina in a pink tutu popped up while a mechanical lullaby began to play.
“It doesn’t have batteries,” Tic told her with a beam in his eye and a hint of pride in his voice. “So I thought you could play it whenever you missed music. As long as the gears keep working, it won’t ever go bad.”
She reached out her arms and gave Tic a hug. With her cheek pressed against his shoulder, she allowed a few tears to stream down her face.
“It’s the kindest thing in the world. Where did you find it?”
“In the boxes of baby stuff. I was digging in there earlier looking for things Leo might like. I saw it then but didn’t think much about it until what you said at church. I know I should have waited for the morning, but I couldn’t sleep and …”
“It’s a perfect gift.” She turned her gaze away before she got even more emotional. 
“Just probably not perfect enough to get shot over, right?” Tic flashed her a mischievous grin.
It wasn’t a joke she thought was particularly funny, but she made herself laugh anyway, if only from relief that Tic hadn’t been in the garage doing drugs or any of the other horrible things she’d envisioned.
“So, um, in the morning I guess I’ll apologize to Miss Cecelia too?”
“That’s not a bad idea. I’m just glad you’re safe. And as scared as I got, I do love my gift. Thank you so much.”
“You’re welcome.”
“Now you better try to get some sleep,” Rachel said.
“I’ll try.” Tic leaned over and gave her one more hug. “Good night, Mom.”
“Good night, sweetie.”

***

Caleb woke up with that groggy sensation of a dream that vanishes a moment before you can summon it to your full awareness. 
Rachel had been there. He remembered that much, knew that was why he felt a sense of lingering peace before the chill and the ache in his bones and body fully set in.
He jerked himself awake, terrified that he had slept for too long and let the engine freeze. Without a block heater, if the cold made the oil too thick to circulate, the car that had taken so long yesterday to repair would be a total loss.
Somewhere in the back of his mind, he had a vague recollection of Griz’s gruff voice speaking to him in the middle of the night with uncharacteristic gentleness, telling him to get some sleep. Still, his anxiety wouldn’t subside until he turned the key in the ignition and finally let out his breath when the engine turned over. The heater kicked on, spraying out old dust and noise pollution, if not yet any actual warmth. Caleb lifted up a prayer of genuine thanksgiving. For this car, this heater, for a traveling companion who knew how to fix a cracked coolant hose with duct tape and paracord.
He glanced at the empty seat beside him. Where had Griz wandered off to? 
A happy, joyful bark carried over the engine sounds, and he looked out the window to see Arsenal and Griz wrestling beside a modest fire. Griz had wrapped a colorful scarf around his forearm and was dangling its end in front of Arsenal like a Spanish matador. Arsenal responded by rearing up on two legs, grabbing the scarf, and shaking Griz’s entire arm so aggressively it was a wonder he didn’t pull it out of its socket. But his tail never stopped wagging, and his happy bow in between jumps made him look more like a harmless puppy at play. 
Caleb was reluctant to intrude on this tender private moment. But of course Griz would have heard the car starting. Caleb bundled himself up and went out to wish his friend a good morning. By the time he was out of the vehicle, Griz’s expression looked as stoic as ever except for his one eye, which carried the hint of a gleam. Exhausted from the exertion, Arsenal lay several feet away from the fire, rubbing his back against the snow and panting loudly.
“Ptarmigan?” Griz gestured a makeshift spit, where two plucked birds were beginning to brown and crisp over the open flames.
It was a dry and grainy breakfast, but the fire began to thaw out the ache in Caleb’s entire body, and the meat settled contentedly in his stomach even if the same couldn’t be said for his taste buds.
“You shoot these?” Caleb asked once his brain had woken up a little more.
Griz shook his head. “Saw some prints last night. Decided to put out a couple snares while you were getting your beauty sleep.” 
It was the first time Caleb could remember Griz making a deliberate joke. Griz tossed part of the carcass to Arsenal, who had been drooling so much the snow around him was a melted puddle.
Once fed and relatively warmed up, it was time to resume the task of getting the station wagon out of the snowbank. Thankfully, this was something Caleb had been able to help dozens of people with over the years: Rachel, who got her Jeep stuck in the driveway at least a few times every winter when the snow was deep and the conditions were icy; Yaya, who had an uncanny knack for getting stuck nearly anywhere, especially during her first winter driving; and various of his colleagues from church and school, especially the new teachers who came up from the Lower 48 looking for a great Alaskan adventure and were totally unprepared for their first real taste of winter.
He and Griz systematically and wordlessly performed every task on the checklist. Shoveling to clear even more space around the tires. Laying down gravel from a sandbag (a staple in any true Alaskan’s emergency vehicle kit). Caleb slowly pumping the gas and rocking the wheel from side to side while Griz pushed from behind.
By the time the sun was starting to rise over the valley, the men had burned through all the calories the ptarmigan had provided, a gallon and a half of gas since they went to bed last night, and still they were no closer to getting the car out of the bank.
“We need to trample down this snow,” Griz said. 
He grabbed the sled from the trunk and let out one sharp whistle. Arsenal, who was lying contentedly in the snow by the fire and chewing on an aspen branch, sauntered over and looked up at him.
Griz looked over to Caleb. “About time he earned his keep.”
Arsenal, who less than an hour earlier had been bounding playfully like a puppy a tenth his size, was now the epitome of focus and determination as his owner hooked up his harness to a sled laden down with heavy duffel bags. With only a single word and a few gestures from his master, Arsenal began dragging the sled back and forth across the snow behind the car. 
Caleb, who had been watching without hope that Griz’s plan would bear fruit, started to wonder if he had underestimated both Griz and his dog.
“Smart animal,” Caleb observed.
Griz simply shrugged. “Only thing he’s good for.”
It took a few more shovelfuls of gravel and more maneuvering and manpower than Caleb’s back was happy with, but by the time the morning light was spreading across the sky and the flames from their fire were beginning to dwindle, the station wagon was finally freed from its icy trap.
“You wanna drive some?” Caleb asked, stepping out from behind the wheel.
Griz shook his head. “Did you teach your kids how to ride a bike?”
“Of course,” Caleb answered, with no clear idea how this conversational turn came about.
“And what was the first rule if they fell off and got themselves hurt?” Griz continued.
Caleb grinned. “Don’t tell Mom.” He was the only one to chuckle, but he understood Griz’s point. After last night’s crash, if he didn’t get back behind that wheel now, it would be that much harder to do so the next time.
Caleb buckled himself in and waited for Griz to do the same. He held his breath as the station wagon slid over the last snow berm then picked up speed to resume its course along the Richardson. The early-morning sun lit up their way from behind, and snow crystals on the spruce trees sparkled in the glow. 
Arsenal, noticing some ptarmigan gristle in Caleb’s long beard, kept trying to lean over the driver’s seat to get a better smell or taste.
“Stupid animal,” Griz huffed.
Caleb glanced at the clock. In an hour and a half, they’d reach Delta. He could finally leave a message for his anxious wife so she could stop worrying so much about him, and hopefully he could stop worrying so much about her.
Then, if everything went according to plan, they’d arrive in Fairbanks by evening. Which was admittedly a big if. Griz’s patch-up job under the hood of Miss Cecelia’s artifact on wheels was better than what Caleb could have done by himself, but still it would take a miracle for the station wagon to last all the way to Fairbanks and back home again.
Then again, didn’t Caleb believe in miracles? He thought about the Israelites wandering in the desert, how for forty years their sandals didn’t wear out. Maybe if he prayed hard enough for this decade-old station wagon, God would decide to stretch their miles like he had the Israelites’ shoes. Or maybe it didn’t really depend on his prayers at all. Didn’t the Bible teach that the Lord knows what we need even before we ask? Questions of God’s sovereignty had been crashing around in Caleb’s mind for the past few days, the theoretical now made real as he ventured into a world he’d never dreamed of navigating. 
Since God knew the crash was going to happen, that meant it must be part of his master plan. But how could Fairbanks burning to the ground and Yaya being alone and terrified bring glory to a God of love and hope and compassion?
Unfortunately, Caleb hadn’t gotten much sleep last night, and his brain wasn’t alert enough for theological debates. Still, he needed something to keep his mind off his anxiety or it would start to spiral out of control. There wasn’t a single song on the mixtape that wouldn’t remind Caleb too acutely of home, so he turned to Griz. 
“So you grew up in the Copper River area?” he asked.
Griz grunted an affirmative reply.
“And then the Marines, right out of high school?”
“Yup.”
This conversation was off to a great start.
“What about your family? Any brothers or sisters?”
“Haven’t talked to them in twenty years.”
“Oh.”
Griz’s confession got Caleb thinking about Tic and Yaya. When Yaya was just three and a half, she’d been so excited to welcome home her little baby brother, a bliss that only lasted until she realized that his colicky cries would interrupt her playtime and keep her parents from giving her their full attention.
She hadn’t been a resentful big sister, not really, just... disinterested.
Caleb couldn’t understand it. Not at all. Growing up as a single child, he’d longed for siblings. Felt like something had been intrinsically missing from his life when he watched movies about large, happy families. Even seeing the neighbor boys fight with each other at the bus stop made him jealous. 
Still, he knew that his son and daughter loved each other in their own way. Tic was fiercely protective, taking on the role of an older brother despite being the “baby.” And quiet, studious Yaya never once complained when so many hours of her high school years were spent driving Tic around to sports practices, school events, and friends’ houses.
They had just passed the small town of Paxson, with its single row of tiny cabins. Caleb wondered how many people were out here right now, and how many only came out here to their lakeside property in the summer. Smoke rose from one single chimney, a stark reminder of their isolation but also a beacon of humanity in the midst of this wintery wasteland. 
He slowed down when he spotted a truck in the road. This wasn’t the first stalled vehicle they’d come across, but it was the only one that would be difficult to pass. 
On one side was the hillside covered in snowy embankments. Caleb didn’t want to get stuck a second time, but to pass on the left only gave him the equivalent of three-quarters of a lane before he’d be practically driving out onto the frozen lake.
He slowed to a near crawl, hoping that whoever stalled out here had found shelter in that cabin with a fireplace. 
He was right behind the truck when a rock hit his windshield. White snow splattered on the glass. 
No, not a rock. Was that a snowball?
Griz tensed in his seat. “Back up,” he demanded. “Now.”
Before Caleb could respond, two men jumped in front of the car and one to the side, all brandishing rifles aimed right at them.
It was an ambush.
 


18. UNDER ATTACK

The sun was rising, but still Rachel couldn’t pull herself out of bed. She knew the moment she pulled back her cocoon of blankets, the cold in her bedroom would be unbearable. 
Just not nearly as unbearable as going to bed without any word from Caleb and waking up without him beside her.
She wanted to pray but couldn’t. She felt the dreaded onset of the bleak dark blackness that could sometimes swallow her whole. She hadn’t fallen victim to it in years, not since the kids were tiny, but now…
Cammy lay with her head on Rachel’s chest, as if her only job was to keep Rachel pinned down with her love. She always grew protective of anyone in the family who was sick.
The difference was that Rachel wasn’t sick. Not today. At least not physically. She was cold and she was tired and she was dejected, her brain so foggy she couldn’t even think a single cohesive thought, let alone try to pray.
Any other day, she would simply tune into KWJD and let Dev’s Devos or the Christian music fuel and encourage her soul. Or she’d stream one of her favorite worship playlists, check in on one of her favorite podcasts, or listen to her audio Bible. Now, she couldn’t do any of these things. She couldn’t even turn on her phone to see what time it was.
There were noises drifting up from the rest of the house: Angel clanging pots and pans around in the kitchen she had basically commandeered, baby Leo fussing, Miss Cecelia’s gruff voice surprisingly soft as she tried to soothe him. Even Tic and Benji had pulled themselves out of bed and were laughing at something hilarious.
Just a few more minutes, Rachel told herself.
And then she remembered Buster in Delta. Of course. Within seconds, she was tying the sash of her fuzzy bathrobe and racing downstairs.
“Has anyone tried the radio this morning?”
Tic looked over at her and smiled wryly. “Good morning to you too,” he teased.
“Good morning.” Rachel hurried over to the coffee table. By now, she knew every knob and switch of that small ham radio by heart.
“Buster?” She was practically shouting into the receiver. “Buster, are you there? Do you have a copy?”
There was silence for a moment. Too long. Maybe it hadn’t gone through. Maybe she drained the batteries yesterday when she couldn’t bring herself to put the radio down or go to sleep.
She was about to try her transmission again when a voice came on the air.
“Haven’t heard Buster check in today.”
Rachel stared at the clock on the living room wall. What time did he usually get up? Was he late? Delayed? Had something happened in Delta?
“I’m waiting for word from my husband.” Her voice came out frantic and panicked. “He was traveling yesterday from Glennallen to Delta and was supposed to radio in last night.”
“You must be Rachel,” the man said. “I thought I recognized your voice.”
She couldn’t care less if this man knew who she was. She only cared if he could tell her what happened to her husband.
“Do you have any word about two men traveling to Delta in an old station wagon?” she asked.
“I’m down here in Eagle River, ma’am. Afraid I can’t be much help to you. Buster is usually getting on sometime hereabouts. I recommend you just check in later.”
Check in later. If Rachel ever wrote an autobiography, that may as well be its title.
Her hands shook as she held the receiver. “Okay, thank you.”
She sensed multiple eyes staring at her. She reached out with an unsteady hand and flipped the radio off. The switch filled the room with echoes of a heavy, disheartening thunk.

***

“Hit the gas!” Griz ordered, but the sight of three guns aimed at his head and Griz’s frantic commands muddled Caleb’s reflexes. 
In what appeared like one fluid motion, Griz had simultaneously grabbed his sidearm and with his other hand snatched a rifle from the back seat and tossed it onto Caleb’s lap.
“Move!”
But Caleb couldn’t. There wasn’t room to get around both the truck and the men.
“Floor it!” Griz snapped, and Caleb realized that his friend wanted him to plow straight into living human beings.
There was a grotesque standoff as one of the men tapped on the passenger window, his face contorted in a lopsided grin that showed a large gap between his front teeth.
Griz rolled his window down. “Help you boys?”
“Nice wagon.” The man patted the top of the car like a used car salesman showing off its sturdiness.
“How about you two get out and hand over the keys, and nobody will get hurt.”
Everything that happened next happened instantaneously. Griz fired. The gap-toothed man fell back as the explosion rippled through the cabin. His colleagues started shooting wildly, and Caleb, finally snapped out of his paralysis, slammed on the gas, bracing himself for a sickening impact, and peeled past the truck.
Shots rang out from behind, but he didn’t slow down to look in the rearview mirror. He raced ahead, his heart racing erratically, his brain caught in some type of loop while trying unsuccessfully to process everything that had just happened. 
The highway meandered but Caleb raced ahead, taking each turn at speeds he wouldn’t have dared even in the summer without any ice on the roads. At every new bend, he was certain another shot would ring out and kill him. Or Griz. Or both.
“They’re gone. You can slow down now,” Griz told him, while Arsenal, on heightened alert, raced from one back window to the other, panting loudly, ready to fight off trouble when it came.
Now it was Caleb who couldn’t find the words to say. What had they both done?
Caleb always prided himself on his ability to think quickly on his feet. It was what allowed him to manage classrooms full of unruly middle-school students and was how he’d gotten that black eye breaking up a fistfight in the lunchroom. No matter the conflict, Caleb had never frozen in place before.
But this ambush was nothing like a cafeteria brawl or anything else he’d ever experienced. Thinking about fight or flight, Caleb always assumed that as a strong man with strong protective instincts, he would jump into fight mode whenever the situation warranted (exhibit A: the black eye from school).
He had no idea how Griz sensed the danger so much earlier than he had. In those critical seconds when Caleb still had the time to back the car up or speed forward before the men blocked the road, he’d done nothing. 
Absolutely nothing. 
Like a deer in headlights, except even more helpless. Because Caleb wasn’t a deer. He was a grown man, for crying out loud.
If he had reacted faster, he could have gotten them out of that situation before things escalated. Before Griz…
“Did you shoot that man?” It was a stupid question. Caleb literally held a front-row seat to the gruesome scene that he was certain would replay in his nightmares from now on. But his glitching brain needed help accepting reality.
“I shot him. And yeah ... I hit him.”
There was no emotion in Griz’s tone, and Caleb wondered how in the world that could be. How you could be driving down a deserted highway one minute, praying for your wife, and thinking of home, fighting off a dog who won’t get out of your beard, then a moment later you’re in a gunfight? 
A man was shot. Possibly killed. And the two others? Caleb hadn’t felt the station wagon hit either of them when he slammed on the gas, but it was a snowy road. Anything he’d thought was a pothole or frost heave could have been something else. 
Couldn’t it?
“You all right?” Griz asked, and even Arsenal diverted his attention away from growling at the window to give Caleb’s shoulder a gentle nudge with his nose.
Caleb could only shake his head as Paxson Lake disappeared in the rearview mirror. “No. I’m not all right at all.”
Speeding down the highway, doing his best to put the miles and the terror as far behind him as possible, Caleb doubted he would ever be all right again. 

***

The moment Rachel stopped moving and started thinking, she’d crumple. Caleb should have reached Buster’s last night. Even if he’d arrived in Delta after dark, he could have gotten to Buster’s first thing in the morning and let her know he was okay.
Which meant he wasn’t okay.
Any other time, she would have texted him, and if he didn’t answer, she would have called. If that didn’t work, she’d use the Find My Friends app and pinpoint exactly where he was. 
The fear and worry would debilitate her if she gave in to even a moment of anxiety. Instead, she spent all morning downstairs with Angel and Priyah, inventorying everything in the freezer, then the pantries, and coming up with a comprehensive plan to feed ten people over the next three months with nothing but what they had here in their home.
And yes, Rachel was planning on feeding ten: the four Singhs, Miss Cecelia, all four members of her family, plus Griz if he agreed to stay. Because in spite of Caleb’s silence, she refused to believe that God wouldn’t bring her husband and daughter home safe and sound. And soon.
It didn’t make sense that the Lord would provide them a gift like Miss Cecelia’s station wagon if he didn’t intend for them to use it to bring Yaya safely home. With a stubborn hope fueling her resolve, she even pulled out the ingredients for biscuits and gravy, Yaya’s favorite breakfast, to have ready for her first morning back.
Whenever that miraculous day might come …
Priyah had inherited her father’s engineering brain and was focused on making what she called an “analog spreadsheet” (which was really just a chart) where she listed all their meals, ingredients, and best-by dates. They needed to use up all the fresh produce and dairy and food in the fridge first. The basement was cool enough that everything in the freezer remained frozen solid. Once the food began to thaw, they would have to act quickly. The salmon and meat would end up getting eaten right away or canned. The make-ahead freezer meals and Ziploc bags of soup Rachel stored up would add variety to a diet that would consist of a lot of pastas, rice, and beans. It might be tricky figuring out how to cook a frozen lasagna on the stovetop, but they’d make it work.
While the women were organizing the food stores, Tic and Benji drove the snow machines to and from the Singhs’ home, gathering shelf-stable foods and perishables that needed to be used up before going bad. Miss Cecelia was upstairs with the baby, and Dev was checking on neighbors and spreading the news around town that the food pantry would be open this afternoon for anybody in need. 
They were all busy, which was exactly what Rachel needed to keep her mind distracted.
Her husband hadn’t radioed in. She didn’t know where her daughter was. But her family wouldn’t starve.
“I feel like I’m nesting again,” Angel mused. “Two weeks before Priyah’s due date, Dev came home and I had painted the nursery, baked six dozen muffins, reorganized every single book and toy and article of clothing Benji owned, and started crocheting a baby blanket.”
Rachel was thinking it sounded no different than Angel’s regular flurry of domestic activity, pregnancy or not, but didn’t say so.
“You made me a baby blanket?” Priyah asked, her eyes widening.
Angel chuckled. “I started to, but I went into labor that very night. I don’t think your father ever forgave me for the scare I gave him, either. What about you?” She turned to Rachel. “Did you nest with your two?”
Rachel wished the conversation hadn’t taken this turn. “My pregnancies weren’t what you’d call easy,” she admitted. “If I got nesting instincts, I wasn’t well enough to act on them.”
Angel’s frown showed all the right signs of sympathy, but Rachel knew her friend would never understand. “Were you on bed rest?” she asked.
No, Rachel hadn’t been on bed rest, but it sounded like a heavenly alternative to what she actually went through. What she experienced during her pregnancies wasn’t a problem with her body, but her mind.
“Pregnancy hormones and I weren’t a very good match,” was all Rachel answered, refusing to describe the bleakness and despair, followed by so much nothingness. It was the nothingness that terrified her the most. But there was no way to explain it to someone like Angel, who took to motherhood and mothering so gracefully and naturally, she may have waltzed straight out of Proverbs 31.
“That’s hard,” Angel admitted. “Your family’s from here, right? Were they around to help?”
“No. My mom died when I was in high school, and my dad right after I got married. Thankfully, Caleb was home on summer break during the last trimester of my pregnancy with Tic or else Yaya might’ve starved or died from neglect.”
Angel let out a polite chuckle, and Rachel didn’t bother explaining she was serious.
“Mom,” Priyah asked, “what’s nesting mean?”
Rachel was relieved for the shift away from those nightmarish years.
“Well,” Angel said, immediately adopting her homeschool mother tone, “God designed women so that sometimes during the last part of their pregnancy, right before the baby comes, they get an extra dose of strength and energy that makes them want to do things to get ready for a newborn. So maybe they’ll bake a bunch of freezer meals or get all caught up on all the laundry or change the sheets on all the beds, things that will make their life easier after they’ve given birth and don’t have time anymore to think about stuff like that because they’re so busy taking care of the little bundle of joy God’s given them.”
“Was I an easy baby?” Priyah asked.
“Yes.” Angel sighed. “You were a delightful little baby. You slept like a cherub and you had adorably squishy cheeks and your brother loved you so, so much. You were going to share a room with Daddy and me, but as soon as you grew out of your bassinet, Benji threw a fit until we set up your crib next to his bed. Some mornings, I’d wake up and he crawled in with you in the middle of the night, and you’d both be tucked under your blankets, just like two perfect little angels. It was the most precious sight for any mother’s heart.”
Rachel winced at her friend’s words, still unable to accept the way she had lost so many of those sweet, cherished moments she could have had with her own children. Her difficult pregnancies had turned into full-fledged postpartum depression, the kind that didn’t lift until the kids were riding tricycles and attending preschool. She was so thankful for the healing that God had brought her through, for how much healthier her body and mind were. Now, she might lose a week here and there to the despair, but not entire years of her life. 
But the questions still remained: Why had God allowed her to go through such bleak suffering to begin with, to allow her to feel such desolation and abandonment?
And what could Rachel do now if that same oppressive darkness came and tried to swallow her once more?

***

Caleb was fairly certain he might eventually die of a heart attack. He just didn’t expect it to happen today.
Overnight, the snow had blown in drifts across the Richardson. With no DOT snowplows to clear the highway or tracks from other vehicles to follow, he was driving blind through several inches of snow on top of a sheet of ice … and all of that while his chest was constricting so hard he could hear himself wheeze.
“Pull over,” Griz told him.
“I can’t.” They had to get to Delta. He had to reach that radio.
And he had to get more distance between himself and those men…
“Pull over. Now.”
There was a large snow-covered turnout just up ahead, but Caleb would have driven straight past if Griz hadn’t turned the wheel himself.
“What are you doing?” Now Caleb was convinced that if a heart attack didn’t get him first, another crash would.
“You need to calm down,” Griz said once the car was parked at that scenic outlook tourist trap.
“I don’t want to calm down,” Caleb snapped. What did Griz expect? For Caleb to take a few deep breaths, imagine himself on some beach, and then completely forget everything that happened?
Now that he didn’t have the road to occupy his thoughts, the panic rolling up inside his chest grew even more fierce, the vise-grip on his lungs ever tighter. Suffocating …
Their attackers, at least those who survived, could be right behind them. He and Griz couldn’t afford to stop here like sitting ducks, just waiting for a second ambush. And if a town like Paxson with fewer than a hundred residents had already devolved into that sort of violence, what lay ahead of them in Delta? What was Yaya already experiencing in Fairbanks?
His body was shaking. Why was it so cold, even with the heater blasting? Why couldn’t he catch his breath?
Griz opened the car door, letting in an explosion of freezing air. What was he thinking?
While Griz fumbled around in the back seat, Arsenal licked Caleb’s hand and then his beard. Canine affection was the last thing he wanted right now, but Caleb didn’t have the energy to push him away.
His head was light, throbbing, and painful all at once. But when he shut his eyes, the certainty that their attackers were right behind them grew even more fierce and unshakable.
“Here.” Griz opened the driver’s side door and gestured for Caleb to get out. “I’ll drive,” was all he said.
Like a nurse helping a semi-invalid, Griz took his arm and led him over to the passenger side. Once Caleb sat down, Griz wrapped a scarf around his neck and ears and tucked an unzipped sleeping bag around his body until he felt like a suffocated mummy.
He had to cough, but he couldn’t. He had to breathe, but each breath took in less and less actual oxygen.
“I think I’m dying.”
“You’re not dying.” Griz shut Caleb’s door and walked around to the driver’s seat. He turned up the heater.
Did he think this was a simple case of mild hypothermia? “I’m having a heart attack,” Caleb told him.
Griz shook his head, but didn’t look over at him. “No, you’re having a panic attack. And you’re in shock.”
“You shot that man.” Maybe if he repeated himself, the words might finally make sense. “You just looked straight at him and shot him.”
“Yup. Here. Drink this. And small sips.” Griz thrust a water bottle at him but didn’t start driving yet. 
Why weren’t they moving?
“You shot him.”
“Drink it.”
A few minutes later, Caleb could take a shallow breath without feeling like it would explode his lungs. His chest was still tight, but not to the point that he worried he would go into cardiac arrest at any second out here in the middle of nowhere.
“Bright, clear day.” Griz didn’t seem the type to make inane comments about the weather. 
But like an obedient child, Caleb looked out the window at the expansive, snowy valley lying out before them, with majestic snow-covered mountains shattering the horizon.
No wonder tourists stopped here to take so many pictures.
“A little better now?” Griz asked.
“Physically, at least.”
The facsimile of a soft smile warmed Griz’s countenance. “It takes time to adapt to violence.”
“I didn’t have …” Caleb stammered. “And you were there … and the sound …” His body recoiled.
Griz didn’t say anything. Just took the car out of park and pulled back onto the highway.
“I didn’t think it’d be like this,” Caleb admitted a few minutes later. “I didn’t think it’d be so …” 
He couldn’t find the vocabulary but sensed Griz understood.
“You’re a middle-school teacher, not a soldier.” 
In another context, the words may have sounded derisive, but Griz spoke them with surprising gentleness. Even more surprising was when he continued talking. “Tell me something.”
“What?”
“You have firearms to protect your home, right?”
“Of course.” Caleb didn’t even need to answer. Griz had helped him pack his .44 Magnum revolver, and he was there when Caleb gave Tic instructions on how to use the AR-15.
“And why do you have them?” Griz asked.
