Caleb’s response could have been sarcastic. Because I’m no different than any other homeowner in Alaska. But instead, he simply spoke the truth. “I want to be able to protect my family.”
“Protect them from what?” Griz was easier to get along with when he was completely silent.
“From everything. Bears. Burglars. Everything.”
Griz nodded but kept his eyes on the road. “And when you gave your son your AR before you left, what did you tell him?”
“I told him that if the time came to use it, he needs to be man enough to pull the trigger. To protect himself, or his mom, or our home.” The words hurt as they came out of Caleb’s throat, but he ignored the discomfort.
“And if your son one day has to shoot an intruder, how do you think he’s going to feel?” Griz asked.
Caleb didn’t want to answer and let his frustration roil over. “I don’t want him to have to use it. I want him to be able to.”
“But if he did have to use it,” Griz prodded, “and if he discharged it in an act that saved his mother, you wouldn’t want him to feel guilty about it, right?”
“No. But he still would.”
“Why?”
Caleb was so frustrated by the torrent of incessant questions he let himself explode. “Because weapons are meant to be a deterrent. Nobody wants to actually use them to hurt another human being. Because it does something to you. We’re not meant to live like that. We live in a civilized society.”
At least we used to…
“If I hadn’t shot that man,” Griz continued, his voice infuriatingly steady and emotionless, “what do you think would have happened next?”
“I don’t know.” Caleb was sulking now. He was no soothsayer.
“Give it your best guess.”
“They would’ve stolen the car.”
“Or …”
“They would’ve killed us both and then stolen the car.”
“And …”
Caleb let out a heavy sigh. “And we wouldn’t make it to Fairbanks. We couldn’t bring Yaya home. And Rachel …” Here he couldn’t contain his voice. Couldn’t control his emotions.
Couldn’t stop the heaving sobs.
Griz was quiet, just kept driving down the relentless highway.
Arsenal nuzzled Caleb’s shoulder until he gave the dog some pets. The touch was comforting, soothing, and he let his fingers sink into the dog’s deep, thick fur.
As the car began its slow ascent up the unruly mountain pass, Caleb’s tears slowed, and his hiccupping sobs turned into slow, choppy breaths.
“The world isn’t like it was three days ago,” Griz declared. “Everything has changed now.”
“I don’t know what I would’ve done if I had run over one of those men,” Caleb said.
“You would sit in this car, thankful for a working vehicle. And you would live for another day, able to protect your daughter and bring her home safe and sound.”
The words sounded true in Caleb’s brain, but in his shattered, shaking body, they rang false and hollow.

***

Another fifteen miles up the road, with the terror of Paxson that much farther behind them, Caleb felt his mind gradually release its heightened sense of extreme vigilance. His body, still not convinced it was safe to relax, at least allowed him to breathe more readily, to release the strain in his jaw that had turned into a near-crippling tension headache.
By the time they reached the top of the pass, snow and mountain and crystal-clear sky stretched out in an almost endless panorama. And in spite of everything they had endured since starting out on their trip, Caleb found his soul drawn toward gratitude and awe — gratitude that they had a working vehicle, they had their safety, and that even with the world below in complete and utter chaos, the majestic mountains and valleys and endless winter landscape remained completely untouched and unblemished. The birds that flew up here had no idea that the power grid had been destroyed. The pregnant bear and moose who would deliver their young in the spring were oblivious to the fact that cities were burning, that the government had been taken out in a single instant, that humanity and history had been completely and inexorably changed.
None of that mattered up here, where the spruce trees glistened with icy frost and the snow covered everything in the valley below like a white protective blanket. Even the air looked somehow crisper, brighter, cleaner. If an environmentalist wanted to find a single spot in the world untouched by humanity’s footprint, they simply had to come up here.
“I need you to pull over,” Caleb said as they passed another tourist vista.
“Taking pictures with your phone?” Griz asked wryly.
“No. My gut…” Caleb didn’t need to say any more. This was his third pit stop since Paxson, thanks to his unrelenting nerves. Before long, Caleb’s quads would be even stronger than Tic’s for all the squatting he’d done.
He grabbed their roll of toilet paper from the glove compartment, and when he opened his door, Arsenal took the opportunity to dash out with him into the snow.
“Guess he has to go, too,” Caleb observed.
But Arsenal wasn’t sniffing or circling the ground, looking for the most perfect place to relieve himself. Instead, he stood completely still, every muscle in his body erect, like a pointer dog standing at perfect attention.
“What’s wrong with him?” Caleb asked when Arsenal began trembling, but there was no one in the car to hear him. Griz had already gotten out and stood several paces ahead, staring off into the distance like his dog.
“What is it?” Caleb asked just before he heard a thunderous crack split the still morning air.
He ducked down instinctively, certain that their assailants from Paxson had somehow followed them up the winding mountain.
But it wasn’t a gunshot.
Arsenal let out a reverberating howl, and at first, Caleb thought the entire sound was echoing through the canyon. But the thunderous roar that answered back couldn’t have been made by any living creature.
Arsenal whined, tucked his tail, and raced back into the car, nearly knocking Caleb down in his race for shelter. Caleb’s body stood frozen, his mind on high alert.
And then he saw it.
A plume of dust or cloud or smoke. Snow rising up like mist and then the entire mountain peak in the distance crumpling to the ground, slowly at first, then gathering speed at a terrifying rate.
“Avalanche,” Griz announced, but showed no sign of fear, alarm, or any other emotion whatsoever. Caleb alternated between envying and detesting his friend’s inhuman calm.
“Should we get in the car?” he asked, thinking that Arsenal was currently the safest, and therefore the smartest, one here.
“We’re miles away,” Griz declared, nodding ahead. 
Of course, now that Caleb heard the words, it made perfect sense.
Okay, they weren’t in immediate danger. So what next?
Arsenal barked at them from the station wagon. Caleb didn’t know what he and Griz were doing out here, standing like tourists. His body was poised for action. It was time to get back in the car. 
Move. Go. Leave. 
And still Griz kept standing.
Caleb was certain he felt the earth rumble beneath him, but maybe his mind was playing tricks on him. His body, so stuck in fight or flight for the past three days, was exhausted, confused, and cold. The discomfort in his gut had subsided, but now he just wanted to get going.
The movement on the mountainside slowed, the rumbling faded, and all that was visible were billowing snow clouds pluming up from the valley below.
“Griz?” he finally asked.
No response.
He took several steps closer. “Griz?” 
Still nothing.
Standing now nearly shoulder to shoulder, Caleb looked over at his friend. Tears glistened on Griz’s cheek, streaming down from his one good eye.
It felt like hours passed, lifetimes passed, as the two men stared in silence at everything ahead of them — the valley, the snow-crumpled mountain now resting in perfect stillness, the pristine clear sky.
Caleb didn’t feel the cold. His body forgot the shock of everything they had left behind on the Richardson. For a timeless moment of eternity, there was no ambush, there was no power failure, there was no avalanche. Only this mountain, tall and firm and steady as it had been for millennia, as it had been since the day God made it. This mountain had stood here for thousands of years, and here it would remain until the day of Christ’s return.
And the sky, in all its vastness, bore witness as Caleb and Griz stared into the face of eternity. Stared into the face of God himself, who, just like this mountain, was unyielding, unchanging, the same today as he had been when he gave birth to this hallowed piece of earth. He would remain even more glorious and powerful and worthy of awe long after Caleb’s body returned to the dirt and his spirit departed to see his Maker face to face.
He had no idea if Griz was experiencing a similar sense of awestruck wonder or something else entirely. The men never spoke. But at the same moment, perhaps due to some subtle shift in the wind or cues too subconscious for their logical brains to detect, they turned in unison toward the car.
Griz got in the driver’s seat. Caleb buckled up beside him. Arsenal greeted them with a cold nose pressed against both of their cheeks in turn.
Then Griz pulled back onto the highway, and the Richardson stretched endlessly ahead of them.
 


19. DEBRIS

“You ever seen an avalanche close up before?” Caleb asked as they drove through the winding pass. 
Trees blocked the view on all sides, so that if they hadn’t stopped at that pullout, they would have no idea about the catastrophic snowslide just a short time earlier.
“You say something?” Griz turned down the blaring heater.
“I asked if you’ve ever seen an avalanche before.”
“Yeah.” Griz spoke the word with finality, and Caleb didn’t ask for more information.
Hoarfrost on the trees glistened in the sunlight, and Caleb was surprised that the morning had returned to such an unshattered calm so quickly. What he didn’t know was if this calm would last them the entire drive back down the mountain pass.
“Not as windy as you sometimes see it up here,” Caleb commented. Anything to keep the conversation moving, anything to keep the road ahead clear and unobstructed.
What Caleb was really thinking about was what neither man dared speak aloud. Depending on the extent of the avalanche’s damage, they might not make it to Fairbanks today. They might not make it down from this mountain at all.
It was a possibility Caleb refused to contemplate.
“At least we’re not like the hobbits trying to cross the Caradhras pass barefoot in a snowstorm,” he joked.
“What?”
“You know, Lord of the Rings?”
Griz stared back blankly.
Caleb couldn’t believe it. “Tolkien? Peter Jackson? Greatest trilogy of all time?”
Griz just shrugged.
“Sheesh, where were you in the early 2000s?”
“Afghanistan.”
“Oh.” Caleb might have pressed further to learn more about Griz’s military career, but there was no point. He’d been keeping up this inane chatter to distract himself from the terrifying thought that the avalanche might have demolished the one road that could get them to Fairbanks. To Yaya.
As Griz pulled the station wagon to a stop before the endless mountain of snow blocking the Richardson Highway, Caleb had to face the truth.
Griz killed the engine. Arsenal, who’d been dozing in the back seat, perked up and took turns looking out each window. Just like before, Caleb and Griz stared in silence as they took in the devastating power the avalanche carved on the mountainside.
Except this time, there was no awe, no wonder, no sense of lofty grandeur.
Nothing but bleak futility. The road was covered in uneven sheets of snow and massive chunks of ice. Splintered tree trunks, jagged rocks, and boulders jutted out from the destruction like permanent monuments to the disaster.
“Now what do we do?” Caleb dared ask after the silence grew unbearable.
Griz unbuckled his seatbelt. “Now, we dig.”
Caleb was too shocked to respond but simply stared as Griz got out and grabbed the shovel from the back seat.
“Come on, you big Arse,” Griz grunted, and Arsenal happily jumped out of the car.
Caleb remained just as dumbfounded when Griz started attacking the avalanche debris with his snow shovel. What was he thinking? It had taken hours just to get the car out of a five-foot snowbank last night. 
Caleb was already making the mental calculations. If they turned around now, they could get back to Glennallen in a couple hours, fill up on gas, and take the Glenn Highway to Wasilla, then drive the Parks all the way up to Fairbanks. It would more than double the length of their trip, but what other choice was there?
Griz’s work with his twenty-dollar snow shovel was like building a sandcastle one grain of sand at a time. Their food would run out before the task was complete, not to mention their energy. And what about the cold? They couldn’t run the heat in the car every night. They’d run out of gas and freeze to death.
Caleb wondered for just a moment if it was a matter of trust. Did he simply not believe enough? After all, didn’t Jesus himself teach that faith the size of a mustard seed could move a mountain? 
Right now, they didn’t need a mountain moved, just several metric tons of snow …
But Caleb was pretty sure that Griz was acting out of sheer stubbornness, not any type of faith, and he refused to let this well-intentioned man waste his finite energy.
He stormed out of the car. “You’ll never be able to move this much snow.” Caleb hated to be a naysayer, but talking sense into Griz could truly prove to be a matter of survival.
“No other choice,” Griz said, tossing a shovelful of snow aside. “Not if you want to get to your daughter.”
“We can turn around and take the Glenn up to Fairbanks and still get there faster than this.”
Griz shook his head but didn’t slow down. “Too many people. We’d never make it.”
“What do you mean?” Caleb tried not to bristle at his idea being discarded so dismissively. What happened to communication? Discussion? Two men coming to an agreement together?
Griz leaned on his shovel and leveled his gaze at him. “Remember Paxson? That’s a town of fifty. What would happen if we drive through someplace like Wasilla?”
Caleb threw his hands in the air in frustration. “I can’t just leave Yaya in Fairbanks, can I?”
Griz’s eyes narrowed, then softened for a moment. “Help me dig, and you won’t have to.”
Caleb had no idea what to say in response. It wasn’t as if he could leave Griz and Arsenal here with nothing but a snow shovel. Besides, he begrudgingly had to admit that his friend was right. If they barely survived the ambush in Paxson, how much worse would things get in a crowded suburb like Wasilla?
But all that snow and ice and debris … It was impossible. They could never move it. This was a job for bulldozers and excavators and a round-the-clock crew of dozens of trained and skilled men. He and Griz were two middle-aged men, one fit and one overweight, with one standard snow shovel and one plastic piece of junk between the two of them.
It would never work.
Then again, neither would turning back.
Arsenal stood poised at attention. “Grab the sled,” Griz grunted.
And so the team got to work. By late afternoon, Caleb’s face was burning not only from the cold but also the bright sunlight. His back was throbbing, but he learned to ignore the pain.
After a few clumsy starts trying to use both shovels, he and Griz found a workable routine. One would shovel snow and debris onto the sled, and Arsenal would pull it to the other to discard. When they got tired, they’d switch. At this rate, if the men worked nonstop, they might actually manage to clear the highway in a couple months.
“We’re never going to get a path wide enough to drive through,” Caleb noted when the men stopped to eat and give Arsenal a well-earned rest.
“No, we won’t,” Griz agreed.
“Then what are we busting our butts off for?”
“I thought you wanted to get to your daughter.”
“I do. But at this rate, we may as well save our energy and wait for the spring thaw.” 
Caleb did the math in his head. “We’ve been working nonstop for the past four hours, and we’ve cleared what? Five percent of the debris if we’re feeling generous? We’ll run out of food before we even see the other side. And look how narrow this path is. We’ll need to clear at least six feet just to get the car through.”
“We’re not clearing six feet.” Griz spoke with authority.
“And why is that?”
“You said it yourself,” Griz finally conceded. “We can’t dig a path wide enough for the car.”
Caleb wanted to explode. “Then why did we waste half a day working ourselves to the bone?”
“All we need is a path wide enough to walk through,” Griz stated calmly. “The rest of our journey will be on foot.”

***

In the past, Tic’s biggest danger during prolonged power outages was the boredom, but that wasn’t going to be a problem today. Not with his mom in Chief Food Officer mode. He and Benji had spent all morning carting canned goods from the Singhs’ house on sleds, then Mom needed his help moving heavy tubs of rice and beans up from the basement to the kitchen.
At least Mom was polite when she needed him to do something, but now he was at the food bank with Miss Cecelia, who was anything but. They’d been preparing boxes for everyone in the community who stopped by. He didn’t mind the work. Dev had reminded him and Benji several times that morning about Jesus’ command to be a good neighbor, and Tic wasn’t upset about helping out.
He just wished Miss Cecelia could be a little … well, less like Miss Cecelia.
“Tychicus.” She was one of the only people in town who always insisted on using his full name. “When you put the moose in the boxes, you need to make sure it’s fair. If it’s a bigger package of moose, they get a smaller bag of rice.”
“The rice bags all say five pounds,” he said, holding up one of the Ziploc bags.
“That might be what they say, but see? This one looks bigger than that one, so it has to go in with one of the boxes with a smaller package of moose. You have to make them even.”
Even if Miss Cecelia had a point, the discrepancy in food distribution would be measured in grams, not even ounces, but Tic had learned years ago, back when she was his Sunday school teacher, that it was easier to simply listen and do whatever the old woman said.
“And I see you’re limping, young man. You don’t have frostbite on your toe from driving that sled around all morning, do you? I told your mom it wasn’t safe to send two boys out in the cold like that.”
“My foot’s fine, Miss Cecelia.”
“If it’s fine, then why are you limping?”
“Because I have a hangnail.”
That seemed to quiet her up, at least for a little bit. So far, Tic had prepared thirty individual boxes filled with rice, canned goods, and moose meat. He wasn’t sure how thirty families would know to show up here and pick it up without any phones or texts or Facebook posts, but that part wasn’t his problem.
People began shuffling in a short time later, many more than Tic would have expected without any reliable means of communication. Glennallen had a few homes clustered in what you could call neighborhoods, but most people lived on vast acreages out in the middle of nowhere. His family’s house was tucked away and private, but at least within walking distance of the school and the food pantry. 
A lot of the people he knew lived ten or twenty miles out or more. 
Still, they showed up, some on foot, some on snow machines, nearly all with sleds. Tic knew just about all of them by sight if not by name. There was Pastor Tom from church, an old man who looked just as ancient today as he did in the wedding photo Mom had hanging upstairs.
Then came Mrs. Bell, his science teacher. She lived up the hill, he thought, with a husband, and grown kids somewhere in the Lower 48. She was so old, she’d actually taught his mom back when she was a student at the school.
“Hi, Tic.” Her voice was slow and sad when she asked, “How are you and your family holding up?”
“We’re doing okay, Mrs. Bell. How about you?”
She simply sighed and accepted the box.
Tic’s heart did that stupid little flutter-jump-kick that made him feel like an awkward eleven-year-old when AJ Jacobson walked in the front door at the very end of his shift.
“Hi, Tic.” She smiled gently. If she was surprised to see him here, she didn’t show it.
“Hey, AJ.” It wasn’t until she stepped closer to the light from the window he saw her eye and gasped. “What happened?”
AJ didn’t answer. Tic knew what that meant. He’d never actually met AJ’s uncle Dick, but the first time he saw the man lay a finger on her, he’d kill him. He lowered his voice. “You all right?”
She shrugged. “I’m okay. How about you? Is your house warm enough?”
“We have a wood stove. Upstairs gets kinda cold, and the basement is freezing, but the main level is fine.” Tic hated himself for going on and on like that and worried that AJ, who lived with her brother and uncle in an apartment complex behind the school, would think he was flexing about his home. He tried to think of anything else he might say to change the subject. “At least today’s warmer than it’s been.”
AJ just smiled. 
Tic shifted from one foot to another. “So, these are the boxes.” He hefted one easily onto the table. “They come with rice and canned green beans and canned corn, and this one has some applesauce and a box of crackers and a package of half-thawed moose meat. And we’ll be open this same time every Monday, Wednesday, and Friday until the food runs out or the power comes back on.” He gave her a grin.
“Wow, your family put all these together for everyone?”
“It’s from the food bank,” Tic explained. “My family just volunteers here sometimes.” Was that a cringey thing to say? 
He heard voices outside but didn’t want anybody else to come in. Not yet. He leaned closer to her. “Um, AJ?” 
“Yeah?”
“Are you all right? With your uncle, I mean?” He kept his voice low so Miss Cecelia, who was in the back and wasn’t wearing her hearing aids, didn’t have any chance of eavesdropping.
AJ didn’t lift her eyes to his. “It’s a hard time for everyone,” she answered without answering. 
“Yeah, um, I guess that’s why it’s nice to see people helping each other out.” Tic wanted to roll his eyes at his own words. He sounded like such a tool. To make up for his horrific awkwardness, he grabbed another package of moose meat and a few more boxes of crackers from the unsorted goods on the floor. “Here. A gift. From me to you.”
He held her gaze for just a second. The voices outside got louder, so he added quickly, “And you know where we live. Bring Junior and come stay with us if you need. It’ll be all right. It’s just me and my mom and a couple others. We have plenty of room.”
Should he have added that last part? Would it sound like bragging?
AJ’s smile was the best gift she could have given him. “Thanks, Tic. You’re always so sweet.”
She took the box and a second later was out the door.
Sweet. AJ Jacobson had called him sweet. That’s a good thing, right? I mean, it could be. People like things that are sweet. Brownies and candy bars and ice cream. 
But girls say babies are sweet, too.
What kind of sweet did AJ mean?
He was still trying to decide when three men stomped into the food bank, tramping snow in along with them. It was a good thing Miss Cecelia was in the back, or she’d have a meltdown at the mess.
“Came for the food,” one of them said. 
Tic had been here for the past three hours, and this was the first batch of recipients he didn’t recognize.
He set a box on the table. “Okay, here you go. It’s one per household. Do you live together, or …?”
The men exchanged glances. “What do we look like, a bunch of queers?”
Tic was taken aback. “I just meant that if you live in the same house, you can only have one box.”
“Three of us. Three boxes.” He stepped up close to the table.
Tic just shrugged. The food bank was going to close in five minutes anyway, and they still had enough.
“All right, the boxes are down there. Help yourselves.”
The shortest of the bunch squatted down and rummaged through a box. “Don’t eat rice. Don’t like green beans.” Shorty tossed the items on the floor. “Trade me these for another package of that moose meat.”
“It’s actually just one moose pack per box.” Tic felt like he was repeating himself to a toddler.
Shorty stepped up close to him, close enough Tic could smell his stale beer-breath. “Then how come that little girl who just left walked outta here with two?”
Tic looked at the floor. There were a couple more moose packages that weren’t assigned to a specific box, and it was almost the end of the day. It wouldn’t hurt to give them the extra just to get them out of here …
Shorty’s friend, a man with an almost invisibly-small mustache leaned down and picked up several canned goods straight off the bottom shelf. “I’ll take these.” He reached for some boxes, deliberately sliding his arm along the shelf and sending them all cascading to the ground.
“Hey, you can’t …” Tic began but was interrupted when Miss Cecelia bustled toward them.
“What in the world is this ruckus out here?” She stared at the mess then thrust her hands onto her hips, and even though she was half a foot shorter than even Shorty, it looked like she was staring down at all three.
“Is there a problem?” she demanded.
“No problem, ma’am. We were just taking our food and getting ready to go.”
“It’s one box per household,” she stated, “and one package of moose meat per box.”
“We’re not a household,” Shorty insisted, grabbing his overflowing box, “and I’m trading a few things ’cause I’m no rice-eating sissy.”
He stepped toward the door, and before Tic could stop her, Miss Cecelia was blocking the exit.
“Let me go,” Shorty grumbled. “I’m outta here.”
“Not with extra moose meat, you’re not. Now I’m not gonna make you clean up after yourself. Consider that a parting gift. But the meat stays here.”
As if she were managing a classroom full of unruly Sunday school students, Miss Cecelia yanked the meat out of Shorty’s box, waddled to the back room, and came back empty-handed.
“Now,” she declared, staring at each man in turn, “I suggest you take your drunken selves and leave. Here’s a box for you, and here’s a box for you.” She loaded the other men’s arms and practically pushed them out the door.
“Now we have a mess to clean up, so thanks for coming and good-bye.” She shut the door, turned around, and gave Tic a wry smile.
“And that, son, is how you take care of problem men like those.”

***

Yaya’s mind was reeling from how much had changed just since the initial shock of waking up to dorm fires, detonated cell phones, and dead students. Boone’s abdomen gash was healing well, and he was strong enough to stay awake talking with her for most of the day.
Several of the students who’d initially taken shelter in the seismic lab had gone. Some left to congregate in other areas on campus. Others returned to the areas in the dorms that weren’t exposed from the fire damage. Lilly, Ozzie with the blue hair and deadpan, and Zeke the lifeguard were the only original members of the group who remained in the lab. They had been joined by some of Boone’s other students, several of whom Yaya already knew. In the midst of the chaos and uncertainty that each new hour brought, it was nice to share the time with familiar faces.
And with Boone.
Because he’d worked in the GI building, he knew where to find the most critical supplies, like five-gallon jugs of deionized water and a better-equipped first-aid kit with hydrogel dressings to keep his wound clean. He also knew where the janitor kept the master keys and which professors kept the best stashes of snacks, candy, bottled water, and even alcohol. 
As the only adult over 21, he didn’t let anyone touch the booze but kept it hidden to use as a bartering tool if they ran out of supplies before normalcy was restored. Thankfully, they had enough around to cover for their immediate needs. With the keys to the breakroom, the vending machines, and all the offices, the students sheltering in place inside the GI building didn’t even need to venture out to look for the lunch bags from the cafeteria, the contents of which were growing scanter. 
Lilly went out each day, still looking for her boyfriend, still posting notices on all the open spaces that had turned into impromptu bulletin boards to tell Kai where to find them.
As for Yaya, she was content to stay inside, out of the cold, away from the smoke and the wreckage. They had raided different offices to piece together an eclectic collection of wall art and furniture, thanks to Lilly’s artistic abilities, so now their space felt at least less sterile than it had. On one table, Lilly had arranged an African tribal mask, a vase of gaudy fake flowers, and a garland of Mardi Gras beads. Across from that was one of those old-fashioned ships in a bottle next to a few funny looking miniature frog statues and a metal 3-D puzzle none of them had figured out yet.
One of the students stumbled across a deck of cards in an old lost and found. Yaya now knew all the rules of rummy and Texas Holdem, even if she wasn’t very good, and she was beginning to become at least decent enough at hearts that she didn’t lose every single round. 
Yaya’s favorite way to pass the time, though, was sitting with Boone on one of the small couches they’d appropriated from the break room. Sometimes they’d talk about Yaya’s design work, and she could almost imagine she was at a regular check-in meeting with her academic advisor. Other times they’d talk about home, about Yaya’s uptight religious family in Glennallen or Boone’s early years as the child of research scientists. 
And sometimes they’d just sit together, their minds wandering in tandem even though she had no idea what he was thinking about. It was a peaceful balm in the midst of chaos, a welcome reprieve from the stress and the trauma swirling around outside.
They had food and water, at least for now, and some way to pass the hours. What they didn’t have was a better system to stay warm at night. Fire extinguishing blankets became sleeping bags. Lab coats became nightgowns. And they became each other’s heat as they tried to sleep as close to one another as they could. It was awkward and hard to get used to, but it beat freezing.
If she wasn’t thinking about her family, if she wasn’t terrified about the uncertain future ahead of her, Yaya could just barely manage to cope, which at the moment was all that could be expected of anyone.

***

“And you’ll make sure to lock up, right?” Miss Cecelia asked as she bundled up in her winter gear. 
Benji had just arrived to take her home on his snow machine, and Tic had volunteered to stay a little longer to clean up the mess Shorty and his two friends had made. Now that the drunk men were gone and the foot traffic died down, the quiet felt strange. He hadn’t realized how weird it had been going so many days without seeing anybody from school or town, or even leaving his own house. 
There was one visit in particular that he kept running through his mind as he put boxes of macaroni and cheese back on the shelves.
“Tic?”
He hadn’t heard the front door open and jumped to his feet with a start.
“AJ? What are you doing here?” He stared down at himself and dusted dirt and tracked-in mud off his jeans.
AJ stood in the doorway, her long black hair cascading down her shoulders, along with her brother, one of the middle-school students in Tic’s dad’s class. She shifted her weight from one foot to the other and didn’t raise her eyes.
“I was wondering if you meant what you said earlier. About a place to stay.”
Tic’s eyes widened, but he tried not to allow surprise or any other emotion onto his face other than sincerity.
“Of course. What about your uncle?”
“It doesn’t have to be long,” AJ continued as if she hadn’t heard him, “just until the power and heat come back on. We can’t… We shouldn’t…” She glanced at her brother. 
Junior, known throughout school for his bubbly demeanor and class- clown nature, now had eyes that were dark and downcast, his lips split with dried and frozen blood.
“I understand,” Tic said. “Come on. I just need to finish picking all this up, then you can come with me.”
“You sure your parents won’t mind?” AJ’s eyes kept darting to the door.
“They won’t mind,” Tic promised. “Right now it’s just me and my mom. And the Singhs. Miss Cecelia too. It’s no problem at all. We have plenty of room.”
AJ let out her breath. “Okay, if you’re sure.”
Tic held her gaze. “We’d love to have you.” It was perhaps the most honest and truthful thing he could have said.
A few minutes later, after making sure the shelves were tidy enough for Miss Cecelia’s keen eye and the doors were locked and secured, Tic led AJ and Junior down the road and up the mile-long driveway leading to their home. He wanted to talk to AJ more about her uncle, learn exactly what Dick had done, but he figured that she would tell him when or if she felt like it.
“Sorry the snow’s so deep. We would’ve plowed it for you but…”
Tic’s attempt at a joke was cut short by a man yelling. “Just where do you think you’re sneaking off to?”
AJ froze in her tracks. Junior slumped his shoulders as if he could make his large frame just a little less conspicuous. Tic was the only one to turn around.
Shorty?
“I thought I smelled you slinkin’ off this way.”
Tic couldn’t believe it. Dad had warned him about the outage bringing out the worst in people, but for a stranger to follow them home because he hadn’t given him an extra pound of moose meat?
“You two little punks better look at me when I’m talking to you,” the man snarled, and the gears in Tic’s brain clicked into place.
“That’s your uncle?” he whispered.
AJ nodded subtly.
Tic stared at the man at the bottom of their driveway and considered the long walk through the snow to get to their home. He knew what he had to do. He raised his hand in greeting and forced himself to smile, as if Shorty were a relative stopping by for an unannounced visit.
“Hi. I told AJ and Junior they could come over for some board games for a bit. We won’t be all that long.”
Shorty was quickly traversing the distance between them.
“No,” he growled. “They’re coming home with me where they belong.”
Tic again tried to force a ridiculous smile onto his face. Anything that would make him seem less threatening.
“It’s just some board games.” He shrugged and tried to make his voice sound whiney like an unassuming little kid who just wanted to get his way.
Shorty wasn’t fooled. He reached out and grabbed AJ by the scruff of her winter coat, yanking her face close to his.
“Think you can sneak off so easy?” he snarled at her.
Tic shot a glance at Junior. He would give anything right now for telepathic abilities.
Go, he would have urged. Run to the house and call for help.
Junior, like nearly all his dad’s middle-school students, had been to their home before, either for the annual end-of-the-year picnic or for trick-or-treating on Halloween. All he had to do was follow the path. But he didn’t move.
Nobody did.
Tic took a step closer toward AJ’s uncle.
“Let go of her.” He stopped trying to make himself sound like a small boy begging for an afternoon playing board games.
Shorty’s lip curled up in a sneer. “But it’s such a nice coat, ain’t it? Shame if anything happened to it.”
The hunting knife was in Shorty’s hand before Tic’s conscious brain had registered the danger.
Thankfully, his body had already decided to spring into action.
Using all his weight, Tic flung himself at AJ, breaking her uncle’s hold. She scrambled to keep her balance instead of falling into the snow. The moment she was behind him, Tic turned the entirety of his attention onto her uncle.
“Why don’t you put that away,” Tic suggested, “and just let us know what time you want AJ and Junior to meet you back home.”
He could tell by the glint in Shorty’s eyes that diplomacy had failed. 
Tic easily dodged when Shorty took a fumbling swing, nearly knocking himself over in the snow. He was still slow. He was clumsy. And he was drunk. 
Tic just hoped that AJ and her brother were well on their way to his house by now. To safety.
Another lumbering blow. A wide miss. Tic was sure he could outrun Shorty, but he wanted to give AJ and Junior a head start to the house.
“Get over here, you squirrely little —”
Shorty moved his knife to his other hand and took a swing. 
Now that his opponent wasn’t fumbling blindly with his fists at a drunken half-speed, Tic felt the first real pangs of fear, but the danger just sharpened his senses, made him that much more alert.
Shorty lunged forward, brandishing the knife and cursing loudly. 
Tic jumped back, twisting his ankle on a tree stump. He fumbled hard into the snow, branches clawing at his face, but he wouldn’t let that slow him down. He sprang to his feet, flailing his arms, trying to find a weapon to grab.
He held out a stick as if it were a sword, even though its length was no more than a dagger and certainly no match for Shorty’s long hunting knife. Still, something was better than nothing.
The next time Shorty slashed at him, Tic took the opportunity to jab at Shorty’s face and eyes and any exposed skin.
Shorty let out an angry roar, and there was blood on his cheek when he pulled back. This only seemed to enrage him even more, and without any regard for Tic’s pathetic little stick, he charged at him full force, slamming his shoulder into Tic’s chest.
Tic fell back into the snow, one glove now lost, his assailant right on top of him. He grabbed a handful of ice and slammed it into Shorty’s face along with the full force of his palm. The distraction gave him enough time to wriggle out from underneath Shorty’s frame and spring back to his feet.
Shorty was still lying in the snow. Still too close.
As if endowed by a new type of sixth sense, Tic could see the action take place before it occurred, could see the arc the knife would take, could anticipate exactly what part of his leg the blade would slice.
What he couldn’t anticipate was the shriek of searing pain, the immediate tunneling of his vision, and the sensation that in this one single moment, his body would never be the same again. 
 


20. WHEN DARKNESS FALLS

Night would close in on them soon, and Caleb and Griz still weren’t done clearing the makeshift trail that would allow them to walk over and through the debris pile left in the avalanche’s violent wake. Just like the Christian journey, the narrow way wasn’t straight or pretty, and it didn’t even appear particularly safe, with jagged rocks and ice chunks jutting out, but it was passable. 
Hours earlier, when the men were hacking at the worst of the ice and debris, they paused to ask themselves if they should just hop in Miss Cecelia’s ancient beater and turn around. Assuming nobody else tried to ambush them and steal their car, they could be back in Glennallen by nighttime. Caleb would be back at Rachel’s side, back at home where he belonged.
But Yaya wouldn’t be there. Caleb wasn’t going to fail his daughter.
The mountain of snow blocking the road was now an obstacle course of ice and debris, with some parts sturdy enough they could walk on top, and other parts requiring the men to dig through several feet of snow to allow them to pass. It was like the Israelites at the Red Sea, just without a staff or divine intervention to make a smooth path magically appear.
Caleb wanted to keep working, but it was a classic case of Matthew 26:41: The spirit indeed is willing, but the flesh is weak. He picked at the snow halfheartedly while Griz worked on starting a fire. 
By the time the flames began to crackle, Caleb knew he had to stop for the night. Even if they cleared the entire path, they couldn’t go anywhere till morning, when they’d gather what gear they could into backpacks and onto their sled to make the remainder of their journey on foot.
Arsenal, who had covered more ground and moved more snow than both of them combined, got a whole bag of jerky and half a portion of canned salmon for dinner. Caleb and Griz spread the remaining fish over crackers and stared silently into the fire.
“You know,” Caleb confessed, “I'd never tell her this, but there are times when I get so sick of Rachel's go-to stews and casseroles. But right now, that's the only thing I feel like eating.” His mouth watered at the thought of rich cheesy dumplings floating in creamy chicken soup.
The fire popped. As soon as he finished eating, Arsenal curled up in a donut, tucked his nose beneath his bushy tail, and immediately fell asleep.
“Someone’s tired from working so hard,” Caleb observed.
“The mutt’s good for something,” Griz stated, with a hint of pride and admiration in his voice.
Surrounded by mountains on all sides, daylight waned quickly the moment the sun dipped beneath the peaks.
“Temperature’s gonna drop soon,” Caleb noted.
“Yup.”
“Guess it’s our last night in the station wagon.”
“Yup.”
“We got a long road ahead of us. We should get our rest.”
“Yup.”
Caleb sighed. “Has anyone ever told you you're awfully quiet?”
Griz turned and looked over at him, a sly smile spreading across his face and reaching up to his eye.
Caleb knew how he was going to respond before the word left his lips. 
“Yup.”
Half an hour later, both men and the dog were back in the shelter of the station wagon. Griz was asleep in a matter of minutes, with Arsenal’s head drooped over his lap like a fluffy drooling blanket. 
Caleb stretched out in the back seat as best he could, turned on his flashlight, and read another of Rachel’s love letters.
Dearest, darlingest, most wonderful Caleb,
Unless you peek ahead (shame on you!), you should be reading this on the second day of your mission trip to Siberia. My hope and prayer for you is that you have had an amazing time meeting new people and spreading the love of Christ to everyone you encounter. I am so excited for when you come back home and I get to hear all about your adventures and the people God brought to your path and all the ways He provided for you.
I’m writing this before you leave (obviously), but I can tell you this: I have not stopped praying for you since the moment you left my side. I am praying for God to give you good health, to help you recover miraculously quickly from any jet lag (or not experience it to begin with). I am praying for travel mercies, good rest, great food, wonderful fellowship… anything I can think of that would be a blessing to you. And even the things I wouldn’t know to pray about, I’m asking God to bless you in all those ways as well.
I keep thinking about your trip and about how hard it’s going to be for us to be separated from one another for the next two weeks, and then I remember Psalm 84 and I’m so encouraged. David says that he would rather be a doorkeeper in the house of the Lord for just one day than spend an eternity anywhere else. That’s how I feel about you and me, my darling, sweet Caleb. One day with you would be better than a thousand days at Disneyland or the Grand Canyon or (fill in the blank with anything that anybody anywhere could ever desire to visit or do or see) and that’s how I feel about you.
I know you haven’t traveled all the way across the world just to sit around reading letters from home, so I’ll go ahead and sign off now. Just know that every single waking moment (and probably even a large portion of my sleeping ones), I’ll be thinking of you and praying for you and wishing that God would hasten the day when we are back together again … this time, forever.
All my love,
Your Rachel

***

Rachel hadn’t checked the radio in several hours. As long as she kept working, as long as she had something productive to occupy her hands with, she could keep the horrific, suffocating agony of uncertainty at bay.
Now that the pantries were impeccably organized, she turned her attention to the workbench in the garage, the one that both Caleb and Tic regularly plundered but never lifted a finger to tidy.
Angel had gone back upstairs to start cooking dinner, and Rachel welcomed the time alone. Angel was a good friend, the closest she had in Glennallen. But in some ways, it was a friendship of proximity. Caleb and Dev were best friends. Tic and Benji were best friends. Rachel and Angel were too, but more by default than anything else.
Rachel respected Angel, admired her and her unending reservoir of maternal gentleness and parental wisdom. But aside from being Christian moms who loved their families, the women couldn’t have been any more different.
Angel and Dev both seemed to be facing this power outage with a divine grace that bordered on complete denial. Every time Dev spoke about the state of the world, it was about trusting God’s provision and thanking him in all circumstances — commands and admonishments that Rachel agreed with on the surface, but the way Dev prattled them off was tone deaf if not entirely unhelpful.
Rachel couldn’t talk to Angel about her worry for Yaya or Caleb, not without expecting a twenty-minute lecture on all things working together for good and not even a sparrow falling to the ground apart from the Father’s will.
Rachel couldn’t care less about the sparrows. She wanted her family home. She wanted to know her daughter and her husband were safe.
She wanted to know why Caleb hadn’t radioed in.
But she wouldn’t think about that. Not right now. Instead, she would put all of these stupid screws back in this stupid coffee canister, because apparently she was the only member of her family who possessed the hand-eye coordination or finger dexterity to do so.
Cammy, who hadn’t left Rachel’s side all day, lifted her head and perked up her ears.
“What is it, girl?”
Cammy let out a frantic yip and ran to the garage door. Rachel’s heart sped up. Ever since last night, the fear of a home invasion had never lessened its grip.
She glanced around hurriedly, looking for anything she could use to defend herself. Her eyes landed on the fish club they used when dipnetting for salmon, and she grabbed it as a voice from outside called out, “Mrs. Winters! Mrs. Winters!”
She flung open the garage door, startled to see the two Jacobson children, looking haggard and frantic as they stood there in the snow.
“AJ? Junior? What’s going on?”
“It’s Tic,” AJ stammered. “My uncle … Hurry.”
Rachel was only in her house shoes, but she ran through the snow ahead of both children. Cammy raced even faster, barking frantically.
At the turn in the driveway, Rachel’s legs threatened to give way beneath her as her brain took in the sight all at once: a man with his back to her, her son who stood facing him with nothing but a stick to defend himself. She wasn’t close enough to see, but instead sensed the terror in her child’s eyes.
She had to get to him.
Fighting inertia, she charged forward, breathless, frantic, her prayers both violent and defensive. 
The man knocked Tic over. Her boy was on the ground, in the snow. She could make out the attacker’s face now. Dick Jacobson, an alcoholic and known child abuser.
Why was this full-grown felon attacking her boy?
“Watch out!” she shrieked as her eyes saw the knife. “Tic!” she yelled. 
She wasn’t going to get to him in time.
Cammy slammed into Dick, biting his arm, but he wasn’t deterred. Tic was struggling to get off the ground.
Get up! Rachel wanted to yell, but her breath had deserted her. She was still running through the snow, but it was just like in those nightmares where you’re putting one foot after another, exerting all your energy and more, but your body doesn’t budge.
And then that one horrifying, gruesome movement, that one irrevocable slash as Dick’s knife sliced her child’s leg.
She was too late.
Tic screamed, the sound of a grown man in agony and not a little boy in fear. It didn’t fit with the picture Rachel was seeing in front of her.
Tic was on his back in the snow, with Dick standing over him, knife raised high above. Before he could make the fatal blow, Cammy placed herself between Tic and the weapon, yelping in terror and pain as Dick brought the blade down.
“No!” Rachel yelled, finally reaching the grisly scene. Unwilling to lose either her son or her dog, Rachel raised the fish bat high overhead and brought it down. 
Again. And again. And again.
Her first blows landed mostly on Dick’s back and shoulders, which were covered by his coat. Then she aimed higher and connected to his skull as the bat let out a soul-satisfying thwack.
Dick cursed and stumbled sideways, blood spurting from his face and head. She’d surprised him, but it wasn’t enough. He was still on his feet, still had the knife in his hand. 
A knife covered in blood.
Cammy was motionless in the snow by Tic. 
Her son. Her baby boy and her loyal dog, both bleeding.
Were they even alive?
It was just Rachel now.
Rachel and this monster. This monster who attacked her son with a knife and may have already killed her dog. Taken the very last pieces of her crushed and damaged soul.
Not today, Satan.
She stood facing him, breathing hard while Dick smirked at her even as blood gushed out of his nose.
“Get out of my way, little lady.” His voice was icy cold and dripping with venomous sarcasm. 
Rachel’s entire body reacted in revolt and disgust, and she brandished the fish bat.
“What are you gonna do?” Dick taunted. “Smack me again with your little rolling pin?”
He lunged forward. 
There was nobody else here, nobody else to stand between this crazed man and her child.
She ignored the knife, didn’t even look at where it was slashing. All she saw was Dick’s blotchy red face. All she wanted to do was smash his skull in with the wooden fish bat. 
Thwack. Thwack. Again. 
Thwack.
She was crying and screaming, ugly profanities she’d never uttered in her entire life spewing out of her mouth like water out of a fire hydrant. There was no way to quantify the rage that surged through Rachel’s being. She was the mama bear every man is terrified to stumble upon in the wild.
At least every sane man.
Dick stumbled back. Dropped the knife. Held his hands up.
“All right. All right.” His face was unrecognizable. 
Rachel held the bat even higher. Nothing could compel her to let go.
“I’m leaving. You can keep the brats and good riddance.”
She stared at him, trying to read his expression for any signs of a bluff, but his face was so battered and smashed, she couldn’t even see his pupils.
“Get off my property.” Rachel’s voice was full of iron and steel. She had never been one to swear, not before today, but her tone was as resolute as her will as she leveled her gaze and repeated, “Get the hell off my land.” 
It wasn’t until he slunk away that she realized how tiny of a figure he actually was. But Rachel didn’t have time to study his stature. She dropped her weapon and rushed to the side of her wounded son.

***

“Mom?” Tic looked up, certain his mind was hallucinating. That hadn’t been his mother he heard yelling like that. But there she was. She looked the same as normal, except now even more worried than he’d ever seen her before.
“Mom?” he repeated.
“Shh. I’m here.” She ran her eyes over him, and Tic’s first thought was She can’t see me like this. If she freaked out when he popped his knee out of place at a basketball game, how would she recover from the shock of this type of injury?
“Cammy …” He tried to roll over. Cammy was lying on her side next to him, her labored panting loud and slow.
He turned and saw her bleeding as the realization sank in like dead weight on his chest: She saved my life.
“She’s bleeding.” His words came out more like a whimper than a statement.”
“It’s all right,” Mom assured him. “You’re both going to be okay. What hurts? Did he get your leg?”
“I don’t know.” Tic didn’t care what was wrong with him. Cammy was cut open. “Mom, look at her shoulder.”
“I see it, buddy.” Mom’s voice was uncharacteristically calm. Eerily so. What happened to the woman who passed out at the mere thought of him pulling a muscle while lifting weights?
“You have to make sure she’s okay.” Tic heard the extra strain in his voice and didn’t want to worry Mom any more than she already was. He cleared his throat then added, “You’ve got to take care of her.”
“I’m going to take care of both of you,” she told him softly.
Tic glanced at the wooden bat she still clutched in her hand. Saw the blood on it. “Mom …”
She followed his eyes then tossed the bat aside, out of his field of vision. “Don’t worry,” she said. “Everything is going to be all right.” 
“But you …” Tic didn’t know what he was trying to say. What he was trying to ask. He just knew he didn’t want to hear the answer to the questions that swirled around unformed in his head.
“I did what I had to do, honey. That’s all God expects out of any of us right now, all right?” She brushed the hair off his forehead like he was ten years old and home from school with a fever. “Now I want you to pay attention and tell me if you can feel anything else that’s hurt besides your leg. Here, let’s take off your belt so we can wrap it.”
She knelt beside him in the snow, and Cammy tried to roll over to face them both but let out a heart-wrenching whine. “Mom?” Tic’s voice shook. He had to get it under control. “She’s hurt real bad, Mom.”
“You’re hurt, too,” she answered, her voice never changing its steady, level tone. “So let me tell you what we’re going to do. We’re going to tighten this belt around your leg like this.” 
Tic didn’t even feel the pain as she fastened the buckle tight around his thigh. 
“Then,” Mom continued, “we’re going to get you out of the snow and by the heater.” She looked behind her then stood up.
“AJ? Junior? Can anybody hear me?” 
“We’re coming,” rang out Miss Cecelia’s gruff voice. 
“We need a sled,” Mom called out. “Hurry.”
She knelt back beside Tic and held his hand. “You’re gonna be all right, baby. It looks like …” She glanced down at his bloody leg and didn’t finish her sentence. “I don’t want you standing or putting any weight on it till we’ve had the chance to check it out.”
Miss Cecelia, Dev, and Benji ran down the path with a sled. AJ and her brother stood just behind them watching.
“We need to get him in the house,” Mom directed.
“Not till I’ve looked at that leg,” Miss Cecelia declared. She grunted as she squatted down. “Tic, can you hear me?”
What did she think? That the knife had missed his leg and sliced off his ear? “Uh-huh.”
“Okay, I want you to look at me. Right here.” She pointed to her nose as if she were an eye doctor and stared unblinking at him. Then she reached out and felt around the back and top of his head with probing fingers.
“No obvious head injury,” Miss Cecelia announced. “Let’s take him in the house. Get him warmed up and tend to that thigh. Benji. Dev. You each take one side like this.” She mimed a scooping behavior then continued, “Benji, you take his injured side. Be careful with that leg. You gotta get in there and make sure it doesn’t dangle. You aren’t squeamish, are you?”
“No, ma’am.”
Satisfied, Miss Cecelia turned back to Tic. “It’s gonna hurt, son. No way around that.”
Tic would have shrugged if Benji weren’t already bent over him, tucking one forearm beneath his armpit. “I’m ready.”
He tried not to wince when they hefted him onto the sled. He didn’t want to give Mom any more reason to worry.
“You doing okay, son?” she asked once he was situated in place.
He forced a smile. “Piece of cake.”
“Okay, Benji, you pull him slowly and steadily back to the house,” Miss Cecelia ordered.
“Wait a minute,” Tic interrupted. “What about Cammy?”
“Go ahead and say your goodbyes,” Miss Cecelia said, “but make it quick. We can’t let the cold and shock set in.”
“Goodbyes?” Tic felt all the blood that remained in his system evaporate from his body. 
Mom stepped forward. “We’ll bring Cammy inside, too. We can assess her better by the fire.”
“There’s nothing to assess.” Miss Cecelia’s stage whisper could have been heard all the way from the village of Gulkana. “That knife spliced her shoulder near open. I know it’s a good idea to conserve ammo, but if you want to do the most merciful thing and make it quick …”
“Cammy’s coming in with us,” Mom declared fiercely. 
Cammy didn’t whine or protest when Mom scooped her up as easily as if she’d been a five-pound chihuahua. 
Tic’s sled ride was almost as excruciating as his initial injury, but more prolonged, with every bump in the snow or ice sending electric jolts of pain surging through each individual muscle fiber. He forced his mind to picture the fireplace, heated blankets …
“Tic?” 
He glanced up and gave AJ a smile. “Hey.”
She tried to mimic his expression, but it was contorted like the Joker’s grin. He almost wanted to laugh. 
“I’m all right,” he answered her unasked question. “Come on, let’s go inside where it’s warm.” 
With Mom on one side, carrying Cammy in her arms, and AJ on the other, Tic focused on steadying his breath as the sled lurched forward inch by inch until finally home came into view.

***

It would have been infinitely easier for Rachel if Tic had been crying or thrashing or even swearing from the pain. Instead, his quiet and brave stoicism as they laid him out on the couch broke her heart even more so than the sight of his injured leg.
Miss Cecelia had shooed everybody out of the living room, except for Rachel and Benji, who was far less squeamish about blood than his father proved to be.
“We need scissors to cut those pants open,” Miss Cecelia declared.
“I saw a pair in the kitchen.” Benji jumped up.
“I could work around this wound easier if we got the dog off his chest,” Miss Cecelia observed in a grouchy tone.
Rachel took a glance at Tic and saw the first real look of fear in his eyes. 
“We’ll leave Cammy where she is,” she calmly replied.
Benji returned with some scissors, and Rachel helped cut away Tic’s pants leg, wincing when she saw the blood and exposed muscle.
“Lucky he missed the artery,” Miss Cecelia announced then turned to Rachel. “I am going to loosen the belt and have you press down on the wound with the gauze. Ready?”
Ready or not, Rachel was going to do whatever she had to do to make sure her boy got the medical attention he needed. The wound was deep, the kind she was certain a provider at the clinic would need to stitch up. But for now, all they had was Miss Cecelia’s medical background and regular household items, and Rachel was nervous.
This wasn’t the type of injury you could cover up with a Band-Aid.
“Does it hurt?” She kept her eyes on Tic’s face as she applied pressure on his leg, terrified of causing him any more pain than what her poor child had already suffered.
“I’m fine.”
Unfortunately, Rachel couldn’t tell if he was putting on a brave face for her sake or if he really was able to handle the pain as well as he appeared to be. As Miss Cecelia examined and prodded, all Rachel wanted to do was talk to her son … and not about the injury to his leg. How did he feel? Was he scared? Angry? Was he acting calm because he thought that’s what she expected or needed, or was he in some kind of shock?
Miss Cecelia frowned at her work. “I don’t suppose you have saline rinse anywhere in the house, do you?”
“I don’t think so,” Rachel responded.
“Well, I guess we’ll have to clean it out with water,” Miss Cecelia decided. “But I need something cleaner than melted snow. It doesn’t have to be sterile but …”
“We have a couple gallons of distilled water in the basement.” Rachel told Benji where to look, and he hurried downstairs.
“Once we get this rinsed,” Miss Cecelia said with a pout, “I’ll be able to decide if we’ll have to stitch it. Without proper tools to clean it better, I’d hate to close it up and trap in dirt or bacteria.”
“We have peroxide somewhere,” Rachel said. “Rubbing alcohol, too,” she added, although she hated to think of the pain Tic would experience if they poured disinfectant straight onto his gaping wound.
Miss Cecelia shook her head. “Too much tissue exposed. But if you have some antibiotic ointment, we’ll put it around the skin. Tomorrow, I’ll have Dev or Benji take me over to the clinic. He’s going to need antibiotics…”
Rachel didn’t have time to worry about how Miss Cecelia would retrieve antibiotics from a clinic she’d retired from years ago. All that mattered was Tic’s health and safety.
Her child could have died. The truth rang through the empty caverns of her soul like a hollow, macabre church bell. That man could have killed her son, and then what would she have left?
“Mrs. Winters?” The quiet voice startled Rachel out of her depressing spiral.
“Hi, AJ,” she answered wearily. “Tic’s doing okay. Right now, we’re still just trying to get him cleaned up and bandaged.”
The poor girl looked as if she had been crying. Rachel wished she had an unlimited reserve of compassion and sympathy to offer, but her entire focus was on her son. Right now she barely possessed the extra energy to worry about her dog, who lay on Tic’s chest in apparently even worse shape than he was.
“I just wanted to know if Tic needs anything. Like a drink of water or something?”
Rachel glanced at Miss Cecelia.
“A cup of water would be nice, dear,” the old woman said with uncharacteristic softness.
A moment later, a quiet AJ was handing Tic a small glass. Rachel pretended not to notice when she saw their fingers brush against each other’s.
“Thank you, AJ,” Rachel said. “We’ll let you know if we need anything else.”
When Benji returned with distilled water, the women focused on cleaning the wounded area. Rachel never took her eyes off her son’s face, not only because the gaping gash in his leg was too painful to look at, but because she needed to watch his eyes and convince herself he was all right.
As a little boy, her son had cried at the smallest cut. Once, he locked himself in the bathroom for an hour instead of letting her use her tweezers to pull a splinter out of his finger. She had learned how to comfort a scared and hurting little boy. 
What she didn’t know was how to care for a scared and hurting young man.
What did he need from her?
He obviously didn’t want her to fuss over his boo-boos like she had ten years ago, and the more she fretted, the more worked up she’d make him. But where did that leave her? She was nothing but a nurse’s assistant, the woman who passed out bandages or told Benji where to find supplies.
“Well,” Miss Cecelia declared, a hint of satisfaction in her voice, “I think we can forgo the stitches. We’ll just need to change his dressing a few times every day, make sure the area stays as clean as possible, and keep our eyes open for any sign of infection.”
“How long until I’ll be able to squat again?” Tic asked.
Miss Cecelia looked confused. “Squat? Like in the outhouse? Don’t worry about that. We’ll bring you a chamber pot.”
“He’s talking about weights,” Rachel explained. The realization that her son was thinking about his lifting career at a time like this sent pangs of sadness through her soul.
Miss Cecelia stared at Tic with the same consternation as if a tarantula had crawled out of his body and began tap dancing on his injury. 
“You won’t be putting any weight on that leg for a couple weeks,” she declared definitively.
Tic’s eyes widened. “Weeks?”
Rachel needed to change the conversation. “It’s a bad wound, sweetie. It’s going to take a little bit of time to heal.”
“Weeks?” Tic repeated again, his voice quivering just a bit.
On top of his chest, Cammy must have sensed the shift in his mood. In spite of the bloody wound on her own shoulder, she weakly lifted her head and tried to lick his chin.
Rachel put her hand on the dog’s head, saying prayers of healing for both her son and their beloved pet who’d saved his life. God couldn’t take either of them away. Caleb had always balked at the adage thrown about by some Christians, a pseudo-Biblical promise that God won’t give you more than you can handle. But certainly in some circumstances like this …
God, Rachel pleaded, not my son. And please, Lord, not our dog.
It was the extent of the prayer energy Rachel had left that night.
As the sun sank low, Angel fed anybody with an appetite. She probably would have force-fed her potatoes and moose roast into Rachel’s mouth bite by bite, but Miss Cecelia encouraged her to let her be.
“I should radio into Delta,” Rachel said absently when Tic and Cammy were both sleeping by the fire, but she didn’t move. Even if she got ahold of Buster, what would she say? And if by some miracle Caleb was there and heard how badly Tic had been injured, it would just make his journey that much more impossible.
She stared at the silent radio then let her eyes wash back over her son. Cammy hadn’t moved from his chest, even when Rachel helped wash her wound and held her while Miss Cecelia crudely stitched up her shoulder. Her breaths were ragged and choppy, Tic’s short and shallow, as he rested with his hands clutching her silky fur.
But at least they were both asleep.
“Our family’s ready to call it a night,” Dev told her in a whisper, and she was surprised when she looked at the clock and saw how late it was. 
“Do you need anything before we go to bed?” He could have been speaking in Hindi for all the sense his words were making in Rachel’s exhausted brain.
“AJ will sleep on the floor in our room,” Angel said. “Benji and Junior are upstairs in Tic’s room.”
Rachel didn’t want to admit she had forgotten their found family had just accepted two additional teenagers. At least Angel was good at extending hospitality when Rachel failed.
Rachel had already put on her fuzzy bathrobe and brought down her blanket and pillow. Nothing tonight but the hand of God himself would compel her to leave her child’s side, and even that would come with a fight that would make Jacob’s little wrestling match with the divine look like a playground scuffle.
“Miss Cecelia says she’ll stay here in the living room,” Angel went on to explain. “We’ll move the bassinet into our room and take care of baby Leo.”
Rachel found herself shaking her head. “It’s too cold. He needs to stay by the fire.”
“He’s still waking up several times a night,” Angel argued softly. “You and Tic need all the rest you can get.”
“I’ll wake up with the baby,” Miss Cecelia declared and stretched herself out on Caleb’s recliner.
Before retreating, Dev insisted on saying a prayer for Tic, but his words felt formulaic and void, full of “if it’s your will” and “surrender to your hands” — platitudes that probably worked well enough if you were praying for your neighbor’s aging cat or your great-uncle’s sinus infection, but they were not the prayers a mother would pray over her grievously injured child.
And then Rachel was left with the silence and the darkness and the pain in her soul that knew no remedy. For the first time, she thought she understood Simeon’s prophetic words to Mary when he first met the holy child: A sword will pierce through your own soul also.
Rachel knew she wasn’t the only mother fated to watch her child suffer. What she didn’t know was how women could persist, how their lungs could continue to draw breath and their muscles continue to move.
“Try to get some sleep,” Miss Cecelia mumbled. 
Rachel knew sleep would elude her, so instead of lying down, she sat at Tic’s feet and wrapped prayers around him while he slept.
Cammy stirred to look at Rachel with sad, understanding eyes, and Rachel stroked her silky fur, sending up impassioned prayers, blessings for both of these creatures she loved so much. For her son, who was accepting his injury with more courage than Rachel had ever displayed during her entire lifetime. And for Cammy, who had quite certainly saved her child from an even more devastating fate.
But what she didn’t pray for, what she couldn’t pray for, was the fiend who had done this to them. She knew that one day God would prompt her to repent of the bitterness and hatred and resentment building up inside her, that had spilled out in the snow as she protected her child from death.
But tonight, she wasn’t sorry for her behavior or her hardness of heart or this feeling that anyone but a Christian would easily call hatred.
No, Rachel wasn’t sorry at all.
 


INTERLUDE

“Then they said, ‘Come, let us build ourselves a city and a tower with its top in the heavens, and let us make a name for ourselves, lest we be dispersed over the face of the whole earth.’ And the Lord came down to see the city and the tower, which the children of man had built. And the Lord said, ‘Behold, they are one people, and they have all one language, and this is only the beginning of what they will do. And nothing that they propose to do will now be impossible for them.’”

Genesis 11:4-6
 


21. ORIGINS

Pentagon, 1979
“Gentlemen, what I'm proposing isn’t just a computer program. At its very core, its mission is to protect and preserve the American way of life.”
Twenty-one-year-old Joey Hastings couldn’t believe he was here, listening to his mentor address this impressive room of government and military leaders. As usual, Dr. Grayson spoke with the authority of an army general combined with the charm and charisma of a tent-revival preacher.
“Our machine will think faster than humans, computing thousands of complex calculations in nanoseconds. When you couple that with the ionospheric research we’ve already begun, the implications for enhancing our military strength can’t be exaggerated.”
As Joey glanced around the room, at all these men who combined possessed centuries’ worth of top military and political experience, he felt his chest swell with pride. Pride in his country. Pride in everything Dr. Grayson had accomplished until now, including this meeting at the Pentagon.
“Although still in the early stages at the moment, my associate and I” — Here Grayson gave Joey a glowing gesture that nearly made the young man blush — “remain thoroughly convinced that with your cooperation, intel, and funding, we will create a defense strategy that will think a million times faster than man, and without the vulnerability to accidents, mistakes, or biases that are part of the human experience.”
One of the generals let out an impressed whistle. Others nodded. From the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff to his aide passing out papers, it was clear that everyone was captivated by Grayson’s persona, even if not all were yet sold on the project itself.
“I assume you’re talking about a heavy price tag.” This comment from the Chairman of the Senate Armed Services Committee was expected, and Grayson smiled at him readily.
“Senator Gillian, my colleagues and I have already conducted preliminary site surveys and identified a singular parcel of land that fully satisfies the project’s stringent operational, climate, and security requirements.”
This was Joey’s cue, and even though he didn’t possess the magician-like charisma of his superior, he tried to add a dramatic flair when he tore off the top sheet of the large flip pad to reveal the site plans so painstakingly prepared for today’s briefing.
Chairman Gillian chuckled so loudly the sound came out more like a snort. “Alaska? You’ve got to be kidding me.”
“Actually, it’s perfect.” Joey hadn’t planned to jump in here, but because he’d taken such care to help Grayson select the site, he felt he needed to defend his boss’s choice. Although he may have lacked Grayson’s power of natural persuasion, he knew the ins and outs of the remote location by heart and ticked off each benefit in a row:
“The region we’re talking about has the same acreage as Ohio but an average of only one human resident for every ten square miles. For perspective, Area 51 has more than double the population density and not nearly the same degree of seclusion. Obviously, this helps with the secrecy required for a program of this magnitude, but the remoteness will also minimize RF noise and stray electromagnetic interference. The Copper River Valley has access to cool groundwater, which we’ll need to maintain stable underground temperatures for our subterranean power supplies. It’s also one of the only regions we’ve discovered with the specific geomagnetic field conditions we need to aid our ionospheric experiments and resonance studies.”
Senator Gillian turned to Grayson. “Does your aide speak English?” he asked derisively.
“Joseph Hastings is not my aide,” Grayson corrected, and Joey felt his cheeks burn. “He’s the foremost autonomous decision-making systems design expert in the world. He’s earned a master’s from Stanford and two PhDs from Princeton and has an IQ higher than everyone in this room combined. You will not find a scientist of his caliber anywhere else on the planet, I can assure you of that.”
“Well then,” the chairman blustered, “at the very least maybe you can translate what this expert of yours just said.”
Joey wished he could disappear through the floor, but all eyes were on Grayson as he deliberately straightened his tie. “As my associate so eloquently explained, we have selected this region for its size and remote location. It’s big. It’s hidden. Plus, it’s got exactly the right weather and atmospheric conditions we need to make it work.”
Gillian still appeared unimpressed. “Perhaps, but it would be a massive endeavor building the kind of structure you’re dreaming about.”
Grayson grinned. “Actually, Mr. Chairman, it won’t be nearly as costly as you might imagine.”
At Grayson’s signal, Joey flipped the page to reveal the next image on the presentation pad, listening raptly as Grayson explained their plans. 
“Gentlemen, what you’re looking at is a decommissioned silo in the Alaskan wilderness. The structure is already built, still in operable condition, and one-hundred-percent EMP resistant. It’s a concrete structure capable of housing at least a dozen researchers and scientists to work round the clock to make the world’s first and only self-contained, power-generating underground computer network capable of monitoring national defense systems, controlling key pieces of infrastructure, and adapting to real-life geopolitical shifts.
“Imagine an automated command-and-control system that combines the totality of human knowledge in one database. It would be like having the best military minds who have ever lived working together in the same room, understanding every single possible conflict contingency, and coming up with instantaneous, fail-safe tactics for guaranteed success.”
Fifteen minutes later, several of the key movers in the room — including the Undersecretary of Defense for Research and Engineering and the Director of the Defense Intelligence Agency — had enthusiastically voiced their support for Grayson’s proposal, while others nodded in agreement. It was clear Grayson and Joey had passed this first major hurdle in getting Project Black on its feet.
Even Senator Gillian seemed to offer reluctant approval as he extended his hand to Grayson. “I’ve got to admit, your project is ambitious, but I like ambition in young fellows like you.” He said this even though Grayson was at least fifty years old. “We’ll see what the boys in DARPA and Appropriations say, but you’ve got my recommendation.”
“Thank you, Senator.” Grayson looked so calm and collected, as if he’d never even considered the possibility of this meeting failing.
“A computer that controls national defense with a button.” The chairman shook his head. “Who woulda thought. What’d you say you’re naming your newfangled contraption, again?”
Grayson hadn’t let go of the Senator’s hand. “Leviathan,” he answered with an unwavering gaze.
The following evening, upon the return from DC, Grayson took the entire team out for drinks to celebrate their project’s success.
Joey, although exhausted from his travels and meeting so many people in such a short time, couldn’t come up with a good excuse to bow out, especially not when Grayson showed up at his apartment to pick him up.
“This project wouldn’t exist without you, son. Tonight is your victory as much as anyone else’s. Besides,” Grayson added with a chuckle, “you were so busy with the proposal last month we never celebrated your birthday. This will be the first night out that Jenny won’t have to buy your drink for you.”
Joey felt his face flush at the high praise, even more at the mention of Jenny, the only team member even remotely close to Joey in age and one he’d come to look upon as a cool older sister.
At the bar, Grayson lifted up his glass.
“The celebration is for us. For the sleepless nights, the migraines, the weeks without sunlight. But we did it. A little more red tape, and Project Black will no longer be the crazy dreams of an ambitious few, but a reality that will usher in a new way of life, a new era of humans cooperating with technology in ways that Edison and Tesla and Einstein couldn’t have dreamed. So, here’s to all the crazy mad scientists who came before, who built the foundation upon which Leviathan will stand. To the geniuses of the past,” he toasted, and everyone raised their glasses.
“Was he that good of an orator in DC?” Jenny asked, sidling up beside Joey.
Swelling with pride at the memories of their meeting at the Pentagon, Joey couldn’t help but grin. “Even better.”
Following their leader’s example, several other team members were offering up toasts of their own. “To my ex-wife, who will never understand why I chose this life over her.”
“To Project Black. May the future be even brighter than we anticipate.”
The elation was contagious. Without pausing to think, Joey grabbed his friend’s hand and held it tight in his grasp. “To Jenny,” he exclaimed, “and to her beautiful designs. You should have seen the way those men all gawked when they saw her plans in that room.”
Grayson, who was seated directly across from his protégé, gave Joey a paternal smile and raised his glass once more. “To friendship.” His eyes beamed. “To love and family.”
Joey’s heart swelled.
“And to the algorithm,” Grayson finished, citing an inside joke from one of their many sleep-deprived nights at work.
The comrades around the table clinked their glasses.
“To the algorithm!” they repeated in unison.

Seattle, 1981
Joey’s back ached by the time he taped up the last box. “It’s like packing up a lifetime of work,” he mused.
From the office entrance, Grayson chuckled. “Says the prodigy in his twenties. Trust me, son, you have your best and most brilliant decades completely ahead of you. And don’t forget.” He leveled his gaze. “What we’re building will outlast any of us here today. Outlast you, outlast me.”
“You’re talking like you’re planning to kick the bucket soon,” Joey joked.
Grayson smiled. “I have no intention of slowing down, thank the good algorithm.”
Joey never got tired of the jest. “Thank the algorithm,” he repeated with a knowing grin.
Jenny came in from the office break room, carrying an open box filled with their ancient coffee percolator and mismatched collection of mugs. She rolled her eyes when Joey reached out and took the box from her.
“I swear, you think I’m as fragile as an electron tube,” Jenny complained, but her tone was good-natured.
After carrying the last of the breakroom commodities to the van, Joey returned, wiping sweat from his brow. Hit with a sudden wave of emotion, he stared at the empty office where he’d worked since Grayson found him wasting away in that MIT think tank. Much as it surprised him to admit, he was going to miss this place. The late nights, the camaraderie …
“Look at that,” Jenny declared, eyeing Grayson. “Now that his precious computer’s dismantled and packed up, he doesn’t know what to do. How is he going to survive a whole week on the ALCAN without his electronic baby?”
“It won’t take a whole week,” Joey replied defensively, snapping himself out of his reverie. “If all goes as planned, we’ll arrive at the new site by Tuesday.” He gave Jenny a playful nudge in her ribs. “That is if you two ever stop gawking so we can hit the road.”
The sooner they reached Alaska, the sooner they could start the next stage in their research.
