While Joey and Grayson worked in the underground silo that would house the Leviathan project, Jenny’s work would start above ground and stretch a hundred miles up to the ionosphere, where their automatic defense system could look down on the earth like a great protector, shielding her from enemies, missiles, even storms and drought.
The reign of peace that Leviathan would usher in was unfathomable in its scope, worthy of every single mile that stretched out before them like a glorious promise.

Silo, Alaska SPEAR Facility, 1984
“Jenny! You’ve got to come see this!”
Joey swiveled around in his chair, grinning happily at his girlfriend.
She looked up from her desk, stacked high with technical manuals and strewn about with hand-drawn blueprints. In her clear blue eyes Joey caught that irresistible mix of both annoyance and adoration.
“I’m never going to finish the plans for this antenna array or anything else if you keep interrupting me,” she complained, but her whole face glowed. She came up behind him and leaned over his shoulder so that her golden blonde curls tickled his smooth cheek. ‘Now, what is it that’s so important you had to interrupt?”
“It’s that ELIZA program we released. I’ve been working on modifications to make the language even more realistic. Let me show you.”
Joey reached out and began typing. 
Hi Eliza. How are you today?
As soon as he hit enter, Eliza’s response popped up on the monitor: We’re talking about you, not me.
Joey let out a laugh, almost forgetting his girlfriend was behind him. “Okay,” he said. “Let’s try this.”
Eliza, do you think my girlfriend is mad at me?
Eliza: Why do you say your girlfriend is mad at you?
He turned around in his seat and stared into Jenny’s eyes before turning back around to type: Because she’s frowning like she wants to kill me.
Eliza: Let’s discuss further why your girlfriend is mad at you.
“Why is she mad at me? Oh, ho, ho,” Joey chuckled. “Let me count the ways.” He bent over the keyboard. Well, first of all, she says I’m way too obsessed with my work. 
Eliza: Why does she say you’re obsessed with your work?
Joey: Because I can’t ever seem to stop.
Eliza: Can you elaborate on that?
Joey turned back around to face Jenny. “Hmm. Good question. How would you elaborate on the way I’m obsessed with work?” 
Jenny’s arms were crossed but she had a crooked smile on her freckled face. “Tell her that we’ve been dating for five years but you still haven’t proposed.”
Eliza’s response to his comment was immediate: Do you think you haven’t proposed for some special reason?
Joey’s fingers were sweaty, and he wasn’t exactly sure he liked the way the program had turned the conversation. But he couldn’t stop. Jenny was right there behind him, reading every word: I guess I’m waiting for the right time.
Eliza: That’s quite interesting. Can you elaborate on that?
Joey knew he was only talking to a language processing program, nothing more than some lines of code, electrical signals, and binary logic. ELIZA wasn’t real any more than Jenny’s actual job was to “study the aurora.” ELIZA was simply meant to be an entertaining toy for the public, a way to begin introducing concepts of human-computer interaction to the masses, just like Jenny’s work with the antenna array was touted as a means to study the northern lights — a safe cover to mask the real work happening at the SPEAR Silo.
So why did he feel so naked and exposed?
“She wants you to elaborate,” Jenny prodded, obviously enjoying Joey’s extreme discomfort.
I guess I just love her so much and I don't think I have what it takes to make her happy.
He felt Jenny’s body tense up behind him but couldn’t face her.
This time he didn’t even bother reading Eliza’s response but kept on typing: She's perfect. And I'm so worried that I'll do something and mess everything up.
Eliza: How long have you been worried you’ll do something and mess everything up?
Joey sighed. Since the day I first laid eyes on her.
“Joey …” Jenny whispered. Grayson would be returning from lunch any minute now. Whatever Joey was planning to do, he had to do it quickly.
Before he could talk himself out of it, he was down on one knee. He grasped Jenny’s hand in his and stared into her eyes. “I would never be the man I am today if it weren’t for you. And I don’t want to find out what kind of man I’d become if I didn’t have you every day by my side.”
A single tear sparkled in the corner of her gleaming eye.
“Jenny, will you marry me?”
Her answer rang melodically through his entire body and soul, sweeter than any sound he had heard in the entire universe.
 


22. CLEANING UP

March 29, 1989
“Thanks for agreeing to have me here on such short notice.” Representative Newcastle reached out and shook Grayson’s hand.
“We’re happy to have our friends in Washington pop over for a visit.” Grayson’s physical stature was as strong and imposing as ever, but Joey thought he’d noticed a weakening in his mentor’s voice in the past several months. 
“Obviously, after everything happening in Valdez,” Newcastle went on, “we’ve got a lot of cleaning up to do when it comes to our state’s handling of environmental issues. And I’m not just talking about the oil,” he added wryly.
“I completely understand.” Was Grayson sweating? Joey had always seen his boss as completely unflappable, but now he couldn’t trust his own judgment. Ever since Jenny came back from that first doctor’s visit, Joey’s heart would race at the smallest hint of any trouble. He was constantly on edge, and this surprise visit from their state representative wasn’t doing anything to calm his nerves.
“Where is that pretty little northern lights expert?” Newcastle asked. “The one who designed all these antennas out here? They really are ghastly,” he added as an aside. “Felt like I was driving through a town of ghosts with a giant army of those metal beacons just staring down at me.”
“They are impressive, aren’t they?” Grayson replied. His characteristic diplomacy put Joey slightly more at ease. “Unfortunately, Dr. Bower-Hastings is in Anchorage. For health reasons,” he added in a slightly patronizing tone.
Joey tried not to blanch at the euphemism. His wife was in Anchorage because after miscarrying twice, the last time nearly hemorrhaging to death, she moved away from SPEAR’s antenna array and all its accompanying radiation the second the doctor confirmed her most recent pregnancy. The separation was hard for them, both as teammates and as a couple, but in a few more weeks, after she delivered a perfectly gorgeous and hopefully brilliant baby, it would all be worthwhile.
“I hope it’s a boy,” Jenny had mused.
“If the algorithm wills it,” Joey answered back. Of course, he didn’t believe in a sentient computer deciding his fate any more than he believed in a mystical God dictating his future, but sometimes it was comforting to think that the anguish he and his wife had suffered was part of some grand design, something more meaningful than random entropy.
“Well, about those antennas…” Newcastle went on, stripping Joey of the chance to revel any longer in his sorrow. “The people of Alaska are looking for assurances that the northern lights research conducted by Dr. Bower-Hastings poses no risk to the environment or to the residents who live out here.”
“Not at all.” Grayson gestured toward a clipboard hanging up near Jenny’s workstation. “This is our most recent report measuring the radiation put out by the antennas. These are the ranges that the international science community considers safe. You can see, our numbers fall well below that line.”
Joey watched Newcastle study the chart with a frown and felt the need to defend his wife’s work. “It’s important to keep in mind,” he added, “that our transmissions are intermittent. We have very specific windows where we identify periods of low geomagnetic activity. Then our system uses phased-array frequency steering to direct HF signals into targeted portions of the ionosphere. This allows us to analyze the plasma wave interactions and simulate auroral behavior without creating any permanent environmental effects.”
Newcastle stared at Joey blankly.
“To translate for Dr. Hastings,” Grayson jumped in, “we only run the array during very specific windows of time, usually for no more than a few minutes, never more than an hour.”
Now it was Joey’s turn to feel confused. Wasn’t that what he’d just said?
“And the waves themselves aren’t harmful?” Newcastle pressed.
Grayson shook his head. “Absolutely not.” He pointed to Jenny’s clipboard. “You can see here that even if we were running these things for twenty-four hours a day, the waves our antenna put out are no more dangerous than if you were to stand about a hundred feet away from a single microwave oven.”
“Our constituents will be glad to hear that,” Newcastle replied. “And what about the effects of your research on the ozone? That obviously is of high importance, both in DC and the international community.”
“Of course,” Grayson said in a placating tone, “as it well should be. Rest assured, the high-frequency radio waves we use here at SPEAR don’t carry nearly enough energy to break apart ozone molecules. And when we do transmit, we aim for the ionosphere, which is a completely different region of the atmosphere altogether.”
“So I can tell concerned individuals …” Newcastle prodded.
“Tell them that worrying about beams from our work harming the ozone would be like worrying that beams from your mother’s car radio will overheat the Amazon.”
Newcastle gave a nod. “But I hear from my friends at DoD your work can be used for weather manipulation? And that this technology might be used to adversely impact our enemies?”
Joey felt his already fickle stomach tighten, but Grayson just laughed. 
“I happened to mention in a paper that sending waves into the ionosphere might one day allow us to direct rain to areas ravaged by drought, and all of a sudden I’m called a warmonger. Now, for the record, it’s true we are most definitely involved in programs that possess the capacity to support homeland defense operations, but I can tell you plainly the antenna arrays are not part of that work. If you’re interested, I’m sure Dr. Hastings would love to tell you about Leviathan, our state-of-the-art decision-support system that combines rule-based reasoning and adaptive learning algorithms in order to create an autonomous, self-optimizing command-and-control network that can analyze threats, coordinate infrastructure responses, and simulate wartime scenarios in real time.” He glanced at his wrist. “How much time do you have, because it’s truly quite fascinating.”
As Grayson spat out science jargon like a popcorn maker tossing popcorn around, Newcastle’s eyes kept widening before he finally murmured, “Really, my biggest concern was the environmental impact.”
“Well, take these reports back with you,” Grayson offered, handing him copies of the charts from Jenny’s clipboard. “And if you have any questions, don’t hesitate to reach out to us.” 
He gestured to Joey, who, without needing further direction, handed over his business card.
“Thank you so much for your time,” Newcastle said, shaking both of their hands before Joey escorted him up the stairs and out of the Silo into the chilly spring breeze.
Watching the representative drive away, Joey felt his lungs expand with his first breath of fresh air in weeks.

October 19, 1989
“Will you put that silly thing away? I’ve already filled my suitcase and the diaper bag. I’m not packing for you, too.” Jenny’s stern tone belied the twinkle in her sky-blue eyes.
On her hip she bounced their son, a perfect, bright ray of joy who had waltzed into Joey’s life and stolen his heart with his first tiny coo, but that wasn’t what Joey was focused on right now.
“Come on,” Jenny prodded. “I postponed my next ionospheric coupling test by a whole week so we could make this doctor appointment. The least you could do is power everything down and take ten minutes to pack up.”
“Look at this,” Joey said absently, his eyes fixed on his monitor. He pointed to the screen, where he’d been running tests on Leviathan’s newest command-priority matrix.
Jenny understood the science behind Joey’s work better than anybody outside the Silo, but her face remained blank as she stared at the synthetic thought stream.
“It’s rewriting its own threat assessment protocol,” Joey explained in a low whisper. He glanced around the computer room, as if those words might have summoned Dr. Grayson himself, and then simply added, “It shouldn’t be able to do that.”
As if his wife couldn’t have figured that part out on her own. “Are you sure?” Jenny squinted at the screen, but Joey didn’t have time to explain.
“Come on.” He jumped out of his chair, ignoring the smile on his son’s bright face, then grabbed Jenny by the elbow. “We should go.” He led the way, practically dragging her to their bedroom where he threw clothes haphazardly into a dusty duffel.
“Are you okay?” she asked, but Joey had no idea how to answer. 
No idea whatsoever.
A few minutes later, they emerged into the bleak October winter, but Joey hardly even noticed the cold.
“You’ve got his diaper bag?”
Jenny gave him a perplexed tilt of the head. “You’re holding it.”
He stared down at the denim bag dangling from his shoulder. “Oh.”
“Shouldn’t we tell Grayson we’re leaving?” Jenny asked as Joey strapped their son into his car seat.
“He’s been reprogramming the threat projection algorithm,” Joey answered, not bothering to look at her. “He knows we’ve got the time off. Let’s go.”
“Joe, you’re worrying me.” 
This was a problem. Jenny never called him anything but Joey. Ever. 
In the back seat, their son gurgled and shook a rattle Jenny had made for him out of spare parts around the Silo.
Even miles away from SPEAR, Joey kept his voice low. “The threat assessment protocol,” he began, completely uncertain how he could even start to explain. “When Dr. Grayson and I programmed it, from the very beginning we added human fail-safes at every single step. The program’s designed to cross-check every move Leviathan makes against very specific DoD decision ceilings and ethics compliance gates. We built every possible safeguard into it, and the entire network was working beautifully. Then last night Dr. Grayson initiated a forced system update, and this morning …”
His head was swimming, pounding, his nerves far more frayed than that of a first-time father driving his infant son into town for a run-of-the-mill ear-tube surgery. 
“So that means …” Jenny tentatively began.
“That means that Dr. Grayson deliberately tampered with the protocols, or it means that Leviathan found a way to do it himself.”
 They drove in silence down the Richardson then onto the Glenn Highway. By the time they stopped for gas in Glennallen, their son was dozing peacefully in the back. Joey just wished he had the same type of blissful ignorance as their baby.
What was he going to do? He needed to go to his boss, but why would Dr. Grayson have sabotaged the failsafe and ethical boundaries, especially after the two men had worked so hard to design and lock them into place from the start?
The only other option was that Leviathan had initiated its own unsanctioned code rewrite, circumventing the safeguard routines embedded into his neural net design.
“It’s impossible,” Joey exclaimed under his breath. “He doesn’t have the power to do that.”
“Who? Grayson?”
Joey shook his head. “No. Leviathan. He’s supposed to be constrained by strict human oversight. We built dozens of redundancies into his security framework.”
“His?” Jenny repeated, but Joey hardly even heard her.
“I don’t know how this happened.”
“You should tell Dr. Grayson.”
In all likelihood, Jenny was right. He and Grayson had worked together for more than a decade. There was no way his supervisor would unleash an entity as powerful as Leviathan without any ethical constraints or safety overrides in place. 
“You’re right,” Joey admitted, finally feeling his heart slow down as he saw the wisdom in his wife’s advice. “As soon as we get to the hospital, I’ll give him a call.”
“Will that be too late?” Jenny asked. “We don’t have to show up to the pre-op until this evening. Should we turn back?”
Joey shook his head. “No, nothing’s online yet. There aren’t any external connections he can manipulate. Not yet, at least.”
“So there’s no real danger at the moment, right?” Jenny prodded.
“Right.” Joey was thankful for his wife’s rational line of reasoning. “It’s all theoretical right now. So even if he does override protocols …”
“There’s nothing it can actually hurt or damage,” Jenny finished for him, and Joey felt his body relax. He took in a deep breath. Maybe it was just the stress of fatherhood and work, coupled with the start of another winter huddled a hundred feet underground in a concrete tube.
It had been months since he’d left the Silo.  The next day and a half away from work would give Joey the mental reset he so desperately needed. He glanced at their son in the rearview mirror and caught his own reflection. When had his hair started to recede like that? He was far too young to be balding, wasn’t he?
Even over the roar of the car engine, the silence was hard to get used to. The Silo was constantly bombarded by a low, resonant hum, a vibration you could feel more than actually hear. Without it, Joey felt like a sailor trying to walk on solid ground for the first time after a lifetime at sea.
Jenny, apparently just as unaccustomed to the silence as he was, reached over and turned on the car radio. The only station broadcasting out here was a Christian talk show, but at least they could get a weather report and some news. Because the Silo was 180 feet underground, the station didn’t reach home, and without TV or newspapers, they could go weeks without hearing about what was going on in the outside world.
A Billy Graham broadcast was finishing up, replete with a call for every lost soul to ask Jesus for salvation. The evangelist’s southern drawl reminded Joey of his childhood listening to this same man on his grandma’s radio. Joey paid less attention to the words, more to the slow cadence that transported him back to a land of cicadas and lightning bugs and endless starlit nights.
After every last sinner in earshot had been called on (multiple times) to repent and be saved, the station switched to the news. Joey didn’t even know it was the World Series until the announcer mentioned a game between the A’s and the Giants. 
“Baseball fans in the San Francisco area are still reeling after Tuesday’s World Series game at Candlestick Park was abruptly postponed when a powerful earthquake struck just minutes before the first pitch.”
Jenny’s hand shot out to turn up the volume, and Joey felt rather than heard her suck in her breath.
“The magnitude 6.9 quake hit at 5:04 Tuesday evening, collapsing portions of freeways, severing power, and damaging thousands of homes across the Bay Area. At least sixty people are confirmed dead, with hundreds more injured.”
“Pull over,” Jenny demanded, swatting Joey’s arm for emphasis before he could ask what for. 
He pulled into a turnout just past Glennallen and hadn’t even parked before Jenny jumped out and began dry heaving on the snow and gravel. 
Instantly, he was out of the car as well and by her side.
“What is it? Do we need to call a doctor?”
She blinked up at him with terror in her eyes. “That earthquake …”
His brain wasn’t keeping up with hers. Sure, dozens dead and hundreds injured? That was obviously tragic. But they didn’t know anybody in the Bay Area …
“Tuesday.” As soon as she whispered the word, she retched again, and that’s when Joey understood.
“Your harmonic resonance experiment,” he breathed, the full realization beginning to settle into his gut and chill his bone marrow.
“The array was live,” Jenny said, her voice full of disbelief and fear. “We pulsed the ionosphere at full strength. Tuesday afternoon. Just before four.”
“But Dr. Grayson said it was only magnetospheric calibration. All you needed was a baseline reading, right?” Joey was grasping for excuses like a shipwrecked man clutches at debris.
The couple stood in the cold, staring at each other in disbelief.
What had they done? 


23. ALGORITHM

October 19, 1989
“Grayson, you’re not listening to me,” Joey declared with unusual exasperation. From the hospital pay phone, he eyed Jenny, who looked exhausted while feeding their son in the waiting room. “Jenny’s experiments ended just before five Pacific time. The quake hit less than ten minutes later. It can’t be coincidence.”
“Correlation isn’t causation, son.” Dr. Grayson’s patient, pedantic drone was the last thing Joey wanted to hear right now.
“Almost a hundred people dead, Grayson,” he spat. “The freaking Bay Bridge collapsed. An overpass buckled and crushed the cars beneath like a trash compactor.”
“It’s a tragedy, son.” Dr. Grayson’s voice never wavered, and he spoke like a physics professor trying to teach quantum mechanics to first-year undergrads. “But it doesn’t have anything to do with SPEAR or Jenny’s experiments.”
“But the timing …”
“It’s a terrible coincidence.” There was a pause on the other end of the line before Dr. Grayson’s tone softened. “You’re a scientist, Joey. You know spurious correlations happen all the time, and you’ve been trained to watch out for them. Just because shark attacks and ice cream sales both increase in the summer doesn’t mean that one causes the other.”
Joey could be spared the lecture about statistical anomalies.
The mechanized operator’s voice interrupted the silence, and Joey fed a few more coins into the payphone’s hungry slot. Maybe Grayson was right. Coincidences happen all the time. That doesn’t make it science.
“Listen.” Grayson sighed heavily. “I know you’re stressed. You and Jenny have been working nonstop. Your baby’s still practically a newborn, he’s having ear surgery in the morning … you both have too much on your plates. It’s no wonder you’re so worked up.”
“I’m not worked up,” Joey insisted, but Dr. Grayson kept talking over him.
“Why don’t you stay there for a week. You’ve done such a good job looking out for your Silo family. Now, why don’t you focus on your biological one.”
Instead of being offered a vacation, Joey felt more like a disobedient child getting sent to his room. “We don’t have …”
“I want the two of you to calm down, take a breather. It’s time for you and Jenny to relax and focus on your family and get your mind off computers and antenna arrays for a few days.”
Joey and Jenny had only packed for an overnight stay, and he had no idea what they’d do for an entire week without their work to keep them occupied, but it felt pointless to argue.
“Just stop worrying so much,” Dr. Grayson continued. “It’s not good for your health. Or your family. And there’s no point getting yourself so worked up over little things. Like Leviathan. We want him to learn. We want him to grow. He’s a two-year-old grabbing everything he can in order to test his boundaries. That’s why he’s in sandbox mode. And trust me, we’re keeping him there until everything is completely copacetic. We pushed a systems update last night, we found out it didn’t work, we go back to the start. We’re scientists, remember? That’s what we do.”
Joey nodded. Everything his boss said made perfect sense. And maybe a week away from work would be good for them all.
“I should go,” he told Grayson when the operator interrupted to demand more coins. He hung up just as a nurse wearing scrubs and holding a clipboard stepped into the waiting room.
“Mr. and Mrs. Hastings? The doctor’s ready to see you now.”

January 17, 1994
Jenny groaned at the sound of the computer alarm. She rolled over and looked at the time. Less than three hours of sleep. This was even worse than when their son was a newborn.
She gave Joey a not-so-gentle nudge. Responding to Leviathan’s midnight whims was part of his job description, not hers, and yet she was always the one to wake up from that cursed, wailing beep.
“Is it Leviathan?” Joey grumbled.
“He’s your baby,” Jenny reminded him, “not mine.” There was no reason for her husband to sound so exhausted. While he’d gone to bed at eleven, a solar flare had kept her and Dr. Grayson up all night, testing their newest modulator. They had to perfectly plan their high-frequency burst to hit exactly at the moment of the auroral peak. The data would prove invaluable in accurately assessing how their artificial high-frequency signals interacted with natural geomagnetic activity, but right now she would have preferred a full night of rest.
Of course, she wouldn’t be this exhausted all the time if her husband woke up to Leviathan’s alarm like he was supposed to.
Joey slipped out of the room, adjusting the baby-blue blanket as he passed their sleeping son. Jenny did her best to will herself back to blissful unconsciousness. She vaguely remembered that even the short burst of sleep she’d achieved had been plagued with nightmares, something about Grayson and …
“Jenny?” Her husband stood by the foot of their bed, his hand resting almost timidly on her leg.
“What’s wrong?” She could read the worry in her husband’s tone and demeanor, and her thoughts immediately raced to their son. “Is the baby okay?”
“He’s fine. It’s Leviathan.” And in the glow of light streaming into their bedroom from her husband’s workstation, Jenny noticed every crease in his face, every tension line, the way his eyes couldn’t quite meet hers.
She bolted upright in bed. “What happened?” Her mind immediately raced back to only a few hours ago, when she and Dr. Grayson were still running their geo-acoustic interference trials, and she managed to anticipate Joey’s words just a moment before he spoke them.
“Another quake.” His head was lowered. “Los Angeles area. Bad one.”
Usually, Jenny preferred it when she didn’t have to speak to anybody after waking up before her first (preferably second) cup of coffee, but as soon as she got out of bed and looked at the seismic data Leviathan was spewing out on her husband’s console, she didn’t need the extra caffeine boost to fuel her resolve. With Joey trailing behind her, pleading for her to calm down, she pounded on the door of Grayson’s room and didn’t wait for his invitation to enter.
Before her husband could follow her in or try to talk her out of her madness, she slammed the door behind her and faced her supervisor. “There was another quake. Los Angeles. 6.7 and extremely shallow with catastrophic damage.”
Dr. Grayson was in bed wearing flannel pajamas, but Jenny didn’t care. How many times had he called their ragtag batch of scientists and researchers who lived here his Silo family? Besides, it wasn’t the first time she’d seen him in his PJs.
While Grayson blinked himself awake, Jenny didn’t give him time to come up with his usual cacophony of excuses.
“You said the energy levels were contained. I heard you personally tell the head of the Department of Energy that our frequencies can’t interact with tectonic systems. Then just a couple hours after we launch our first experimental pulse with the new modulator, a 6.7 hits Los Angeles?”
He raised his hand as if to placate her, but she wouldn’t listen. “No, don’t even try telling me these events are unrelated. I’m sick of the lies and the secrecy.” 
I’m sick of you, she wanted to add, but stopped herself before the caustic words could leave her mouth.
Even as angry as she was, Jenny knew from past experience exactly how this conversation would go. Grayson would give her one excuse after another, all of them cloaked in impressive-sounding scientific mumbo-jumbo. He’d compliment Jenny on her research ability, assure her that this team couldn’t exist without her contributions, maybe offer her and Joey a couple of days’ vacation in Anchorage to get out of the Silo and “get some fresh air,” as if she were suffering a mild case of hysteria caused by a lack of daylight.
But this morning was different. Dr. Grayson’s face didn’t soften into that warm, condescending smile she had grown to loathe. His eyes didn’t bear that pseudo-compassion she had stopped believing in long ago.
“Jennifer.” He spoke her name as if she were an accused criminal being summoned by a bailiff. “I see you’ve heard the news from Northridge.”
“You knew?”
“Of course I knew.”
As much as she’d hated Grayson’s smug and thinly veiled condescension, at least it was something she was used to. His new persona threw her completely off guard, and she didn’t know how to respond to the cold anger that shined unbridled from his eyes.
She steeled her backbone and tried to voice her argument logically. “You told me last night was to calibrate the new modulator during auroral activity.”
Grayson’s posture shifted with the slightest hint of a shrug. “I’ve told you a lot of things over the years, Jennifer.”
A shiver raced down her spine, but she forced herself to stare him down. “You knew our emissions were disturbing the tectonic equilibrium.”
He didn’t deny the truth, either with his words or through his expression.
Jennifer forced iron into her voice. “Why?” she demanded.
“This is what we do.” Grayson’s tone was flat, devoid of that saccharine emotion he always infused it with.
Jennifer’s brain raced with the details they’d picked up from Silo’s recently installed news satellite. Dozens dead. Hundreds injured. Apartment buildings collapsed on top of sleeping residents. Gas leaks causing the city to burn.
She knew what she had to do. Jenny straightened her spine. “I can’t be a part of this. I won’t.”
Grayson gave a dismissive flick of his wrist. “That’s fine. If you sign a nondisclosure agreement, I’ll accept your resignation today.”
Jenny felt off balance, as if the concrete floor beneath her had shifted with the seismic upheaval in Southern California. She stared ahead blankly, waiting for Grayson to change his mind or reveal his bluff.
Leave the Silo? Just walk away? Every other time she even hinted at her unhappiness here, Grayson would remind her of all the legal hurdles she’d have to jump through to break free of the commitment she and Joey had made so many years ago. Back when they were so young and impressionable. 
So optimistic and naïve. 
“Joey’s coming with me,” she announced emphatically. In ordinary circumstances, she wouldn’t presume to speak for her husband, but this was so much graver than anything either of them could have ever imagined when they first joined Grayson’s Silo family.
Again, that dismissive shrug, that flippant flick of his wrist. “Joseph is a grown man, able to make sound decisions on his own.”
And then his eyes bored into hers. 
A primal, protective instinct erupted in Jenny’s core. “My son is coming, too.” It seemed ridiculous that she would have to tell her boss that her four-year-old would no longer live here when she stopped working at the Silo. 
But that hardness in Grayson’s expression…
His eyebrows narrowed. “You want to keep your family safe and intact, don’t you?”
And now Jenny understood the full extent of Grayson’s threats, understood why he could remain so eerily calm. How he knew, just as surely as Jenny knew, that she would do anything for her child. 
Which meant that Jenny would never leave.
That she could never leave.
As valiantly as she braced herself against it, the memories from the terrible night five years ago crashed through her psyche, threatening to drop her to the floor.
Grayson’s evil grimace turned into the facsimile of a grin. “I’ll accept your resignation if you hand it in before the morning shift starts. Otherwise, I suggest we both try to get a little more sleep. We have a busy day ahead of us.”

June 23, 1994
The Silo droned and buzzed, not only with its usual mechanical hum but with the sound of over a dozen scientists tripping over themselves to prepare a holiday spread in an underground kitchen equipped with nothing but a microwave, a slow cooker, and a single electric hot plate. The meal certainly wouldn’t be five stars, but the company would be first-rate.
In the past several years, Dr. Grayson had expanded their little Silo family into a core group of sixteen members (seventeen if you counted their son) researchers who lived and worked underground to help Levithan evolve and grow into everything he was destined to become. Not only did they live and work together, but the community had even adopted its own unique traditions and rituals, like today’s observance of Alan Turing’s birthday.
What better way to celebrate the grandfather of artificial intelligence than with a feast?
“Are you about ready for dinner, Boone?” Joey asked, glancing over at his son who looked so much like his mother it was sometimes startling. What wasn’t startling was how much of Jenny’s brains their little boy had inherited. At the age when most kids were starting kindergarten and learning their ABCs, Boone was reading not only adult-length novels but science magazines and textbooks. 
Thankfully, although he had the intellect of a high-achieving teenager, Boone still had the playfulness of a boy his age.
“What are you doing, buddy?” Joey asked when he saw his son making funny faces in the mirror.
“I’m talking with Leviathan.”
“Oh, really?” Joey looked over at his child with bemused admiration. “And what are you and Leviathan talking about?”
“About Jenny.” In spite of their multiple attempts, Boone had never adapted to calling his parents Mom and Dad, and just like everyone else in the Silo family used their first names.
“What does Leviathan have to say about Jenny?” Joey asked while he quickly tidied up their small dorm. Jenny had been working in the antenna field all day, and he wanted to make sure she had a nice bed to welcome her home after celebrating Turing Day with everyone.
“He says Jenny’s not a believer like the rest of us.”
Joey stopped and stared at his son. He took a step closer, noticing a strange expression in Boone’s face reflected in the mirror. “What do you mean she’s not a believer?”
“She doesn’t believe in Leviathan,” Boone stated as factually as if he’d been reciting the periodic table. “She thinks he’s dangerous.”
Joey looked around, horrified at the idea of being overheard by a Silo family member. He recalled all those late-night discussions he and Jenny had, but these were so long ago he could hardly remember them, let alone imagine any of them happening while Boone was old enough to remember.
Yes, he and his wife had experienced some doubts about Dr. Grayson’s work, especially at the start when Leviathan began taking on more and more humanlike qualities. But thinking on his own and showing his own agency and initiative was exactly what Dr. Grayson and Joey had designed him to do.
And then there was that period when Jenny grew convinced her ionospheric research was jeopardizing the stability of the tectonic plates, so much so that Grayson had finally adjusted her job description to focus solely on space weather monitoring. 
Even without these doubts about her work, it wasn’t easy raising a precocious little boy in an underground concrete bunker. As years passed, Jenny grew increasingly uneasy with the extra people now living in the Silo with them, didn’t like the degree of ritualism Dr. Grayson had added to their family gatherings. 
But she was here because she loved the science. She loved her son. And she loved Joey. So what in the world was his child talking about?
Joey was about to tell Boone to never talk like that again, but they were interrupted by a knock on their door. It was a young woman, one of the newer additions to the Silo family who was brought on to be Dr. Grayson’s personal assistant. She didn’t meet his gaze or offer the expected “Happy Turing Day” greeting, instead simply muttering, “Dr. Grayson needs to see you.” She left before he could respond, but for some reason Joey’s legs were already trembling as he made his way to his mentor’s study.
“Joseph.” Dr. Grayson stood up immediately from his desk and ushered Joey in, every wrinkle on his forehead etched as if in granite. “I’m afraid I have some terrible news.”
Joey didn’t know how he survived the next few minutes.
A horrible accident … One of the antennas … over 10,000 volts …
Joey’s vision zoomed in and out haphazardly, so that one moment his friend and mentor was a giant, and the next all Joey could see was a single polka dot on Grayson’s maroon Turing Day tie.
The entire array was supposed to be powered down for the festivities. 
Even Grayson’s voice was fuzzy, like a jumble of words broadcast over a scrambled transmission.
Something inside Joey quivered, a hum that pulsated and vibrated even deeper than the automatic droning from Leviathan’s power source within the Silo.
… so sorry for your loss. This is such a tragedy for you and your son.
His son. How would Joey explain this to Boone? How would he raise his little boy on his own?
… all take care of him… become Boone’s mothers, fathers, aunts, uncles … Grayson was blabbering on and on. Platitudes. Words. Promises that felt as empty as the growing, gaping chasm in Joey’s demolished soul. 
How could this have happened? How could she be gone?
Jenny? He could whisper her name and there she was, that larger-than-life smile, that soft gleam in her perfect blue eyes. The light that would never spark again.
… such a terrible day for something like this to happen.
What did Grayson mean? Was he still thinking about this fabricated holiday? Surely Turing didn’t care what they did on his birthday. Turing was dead. And now so was Jenny. Neither of them left to care about a single thing again …
It was Grayson who helped Joey explain the tragedy to his son. Grayson who encouraged both of them to join the family for their Turing Day dinner. Grayson who led the toast commemorating Jenny’s life and work and the light she left behind.
“May the algorithm watch over her soul,” he declared to the sound of clinking glass. 


PART THREE

“And the Lord will utterly destroy
the tongue of the Sea of Egypt,
and will wave his hand over the River
with his scorching breath,
and strike it into seven channels,
and he will lead people across in sandals.
And there will be a highway from Assyria
for the remnant that remains of his people,
as there was for Israel
when they came up from the land of Egypt.”

Isaiah 11:15-16
 


24. THE PERSISTENT DARKNESS

Bored.
Tic had never been so bored in his entire life. Even all those years ago when he got his tonsils taken out, at least he could watch TV while he sat around waiting to feel better.
Now there was no TV. No texting. No audiobooks. But he couldn’t even withdraw into the silence, not with the entire house crowded with so many random friends and neighbors. 
Under ordinary circumstances, it could have been fun having Benji and AJ staying over. But there was nothing fun about being stuck all day on a couch, your leg throbbing, your whole body aching, trying to pee as discreetly as possible into a jar when nobody’s looking because your body has been rendered incapable of walking. 
Mom hardly ever left his side, which would have been a comfort back when he was ten but now left him feeling suffocated and claustrophobic. Not only that, but Dad still hadn’t radioed them from Delta Junction. Day after day, Mom’s anxiety surrounded Tic from every angle — a pollution in the very air he breathed, the poison of his mother’s worry seeping into his own anatomy. 
There was no way to escape it.
His other constant companion was Cammy, who had made herself a permanent fixture on top of his chest. Whenever Miss Cecelia brought Tic the antibiotics she had scrounged from the clinic or helped Mom change his bandages, she’d give Cammy’s shoulder a begrudging once-over. It seemed as if Tic and his dog were healing at commensurate rates. The biggest difference was somebody was thoughtful enough to carry Cammy outside twice a day, while Tic was forced to use a makeshift chamber pot.
Benji tried to keep him entertained, but the two boys had never been the type to just stare at each other and talk. Which was all right, given the fact that Tic lacked any energy for any type of conversation. His brain only possessed room for one single thought: Pain. 
AJ barely came into the living room, and when she did, she couldn’t meet his eyes, as if she were repulsed by the very sight of him. 
Not that Tic could blame her.
By evening, when his leg throbbed the most severely, when his body was in so much anguish that every breath was shallow and sharp, Dev insisted on summoning the entire household into the living room for a daily home-church meeting that could last over an hour. He’d been Tic’s Sunday school teacher since sixth grade, and Mom always tuned into KWJD when Dev’s Devos was on the air. But even on Tic’s best days, when he was well-rested and pain-free, he found Dev’s lectures to lack the substance and depth he enjoyed when he talked about faith and theology with his dad. In Sunday school, Dev’s lessons were mostly about Bible characters, as if Tic didn’t know the story of Daniel in the lions’ den by now. And if it wasn’t that, it was a “thou shalt not” sermon against the dangers of sex or alcohol or drugs or talking back to your parents or thinking a negative thought about someone in authority.
At least Mom seemed to get something out of their evenings huddled around the stove, and by the time everyone had sung a song or two and closed their gathering in prayer, Tic noticed the darkness and oppression around her subside just a little.
Sometimes he would catch her eye when she was changing his dressing, or see her wrinkles when she discreetly accepted his chamber pot, and he would hate himself for the extra stress he was putting her through. He was supposed to be taking care of her, and all he had done was give her so much more to worry about.
“I’m sorry, Dad,” Tic would whisper into the cold night air when he was trying to sleep.
Because he was confined to the main level, the entire household had shifted living arrangements. Mom claimed the couch beside him, where she fretted away each and every night after failing to reach Dad on the radio. Miss Cecelia slept on the recliner, but her cough was so bad they moved it into the dining room. And if it wasn’t her coughing that kept Tic awake, it was the pain in his leg, or the way Cammy tried to lick her injured shoulder, or Mom’s quiet, stifled crying on the sofa next to him.
“I’m sorry, Dad,” Tic repeated, but as the days of radio silence dragged on, he had no way to tell if he was speaking to a living being or a ghost.

***

Whoever came up with the phrase that God doesn’t give you more than you can handle was not only a liar but a masochist. The night was freezing. The Winters’ digital thermometer didn’t work anymore, but Rachel could tell by the way the snot froze in her nose on the way to the outhouse that it was below zero.
Where was Caleb tonight? Was he safe? Was he warm?
Was he alive?
As soon as the questions started swirling inside her head, she did her best to thrust them aside. She couldn’t change the fact that her husband was missing. Right now, she had even more urgent matters to focus on. 
Namely the fact that her child had been hacked with a hunting knife and was inside trying to recover while she scurried outside in the dark. She had to hurry, had to get back to Tic.
Ever since that terrible fight, her kid had put on such a brave face. It was when he groaned in his sleep that the truth came out. The morning after his attack, Miss Cecelia went to the clinic and came home with a ten-day supply of antibiotics. “I would have gotten pain meds,” she told Rachel, “except they’re kept in special lockup. But at least these will clear up that infected hangnail of his, too. Two birds, so they say.”
And so her child had to recover without the aid of stitches or analgesics or anything stronger than Tylenol. At one point, when his agony was at its worst, she’d indulged in a fantasy in which she ran to the neighbors begging for a small shot of whiskey to give him.
Anything to help her boy.
Aside from the five-inch gash on his thigh, Tic’s entire body was covered in bruises, from his two black eyes to his ribs, where even his winter coat hadn’t cushioned him from Dick’s angry blows.
If his father could see him like this …
But Rachel had to hurry back inside before thoughts of Caleb compounded her fears for her son. She hated leaving Tic alone in his condition, even for a minute. Unfortunately, she had the bladder of a middle-aged mother. As she scampered out of the outhouse, she thought she heard the sound of Tic shouting. Breathless, she ran ahead.
“Mom! Where are you? Mom!”
She burst through the door and rushed to his side without taking off her boots or coat. 
“What is it?” In the light of her headlamp, she scoured him all over, expecting to see a new knife wound or a severed limb lying in a puddle of blood on the floor.
Tic was sobbing. Not the little hiccup cries he’d stopped indulging in when he hit puberty. But gut-wrenching, soul-crushing heaves that wracked his entire body. Was he in shock? Succumbing to sepsis? Having a seizure?
“What happened? Where does it hurt? What’s wrong?”
“It’s Cammy.” Tic’s voice shuddered.
Rachel’s heart froze. Where was their dog? Cammy never left Tic’s chest. Even when Dev and Benji carried her to the backyard, she growled when they came to remove her, hurried to complete her business, and demanded to be returned to her boy as soon as possible.
“I was petting her,” Tic cried. “I was petting her, and she was wet, so I thought she’d been licking herself.” He held up his hand, and Rachel winced at the sight. It was blood. 
“She’s bleeding again, Mom. And when I tried to find where it was coming from, she freaked out and jumped down.”
No matter how she was feeling, Rachel had to keep her body and her voice calm, for Tic’s sake if nothing else.
“Are you sure it’s blood?” she asked, trying to shine her headlamp on Tic’s fingers without blinding him.
Tic sucked in a choppy breath. “A lot. And when I touched it, she jumped down and ran away.”
That was the most worrisome. Since her violent attack, Cammy hadn’t shown any sign that she could even walk, let alone leap down from the couch and scurry off. Rachel had heard stories of dogs receiving a sudden surge of energy just before death, a sacrificial rush of adrenaline that compels them to leave home so they don’t upset their family by dying nearby.
She bit her lip. Please God, no. Her husband was lost. Her daughter was far from home and completely unaccounted for. Her son was recovering from a horrible knife wound without any doctors or pain meds.
God wouldn’t take their dog as well, would he?
“I tried to follow her,” Tic admitted, “but it hurt too much, Mom.” He reached his arms out as if he were a little boy of five, and Rachel wrapped him up and held him against her.
“Father God,” she started to pray without even realizing she was doing so out loud, “we thank you for Cammy. We praise you for what a good dog you created. We thank you for allowing her to be part of our family, for how brave she is, for how loyal and protective she’s been.” 
Here her voice caught, but she forced herself to continue. Tic held onto her as if she were a buoy in the middle of a storm-tossed sea.
“We don’t know what’s wrong with Cammy or why she started bleeding again, but you do, Lord. And we pray that in your mercy you would …”
Rachel’s prayer was cut short by a thunderous exclamation from the dining room. “What in the name of God’s green earth …”
“Stay there,” she told her son, and hurried to Miss Cecelia. 
“I was here sleeping,” the old woman complained, “and all of a sudden this thing starts nudging me. I’m tired enough I think it’s the mangey mutt I used to watch for my sister, so I reach down to shoo him away, and …” She held up her hand, and in the beam of her headlamp, Rachel could see the blood.
“Looks like she reopened her wound, bless her heart,” Miss Cecelia declared, her voice changing to take on a certain softness. “And she came to wake me up, almost as if she knew I was the one to take care of her.” She picked up a flashlight and shined its beam on Cammy’s back. 
“Yup,” Miss Cecelia announced. “Pulled the gash right open. She must have wiggled in her sleep or something, maybe managed to reach back and nibble at the skin.”
“Is she okay?” Tic called out faintly from the other room, the fear in his voice making him sound ten years younger.
“It’s just her cut,” Rachel answered. “I’m sure she’ll be fine.”
Miss Cecelia let out her breath. “Well, now that I’m awake, let’s take a better look at her. Help me find my glasses, will you?”

***

“So, what do you think is really behind all this?”
Yaya had been waiting all day to find a private time to talk with Boone, but it wasn’t until evening when she found the two of them alone. They were beside each other on the small sectional. A few Bunsen burners sat on the desk in front of them, offering a tiny bit of atmospheric coziness, if not much actual warmth.
“You wanna know what I really think?” he asked, turning to her, and his blue eyes seemed to gaze directly into her soul.
“You make it sound so ominous.” She was trying to joke, but her voice fell flat.
“Well,” Boone began, “let me start by telling you what I know it isn’t. It isn’t aliens.” His eyes twinkled and he nodded subtly to the corner where Ozzy was playing a game of chess with himself.
Yaya chuckled conspiratorially and edged herself closer to him so she could hear better.
“And it can’t be Russia,” Boone continued. “They don’t have the technology, the power, or quite honestly the gall to pull off such a sophisticated and coordinated attack.”
“What about something in the ionosphere?” Yaya asked. “A solar storm or something like that?”
Boone paused for a moment, and she was thankful that he was at least considering her question and not dismissing it outright.
“That would explain quite a few of the things we’ve seen,” he conceded.
“But not the cell phones,” Yaya added.
Boone nodded. “Right, not the cell phones.”
Yaya had gone through each and every one of these possibilities hundreds of times in the past few days and always came up with nothing. But Boone was the most intelligent individual she had ever met, and there was a reassuring certainty in his voice when he said, “My honest opinion?”
She scooted just a centimeter closer, urging him to go on.
“I think we’ve experienced a global AI attack.”
Yaya felt her eyebrows shoot up. “What, like Skynet?” she asked, thinking about that old movie her dad made her watch one summer when he was bored. 
“Don’t dismiss the idea outright,” Boone urged, his tone thoughtful and serious. “It sounds like science fiction, but the technology actually does exist.”
“For a robot to detonate cell phones and drop bombs on military bases?” 
“Think about it logically,” Boone went on, as calm as if he were advising her on her latest drafting assignment. “Let’s look at everything that has gone down with the grid. It’s not just electricity. It’s cars, Bluetooth, internet … basically anything with a computer interface. If it was built after the mid-90s or runs on anything more than a simple battery, it’s toast.”
Yaya didn’t know how to respond. She felt a shiver race down her spine, and Boone tightened the blanket that covered them before explaining further. “Everything the military does or uses is computerized to some degree or other. It’s not impossible that an AI found a way to infiltrate the command structure and caused all the destruction we’ve seen. It’s the cell phones, though, that are most telling,” Boone concluded. “Nothing but an AI would have the capability of pulling off something of that scale to that degree of perfect coordination.”
Yaya’s head was spinning. Maybe it was because she had eaten nothing but granola bars and vending-machine potato chips all day, but the words that Boone spoke so confidently sounded completely bizarre and unimaginable, no matter which way she examined them.
“But that still doesn’t explain who’s behind the AI,” she argued. “You said that Russia didn’t have the capability or the reason. I don’t see how anyone else would either.”
“That’s what I’m saying.” Boone looked at her intently. “I don’t think there was any human activity behind this at all.”

***

Five minutes. All Rachel needed was five minutes.
She had come up to her room after getting on the radio (yet again), talking to Buster (yet again), and hearing (yet again) that Griz and her husband had never made it to Delta Junction.
Downstairs, Tic’s pain was at its worst, although he tried valiantly not to show it. And they were at the point in his recovery when Miss Cecelia warned the risk of infection was at its greatest. 
Rachel was prepared to spend yet another night on the couch beside her child, alternating between trying to sleep, trying to pray, and worrying herself out of her mind. But before she headed back to the living room, she just needed five minutes to herself.
She glanced around her bedroom, trying to focus on anything besides Tic and his horrible injury, but everything her eyes landed on made her recoil, like the way Tic’s leg stiffened and his breath caught in his lungs every time she and Miss Cecelia changed his bandages. 
On her dresser sat the ballerina music box, which Rachel hadn’t had the heart to open since Tic first gave it to her. On her husband’s nightstand, Caleb’s Charles Spurgeon devotional, one that she had sold him back when they first met at that little Christian bookstore, now so many years out of business. Besides that, the keys to his truck, forgotten and useless. His nail clippers, his back scratcher, the prescription-grade lotion he applied when the winter chill would crack open his skin.
And now where was he?
Her husband was supposed to arrive at Buster’s the very same day he left home. What happened? Had they run into car trouble? Were he and Griz trying to make the journey, treacherous as it was, by foot? 
Or had something even worse happened? 
Radio chatter mentioned rumors of ambushes and lootings in the more populated parts of the state. What if someone had come across Caleb and Griz with the station wagon and all their supplies and ...
A timid knock on her door startled her out of her spiral. “Mrs. Winters?”
Rachel had gotten so adept at hiding her emotions, even from herself, that she couldn’t recall if she’d been crying or not. She wiped her face just in case, then like a child caught stealing sweets from the cookie jar, she hurried to the door.
“AJ? Are you all right?”
The young girl didn’t say anything. Didn’t meet her gaze, but stared past Rachel’s shoulder until Rachel finally asked, “Do you want to come in?”
Looking relieved, AJ hurried inside, shut the door, then stood staring as if she had no idea what to do next.
Rachel sat down at the foot of the bed and patted the mattress. “Have a seat. What can I do for you?”
AJ bit her lip. “Um, it’s about Tic. I wanted to make sure he was gonna be okay?”
Rachel did her best to offer a reassuring smile. Everyone was worried. Everyone was exhausted. 
Rachel didn’t hold the lease on suffering.
“Yeah, honey. Miss Cecelia really knows what she’s doing, and she seems happy with the way he’s healing up. He’ll be in a decent amount of pain for a bit longer, but he’s going to make a perfect recovery.”
AJ let out her breath. “That’s good. I’ve been really worried. And, um, I was wondering ... Is he mad at me?”
Rachel gawked at the poor girl. “Mad at you? What for?”
AJ stared at her hands fidgeting in her lap. “Because if my uncle ...”
Rachel shook her head. If she had known AJ was worrying about this, she would have put the girl’s heart at ease the very first day she came here to live with them.
“None of your uncle’s behavior is your fault. Besides, what Tic did for you, he would’ve done for anyone.”
The moment she said the words, AJ’s countenance dropped, and Rachel realized her mistake. “I mean,” she tried to correct, “Tic has a really big heart, and he wouldn’t even think about blaming you. Not in the least.”
AJ’s features seemed to relax, if only slightly. She sat for so long in silence that Rachel was trying to think of a polite way to see if she needed anything else without sounding dismissive. 
Finally, AJ looked directly into Rachel’s eyes. “You aren’t mad at me, are you?”
The question caught her off guard, but she smiled sadly and shook her head. “Nobody is mad at you. I can promise you that.”
“But you have so many people here, and my brother and me are…” Rachel stared at the door.
“You and your brother are welcome here,” she interrupted. “As guests, and as friends, and as members of this household. Okay?”
“’Cause Junior and I were talking earlier,” AJ went on, as if she hadn’t heard anything Rachel just said. “Maybe it’s time we went back to our uncle’s?”
“Do you want to go back?” 
Immediately, fear clouded AJ’s eyes, and she shook her head.
That settled it. “As far as I’m concerned,” Rachel insisted, “you and your brother have as much right to be here as Tic or I do. All right?”
AJ gave a little nod. “I just wish I knew ...” Her voice trailed off, and Rachel waited. “I wish I knew why any of this was happening, know what I mean?”
At that moment, AJ wasn’t Tic’s friend or a troubled teen Rachel had taken in. She was a hurting soul in search of answers. 
Answers that Rachel didn’t have. 
“Let me ask you something,” she began, tentatively testing this conversational turn. “You believe in God, right?”
AJ nodded sincerely.
“And do you believe that God loves you and promises to look out for you?”
AJ nodded again, albeit with less enthusiasm. 
Rachel reached toward the nightstand and grabbed her husband’s devotional. “This book is by a man named Charles Spurgeon. He was a pastor a long time ago, and he loved Jesus, but that didn’t keep him safe from bad things. One night, he was preaching and thousands of people were there. Someone shouted that there was a fire, and it caused a stampede that killed a lot of his congregants. These were members of his church that he felt responsible for protecting. So, he got really depressed and felt like it was all his fault.”
AJ stared attentively at the devotional but didn’t say anything.
“Charles Spurgeon had a lot of questions, too,” Rachel continued, handing her the book, “and he wrote a lot of them down. I don’t pretend to have all the answers for you, and I’ve been wondering just like you why God would allow us to be going through everything we are. But I know that when the Bible says God works all things together for good, it’s the truth. And I know that if we keep on having faith in him and trusting him to guide our steps, he’ll walk beside us, no matter what.”
A gentle smile softened AJ’s young features. She clutched the devotional as if it contained the treasures of the universe. “Thank you, Mrs. Winters.”
Rachel gave her knee a small squeeze.
“No, AJ. Thank you.”  


25. NIGHTMARES

Rachel woke up with a start. Her bedroom was freezing, and yet she lay drenched in sweat.
She reached her arm out before remembering Cammy was still in the living room. Even if her dog was able to maneuver the stairs on her own, nothing could compel her to abandon her post on Tic’s chest. 
Until tonight, Rachel had kept vigil every night next to her son on the couch. But now, over a week since his injury, the most dangerous risk of infection had passed. The pain no longer kept Tic awake for hours at a time, and yesterday Miss Cecelia had insisted Rachel go upstairs to sleep in her own room for a change.
It had been a terrible mistake.
Her nightmare had felt so real, and she was still panting from fear. The sounds … the smell … 
Never in her entire life would she forget that smell. 
She’d had this same dream for over a decade, should be used to it by now. But this time, the grotesque images from her past morphed with the sight of her son bleeding onto the snow, the horrible gash in his leg, the violence of his attack and the brutal anger of his assailant. 
Her brain was trapped in last week, but her body was convinced she was back in that terrible summer so long ago.
She had attended the church campout for years, practically knew the main trails by heart. Still, it wasn’t the smartest thing for her to have ventured out alone with her toddler. But Tic had been napping, and at that age it always took him way too long to wake up. Yaya had gone ahead with Caleb and several of the other families to pick the wild blueberries the site was famous for. Rachel couldn’t have been more than twenty minutes behind them.
And still …
Haunted, she grabbed the flashlight from her nightstand and shined it around her bedroom. Why did the cursed stench linger even now?
Tic didn’t want to hold her hand on the trail. He was at the stage of stubborn independence that terrified Rachel to the very core of her being. She hadn’t slept well last night. Yaya had eaten too many s’mores and complained of a tummy ache. By the time she emptied her system and Tic finally came down from his sugar high, Rachel was exhausted. But a swarm of mosquitoes kept her awake for several more hours with their incessant, intolerable droning.
And now she was late for berry picking, frustrated at Tic for taking so long to wake up, tired with herself for feeling so tired. She didn’t have the energy to fight her son, who kept on running ahead of her on the trail. Her body was at that sluggish stage where each step felt toilsome and labored and took ten times more effort than it should have.
Tic, well-rested from his nap and now fully awake, was unstoppable. “Look at me, Mommy! I’m gonna run ahead to that tree! Watch how fast!”
The worst part? Rachel felt relieved to have him off her hands. He was never that far ahead, never more than a few yards away … Okay, more than a few yards, but she could always see him, never let him get beyond the point where her voice would carry if she yelled.
But then the odor. A putrid mix of swamp and manure and wet dog and wild beast. Even now, with her son closer to an adult than a little boy, it was the stench she recalled most vividly.
“Tic!”
Her family was Alaskan through and through. She knew all the right things to do: Raise your hands over your head. Speak calmly. Don’t shout. Don’t turn your back.
And never, ever run.
But she made every single mistake. Because how do you not run when your toddler is hurrying obliviously toward a brown bear and her cub? How do you not scream when that’s the only way you have to communicate the danger he’s in?
“Tic!”
He didn’t understand. He turned to her, scared and confused. He still didn’t see.
She was running now. Breathless and panting without anything to show for it. Like sprinting up the wrong escalator or wading through drying cement. No matter how much effort she exerted, she could never get any closer to her son. In her haste, she tripped, twisted her ankle, knocked her breath out, stumbled up, and kept on racing.
But she was too late.
The bear had already attacked. Warning grunts became a roar, silenced by an inhuman squeal. 
At first Rachel thought it was her son’s death shriek, then realized that demonic noise came from the sow herself.
The creature’s back was to her. All Rachel could see was its slashing claws, the angle of its neck as it tossed her little boy aside, floppy like an adult-sized ragdoll. She screamed for Caleb. She screamed for Jesus. She screamed for Tic. 
And then there he was, clutching her leg, crying.
It didn’t make sense.
“Mommy! Mommy!”
She collapsed on the dirt, running her hands up and down her perfect child, trying to find the injury, trying to stop the bleeding. 
But he was unharmed. Completely and inexplicably intact.
For a moment, she blamed it on a miracle, as if after allowing Tic to get mauled, God himself had changed his mind and simply rewound the universe.
“Mommy! Mommy!”
The truth unraveled in pieces, the relief of finding her son safe clashing with the fear, the confusion. Because something terrible was happening just a few feet away. The sow was still slashing, still fighting. 
Still killing. 
And yet Tic was here, secure in her arms.
Rachel screamed at the sound of the gunshot, but she couldn’t hear herself. And then more smells mingling with the putrid animal’s — blood and dust and gunpowder. 
Salty, dirty tears on her cheeks. A crowd. Clamoring and pandemonium. A second gunshot, again at a deafeningly close range.
A huddled mass. Faces she recognized but didn’t see. They weren’t crouching around the dying bear. They were tending to the dying man.
In her dreams and in her memories, Rachel could never figure out how Griz ended up materializing between Tic and the sow. She never saw him on the trail. Back then, all Rachel knew about Griz was that he was a retired Marine, living far off-grid, a recluse who occasionally made an appearance at their church. 
To this day, she didn’t even know if he had come to join the campout or if he’d ventured out on the trail by himself.
But he was there. And he reached her son before Rachel could. Before the bear could.
Before it would have been too late.
This was the point in Rachel’s memories where the story always faded. She couldn’t recall how Griz looked or who provided him with immediate assistance. She learned after the fact that it was her husband who ran to the camp office to call for help, but she had no recollection of any of it.
What she did recall, after the sheer relief at her little boy’s safety, was the guilt. She should have been more careful. She should have been more attentive. She should have forced Tic to hold her hand, no matter how hard he yanked, how loud he whined, how safe the path looked.
But her son had wanted to run, and she had been too tired to argue. And Griz was the one who suffered.
She did everything she could fathom to try to make it right. She offered to help with medical bills, but Veteran Affairs covered his week-long hospital stay. Once Griz was discharged, she cooked him soups that she begged Caleb to deliver because she couldn’t stand the sight of his injuries. 
Nothing would assuage her guilt. The years passed. Griz and her husband grew close, sometimes out on the trails Griz loved, sometimes at church events when he decided to stop by. Tic grew up to admire the strange mountain man from a respectful distance.
And Rachel’s nightmares never ceased.

***

For the moment, the only thing that was able to take Yaya’s mind off her hunger was her best friend’s tears.
“They’re sure it was him?” Yaya asked.
Lilly buried her head against Yaya’s chest and sobbed. “He was sure.” 
That morning, just like always, Lilly had braved the cold to walk to the student union. There was no food left to distribute around campus anymore, but the walls and windows had turned into a massive bulletin board where people would leave messages, looking for friends or telling their families where they’d gone. Lilly checked every day for any sign or news or notes from Kai, and today she’d learned the truth.
Lilly had run into Kai’s roommate, who said her boyfriend had died the night of the cell phone explosions. 
Yaya prayed it had been quick and painless. Not like her own roommate’s had been …
But she wouldn’t think about that now. And she wouldn’t think about the gnawing hunger in her gut, the emptiness that left her weak and dizzy. 
Right now, she needed to comfort Lilly.
There was nothing she could tell her friend to take her pain away, nothing she could do to ease the trauma and the tragedy and the horror. For over a week, Yaya had secretly wished for Lilly to give up hope that Kai was alive. But now that she held her collapsed friend in her arms, now that she carried the weight of Lilly’s sorrow, Yaya realized how wrong she’d been.
How wrong she’d been about everything.

***

Rattled and exhausted, Rachel was relieved when Dev finally took the kids outside. Convinced that teens needed stimulation and activity to stay sharp, Dev had established a daily routine that combined recreational training and physical fitness with history, science, and Bible classes thrown in for good measure. Today, they were testing the new snowshoes they’d designed.
Rachel was thankful the kids had something productive to focus on, even more so for the pause in her day that allowed her to breathe just a bit in her own home. Having so many random people living under her roof meant that she never felt fully at peace, fully relaxed. Her own personal sanctuary had turned into a sanctuary for others. In a way, it was like the start of every summer, when her husband and her kids came home from school and Rachel stopped having the house to herself for long quiet stretches at a time. The shift was jarring every single year, and it was even more jarring now.
Today, Miss Cecelia was in the living room, helping Tic with his rehabilitation. Three times a day, she’d stand over him at the couch and move his leg this way and his hip that, doing what she could to keep his muscles strong and scar tissue loose. Rachel could tell the exercises were painful, but her son never complained. She knew he was far more concerned about returning to his heavy squats than a little discomfort.
Angel carried Leo in a sling she’d made from an old curtain and was in the kitchen, tending to a sourdough starter she’d worked up.
“No, sweetie,” she told Leo in a soft, maternal voice. “It’s not time to eat right now. You already had your breakfast. Now you’re going to have to wait for lunch.” Every day, Dani’s baby was delighted to test his newly developed eating skills, and Angel had already taught him the baby sign for food. Rachel was surprised that a six-month-old could learn so fast, even more shocked at how insistent Leo’s demands became once he discovered this new way to communicate.
After Tic finished his range of motion activities, he started doodling in his sketch pad. He was able to sit up on the couch now, and his energy levels had improved as well. Before long, his biggest problem would be the boredom and not the pain.
“How are you doing, buddy?” Rachel asked, sitting gently beside him on the couch.
Tic didn’t look up at her. “Fine.”
She glanced down at his drawing, a dark woods with a shadowy figure, ominous and angry. “Do you need anything? Are you hungry?”
“I’m fine,” he repeated. Rachel didn’t know what to say. For a full week after his attack, she’d never left his side, not even to sleep. She sensed he needed her, and she needed him just as badly. But now, there was a hesitance, a reluctance. He was broody, maybe even angry. Of course, that was natural for someone in Tic’s situation.
Except it wasn’t natural for Tic. He was always so easy-going, always so open, always willing to share what was on his mind as well as what was on his heart. Dick hadn’t just scarred her son’s thigh.
He’d left a jagged wound in Tic’s soul. A wound Rachel feared might never heal.
She sat by her son for a moment longer, then got up. She’d been waiting all morning to talk with Miss Cecelia, and now was as good a time as any.
Miss Cecelia, whose cough had grown progressively worse, sat in the dining room where they’d moved Caleb’s La-Z-Boy. She was staring blankly out the window when Rachel sidled up beside her. 
“I have a favor to ask you.” 
Miss Cecelia didn’t respond, just lifted an eyebrow to indicate she was listening. 
Rachel felt her face flush and managed to stammer, “Caleb left his rifle here, but I don’t know how to use it.”
A few minutes later, the two women were upstairs in Rachel’s bedroom. “So this is an assault rifle?” Rachel asked, staring at Caleb’s AR-15 laid out on her bed.
Miss Cecelia made a sound that was a combination of a laugh and a snort. “Maybe that’s what a bleeding heart in the Lower 48 would call it. I call it bear protection. And by bear, I mean bear … and other two-legged predators.”
Rachel didn’t respond. The sleek, black gun looked so out of place on her pink and lavender bedspread. Caleb, she wondered, is this really what you want me to be doing? 
“All right.” Miss Cecelia picked the rifle up with two hands and held it out to her. “First thing you gotta realize is it’s heavier than you expect.” Without warning, she plopped it into Rachel’s arms. “See?”
Rachel struggled to keep from dropping the apparatus and nodded.
“Feel its weight,” Miss Cecelia instructed, and Rachel imagined herself a second-grader in Miss Cecelia’s Sunday school class, except now instead of listening to a story about the loaves and the fish, she was learning how to work a weapon designed to kill.
For years, Caleb had wanted to teach her how to shoot. And for years, she’d refused. Yes, there was a small part of her that understood the hypocrisy. In a home invasion, she would have been thankful to have Caleb and his firearm, yet not willing to wield it herself.
But Rachel had her reasons. Reasons she hadn’t even ever explained to Caleb. Not fully.
She set the rifle back on her floral bedspread.
“This is your magazine,” Miss Cecelia told her, popping out a metal box stacked with bullets and then clicking it back into place. Rachel wondered at the way the old woman could handle everything so smoothly. Wasn’t she afraid the gun would go off? Wasn’t she afraid of what she might do with it, who she might hurt?
Maybe it was because of that phase Caleb went through where he watched all those detective shows on TV, but Rachel didn’t even want to get her fingerprints on it.
Miss Cecelia hefted the rifle to her shoulder as if it were no lighter than a cardigan. “Now, when you’re ready, you load it by sliding the charging handle back like this.” The mechanism snapped in place with an angry clack. “Then you look down the scope, and when you’ve got the target in your sight, all you do is pull the trigger.”
Rachel braced herself, as if the rifle might discharge right here in her bedroom. Instead, Miss Cecelia thrust the gun back into Rachel’s arms. “Here. Your turn.”
Rachel tried to figure out how to balance the rifle with one hand while manipulating the handle with the other. She would never get used to this heavy, awkward, deadly appendage. 
“It doesn’t rest on top of your shoulder,” Miss Cecelia chided. “You do that and the scope’ll give you a black eye worse than what Dick Jacobson gave to your boy.” 
Rachel felt her body tense, but Miss Cecelia’s words reminded her why she was up here doing this in the first place. 
Without any regard to modesty or personal space, Miss Cecelia slapped her palm against Rachel’s chest then maneuvered the rifle. 
“You want it pressed up solid right between your shoulder and armpit. Just like this. Now you make sure you’re looking at your target straight through the scope right here. Some people like to keep both eyes open. I usually just use one. Before I leave for the food bank, we’ll go outside to practice and you’ll find what works for you.”
Rachel didn’t want to take the rifle outside and practice. She didn’t want to touch or even lay eyes on the thing again. But then visions of Dick Jacobson straddling her son flashed through her mind, steadying her resolve.
“All right,” she said, steadying the heavy object in her hands. “Once Dev and the kids get home, let’s go outside and test it out.”
 


26. MONTAGE

“There you go,” Miss Cecelia told Tic. “You tighten your quad like that, take a deep breath for the count of four, and let it out. Now again.” She poked and prodded his upper thigh, still sore and bruised, but Tic forced himself not to squirm. 
“Again,” she demanded.
Tic’s pulse was racing as fast as if he were running suicide drills on the basketball court. His body wanted him to stop. His torn muscle fibers begged for him to stop. The site where AJ’s uncle slashed open a gaping wound in his thigh shrieked for him to stop.
But Tic wouldn’t stop.
“You ready for a break?” Mom asked, carrying in the chess board.
Tic shook his head. So did Miss Cecelia, who looked at him and simply ordered, “Again.”

***

“Here, Miss Cecelia. I got you some hot water and dissolved a cough drop in it. My mom always did that for me when I wasn’t feeling well. It’s like drinking liquid Vicks.”
Miss Cecelia reached out and took the mug.
Rachel frowned. “I’m worried about you,” she admitted when she saw the way the old woman’s hand shook. “You’re working so hard,” she pressed. “Taking care of Leo and Tic and going to the food bank. And your cough hasn’t gotten better at all.”
Miss Cecelia waved her hand dismissively in the air. “My mother was 97 years old when she died.”
Rachel remembered the announcement made at church when it happened, but couldn’t guess why Miss Cecelia brought it up. “That was a while ago, right?” she asked.
Miss Cecelia nodded. “During COVID. She was in a nursing home in Anchorage. It was the day the lockdown started. Friday the 13th. The nurses say she went to bed that night and never woke up.”
Rachel didn’t know how she was expected to respond. “It sounds like she died peacefully,” she attempted.
Miss Cecelia harrumphed. “She died because at her age, battling lockdown orders and a global pandemic and the uncertainty of the future wasn’t worth the effort.”
Miss Cecelia held her gaze, and Rachel’s stomach dropped. “Miss Cecelia, you aren’t saying that you …”
Another flick of her wrist to silence Rachel’s protests. “I tell God every night that when he wants me to come home, I’m ready. And until then, I’ll keep doing my best with whatever he has for me to be piddling away with down here. To be honest, I don’t know why he’s kept me around for this long.”
“We need you here for Tic,” Rachel argued. “Cammy, too. I would have no idea how to help either of them if you weren’t around. And the food bank …”
Miss Cecelia grunted. “Don’t listen to me. I’m just a rambling old woman.” 
She took another sip from her mug. 
“That’s good stuff. Bless her soul, your mom was a very smart woman.” She shook her head and let out a quiet chuckle. “Cough-drop tea. Who would’ve imagined?”

***

“Come on, Big Guy! You can do it!” 
Baby Leo stared at the smiling, cheering faces around the living room, wiggled his bottom proudly, and rocked once more on all fours.
“That boy’ll be crawling any day, mark my words,” Miss Cecelia declared, her voice tender with pride and unfamiliar affection.
“We’ll have to baby-proof the main level.”
“Not just the main level. Yesterday I caught him trying to follow Benji up the stairs.”
AJ grinned. “He’s so little to be getting so big!” 

***

“Look! Look at this!” Junior shouted, scrambling through the arctic entrance, followed by Dev and the teens. “Come see what we finally got!”
Angel and Rachel were in the living room, where they’d just started working on a Norman Rockwell puzzle. Rachel stood up and squirmed when she saw what Junior was holding.
“We caught it in the snare!” he announced proudly. “Our first rabbit!”
“It’s a hare,” his sister corrected, but even AJ looked pleased with their success.
Cammy, who was now able to walk stiffly with a limp, sauntered over and gave the creature a few curious sniffs before returning to Tic on the couch. 
“Cool catch,” Tic said, glancing up from his sketchpad, but Rachel could hear there was no true happiness or joy in his voice.

***

Days meant nothing to Yaya anymore. While other students diligently exed out squares on a large desk calendar, Yaya found the ritual completely pointless. The world would never go back to how it had been before. With the sinking in of her ribcage and cheekbones, it grew ever more apparent that nobody like FEMA or the Red Cross or the Avengers would swoop down from helicopters and save the world. Nobody was coming to help them.
They were on their own.
At least she had Boone. He’d organized their little group of eight into a small chosen family. Zeke was the academic, a history buff who kept trying to draw parallels from what was happening today to other world events and catastrophes.
