The food shortage and harsh winter were no worse than what Russians endured during the Siege of Leningrad, the exploding cell phones like the attack on Hezbollah pagers. The grid crash was a textbook example of what so many Americans expected to experience during the Cold War, its aftermath like the panic and looting during the New York City blackout in the 1970s.
While Zeke pontificated about the past, Ozzie kept the group entertained with his random store of useless trivia and deadpan delivery. He had the largest repertoire of film and TV knowledge of the group and liked to share interesting storylines from an obscure sitcom or favorite indie film, and if Lilly was bored, she could simply ask him for his opinions about aliens or flat-earthers.
And during it all, Yaya stayed next to Boone. Maybe it was trauma bonding. Maybe it was something else. But ever since she saw him unconscious in his lab, she’d grown to realize how much he meant to her, how empty her life would feel without him by her side.
Sometimes she’d catch him staring at her with a depth to his gaze that nearly sucked her breath away, that made her want to find any way possible to steal a quiet minute together with him. He had a dimple on his left cheek she’d never noticed before.
While their roommates played cards or discussed conspiracies, she and Boone would slip away to their favorite sectional. Surrounded by everyone else, they enjoyed time together in their own private world, where the flame flickering from a Bunsen burner warmed Yaya’s skin, and the company she was with warmed both her heart and her soul.

***

Same setting, different dream. The smells are identical. The sounds. But this time Tic is older. And long before the danger becomes imminent, he sees the bear. Even before the sow charges, he’s climbed a tree to get away.
Good boy, Rachel thinks to herself, and then she looks down. Why is she holding her husband’s rifle?
“It’s loaded for bear,” Caleb says, but she can’t see him.
Curiously, she’s not afraid. Not really. More like pleasantly surprised as she stares down at the weapon. This could come in handy.
She hefts the AR-15 onto her shoulder, and the heavy awkward thing doesn’t feel quite so heavy or quite so awkward. 
“Now line your target up in your scope.” Miss Cecelia has materialized by Rachel’s side. “Either close one eye or keep them both open. It’s up to you.”
The bear is making that grunting, panting, snorting noise. She hasn’t even started to roar. She’s closer to the tree now, but Tic’s high up. He’s older and stronger and has found shelter in the top branches.
He’s safe.
Maybe I don’t have to shoot her at all, Rachel thinks with relief.
“You’ve got to act now.” Miss Cecelia is adamant. 
So is Caleb, who’s still invisible but is shouting at her nonetheless. “Shoot while you’ve got her in your sights.”
“It’s either kill her or watch her kill your son.”
“Don’t think. Do it.”
“It’s just like we practiced.”
“Pull the trigger.”
Except she hesitates, and now the sow is scampering up the tree. Rachel didn’t even know brown bears could climb, definitely not that high or nearly that fast. She knows what she has to do. But Tic is right there. What if she misses and shoots her son instead?
“Why are you waiting?” Caleb shouts. “Kill her!”
“Mommy!” Tic yells, his voice full of fear. “Help me.” He’s a small child now, just a helpless toddler. He starts to cry, wondering why his mom can’t save him. Won’t save him.
Rachel thrusts the rifle at Miss Cecelia, but the old woman refuses it. “You know what you have to do.”
Rachel closes one eye. Then opens it again. 
“Mommy!” Tic continues to plead through his terrified sobs. “Help me!”
But every time the bear is in her sights, so is her son. 
Nice and steady. Now Caleb’s calm voice is right behind her, like he’s an invisible phantom whispering in her ear, helping her aim. Nice and steady, he repeats, and his reassuring tone grants her the resolve she needs.
She shuts one eye. Then the other. 
Then both.
She squeezes the trigger.
“Mommy! No!”
“Tic!” she screams and sits up in bed with a jolt. Her heart is racing. Her bed sheets are in knots and tangles. Her nightclothes are drenched in perspiration.
And in her nostrils — that hideous, unmistakable stench.

***

“Are you sure you’re not going to hurt yourself doing that?” Mom asked.
Before he got stabbed in the thigh with a hunting knife, Tic might have rolled his eyes at her, but now he understood Mom’s fear and her love for him so much more than he ever had.
“Miss Cecelia said it’s fine, as long as I’m not putting any weight on my leg or feel any pain.”
“I still don’t like it,” Mom fussed. She had always been a worrywart, but ever since Dick’s attack …
“I promise I’ll be careful,” he told her. The truth was Tic couldn’t not lift weights. Even if the weights in question were the silly little two-pound things Mom bought back when she did those low-impact Pilates workouts on YouTube.
Back when there was YouTube.
But something was better than nothing, and anything was better than lying around all day letting every single muscle in his body atrophy.
He started with basic bicep curls, surprised at how quickly he tired out, even when using Mom’s dainty little dumbbells. But Dad had always emphasized the importance of small gains. 
Something is better than nothing, Tic repeated to himself.
Mom hadn’t stopped staring. “Does it hurt?” she asked.
He winced, out of frustration instead of pain, then forced a smile. “No. It feels good to move.”
Benji clambered up from the basement, where he’d gone to grab some actual weights. “Hey, gimp,” he said, giving Tic his usual sarcastic greeting.
“Better be careful,” Tic joked and patted his leg. “Once I can stand up on this baby, you’re gonna be sorry.”
Benji quivered in mock terror. “That’s so scary,” he said then started in on his lateral raises.
Tic hated that he was still cooped up in the living room every day. Hated that he was stuck doing bicep curls with girly two-pound weights. 
But the rhythmic motion was soothing and familiar.
This one’s for you, Dad, Tic thought and took another rep.

***

“Looks like Tic’s recovering really well,” Angel said. She and Rachel were in the kitchen, filleting a couple of trout Dev and the kids had brought home after a morning ice fishing in the cold.
“He is,” Rachel agreed. “Miss Cecelia said he might be ready to start hobbling around on crutches before too long.”
“I can’t picture Tic hobbling,” Angel replied with a chuckle. She sprinkled some salt and garlic powder on the fillet then looked at her work for a moment before adding, “He’s doing a great job keeping his spirits up.”
Rachel didn’t respond. Yes, on the outside, Tic appeared to be the same easygoing child everyone in Glennallen admired. But she knew her son, sensed intuitively and decidedly that something within him had shifted the night of Dick’s attack.
What she couldn’t admit, even to herself, was her fear that he would never be the same again, that the scars on his soul were engraved there permanently.
The women worked together in silence until the front door opened with a clang. At first, Rachel assumed it was the teens coming in from outside, but it was Dev and Miss Cecelia, home from the food bank.
“What are you doing back so soon?” Angel asked her husband.
“We closed early,” Miss Cecelia answered for him, her eyes set, her voice even more gruff than normal.
“Is everything okay?” Rachel asked.
“Oh, sure,” Miss Cecelia spat sarcastically. “As long as you don’t mind some men holding you at gunpoint with their Glock and taking the last boxes of meat.” She turned her ire onto Dev. “Of course, if we brought the rifle like any smart, sane person would have, we wouldn’t be in this trouble, would we?”
Rachel felt as if her mind was glitching. “Gunpoint?” she asked, backing up. Had she heard right?
“Why don’t you both sit down?” Angel suggested, the gentleness of saints and angels oozing from her voice as she wiped her hands dry. “Start from the beginning and tell us what happened.” 
“It’s not as bad as it sounds,” Dev assured his wife. “Some men came in waving around a gun. It was all for show. They wanted the rest of the Spam and canned chicken, so we gave it to them.”
“It wasn’t Dick Jacobson again, was it?” Rachel asked, surprised at the hatred welling up at the mention of that horrible name.
Dev shook his head. “They were from out of town.”
“So you gave them the last of the canned meat?” Rachel pressed.
“We only had a few cases left anyway.” Dev shrugged his shoulders. “And I encouraged them to take a Bible and a gospel tract with them, too.”
Miss Cecelia let out a caustic chortle. “They’ll probably use the pages in their outhouse.”
Dev reached out a hand and laid it placatingly on Miss Cecelia’s elbow. “Nothing in that food bank was ever ours to begin with.”
She jerked her arm away. “No, it belonged to the members of this community. And now they’ll have nothing.”
If Rachel hadn’t known her better, she might have thought Miss Cecelia was about to cry.
“I’m just glad neither of you were hurt.” Angel sighed.
Miss Cecelia waved her hand in the air dismissively. “We wouldn’t have been in any danger at all if I had the AR-15 with me.”
“Those who live by the sword die by the sword,” Dev quoted, but nobody responded.
Angel walked over to her husband and wrapped him in a hug. Miss Cecelia stared at the wall as if she could permanently burn it with the fire of her feelings toward Dev, her anger over all that lost food. 
Rachel glanced toward the living room where she’d last seen Tic napping with Cammy on his chest. Had he overheard everything?
“The Bible tells us to give to those in need,” Dev said, his voice imploring as if he needed to find someone to affirm his actions.
Rachel didn’t know what to think. Of course, she hated the idea of violence, but surely it wasn’t fair to give all their resources to the men with the scariest weapons when the whole town was equally suffering. 
“Next time we go back, I go back armed,” Miss Cecelia declared.
Dev straightened his spine. “Then I can’t go with you,” he responded resolutely.
“Fine,” Miss Cecelia spat out, looking smug and somewhat pleased. “Rachel, you and I will go together.”
And that’s how it came to be that three afternoons a week, both women would sling rifles onto their shoulders, pass out food to the residents of Glennallen, and keep the food bank doors open to the public.
The outsiders with the Glock never returned.

***

“So your parents did research for the military? On that AI stuff you were talking about?” Yaya wasn’t sure she believed him, but Boone had never lied to her before. This wasn’t a hunger hallucination, was it? 
She’d lost weight, but she hadn’t lost her mind. 
Boone leaned his face closer to hers so that their cheeks were nearly touching. Behind them, Ozzie and Zeke were in the middle of a heated discussion about the newest rumors circulating that what remained of the Alaska-based military was regrouping to provide aid. The idea was preposterous to Yaya, but then again, so was the idea of surviving an entire winter stuck here in the GI lab with her professor.
“Swear on my life,” Boone responded. “It was all super top-secret. My parents couldn’t leave the site without a ton of special clearances. Once my dad needed to go under anesthesia for an abscessed tooth and they sent him with two men-in-black Feds to make sure he didn’t give away any confidential information.”
His blue eyes twinkled mischievously. Even if he was totally joking with her, she was spellbound by his story.
“Where were you? Area 51 or something like that?”
He leaned even closer. His forehead touched hers. “No, even more remote. A big Silo outside of Gakona. That’s your neck of the woods, isn’t it?”
Yaya stared at him incredulously. How had she known him all this time and he’d never mentioned he’d grown up in her backyard? The revelation made him that much more fascinating. Still, what he was saying made no sense. 
“There aren’t any military installations in Gakona,” she stated. 
Boone’s eyes were cloudy, and she was sucked into their mystery, thankful he was sharing such a deeply concealed part of himself. “We were underground.”
Now he was definitely messing with her.
Boone chuckled quietly, a shared joke just between the two of them. “You don’t believe me, do you? I swear I’m serious.” Again that dimple. The earnestness in his blemish-free face that made her forget the hunger pangs, if only for a moment.
She nudged his elbow. “All right. Where in Gakona were you, then?”
“You know that big field with the huge antenna array?”
Her eyes widened. “You lived at SPEAR? I thought all those rumors about military research there were conspiracy theories.” 
Another subdued laugh. She could listen to that sound forever. Their arms were pressed up close together. Yaya’s palms grew sweaty, and she didn’t want to think about how long it’d been since she’d brushed her teeth. Even the peppermints and gum they’d scoured from the vending machines were long gone.
But when Boone looked into her eyes and held her gaze, none of that seemed to matter.

***

“Psalm 91 gives us the assurance that whatever is going on in the world, we can face it with courage and faith.” 
Rachel sat transfixed while Dev’s face practically shined during their evening devotions. 
The days were getting longer now, and this was the first night Dev read from Scripture with nothing but the light streaming in from the window.
“You will not fear the terror of the night, nor the arrow that flies by day, nor the pestilence that stalks in darkness, nor the destruction that wastes at noonday.” Dev glanced up dramatically from his worn Bible.
From her seat nearest the stove, Miss Cecelia let out a loud, wet cough.
“A thousand may fall at your side,” Dev continued, his voice rising to a passionate crescendo, “ten thousand at your right hand, but it will not come near you.”
He shut his Bible and let his eyes rest on every individual seated around the living room in turn. “It will not come near you,” he emphasized. “Just like Paul tells us, neither death nor life, nor angels nor rulers, nor things present nor things to come, nor powers, nor height nor depth, nor anything else in all creation, will be able to separate us from the love of God. Nothing in all creation. That includes this grid failure. That includes everything happening around the world. That includes Tic’s leg and Miss Cecelia’s cough and all our sorrows and all our fears and all our uncertainties.” 
Rachel gazed at her husband’s study Bible in her lap, her heart holding tightly to Dev’s words of comfort and encouragement. These evening gatherings were the short, nearly miraculous reprieve from her anxiety and worry. She let her eyes gaze on those seated around her living room: Tic with his leg raised in front of him, bruises fading, his black eye now turned to yellow. The Singh and the Jacobson kids, guests in Rachel’s home who felt like family more and more each day. Leo, who had just discovered how to crawl, and Cammy, who looked upon the tiny human with unbridled maternal affection and pride. When the dog wasn’t velcroed to Tic’s side, she was licking food off Leo’s face or lying next to him on the floor. Even with her stiff shoulder, she made sure to follow him every time he tested out his new ambulatory skills.
For half an hour every evening, Rachel’s life and heart and living room were full.
“Holy Week will be here soon,” Dev went on. “During our morning Bible study, the children and I have been discussing the fact that Jesus’ death, not just his resurrection, should be the focus of our thoughts and fervent attention, and every day we should be meditating on the unthinkable sacrifice he made for us while he hung on that cross to take upon himself the punishment for all of our sins.”
He let his eyes rest on the teens. “The kids have been put in charge of preparing all our special services next week, starting with Palm Sunday. Each child here has taken on special roles that showcase their own unique and God-given talents and abilities.”
Rachel was glad to have Dev with them. Yes, he was stubborn and perhaps a bit naïve. Sometimes Rachel worried that if Dick Jacobson or any other ne’er-do-well showed up on her front porch, Dev would hand over all the food in her house if they simply asked for it. Still, she was grateful the children were getting such regular spiritual input. Heaven knew her soul was too weary to provide them much of anything other than shelter, weighed down as she was by worry for Caleb and their daughter. 
And then at night, while Dev encouraged them all with inspiration from the Word, she could feel that heaviness lift off her shoulders.
But then the sun would set, albeit later now each evening. And everyone would head off to their respective abodes, everyone but Tic, taking all the sounds of laughter and joviality with them. For the last few moments of the day, before Rachel crawled into the cold and empty bed she’d once shared with her husband, it was just her and her son in the empty living room. 
And then, away from everyone else’s listening ears and unbearable gazes, Rachel would pull out the ham radio. 
“Buster?” 
“Hi, Rachel. How are things in Glennallen?” The operator’s voice was always kind, always patient, always gentle.
She held her breath. “Any news?”
And then that sigh she knew so well. The sigh that broke her heart into pieces every single night without fail.
“I’m sorry, Rachel. I’ll let you know if I hear anything.”
Some nights she would manage a polite thank you. Others, her throat would constrict and she’d have to kill the battery and rush upstairs before the real tears came. 


27. THE LONE SURVIVOR

Caleb’s only comfort was that his wife didn’t have to see him like this. He’d always known he was out of shape. He just didn’t know how impossible it would be to force his body to push forward, step after step, mile after mile, day after back-breaking day. Meanwhile, his pants grew looser and his soul wearier.
After abandoning the station wagon at the avalanche site, Caleb assumed the first few days would be the hardest. They still had the rest of the mountain to ascend in violently cold weather. Once they reached the summit, once the path along the Richardson began weaving downhill, things had to get easier.
Didn’t they?
When they were lucky, he and Griz hunkered down in abandoned cabins for the night. Caleb would huddle in the cold and think about home, while Griz checked Arsenal’s paws before going back out to hunt for fowl or set snares. 
Their food supply would have been adequate if they weren’t burning thousands of calories just to stay warm, let alone pushing forward through snow and ice and wind on a journey that felt as endless as eternity itself.
On nights when there was no shelter, they pitched Griz’s cold-weather tent and did their best to conserve heat without straight-up spooning. Arsenal, for all he was worth, refused to be touched while he slept or else he might have made a decent body warmer. The animal would suffer the indignity of a short cuddle, some nights even initiating it, but after a few minutes would get up with a disgusted, angry huff. Then he’d curl himself up in the farthest corner of the tent as possible, glaring as if nursing his mortally wounded dignity, before finally tucking his nose under his tail and beginning to snooze.
Griz’s high-tech gear combined with mercifully warmer days on the other side of Summit Pass kept the men from lethal exposure, but not from the discomfort of cracked and bleeding skin or the bone-wearying, soul-destroying exhaustion.
Early into their descent, Griz and Caleb came across a stalled semi in the middle of the highway. The driver’s frozen body was the second corpse Caleb had encountered in as many weeks, but unlike with Dani, now there was no time for a burial or funeral. Instead, Griz removed the unfortunate man’s coat and boots, tossed them in Arsenal’s sled, and handed Caleb a half-eaten bag of potato chips. 
Caleb wasn’t hungry.
One of the unexpected outcomes of their time together was Griz’s willingness to speak more than two or three words at a time. As the mile markers ticked by slowly and the cold burned their skin, Griz spoke about his grandfather, who had been the first missionary to settle in the Copper River Valley, and the young Alaska Native woman he fell in love with and married. 
In spite of Griz’s heritage of faith, Caleb could never put his finger exactly on the pulse of his friend’s beliefs. Maybe he was a cultural Christian who had never made any personal decision to surrender his life to Christ. Maybe he had blended his grandfather’s Christian faith with the animism of his grandmother’s lineage.
Or maybe he was simply a very private man.
But as the days wore on, Griz talked about other aspects of his life as well, like his time in the Marines. Caleb listened attentively while Griz described his tour as a young Marine in Somalia; the friendships and bonds he forged with the others fighting beside him; his later Force Recon missions in Bosnia and Kosovo before 9/11 placed him in multiple back-to-back deployments in the Middle East.
As a history teacher, Caleb was curious to learn about Griz’s firsthand experience in so many major world events, but what he really wanted to know was how an individual could bear witness to so much evil and violence and wickedness and still wake up day after day, still put on that uniform, still believe that this world — in all of its depravity and fallen nature — was something worth defending.
It was a mystery Griz never explained. Or maybe Caleb simply hadn’t found a way to ask the right questions.
After seventeen years on foreign soil, Griz had returned to Alaska to serve as an Arctic warfare specialist, training American troops in cold-weather survival.
“That must have been a nice change from combat,” Caleb said. 
“Best years of my life,” Griz replied.
Caleb was surprised at this uncharacteristically demonstrative display of emotion, but something stopped him from asking the next obvious question: If Griz loved his position so much, why did he quit? For as long as Caleb had known him, Griz had been living off the land and off his retirement, even though he seemed healthy enough to have decades of military service or an entire second career ahead of him.
The answer came one night when the men were about halfway down the mountain. As evening approached with no cabins or abandoned buildings in sight, Caleb asked if Griz needed help pitching their tent. It had become his subtle way of admitting he was too tired to keep on going while still maintaining a small shred of his pride.
“Nah,” Griz answered. “A little bit ahead.”
By now, Caleb understood that a little bit ahead meant something entirely different to his partner than it did to him, and he steeled himself to endure several more hours of walking in spite of the cold and hunger. 
At least he wasn’t starving, not literally. They still had dehydrated meals in their packs, and when they needed it, Griz and Arsenal managed to provide a ptarmigan or snowshoe hare to cook over a fire. But that certainly wasn’t a cheeseburger or a greasy egg-and-bacon breakfast. Caleb had the extra pounds to spare, but was still faced with a profound calorie deficit at the end of every day, not to mention the monotony that came from eating nothing but lean game and camp meals.
“Better than MREs,” Griz would comment, but Caleb wasn’t sure. He remembered a military movie that clearly depicted an MRE with a Snickers bar for dessert.
Blown about by strong winds, the snowdrifts grew even deeper than normal.
“Walk in my footsteps,” Griz instructed.
Hauling the loaded sled, Arsenal held his bushy tail high as he strode forward majestically. Every so often, Griz would reach out a gloved hand and tousle the fur on his dog’s neck. Eventually, Griz led them down a side road and past a large sign: Marine Corps Arctic and Alpine Training Center. 
Caleb turned to his friend. “Is this where you used to work?” 
Griz just kept on walking. A rush of relief warmed Caleb’s whole body when a building came into view. A few minutes later, Griz had broken in. Their first stop was the mess hall. Caleb’s eyes widened when he saw enough food to supply a dozen men on a dozen trips from here to Fairbanks and back again. Griz lit a small camp stove and pulled out various #10 cans of ingredients to prepare what Caleb suspected would be a mouth-watering feast, or at the very least a welcome change from dry, crispy ptarmigan. 
Soon, a second glorious realization struck home: there had to be a toilet somewhere here. A real, genuine porcelain toilet. 
He was sick to death of squatting in the snow.
Leaving Griz to his culinary work, Caleb wandered around, shining his flashlight on the plaques and maps and certificates lining the walls. He was heading straight toward a restroom sign when something else caught his attention.
Uncertain he could trust his eyes, he held his flashlight closer to the picture of three men. They were dressed in heavy winter gear and posed in front of a helicopter perched atop a snowy mountain. It was the Marine in the middle that made Caleb completely forget the real purpose of his meandering.
The man had fewer wrinkles, two good eyes and no hint of a scar, but Caleb had grown to know every square inch of that face. 
Griz.
The most surprising part of the photo wasn’t Griz’s young age or how he stood with his arms slung casually over the shoulders of his two friends. It was the bright and joyful smile on his face.
Caleb read the inscription: In Memory of Staff Sgt. Daniel Harker and Cpl. Michael Raines. Beloved teammates, skilled mountaineers, and brothers in arms who gave up their lives to save others. Lost in the Moose’s Tooth Avalanche, but the memory of their service and sacrifice will never be forgotten. 
In a jumbled instant, puzzle pieces scrambled together to form a cohesive image in Caleb’s brain. He knew about the Moose’s Tooth Avalanche. Everyone in Alaska knew about the Moose’s Tooth Avalanche — the deadliest Arctic disaster since the Gold Rush days. A team of mountain climbers, their guide, and a camera crew were filming near Denali National Park when they were buried beneath ten feet of ice and snow. The only reason anyone survived was because a joint Army-Marines training team was working nearby, running cold-weather drills.
During their heroic rescue, a secondary avalanche engulfed the entire area. Four of the civilians were ultimately saved, but of the military responders, the only two who survived were an Army Ranger and the Marines’ team leader.
Griz.
Caleb knew the story. He showed the heart-wrenching documentary every year in his Alaska history class. The film never named the Marine who survived, had it? But even if it did, would Caleb have made the connection and recognized his friend? To everyone in Glennallen, he was simply Griz. Caleb didn’t even know his last name.
The documentary mentioned one of the military survivors taking an early medical retirement due to PTSD. Had they been talking about the Army Ranger or the Marine? 
Caleb was still standing there in solemn, contemplative silence when Griz appeared behind him. “Food’s ready. Macaroni and cheese.” 
Caleb let his eyes linger on the photograph, at Griz’s young and healthy smiling expression, at the joyful faces of his teammates. There was so much he wanted to ask. So much he wanted to know. 
But he knew his friend better than that. The moment he saw the stoic look in Griz’s eye, he swallowed the questions down and forced a smile. “Macaroni and cheese? Sounds delicious. I’m starving.”
That night, the men lay out on actual mattresses, covered in blankets and with a small Sterno-burning stove between them for warmth. Arsenal huffed loudly and paced the floor, looking for a spot to lie down.
“He’s too hot in here,” Griz grumbled.
But Caleb wasn’t thinking about Arsenal. He was thinking about Moose’s Tooth. About his friend and what he must have gone through.
Everything in his body ached, including his gut now that he’d gorged himself on Griz’s macaroni and cheese. The mattress was flat and nearly as thin and flimsy as one of Rachel’s yoga mats, but it would be like sleeping in a palace compared to the outdoors.
Once he managed to fall asleep, anyway.
After all their effort, Delta was still a week away, and after that another hundred miles to Fairbanks. He didn’t bother tracking the date, but he knew Easter would be here soon. His first Easter without his family.
Of course, that also meant spring was coming. But how would the promise of warmer weather help them if they died from hunger or exposure, or simply sheer exhaustion, before the snow melted? In his more dramatic moments, Caleb imagined falling asleep one night in Griz’s tent and never getting back up. He pictured Arsenal trying to nudge him awake, then Griz building another fire to thaw the ground and bury him like they’d done back home for Dani.
Or maybe just leaving his body for the wolves and the ravens.
Caleb’s mind refused to quiet, but he couldn’t tell if that was because of his racing thoughts or because Arsenal was so restless. The dog kept pacing and stifling down a high-pitched whimper. Eventually he walked to the door and let out a long, exaggerated howl.
“Want me to take him outside?” Caleb offered.
“I got him,” Griz muttered and grabbed his flashlight.
As exhausted as his body was, Caleb couldn’t shut off his brain, so he eventually pulled out Tic’s pocket New Testament. He’d given up reading Rachel’s love letters. They were too raw, reminded him too much of home. But the Bible was meant to give him comfort and assurances. He hoped God wouldn’t let him down.
Caleb found himself in the Sermon on the Mount, the same passage he’d quoted in the goodbye letter he’d left at home for his wife.
Therefore I tell you, do not be anxious about your life, what you will eat or what you will drink, nor about your body, what you will put on. Is not life more than food, and the body more than clothing? 
Caleb thought about the abundant supplies surrounding them right now. He would have never known this training center was located out here if Griz hadn’t shown him the way. The fact that he was going to bed with a full belly was a clear sign of God’s abundant provision.
Caleb made a mental note to be more thankful for the wild poultry they had eaten so many nights on the road. He nearly chuckled, comparing himself to the grumbling Israelites in the wilderness. Quail in the desert, ptarmigan in the tundra … in the end it was all the same.
Look at the birds of the air: they neither sow nor reap nor gather into barns, and yet your heavenly Father feeds them. Are you not of more value than they? And which of you by being anxious can add a single hour to his span of life?
Worrying wouldn’t extend your life. That much was certainly true. And so was the exact opposite. Caleb’s worry had subtracted days, maybe even years from his life, figuratively but perhaps literally as well. 
Even before the power crash, Caleb was constantly worrying. Worrying about his students, his lesson plans, his family. Worrying about finances, how he’d pay for Yaya’s college and soon Tic’s as well. Worrying about his anxiety and what it was doing to his health … the list was never-ending. He knew the Bible told him to cast his cares on the Lord, but there was no step-by-step tutorial showing him how to actually do that. 
Even though Jesus’ sermon didn’t answer all his questions, Caleb still found comfort in the familiar words: If God so clothes the grass of the field, which today is alive and tomorrow is thrown into the oven, will he not much more clothe you, O you of little faith?
Yes, God had kept Caleb clothed. Tonight was the first time his pants had completely thawed out. They’d gotten looser over the miles, but at least he didn’t feel like he was walking in a stiff and frozen cardboard costume. Both men had good winter coats. Griz was as uptight as a drill sergeant about changing their socks. Caleb’s feet were terribly blistered, but not frostbitten. Tonight, he could wiggle each individual toe.
Even though he didn’t have the strength of heart or emotional stamina to read Rachel’s letters, he could still feel her prayers. She wasn’t here, but she knew how to intercede for him. He thought about all the ways God had provided for their journey up to this point. How many of those blessings were a direct result of the faithful petitions of his devoted wife?
He could only let his thoughts linger on home for a short spell at a time, so he turned back to Tic’s Bible. 
A few minutes later, his reading was interrupted by a low, prolonged howl that rattled around in the emptiness of his very soul.
On their journey, Caleb had gotten used to Arsenal’s multiple ways of communicating. The dog had a special pitch and tone for when he was hungry, when he was bored, when he wanted attention, or when he demanded to be left alone. But this sound tonight was new. Pained. Soul-wrenching. How could the entire sadness of the human experience exist in a canine’s cry?
In spite of all the admonitions Caleb had just read not to be anxious, his mind immediately flashed to an image of Griz lying dead in the hallway, his poor dog wailing over him. He sprang up, racing toward the pitiable noise, terrified of what he might find.
But Griz wasn’t dead in the hallway. He was on the floor in front of the memorial to the two dead Marines. 
While Arsenal sat by his side and lifted up tortured and mournful howls, Griz was collapsed on the ground, his entire body heaving with silent sobs.
 


28. THE TRIUMPHAL ENTRY

“Look at that boy go!”
“Back up. Everyone just step back and give him some space.”
Tic was self-conscious from this unnecessary degree of attention, but also proud as he swung forward on the crutches Miss Cecelia had brought him home from the clinic.
“You need a break?” Mom asked, but he shook his head resolutely.
“That kid’s gonna be sprinting around the whole town by the time the snow melts,” Dev declared.
Mrs. Singh clasped her hands together. “Praise the Lord. He really is the God who heals.”
It wasn’t running suicide drills on the basketball court. It wasn’t a 5k cross-country race. But it was worlds better than sitting on the couch day after day. Good-bye, chamber pot.
Benji’s grin was sincere, without a hint of irony. Even AJ looked ecstatic to see him up and about. Tic did one more round, circling the dining room table on his crutches as Priyah snapped the shutter of an old-fashioned camera she’d found in the garage.
When he finished his final lap, Mom gave him a hug. Dev insisted on shaking his hand. Even Miss Cecelia gave him a stoic nod of approval.
The moment would have been perfect.
If only Dad were here.

***

Dreaming again. She and her son, on the path to their favorite berry patch. Her little boy, happy and carefree and oblivious, scampering down the trail, spurred on by the promise of a delicious snack, the delights of a beautiful summer day. Running up ahead, confident that he was safe under Mom’s attentive gaze.
And then the smell … Her senses were instantly on alert.
A moment later she saw it. That two-legged beast directly between her and her baby …
There wasn’t time to warn him. There was only time to surge forward, to protect her child. Charging at full speed. Racing ahead as an otherworldly roar welled up from the core of her being.
The same dream. The same path. The same fear. 
Except this time, Rachel was the bear. Running. Galloping. Roaring. Slashing and wounding and maiming anything and everything that posed a danger to her son.
It didn’t matter what became of her. It didn’t matter if the fight cost her life.
She was fighting for her child’s life, and nothing in heaven or on earth or in hell below would compel her to stop.

***

“You sure you want to keep building on Indonesia and New Guinea?” Dev asked.
AJ let out a little chuckle and blushed. “I don’t know. It would be pretty easy for someone to take over Australia if I didn’t.”
“That’s stupid,” her little brother insisted. “Australia’s never been the center of any world conflict. It’s not important enough to worry about.”
“But I like having the whole continent,” AJ said.
“Leave her alone,” Benji told Junior. “Not everyone’s a history nerd like you. Some people just like to play a simple board game.”
“There’s nothing simple about Risk,” Junior answered defensively. Beside him, Dev nodded in agreement.
Tic glanced up and offered AJ a weary smile. She had spent her first week here completely ignoring him, but recently they had settled into a casual understanding. She obviously didn’t have real feelings for him anymore (if she ever had). And who could blame her, given that Tic couldn’t even walk without assistance, and even then only a few feet at a time? But at least she didn’t treat him with open disdain.
If Tic had to choose between AJ hating him or relegating him to the friend zone, he knew exactly which option he would prefer.
“Leo, you get back in here!” Miss Cecelia yelled. She bustled into the living room after Leo and Cammy, who both scampered under the card table on all fours. Leo let out an exuberant laugh, while Cammy — fully healed now from her shoulder injury — yipped just as enthusiastically, as if they were playing a game of tag and Miss Cecelia was It. 
“Stop that child before he gets near the stove,” Miss Cecelia ordered, but AJ had already swept Leo up and set him onto her lap.
“Look at him,” she gushed. “Can you believe how fast he’s —”
Her sentiment was interrupted by shouts of “Leo, no!” and “AJ, watch out!”
A second later, colorful armies of infantry, cavalry, and artillery flew off the game board, which Leo had obliterated with all the force and destruction of a giggling, oblivious atomic bomb.

***

“Okay,” Boone said, his blue eyes twinkling. “You’re up first. You ready?”
Yaya laughed, surprised as always at how many games they could invent to pass the time with no cell phones or electronics. If it weren’t for Boone, she would have given in to the hunger and despair long ago. But she was holding on because he was beside her, that steadying force she never realized she needed.
“I’m ready,” she told him. “Just go easy on me.”
His dimple flashed, and he flipped over the sand timer they’d scoured from a geologist’s desk. “All right, first question. Tell me about the most embarrassing thing that ever happened to you in elementary school.”
That was easy. Yaya cozied into the corner of the sectional and smiled at the memory. “Okay, it was fourth grade. We were having spirit week and it happened to be pajama day, so I was wearing these Pokemon pajama pants, and for PE we were playing basketball in the gym. I was a pretty good blocker, but I couldn’t shoot worth anything. So I got the ball on a rebound, started to take it down the court. My friend’s trying to steal it from me, and she reaches out, and at the same time I start to trip and she reaches out to help, but she ends up grabbing my pants and pulling them down in front of everybody. Worst thing was my mom hadn’t done laundry in a while so I was wearing this really old, super babyish pair of unicorn and rainbow underwear, and I was totally mortified.”
Boone’s laugh was easy and casual, and any teasing or jokes about her underwear never came. “Oh, that’s brutal.” He glanced at the sand timer, which was now half empty. “All right. Here’s a harder one. Do you believe in God?”
Yaya stared at the grains of sand in the timer, willing them to drain faster. “Well …”
Boone’s smile faded. “I know we said no passing, but I can come up with another question if you’d prefer.”
She shook her head. “That’s okay. I’m not stalling because I don’t want to answer. I honestly don’t know.”
He reached his arm around the back of the sectional. The gesture, the soft touch, it all felt so natural. Like they’d been sitting this way for months. Maybe years.
She bit her lip and stared at the timer. She couldn’t concentrate on her answer when she was so aware of his gaze, his closeness. Then slowly, one word at a time, she felt Boone gently pull the truth from her soul. 
“I don’t think it’s that I ever stopped fully believing in God,” she began. “It’s more like I saw my parents when I was growing up, and they were so into church and religion and prayer and stuff, and I guess in my head I thought to myself that’s the kind of thing that adults do. Right? Adults have jobs and pay taxes and worship Jesus. So when I was little, I assumed that Christianity would somehow just click once I got older. But then it never did. I remember waking up one day, I must have been in tenth grade or something. And I told my dad what I’d been thinking. I said, ‘If you and Mom were Muslims, I’d believe the Koran was true. If you were LDS, I’d believe the Book of Mormon was true. If you were atheists, I’d believe that was true.’ And I guess I was hoping he could say something to make it all make sense, you know, like wave a magic wand and take all my doubts away. But all he said was, ‘Well, the difference is that the Koran and the Book of Mormon aren’t the inspired Word of God.’ And he said it with such finality, you know? Like he honestly thought it was going to be this big mic-drop moment or something. And I remember thinking, if my own dad can’t explain to me why I’m supposed to believe the Bible’s real but all these other religions aren’t, then nobody really can be all that certain of anything, can they?”
Boone stared at her, but she couldn’t quite read his expression. “You were having these thoughts in tenth grade?” he asked, his blue eyes wide.
“It’s not that weird, is it?” Suddenly, she felt self-conscious. Maybe this game wasn’t going to be nearly as fun as she imagined. She reached out and picked up the timer, which had long ago run out of sand. “Anyway. Now it’s your turn.”
“Wait.” He put his hand over hers, his entire palm covering her entire hand. And those intense blue eyes … 
She tried to laugh him off. “Time’s up,” she reminded him. “Those were the rules.”
“Wait.” Now both of her hands were surrounded by both of his. What was happening? “I want to tell you something.”
“What is it?” she asked, but her voice was so quiet she couldn’t even hear herself. Couldn’t even be sure her lips had actually moved.
He never blinked, and she couldn’t pry her eyes away from his. He gave her hands a squeeze. “I’m sorry.”
“For what?” she asked, still trying to sound light-hearted, still trying to pretend that they were just two people playing a silly game to pass the time while the world imploded around them.
“I’m sorry you didn’t have anyone to talk to back then. It sounds …” Here his eyes flitted away from hers, but only for a second. “It sounds lonely,” he finally answered. 
In his gaze, Yaya saw the echoes of unnamed fears, the mirror of nagging doubts, the dream of a universe that might one day reveal its mysteries to them both.

***

“Hey, Mom, will you help me for a minute?” Tic called out.
Rachel was scrubbing laundry in the kitchen sink, but her back and neck were tight from staying in one position, and she was thankful for an excuse to take a break. 
She frowned when she saw Tic on the living room floor struggling with a pair of snow pants. “What do you think you’re doing with those?”
“Getting ready to go outside,” he answered with a grin.
“No, you’re not. Your crutches will sink through the snow.”
“I don’t need my crutches,” Tic argued.
She stared at him incredulously, her hands on her hips. “Then how exactly do you plan to get around? You know what Miss Cecelia said. At least two more weeks before you put any weight on that leg.”
“Benji and Junior are gonna take me cross-country skiing. Dev’s helping them make a seat on the sled right now.”
So now Dev was in on their plan? This was news to her.
“There’s ice under all that snow,” she argued. “You’re gonna get bumped and jostled and hurt your leg again.”
“I won’t hurt my leg again. They’re gonna go slow.” He straightened his expression and stared straight at her. “Mom, it’s like twenty-five degrees. The sun’s out. Don’t you think some fresh air would be good for me?”
Just then, the front door burst open. “You ready, Tic?” Junior yelled excitedly.
“I’m getting my snow pants on,” Tic called back, then looked up at his mom with pleading eyes. 
Rachel knew how to admit defeat. She knelt down and helped Tic with his winter gear.

***

With the treacherous mountain now completely behind him, Caleb looked out across the picturesque view. The landscape was still covered with snow, but brave splotches here and there popped up to reveal mud and dried out grass. In another month, the entire valley would be green and fertile, stretching out endlessly in all directions, as beautiful and peaceful as the Shire.
He paused on the path and smiled to himself. Arsenal stopped pulling his sled. Griz looked behind him with a question in his eye.
“If I take one more step,” Caleb declared, “it'll be the farthest away from home I've ever been.” He laughed, but Griz’s expression didn’t change.
“Fellowship of the Ring,” Caleb explained, then when Griz still looked puzzled added, “Lord of the Rings.”
Griz shook his head and turned back around. 
“They did a lot of walking in that one,” Caleb muttered then hurried to keep up with his friend. Now that the terrain was flat, Griz was pushing their pace. Caleb struggled more than he wanted to admit to keep up, but it was always rewarding to see each new mile marker bearing witness to their forward progress. They were on track to arrive in Delta in just a few days. 
Griz had fashioned himself a walking stick that reminded Caleb of Gandalf’s staff. In fact, he was seeing Tolkien symbols everywhere, like when Griz caught two hares for dinner that night. 
“Too bad we don’t have any taters,” Caleb lamented, but Griz remained unfazed. 
Then and there, Caleb made it his mission to tell Griz the entire story of the Lord of the Rings, from Bilbo’s birthday celebration to the fires of Mount Doom and everything in between. He was more familiar now with the films than the books and did his best to remember every minute detail, every quote-worthy line. At the rate he was going, the Fellowship would reach Rivendell around the same time Caleb and Griz stumbled into Fairbanks.
“So they’re trying to cross the bridge,” Caleb explained a few days into the retelling. More so now than with any other scene, he wished he had a high-definition TV and full stereo surround-sound system to give Griz the full effect. “And they’re jumping one after another, Legolas and Gimli and Aragorn, and they’re tossing the hobbits across, and right when they think they’re all safe, this big fiery whip comes out and snap! The Balrog grabs Gandalf and is about to pull him down into the abyss. And Gandalf is holding onto the ledge, but he knows he won’t be able to hold on for long, so he looks at the other members of the Fellowship and he says, ‘Fly, you fools.’” Caleb tried his best to impersonate Ian McKellen’s low, raspy voice.
“That’s enough movie quotes for today,” Griz snapped. 
Arsenal gave Caleb a nudge on his thigh until he pet the scruff of his neck, and they kept walking.
And walking.
And walking.

***

“Come on, it’s cold back here,” Yaya complained. “What is it you wanted to show me?” Tired and even more hungry than usual, she felt like she and Boone had been walking for miles even though they never left the GI building.
Boone held her hand, a gesture that felt both completely natural and exhilarating at the same time, and he tugged her forward. “Here we are.”
She looked around, trying to get her bearings. “What are we doing outside the high-bay lab?”
“Wrong question,” Boone answered, his dimple pronounced as he grinned her way. “You should be asking what we’ll be doing inside the high-bay lab.”
He fumbled with his ring of janitorial keys, trying to find the right fit. His entire aura seemed to be buzzing. “You’re gonna love this.”
In spite of her hunger-induced fatigue, Yaya’s body experienced a jolt of anticipation and excitement. Everybody else in the lab was lying around, waiting for the military to show up with aid and rations. The moment rumors started circulating that the Alaska military was regrouping, everyone else seemed to stop thinking and acting for themselves and just rolled over, half comatose, waiting for help to arrive.
But she and Boone were different.
He finally found the right key and swung open the high bay door with a flourish. He gestured for her to look in then stood to the side, beaming.
She blinked, certain she was missing the punchline to some great joke.
Boone shuffled his weight awkwardly. “You recognize this?”
She tilted her head as if looking from a different angle might change what she was seeing. 
“An old, rusty school bus?” 
The lower half was painted green, several window panes were missing, and it looked like there were holes in the exterior, whether from bullets or exposure. It certainly wouldn’t have made the list of the top thousand things she would have guessed Boone wanted to show her.
“Don’t you know what this is?” Boone asked, his enthusiasm beginning to wane.
“It’s a bus,” she answered, unimpressed. 
Boone shook his head, seemed to regroup, and pulled her into the high bay. “Into the Wild. Bus 142. You’ve seriously never heard of it?”
“Never.”
“There’s only a handful of people who even know it’s here. They had to transport it by helicopter, and it had to be super hush-hush. It’s only the most famous bus in the entire country.”
“You’re forgetting the Magic School Bus,” Yaya joked, still completely clueless what the big deal was about this hunk of rusting metal.
“Did your parents raise you in some kind of bubble?” he asked, and Yaya didn’t want to admit the way that his words stung or how close to the truth they actually rang. 
“Into the Wild,” he repeated again, as if the words would mean more to her the second time hearing them. “Didn’t you see any movies growing up?”
“My dad watches the entire Lord of the Rings every Christmas break, and my mom can quote Princess Bride by heart.” Just talking about her family back home brought an unexpected lump to Yaya’s throat.
Boone shook his head then straightened his spine and adopted his professorial tone. “Well then, I see we have a lot of learning ahead of us. Come on. It’s not even locked. Let’s look around inside.”
For the next several hours, Yaya was once more Boone’s student. He told her about Christopher McCandless, an upper-class honors student who gave up his college fund and started roaming the country, eventually living off the land near Healy, Alaska in this very vehicle. Yaya became entranced by Boone’s storytelling, so that by the time she realized she was sitting a couple feet away from where this poor vagabond actually died, it didn’t feel eerie.
“After the book came out about his life,” Boone went on, “hikers from all over started looking for the bus. A lot of them got hurt or lost, and finally the park decided it was too dangerous to keep it where it was. It was such a big deal they had to move the bus in secret. They’re going to restore it and put it in the campus museum.”
Yaya stared at the empty hull, wondering if she was still missing something. “So, big-city boy comes to Alaska, finds an abandoned bus, lives in it for a few months, dies from eating poisoned berries, and that somehow inspires thousands of people to venture out into the wilderness? All that to see a bus?”
“It’s not the bus,” Boone answered earnestly, his intense blue eyes boring straight into hers. “It’s everything the bus represents. The idea of shedding the entrapments of society … not to mention the weight of parental expectations.”
He stared unblinking until she had to glance down. Her hands were in her lap, and now his were holding onto hers. He gave her palm a squeeze. “This is a place where you can shake off your old worldview, your old pretenses, your own perception of self. Right here in this bus, you can become whoever it is you were truly meant to be.”
Yaya gave a tired chuckle. “You sound like a speaker at the youth group conferences I used to go to.”
Without warning, Boone reached out and stroked her cheek. It was soft. Gentle. Warming. His face dropped closer to hers. “There’s so much more to this life than your silly little church conferences could have ever prepared you for,” he whispered. 
His breath was hot on her neck, but a cold chill ran down her spine.

***

Palm Sunday had long been Rachel’s favorite holiday. From waving palm branches at her parents’ church as a little girl to singing and dancing to the Hosanna song with her children when they were little, she had always loved the pure joy and innocence of the celebration. Jesus’ entry into Jerusalem might not have been as well known as his birth or his resurrection, but the Palm Sunday holiday was free from any outside commercialization or secularization, and for that Rachel adored it even more.
That’s why she was thankful Dev had gone all out in helping the children put together a Palm Sunday pageant to start off Holy Week. When she wasn’t at the food bank with Cecelia, Rachel was too tired to do much other than worry. She’d given most of the cooking and cleaning jobs over to Angel, and the care of baby Leo was a responsibility everyone in the house shared. Which meant that over the past week, Rachel had been spending more time in bed than was probably wise. 
Sleeping so much was never a harbinger of good news, but what else could she do?
Today, though, she had made an effort. She’d brushed her hair, washed her face, and put on a pink blouse with an elephant pattern she loved. It was something she might have worn to church back in that period in history when people drove themselves places and turned the lights on in their homes and weren’t completely paralyzed by incessant worry about their missing husband and daughter.
Priyah had snapped a photograph of everybody before the pageant. She was nearly out of film, and Rachel had no idea if they would ever find a way to get it developed, but smiling for a camera with a house full of friends reminded her of her childhood, of family meals and holiday gatherings and simpler, joyful times.
A melancholy warmth filled Rachel’s heart as she watched Junior, who stood in front of the wood stove with Dev’s heavy annotated Bible in his hands. Out of all the children in her home, AJ’s little brother seemed to be thriving the most under Dev’s spiritual tutelage. Earlier in the week, he’d made a public confession of his saving faith in Christ, and as soon as Moose Creek thawed in the spring, Dev was going to baptize him. The young boy’s budding faith made today’s celebration even more meaningful.
“When they drew near to Jerusalem,” Junior read, his voice squeaking slightly, “Jesus sent two disciples.”
Tic and Benji emerged from behind the tablecloth they’d stapled to the ceiling as a makeshift stage curtain. The teens had outgrown their church’s Easter and Christmas pageants years ago, but nobody complained or seemed self-conscious about their roles in this year’s performance.
Junior pointed his finger melodramatically and continued reading from Dev’s Bible. “‘Go into the village in front of you, and immediately you will find a donkey tied, and a colt with her. Untie them and bring them to me.’” 
Everybody knew what part of the skit was coming up next, and adults and kids alike tried unsuccessfully to stifle their giggles.
“The disciples went and did as Jesus had directed them,” Junior continued, a mischievous grin spreading over his face as he peeked behind the curtain. He gestured, waited, and was met with stifled laughs.
“We’re ready,” AJ finally whispered, then emerged on all fours, her cheeks beet red but her smile radiant.
Her brother read his next line with the fervor of a sports announcer. “They brought the donkey and the colt and put on them their cloaks, and he sat on them.”
Next to emerge from behind the curtain was Priyah. She was carrying baby Leo, whom she gently placed on AJ’s back. Priyah held him steady to keep him from falling off and recited her lines in hardly more than a whisper: “Hosanna to the Son of David! Blessed is he who comes in the name of the Lord! Hosanna in the highest!”
Benji and Tic, the hype men of the chorus, raised their hands to indicate that the adults should join in.
“Hosanna!” Rachel let her voice lift to heaven, swelling up in her soul past the laughter, past the happiness, past the ache in her heart that commingled with so much joy. “Hosanna to the Son of David.”
Leo, sensing the commotion was all for his benefit, kicked his feet excitedly and pounded his little fists.
“Oof, my hair!” AJ exclaimed as he yanked a fistful of her long locks. Priyah tried to disentangle the mess, and her mom rushed to her side to help.
A moment later, AJ was collapsed on the floor, her scalp and beautiful black tresses no worse off for their suffering. She shrieked playfully as Leo leaned down and gave her shoulder an open-mouthed kiss. Priyah had forgotten her self-consciousness and was laughing nearly as loudly as AJ.
With the grace and agility of an athletic teen boy with a gimpy leg, Tic plopped onto the floor beside his friends. He took Leo’s hands in his. “Hosanna!” Tic said in a high-pitched voice and raised Leo’s tiny arms up to the sky. 
“Hosanna!” they all repeated, and Leo kicked his legs and gurgled enthusiastically.
 


29. DARKEST NIGHT

Exhaustion had crept back in with a forceful vengeance, and there was nothing Rachel could do to ward it off. If not for the fact that Miss Cecelia needed her at the food bank today, Rachel could have spent the entire afternoon in bed. Maybe the entire week.
The AR-15 strapped to her back might as well have weighed a hundred pounds. The melting snow in the parking lot only reminded her of the relentless passing of time. Time that would stop for nothing. The bulletin board outside the community hall was a bleak testament to the disaster they were all living through. Its desperate messages flapped in the chilly breeze.
Miles, Julie and I have gone to stay with Mary in Gulkana. You can find us there. Jess
Needed: Toyo stove repair. URGENT. Will trade smoked salmon or ammunition (or make me an offer). Bruce (red home behind Trading Post)
Has anyone seen Landon Brown? He was up on North Slope. Grandma Virginia
Lost dog. Brown and gray husky. Red collar. Notify the Stricklands at mile 179. Aggressive. DO NOT CHASE.
Need firewood and food. Wheelchair-bound elder caring for four children. Contact Mike on Old School Road, third trailer on left
Each message was a story, each name a face Rachel knew. The devastation this tragedy had brought to their community was more than her soul could bear. There was no way to lift all these burdens up to the Lord. Her prayer list had dwindled down to Tic and Caleb and Yaya, and some days even that was shortened to one single, pathetic word: Help.
A few minutes after she opened up the food bank, the bell tinkled and Pastor Tom walked in, stomping slush off his boots.
“We haven’t had time to grab everything out from the back yet,” she told him. “Give me another minute or two to get myself organized.”
“I’m not here for food,” Pastor Tom told her, and for the first time she noticed the sorrow in his eyes. 
“Is Miss Cecelia with you?” he asked. “I’ve got some hard news I need to share with you both.”

***

“So these are the songs Dev told us to pick from for Good Friday.” Tic slid the open hymnal over for AJ to look at.
She flipped through the titles and let out a little giggle. “I don't think I know any of these,” she admitted.
“It's not that big of a deal,” he replied with a shrug. “Honestly, I probably don’t even know half of them.” He flipped through the pages until something caught his eye. “Let’s start with this one. I know my mom likes it.”
“O Sacred Head, Now Wounded?” AJ read out loud with a lilt in her voice.
When he volunteered to create the worship song list for their Good Friday service, Tic had no idea the task would be so involved or so time intensive. But Dev wanted to make sure every song was specifically about the crucifixion. 
As it turned out, Tic knew five times as many songs about Jesus’ resurrection as he did about his death. At least it gave him and AJ the chance to focus on a tangible goal instead of awkwardly ignoring how uncomfortable everything had grown between them.
Tic missed the days when he and AJ would just sit together in science class, doodling or laughing or poking fun at the long, boring videos Mrs. Bell always put on when she didn’t want to actually stand in front of the class and teach. He missed the days when he could send AJ into a fit of laughter with just a look, when a shared glance between them seemed to communicate more than a thousand soliloquies.
He missed his life before the crash. But he couldn’t talk to AJ about that. He could hardly talk to her about anything anymore.
AJ was running her finger beneath the hymnal’s lyrics. “With grief and shame laid down?” 
Tic recognized the confusion on her face. “Yeah, these are all pretty old.” He read the lyrics again. “I think it’s talking about how when Jesus was on the cross, he was, like … full of grief and shame?” He was glad Dev wasn’t here to listen to him stumbling.
“Now scornfully surrounded,” AJ continued, “with thorns thine only crown.” She glanced up at him, as if waiting for an explanation.
“Uh, see, when Jesus was on the cross getting crucified, they had put … There was this crown, and they made it out of these thorns, you know, and …” Tic hated the way he was tripping over his words. A couple days ago, Dev had pulled him aside and explained that by working on the song list with AJ, it was a great opportunity to teach her more about Christ. By the time they finished selecting songs for the Good Friday service, AJ would probably believe in UFOs and space aliens more than God.
“Why do they have to make it all so confusing?” she asked, and Tic could see her point. Having been raised in the church, he’d gotten so used to these old, archaic phrases that he rarely stopped to wonder what they might mean.
“I don’t know,” he admitted and adjusted his position in his seat.
“Your leg okay?” AJ asked.
“Yeah,” Tic answered, wondering how he could find a way to get the conversation back to Jesus on the cross. He wanted to tell AJ more about his faith. She had Dad’s Charles Spurgeon devotional now, but when she said she didn’t understand it and Tic offered to read some of it with her, he was just as lost as she was. Everything was so flowery and poetic, and you basically had to have a Bible degree and be fluent in old-timey English to get much out of it. 
The front door opened, and Mom walked in followed by Miss Cecelia. Tic still couldn’t get used to seeing his mother coming and going to the food bank with Dad’s old AR-15 strapped to her back. He knew it was necessary, but he didn’t like it. If he weren’t a cripple barely able to get by on his crutches, he’d be the one walking around town armed, not her.
Each time she came home from the community hall, her face looked older and sadder. He’d heard her whispering with Miss Cecelia. The food bank was nearly out of supplies. The convenience store had emptied its shelves within the first 24 hours of the crash, and Glennallen’s only restaurant had been broken into and looted by the end of the first week. 
What would happen when the entire town ran out of food?
AJ was looking up from the hymnal, her face a mirror of worry and anxiety. Something was wrong.
“What is it?” Tic asked. “Why do you look like that?”
If something had happened to Mom, if someone had threatened or hurt her or scared her while he was here convalescing like a useless invalid, he’d never forgive himself.
Mom shut her eyes for a moment and drew in a deep breath. “Is Dev here?” she asked.
“He’s out with the rest of them checking on the snares.” Tic leveled his gaze. “Mom, what’s going on? What is it?”
She sank down into a chair, slumping down as if she’d been wearing a hundred-pound weighted vest. “It’s Mrs. Bell,” she said and shook her head sadly. “I just heard today that she passed away. Pastor Tom came and told me. They think it was the flu.”
AJ and Tic stared at each other in disbelief.
“Mrs. Bell?” Tic repeated, testing the words. How did that even make sense?
He knew AJ was looking at him and didn’t know what to do with his face. Was he expected to cry? It wasn’t like Mrs. Bell was his grandma or anything. She was just the woman who taught him science. And coached his co-ed basketball team in middle school. And led the summer reading program at the library every year. 
“Are you both okay?” Mom asked, and it was the weirdest question. Because Tic didn’t feel any different than he had two minutes ago. A little confused, maybe, but not heartbroken or devastated or even actually sad. It was like hearing on the news about a hiker who died climbing Denali. Something removed, just one of those things that happen.
But to Mrs. Bell? His teacher?
“I’m fine,” Tic answered and reached out to grab his crutches before he knew where he was going.
“What are you doing?” Mom asked.
“I gotta use the outhouse,” Tic decided and swung himself out the front door.

***

Rachel watched Tic leave and resisted the urge to run after him. For a moment, the dining room was filled with the awkwardest of silences, broken only by Miss Cecelia’s raspy cough.
AJ glanced up from the hymnal she was staring at, looking lost and broken and terrified. Rachel tried to think of something, anything, to say to try to comfort her. “You doing okay?” was the best she could manage.
AJ nodded slowly. “Yeah. Um, I think that … uh, yeah. I’m okay.”
Rachel decided not to press her. “What are you and Tic working on?”
AJ appeared relieved to change the subject. “Songs for Friday.” She thumbed through the worn hymnal. “There’s a lot of them in here,” she added with a shy laugh.
Rachel smiled. “Hey, do you know where Angel is?” It was rare to come home from the community hall and not see Angel in the kitchen.
“Down in the basement, I think.” 
Rachel headed downstairs. Back was that feeling of walking through mud, the idea that every inch she moved drained a mile’s worth of energy. But even heavier now.
Ever since the grid crashed, she’d done her best. She’d stayed busy. Kept herself focused on the Lord and all his blessings and gifts. When Tic was bedridden, she had a reason to get herself out of bed, an excuse to stay engaged. But now her boy was moving around on his own, albeit on crutches. Keeping the food bank open sapped the last bit of her strength. Every time Caleb’s rifle clanged against the back of her leg was a reminder that their world had devolved into violence and scarcity.
It was too much. The message board, the empty shelves in the community hall, the cold and the dark and the uncertainty. The news of Mrs. Bell’s death.
Reminders everywhere of the life they had known. Reminders everywhere that her husband and protector was gone, that her daughter was missing and might never come home.
It was all too much.
Rachel’s only comfort was the thought that in a few more days, a week at most, the food bank would have nothing left. She wouldn’t have to strap on that dead weight and tramp through cold and slush then stand there bearing witness to the horrors members of her community were living through. She could stay home and suffer alone and in silence like everybody else, just like her brain and body craved.
After a sluggish eternity, Rachel found herself with the flashlight at the bottom of the basement stairs. “Angel?” she called out. Pastor Tom had asked her if she would help plan Mrs. Bell’s funeral, and secretly she was hoping it was a job her friend would volunteer to take on, Angel with her infinite reserves of warmth and compassion and hospitality and goodness. In spite of everything they were going through, without makeup or hot showers or a washing machine or any sense of normalcy whatsoever, Angel still emerged from her room every day looking calm and poised and radiant.
Rachel would rarely admit it even to herself, but it was enough to make her sick.
“Angel?” she called out again, then saw her friend kneeling by the open pantry door. She clutched Leo in his sling against her chest and was hunched over on the floor. 
Rachel saw the tears on Angel’s cheeks, and for a horrific moment she worried something unspeakable had happened to the baby. “What is it?” she gasped.
Angel shook her head, as if the misery she was experiencing was something impossible to voice.
Immediately forgetting every terrible thought she’d ever directed toward her friend, she dropped by Angel’s side. “What happened? What’s wrong?”
“It’s nothing,” Angel answered, swatting her tears away aggressively. “It’s stupid.”
Rachel ran her hand in soft circles across Angel’s back. “You can tell me,” she coaxed. 
Angel shook her head. “You’ll think I’m ridiculous.”
“I won’t,” Rachel promised. “What is it?”
Angel stared forlornly at the pantry door, her entire face breaking as she sobbed, “We’re out of salt.”
Any other day, any other time in history, Rachel might have laughed. Except now she knew what this all meant. They needed salt to stomach the beans that had become their primary staples, to season the hare and the trout that Dev and the kids brought in. When summer came, they’d need salt to ferment the vegetables they were hoping to grow in the garden, and in the fall to preserve the moose and caribou they would need to survive.
Leo fussed, and Rachel helped release him from Angel’s sling and stood up to bounce him on her hip.
Angel stood up too, laughing quietly at herself as she dried her eyes. “Anyway, God is good. All the time. He always provides. I’m sure we’ll manage.” She forced a smile and headed toward the stairs. Rachel followed, uncertain what she should say. What she could say.
Before they left the basement, Angel laid a hand on Rachel’s arm. “Quick favor to ask you?”
“Anything,” Rachel agreed, her mental exhaustion momentarily forgotten.
Angel let out a chuckle and glanced at her feet. “Don’t tell Dev I was crying, okay? I don’t want to turn into tonight’s object lesson in Christians being the salt of the world.”

***

Delta Junction. 10 miles.
Caleb stared at the sign and didn’t know if he wanted to start running ahead or to stop dead in his tracks and kiss the ground and praise God for bringing them this far.
For the past several days, the road had been flat and relatively easy. The temperature was cold, but the wind wasn’t biting, and the longer hours of daylight bore all the promises of spring and, with them, hope.
Do you remember spring, Mr. Frodo? The scene up on Mount Doom ran through his mind, and he tried to imagine how he’d tell Griz that part of the story when they got to it.
Ten more miles. It would be evening soon, but if everything went as planned, they would reach Buster’s cabin tomorrow. 
Caleb had heard people quote cliches like hope is the best medicine but never believed or experienced the truth of those words until now. The ache in his back was gone. Even if he wanted to, he couldn’t feel the callouses on his feet or the cold in his joints or the hunger in his gut. His pack felt light, as if it were nothing more than the book bag he carried to and from school every day, back when he was simply a middle-school teacher. 
Back when the world was normal.
Even Arsenal seemed to sense the shift and tugged at his harness as if it took all his energy to keep from darting ahead like a sled dog in the Iditarod.
Griz was the only one who grew more morose the closer they got to town. “Gonna be a lot of people up ahead,” he warned and made sure that his .45-caliber pistol was ready in his holster and the AR-15 he wore slung over his back was within easy reach. With each step, Griz’s tension increased palpably, and because he always walked a few steps ahead of Caleb, it was easy to see when he practiced the quick movements he’d need to make for a speedy draw.
But Caleb couldn’t hold those worries in his head. Not with Buster and his radio a day’s walk away. Lord willing, tomorrow he would hear his wife’s voice, or at least leave her the message that he was safe. Since their marriage, they’d never gone a single day without at least talking by phone. The day he left Glennallen, he imagined the worst aspect of the separation would be not knowing what was happening at home, not knowing if she and Tic were safe. But he realized that the physical separation, the inability to be there to protect his bride, was even worse. The entire world was in chaos right now, but that wouldn’t be nearly so daunting if they could face it side by side.
And as much as he needed to be there to watch over Rachel and keep her safe, he needed her to ground him, soothe his troubled thoughts, calm his restless heart. For as long as he could remember, his anxiety had been a dull, chronic ache so deep in the background of his life he barely noticed it. But now, with nothing but Lord of the Rings quotes and snow for miles and miles every day, the spiraling in his brain was like a whirlpool, threatening every moment to pull him under and drown him forever.
He needed his wife. Needed to talk to her. Needed to hear her voice.
The number of stalled and abandoned cars increased the closer they got to town. Thankfully, they didn’t come across any more frozen bodies. People lucky enough to get stranded out here were close enough to civilization they would have found shelter, no matter how crude. Still, the snow-covered vehicles paused forever in the middle of the highway stood like stark and eerie reminders of the way the world had changed.
In spite of these macabre specters that occasionally dotted the Richardson Highway, the sun was bright. It was the first day of the journey with temperatures above freezing, and the snow turned to mush beneath their feet. Griz had them stop and change their socks every few miles. By the time they reached the decommissioned tanks positioned outside of Fort Greeley, Caleb was sweating, and Arsenal’s tail drooped uncharacteristically as he panted and strained against his harness.
It was Griz who stopped first. Caleb thought it was another sock changeover, but he just stood, staring.
That’s when Caleb saw it. The black scorch marks on the buildings and snow below. The scattered debris blowing aimlessly in the breeze.
“What happened here?” Caleb asked, more to himself than anyone else.
“Bombed,” Griz declared.
“By who?”
“Dunno.”
Arsenal whined, and Griz stooped down to pour some water from his canteen into the collapsible bowl that clipped onto Arsenal’s harness. For a long moment that stretched out into the decimated skyline, the only sound to be heard was that of the dog’s greedy slurping.
Caleb glanced over. Did Griz have friends who worked here? Lived here?
Died here?
Griz tugged at the harness strap. “Let’s go.”
Arsenal looked up at him, drool cascading from his mouth like a spring waterfall.
“Let’s go,” he repeated, even more sternly.
Both Caleb and the dog followed.

***

Rachel had known this day would come. It was an inevitability, after all. And yet she still wasn’t prepared.
She had the feeling that she should do something, but all she had energy to do was stare at the food pantry shelves, now completely bare as the postmaster carried out the very last box, a meager offering with a bag of egg noodles, two cans of stewed tomatoes, and a pound of dried black beans. 
Miss Cecelia grabbed a hand towel and attacked a thin layer of dust on the empty shelf. “I guess that’s it,” she declared and let out a phlegmy cough.
The women hadn’t discussed what would happen next. Rachel had been too exhausted to plan that far ahead. Even now, she was thinking of bed more than the hungry residents of Glennallen.
“I better make a sign,” Miss Cecelia said and hurried to her back office. The woman might be old enough to be Rachel’s grandma but was still bustling, while Rachel trudged each day through a molasses-encrusted haze, a fog of cement that seeped into her joints and bones and soul.
Her only relief? Now that they didn’t need her anymore at the food bank, she could go home and go back to bed.
The bell tinkled, and someone came in. “Hello, Rachel.”
Her eyes took several moments to send the proper signals to her brain. Pastor Tom. She tried to smile but decided it would take too much effort.
Tom cocked his head to the side. “You okay?”
“We’re out of food,” she announced, her voice flat.
He looked around the empty room as if taking it all in for the first time. “That’s a shame.”
She stared down at his winter boots. “Yup.”
“Well, I’m not here to pick up food,” he went on. “I came to ask you about the funeral. Mr. Bell said he’s thinking of a week from Wednesday. That’ll give him the chance to spread the word. Does it give you enough time to make the preparations? He’d like to hold it here since it’s more centrally located than the church.”
“That’ll work,” Rachel told him, knowing she’d forget the moment he walked outside. She hadn’t even approached Angel yet to ask for help making arrangements. With everyone at home so busy getting ready for Holy Week, there simply wasn’t time. No, that wasn’t quite right.
With little else to occupy her attention, Rachel had the time. What she lacked was the energy. The incentive.
The ability to do anything beyond wake up each day and eventually manage to pull herself out of bed.
Tom hesitated at the door. “Should I just come to your house one of these days to get everything finalized?”
“Sure,” Rachel answered. “That works.”
Her tired brain wouldn’t even process whether or not he said good-bye or simply left. The bell on the door jingled as he walked out, its echo ringing hollowly in Rachel’s weary heart.
 


30. LAST SUPPER

AJ’s hands were shaking as she held the piece of paper. Tic felt sorry for her and was ready with a reassuring smile if she happened to glance his way.
It was the Thursday before Easter, and everyone was gathered around the dining room table for a Passover celebration. Well, everyone except for Mom, who’d gone to bed early. 
“So this is the thing I wanted to share,” AJ said. Dev had asked everyone, kids and adults alike, to bring a Bible verse or song lyric or something meaningful to read while people ate. Dev and Angel had prepared a full Seder meal, or at least the best Christian version they could come up with in a town where nobody raised or sold lambs or any of the other special Jewish ingredients. 
AJ straightened out her piece of paper. “Um, Mrs. Winters let me borrow a book by a pastor named Charles Sturgeon.”
Tic was relieved that Dev didn’t draw attention to her mispronunciation. “I’ve been reading it in the morning,” she said, “and sometimes it’s really hard to understand. So yesterday I did that thing that Mr. Dev told us about, and before I picked it up I prayed that God would help me learn something from it. And this was what it said.”
She shot Tic a quick glance, and he was ready with his encouraging grin.
AJ cleared her throat and focused on the words on the page. “And being in an agony he prayed more earnestly: and his sweat was as it were great drops of blood falling down to the ground.” She licked her lips. “And what I think this means,” she said, no longer reading but still staring at the page, “is that when Jesus was praying, it was like a symbol that he was really upset, and the blood can kind of represent his sacrifice, sort of like the grape juice we’re drinking.”
It was actually well water with a few drops of blue and red food coloring, but everyone got the idea.
AJ flushed and sat down in her seat. “And that’s what I wanted to share.” She looked up at Tic, and he started to clap before he knew what he was doing.
