Others joined in until Leo, emboldened by the distraction, reached out to Priyah’s plate and grabbed a slice of raw onion, cut to represent the bitterness of sin and the Israelites’ slavery in Egypt.
“Don’t let him eat that!” Mrs. Singh exclaimed, and multiple hands shot out to yank it from the baby’s mouth. They were all too late. Leo scrunched up his face then smacked his lips together until Miss Cecelia used her sleeve to wipe the onion off his tongue. It was gross, but at least everyone was laughing.
Across from him at the table, AJ shot Tic a quick smile.

***

They were long overdue, but they had finally arrived.
With daylight to spare, Caleb and Griz made their way through snow and mud and muck down the long, winding road that would lead them straight to Buster’s homestead. With each cautious step, Caleb’s mind replayed the devastation they’d seen arriving in Delta. It began at the military base and only intensified the closer they got to town — looted stores, burned cars, a few snow piles with crude crosses on the side of the road, makeshift memorials to those who had already succumbed to cold or hunger or violence.
Delta had been hit harder than anywhere else they’d seen, first by the bombs at Fort Greely and then by the destructive force of a fearful mob. Every home was shuttered against the cold and darkness and danger, and there wasn’t a living soul in sight. Initially, Caleb and Griz had wondered if they should even stop at Buster’s or keep on going. His homestead would take them hours off the highway. When all was said and done, they’d give up an entire day of travel to see a man who might not welcome them.
Who might use deadly force to keep them away.
But in the end, Caleb knew what he had to do. He had to contact his wife.
When they made their initial plans, the radio operator sounded hospitable. He was understandably cautious but willing to help the travelers out, especially when they offered to trade him ammunition for a hot meal and the chance to use his radio. But Caleb’s anxiety grew and Griz’s hand inched closer to his holster as a modest cabin covered in solar panels came into view. 
“Should we call out?” Caleb asked, his mind going back to the TV series with the frontier teacher Rachel liked so much. In that era, people were so mistrustful of strangers you had to shout “Halloo the house!” before approaching someone’s property or you’d get yourself killed.
Was that what the world had devolved into?
Griz stopped a full stone’s throw away from Buster’s home. A hand on Arsenal’s back set the dog onto his haunches, and they all watched. 
And waited.
The air was as thick as the mud caked into the soles of Caleb’s boots. Then an old man in overalls stepped cautiously onto the porch, with a shotgun aimed right at them. 
A shotgun he didn’t lower when they both slowly raised their hands in the air. 
Caleb decided that he was the least intimidating of the three stragglers and spoke first. “I’m Caleb. From Glennallen. Are you Buster?”
“Yeah, that’s me.” He didn’t lower his firearm. “But how do I know you’re who you say you are?” He was talking to Caleb but had his eyes fixed on Griz and Arsenal, who was now lying on his belly in the snow.
“My wife’s Rachel. She’s been waiting for me to get here to let her know I’m okay.”
“Anybody with a radio coulda heard that,” Buster replied. 
Caleb was stuck. If Buster didn’t trust them, there really wasn’t anything he could say to change his mind. So instead he implored him as a human being. “My wife and I are sick with worry for our daughter. Her name’s Yaya. She’s up at the University of Fairbanks. She’s studying architecture and engineering there. Smartest kid you’ll ever meet. I left home in a station wagon with my friend here to pick her up, but we got stuck, had to ditch the car on the pass and have been walking ever since. I need to know about my wife. I don’t care about anything else, I just need you to tell me if Rachel’s okay.”
Buster let a tired, soft smile crack through the wrinkles on his face. “She’s still radioing in. Fact, we’re not too far from around the time she usually calls.”
He still didn’t lower his gun or invite them forward. He scrutinized them both then finally asked, “You got ID?”
Caleb nodded, and Buster stepped off the porch, his legs bowed out like a man who would be more comfortable sitting on a horse than a chair. Slowly, Caleb showed him his driver’s license and Griz did the same.
Buster let out a low whistle. “James Ridley? From the Moose Tooth Avalanche?” He shook his head and stared at Griz in disbelief. Caleb realized again just how much about his friend he’d never known, including his full name.
Buster threw his arms around Griz, who stood unmoving in the snow. “James Ridley!” Buster repeated, letting out a jubilant laugh. “Well. I’ll be. Come in. I didn’t mean to make you stand out here in the cold. Get inside. Let’s warm you both up. I was just about to put away some caribou chili. There’s plenty left over, then we’ll get you both a hot bath, a warm bed ... James Ridley,” he exclaimed again. “After all this time, who woulda thought it?”
 Caleb glanced at Griz, who didn’t seem flattered by the recognition or pleased by the effusive display of hospitality. But Caleb’s mind was on other matters. It wasn’t the hot food or the hot bath or the hot bed Buster had mentioned. It was the promise of hearing his wife’s voice again.
Inside the cabin, Buster set generous portions of chili in front of his guests and tossed Arsenal an entire smoked salmon filet. Caleb’s head was swimming as blood rushed to his brain and his gut and his extremities now that he was out of the cold.
“This here,” Buster said, placing a picture in front of Griz, “that’s my boy. You recognize him?”
Griz barely lifted his gaze. “I recognize him.”
Buster turned to Caleb. “Your friend here? This traveling buddy of yours? He’s the one who saved my boy all those years ago up on Moose’s Tooth. Real, genuine American hero he is.” Buster got up and pulled a school photo off his fridge of a smiling boy with a missing front tooth. “This here? This is little Jake, my only grandbaby. He’s only alive ’cause of what you did that day.” Buster cleared his throat then tenderly put the photo back in place with a magnet. 
“You know, I looked for you. Wanted to find you to thank you in person for saving my boy. You been hiding out there in Glennallen all these years?”
“Something like that,” Griz mumbled. 
Sensing Griz’s discomfort, Caleb tried to change the subject. Honestly, he was just as curious as Buster was, but he had grown to respect his friend’s deep need for privacy, whatever the reasons might be.
“How old’s your grandson?” Caleb asked.
Buster stared at the old fridge, a huge faded yellow thing that looked like it could have been older than Caleb. “He’s seven. Or maybe eight now. I forget. He’s in San Francisco.” Buster’s eyes clouded over. “God help him, I haven’t heard from my boy since all this happened. That’s why I’m more than willing to help you reach Fairbanks, help you get to your daughter.”
“And Rachel …?” Caleb prodded. 
“Like I told you, she calls here every night.” A tender smile spread across Buster’s wrinkled face. “She loves you very much, that little wife of yours.”
“Is she okay? Are she and Tic safe?”
Buster shrugged. “Far as I know. She don’t talk much. Just wants to know if I’ve heard from you. Breaks my heart every single day when I have to tell her no.”
“We’ve had a rough journey,” Caleb admitted.
“Sounds like it.” Buster shook his head. “Tell you what, you two finish up here. I’ve got my radio set up in the back. You take your time, eat as much as you want, get yourselves warm and comfortable. I’ll be listening in and holler for you when she calls.”
The smoky spice from the caribou chili, the kindness of this stranger, the cozy heat emanating from Buster’s two wood stoves … none of these compared to the promise of hearing Rachel’s voice again, the sweetest blessing in all the world.

***

Tired. Hour after hour, minute after minute, Rachel was just so tired. 
Earlier that evening, Angel had knocked on her door, asking if she was going to join them for their Seder dinner.
No. Rachel wasn’t hungry. Especially not for a smorgasbord of random foods that didn’t constitute an actual meal and weren’t even made with any of the traditional Jewish Passover ingredients. Lamb had been replaced with wild hare, the charoset with chopped up raisins mixed with peanut butter, so basically ants on a log without the celery. The only slightly authentic part of their dinner was the unleavened bread, made of course without any yeast or any salt.
Next to bother her was Priyah. “Mrs. Winters?” Her voice was so soft, so demure. Sometimes Rachel wanted to grab the girl by both shoulders and shake her. You’re a human being, not a ragdoll. Talk like you have a spine!
But instead Rachel just peered out from her pile of blankets that did nothing to keep the terrible chill out of her soul.
“My dad wants to know if you’re coming down for the evening devotions or if we should start without you.” Her eyes never left the floor, and Rachel was disgusted by the child’s obvious discomfort. Was little Miss Prim and Proper embarrassed to see an adult woman in this condition?
“I still have a headache,” she told Priyah. “You guys can go on and start without me.”
Before leaving her in peace, Priyah turned around to ask, “Want me to leave the door open so you can listen in?”
“Sure,” Rachel replied. She’d get up and slam it shut the moment she found the strength, but at least for now she could let Priyah pretend she was doing her some kind of favor.
Unfortunately, the burst of energy required to get out of bed and walk the ten steps to the door never arrived, and Rachel gritted her teeth through out-of-tune singing and then Dev’s teaching voice rising and falling in pitch from downstairs. The sound was just far enough away she couldn’t make out the words, but she couldn’t ignore it either. The mental effort necessary to even exist in the same space as the dull, indecipherable noise was tortuous. 
When a hush fell over the house, she guessed they were closing their evening in prayer. Probably praying for her and her so-called “headache.” Let them. If God wanted to come down and heal her, he would. And if not, it wasn’t her fault that she was bedridden. She’d spent so long focusing on Tic and his recovery, sacrificed so many nights of sleep to hold silent, prayerful vigils. Wasting her days trudging to the food bank. Worrying incessantly for her family. It was too much for her mind to endure, so now that her son and the food bank didn’t need her every second of every day, her body had shut down.
Nobody could blame her for that, could they?
The familiar sounds of people going their separate ways. Good. Once they were all in bed, they’d leave Rachel completely alone. 
But then another set of footsteps coming up the stairs, firm and deliberate. Footsteps Rachel recognized. She wanted to groan but couldn’t muster the strength. 
“Rachel Winters?” Miss Cecelia snapped. “What do you think you’re doing still lying around in this bed?” The old woman stood with her hands on her hips, glaring angrily.
“I have a headache,” Rachel mumbled.
“No, you don’t.” Miss Cecelia grabbed the pile of blankets and tried to tug them off her. “You’re depressed because you haven’t heard from your husband, you’re worried about your daughter, you’re exhausted from caring for your son and everyone else, and you’ve given up hope.”
Rachel made a grab for the comforter, and the women engaged in a physical struggle that to Rachel’s addled brain felt like it was happening in slow motion.
“Your mother and father would be rolling over in their graves if they saw you so despondent.”
“I’m not despondent,” she grumbled, flopping her head back on her pillow once she’d gained the upper hand in their weird tug-of-war. “I’m tired.”
“Of course you are,” Miss Cecelia said. “You’ve been worrying yourself sick over your son. I know how upset it made you, how much sleep you lost for that boy. But you did what you had to do because you’re a mother, and that’s what mothers do. Now Tychicus is getting better. He doesn’t need you doting on him every moment of every day, so you turned your energy over to the food bank and now that’s closed, so you’ve decided it’s time to go ahead and start wallowing in despair, and I won’t let you. How long has it been since you ran out of your Prozac?”
Rachel stared. It was none of Miss Cecelia’s business what medication she was or wasn’t taking.
“Don’t give me that look,” Cecelia snorted. “I worked at that clinic for thirty years. I know what you take. So, are you out of your pills? Is that what’s causing this?” She gestured around the room, as if everything her eyes touched could be explained by the absence of a small white 40-mg capsule.
“When did you run out?” she demanded.
Rachel sighed. “A few days before Palm Sunday.”
Cecelia nodded as if she were a detective making a positive ID. “You been dizzy? Nauseous?”
“A little,” Rachel admitted.
“It’s the withdrawal. You should have told me. You can’t quit Prozac cold turkey.”
Rachel didn’t respond. It wasn’t as if there was a little sticker on her pill bottle that told her what to do when the world completely shut down.
Miss Cecelia sighed and softened her gaze. “It’s a hard thing, Rachel. I won’t pretend like it’s not. You’ll need a little time for your system to rework itself, and then you’ll start to feel more like you again. Remember, women’ve been caring for their families for centuries, both in sickness and in health. That’s just what mothers do.”
Rachel needed to shut her eyes, but there was no way to tell this nosy busybody to go away.
“For now,” Miss Cecelia concluded, “let me tell you what’s gonna happen. You’re gonna get out of this bed and change out of those old clothes. Angel and Priyah did the wash just a few days ago, so I know you’ve got something clean to put on. Then you’re gonna brush your teeth and your hair. You’re a mess.”
Rachel nodded like a schoolgirl who’d just been scolded. She was embarrassed, ashamed of herself, and mad at Miss Cecelia for calling her out. But that primal fear of getting in trouble did something inside her that no amount of praying or wishful thinking could have.
It got her out of bed.
By the time Rachel was dressed and had washed her face and brushed her teeth, she did feel surprisingly better. Miss Cecelia returned, still pouting, and gave her a solid nod of approval. “Now, you ready for bed?”
Rachel shook her head. “I think I’ll at least go downstairs and say goodnight to Tic.” She stopped at the door and faced her friend. “Thank you, Cecelia.”
Miss Cecelia harrumphed and left the room.
At the bottom of the staircase, Rachel paused and stared at her son, nearly gasping when she realized how much older he looked. What happened to her little boy?
Tic glanced up from his sketch pad, and Cammy wagged her stump at Rachel’s approach.
“Hey, Mom.” He offered her a small smile, and she recognized the questions that hid behind his gaze: Is this woman my mom or the empty shell she’s become?
She forced herself to look at him. Really look at him. “Hey, buddy.”
His tentative smile became more solid, more convincing.
She sat down next to him on the couch. “How was the Seder?” 
He shrugged. “Kinda interesting, I guess. It didn’t really feel like a meal, though.”
“You still hungry?” she asked. She didn’t like the way his cheeks had sunken in lately.
“Nah.”
Rachel glanced down at his drawing pad. “What are you working on?”
He looked away but didn’t close his book. It was a pencil sketch of Cammy, a huge smile on her face, her ears perked up like she was ready to play. 
“She looks like she’s waiting for you to throw her ball,” Rachel remarked, only realizing her mistake when Cammy jumped off the couch at the sound of her favorite word and grabbed a tennis ball that had rolled under the TV stand.
Tic gave it a half-hearted toss, and Cammy’s nails clicked and clacked on the floor while she tried to find the leverage she needed to chase after it.
Rachel let out her breath and tried to remember if she’d been downstairs at all today.
“How’s your headache?” Tic asked, and Rachel brushed his question away. “I’m fine,” she answered, forcing a smile. “Just tired. I wanted to say hi before I went to bed. You need anything?”
“Nope. I’m all set.” Tic’s eyes rested on the ham radio. “Have you checked in with Buster today?”
Mom shook her head. Why should she? His answers were always the same. Miss Cecelia was right. Rachel needed to stop wallowing. She needed to get her body and her brain functioning again. And she couldn’t do that if every day her hopes were dashed to pieces by Buster’s old voice on the other end of that stupid piece of machinery.
“I’m pretty tired,” she repeated. “Maybe we can just check in tomorrow morning.”
Tic’s face fell, and Rachel hated herself. Were these calls just as important to Tic as they were to her? She’d never even thought about that.
Something else Miss Cecelia told her replayed in her mind: Women’ve been caring for their families for centuries. That’s just what mothers do. 
And it was true. Somehow, even so long ago during the worst of her postpartum incapacitation, Rachel had managed to make sure her children were fed and clothed. And now, through the sleepless nights, the terrible anxiety for her daughter and husband, Rachel had made sure Tic’s leg healed up.
A woman will do anything to take care of her family. 
Rachel sighed. She knew the pain would pierce her soul like a knife and leave a wound that could never heal over. But she flicked the switch on the radio, even though the small lever felt like it could have burned a blister into her skin.
After making sure she wasn’t interrupting anyone else’s transmission, she pressed down on the mic. “This is Rachel Winters in Glennallen. Buster, do you have a copy?”

***

Tic had seen his mom cry before. She cried at Dani’s funeral. She cried when Dad left. She cried the first time Miss Cecelia changed his bandage after Dick’s attack.
But he’d never seen her break down completely like she did when Dad’s voice came on the other end of the old, beat-up ham radio.
“Caleb? Oh, thank God. It’s really you. Thank you, Jesus. I’ve been waiting so long … I almost didn’t call in. Thank you, God, thank you, God. Are you all right? Why did it take so long? Is everything okay? It’s really you, isn’t it?”
Dad’s voice sounded different, but Tic didn’t know if that was because of the radio or something else. He strained his ears while Dad explained how he and Griz had to walk all the way to Delta, how long it had taken, how cold it had been. 
It could have been a stranger speaking for as much as it sounded like his father.
“But you made it to Delta?” Mom insisted on hearing the news several times. “You’re at Buster’s now, and you’re all right?”
“We’re at Buster’s now,” he repeated. “How are you? How’s Tic?”
“He’s here,” Mom answered, wiping her tears and holding out the microphone.
Tic stared at it lamely for a second. “Uh, hi, Dad.”
“Hi, son.” So that was it. No name-calling. No moron or idiot or puny baby. Just hi, son.
What had happened to his father? What had happened to both of them?
“You doing okay?” Dad asked, and for a minute Tic wasn’t sure if he should mention his leg. It wasn’t as if there was anything Dad could do about it all the way out in Delta. He glanced at Mom.
“We had an incident,” she said, and Tic could hear the forced cheer in her voice. “Everyone’s fine now, but Dick Jacobson came over here wanting to cause trouble. Tic got his leg hurt, but Miss Cecelia’s helping him out. He’s healing up fine and doing great.”
Her hand was shaking. There was silence on the other end. Was she still holding the talk button down?
“Dick Jacobson?” There was ice and steel in Dad’s voice. Now he sounded even more like an imposter.
Mom cast a nervous glance around the room, but Tic had no idea what she might be looking for. “It’s fine now,” she reassured the stranger on the other line. “AJ and Junior are here staying with us, too.” There was more false happiness and bravado in her tone when she added, “The kids are actually doing really well. Dev’s been giving them all skiing lessons …” Her voice trailed off, and she bit her lip.
Tic didn’t know if Dad’s silence was a lag on the radio or something else, but he didn’t wait to find out. He took the mic from Mom. “Hey, you staying warm enough, old man?” He hoped the transmitter didn’t catch the slight quiver in his voice.
“Warmer than you’d be, shivering off your skinny little butt.”
Tic grinned, and for a flash, he could feel his Dad’s warm love and approval.
The three of them talked for a few more minutes. Dad and Griz would stay with Buster for one night, maybe two, while they gathered their strength. They still had a long journey ahead of them. If it had taken them this long just to get to Delta …
“I need to go,” Dad finally said. 
Tic felt a lump in his throat. Mom just nodded at the radio and clutched the microphone close to her heart.
“I think of you every minute of every day,” Dad continued, speaking into the silence. “When I get tired or cold or hungry, it’s you and Tic and Yaya I think of, and that’s what gives me strength. You are everything to me, the most important treasure in the entire world. Rachel, there’s nothing I wouldn’t do to bring us back together again. I miss you every minute, with every fiber of my being. I love you so much, baby.”
If Tic weren’t so tired, he would have grabbed his crutches and given his parents some privacy, but his leg ached, he had nowhere else to go, and he had no idea when he’d hear his father’s voice again.
“I pray for you every single day,” Mom promised.
“I know,” Dad answered. “I can feel it. Don’t stop. Please don’t stop. I’d be lost without you.”
“I love you more than anything. We love you,” Mom emphasized.
Suddenly Tic was seized with the full realization of how many more miles his dad had to travel before they’d see each other again, how many things could go wrong … “I love you, Dad,” he added impulsively. “You’re a great dad. For an idiot.”
“I love you both.” Dad’s voice sounded tearful. “I promise …” He stopped and Tic could hear him suck in a deep breath. “I don’t know how long it’ll take, but I promise that I’m going to do everything I can to bring us all back together.”
“I know.” Mom was openly crying again, and Tic realized that he was squeezing and tensing every muscle in his body. His jaw ached. His leg throbbed.
“I love you both,” Dad said. “So much.”
“I love you, Dad,” Tic called out, practically shouting the words as if that would make them carry more effectively, as if that would make his dad feel their truth even more intensely.
He and Mom both stared at the small radio in silence for several minutes even after the transmission ended.
 


31. SUNDAY’S COMING

Together again. Caleb and Yaya, happy and safe and sound. Beside them Tic, his leg fully healed, and Cammy, happily wagging her stump at this gathering of everyone she loved. The whole house full of guests welcoming the weary travelers home. 
They were feasting on the clam chowder Rachel always made for special occasions. It paired perfectly with Angel’s sourdough. Everything was exactly as it should be and better, the food and the company and the entire scene bringing warmth and a sense of safety she hadn’t felt since the day her husband left.
“I’m so happy we’re together again,” Caleb told her, holding a history of months and years and lifetimes in their shared gaze. “I’m so happy that you’re mine.”
“I’ll always be yours,” Rachel responded. Leaning into him, she felt his warmth, his strength, seeping courage and healing power into her soul. 
“I’m not the same man I was when I left home,” he admitted, but she shook her head, refusing to hear any more. “You’re my husband. You’re all I ever wanted.” She gestured to her family, their friends, the feast spread out on the table before them. “This is all I ever wanted.”
“And Lord willing, you’ll have it all again,” Caleb whispered in her ear. “Because I’m coming back home to you. I promise.”
“I know,” she whispered, and even after she woke up, the warmth of her husband’s love surrounded her like the mist on the mountains on a cold winter morning.
“Lord willing,” she whispered again, and before she had time to change her mind or give in to the all-familiar paralyzing heaviness, she got out of bed and dressed for the day.

***

“Get up. They’re here.” Lilly’s voice was filled with hope and happiness as she shook Yaya awake. 
Yaya stared at her friend’s joyful face and tried to focus on what she was saying. “Who’s here?” Yaya asked, trying to blink the grogginess and cold and hunger away.
“The soldiers,” Lilly practically squealed. “They’re here. They have food and water and are setting up first aid stations over by Bartlett Hall. Zeke already went and came back with a sandwich and canned peaches and guess what else? They have coffee!” She yanked Yaya’s arm. 
“Okay. I’m awake. I’m up.” 
Was this a dream? Had she actually heard right? “Did you say coffee?”
“Yes.” Lilly was tugging, giggling, buzzing with joy. “And some of them are cute, too. Come on.”
Yaya was fully awake now, as if that first jolt of hot caffeine had already rushed into her veins, and she looked around for her coat.
“Wait.” The voice was deep. Harsh. Familiar but not exactly.
She stared up at Boone. 
“Wait,” he repeated.
She offered him a smile. Was he still tired? Was that why he looked the way he did? Like he was angry. Or threatened. Possibly even the slightest bit scared. 
But she knew him so much better than that. Especially now, after all this time they’d spent together …
“You can’t leave.” His fingers gripped her arm, and she’d lost so much weight his whole hand wrapped itself around her bicep easily.
“Hey, let her go.” Lilly stepped toward him. When he didn’t move, she actually shoved his chest. 
Recently, Yaya had sensed that her friend was jealous of the extra time she was spending with Boone, but she’d never seen her react toward him with open hostility like this. 
“What are you doing?” Yaya demanded.
“He can’t keep you here. I’m telling you, there’s food out there. Food and coffee and fresh water. Drinking water.”
Boone shook his head, an air of authority and finality in the one simple gesture. “We’re not going anywhere.”
Lilly waved her hand in the air. “You do whatever you want, but Yaya’s an adult and she can make up her own mind.” She tugged again on Yaya’s arm, the one that wasn’t stuck in Boone’s vise-like grip.
“Come on,” Lilly said. “Let’s go.”
But Yaya didn’t move. She couldn’t.
She didn’t know why Boone wanted her to stay, but he must have his reasons. Maybe this strange arrival on campus was some kind of ploy. Maybe the soldiers weren’t American at all. Maybe this was a trick or a trap or … who knew?
She looked into Boone’s eyes, her entire body melting with relief when she saw the fierce protectiveness in his blue eyes, the assurance that he was looking out for her. That he cared about her.
“Tell your friend she can do whatever she wants,” he told Yaya, never blinking, never taking his gaze away from hers. “You and I have all that we’ll ever need right here.”

***

Tic couldn’t remember ever attending a Good Friday service before. Maybe his church did them and they just blended into all the other church services he went to, but nothing about tonight jostled his memory.
Dev insisted that none of the songs they sang tonight could be about the resurrection. The only goal today was to focus on Jesus on the cross. Tic thought it was rather depressing, and after last night, he couldn’t remain completely stoic and impassive while he led the singing on the guitar.
After days of scouring and searching, he and AJ had finally found enough songs about the crucifixion. There was Dad’s favorite hymn, The Old Rugged Cross, and the one Mom liked, O Sacred Head, Now Wounded. Then Tic had found Were You There, which the hymnal said was an African American spiritual, and a few others that felt appropriate. He was glad when the complete set was over and hoped that he’d picked the right balance of music to match the solemn occasion.
The day commemorated the saddest, most tragic event in human history, but Tic’s heart was soaring, racing, rejoicing.
Dad was alive. 
After the singing, Junior read Psalm 22, the same passage Jesus quoted when he hung on the cross. Then Priyah sang another song while her brother accompanied her on the guitar. For being so shy and quiet when she talked, she could actually sing really well. 
Finally, it was time for Tic and AJ to share the last part of the Good Friday service they’d planned together. AJ was too self-conscious to read it out loud with him, so Tic held his notebook and began by himself.
“So, every Good Friday, my dad liked to play this thing on YouTube,” he explained by way of preamble. “It’s this clip from a pastor and it’s called Sunday’s Coming, and it sort of became like a tradition in our family. And I couldn’t remember all the words or anything, and obviously we can’t stream it anymore. So AJ and I worked together, and we made up our own version.” He stared down at his notebook and cleared his throat.
“It’s Friday,” he began. He had already decided he wasn’t going to try to match the pastor’s low, melodic voice but would just read the words as he and AJ had written them down. “Jesus is on the cross. Mary is crying. The disciples are scared. But Sunday’s coming.”
Interested in what was happening, Cammy came over, resting her head on his lap and nudging his hand until he reached out to pet her silky fur. 
“It’s Friday,” he continued. “Judas is full of remorse. Simon Peter is full of shame. The dying thief is full of fear and pain. But Sunday’s coming.”
He felt his voice rising, his pulse quickening. “It’s Friday. The sky is dark. The heavens are weeping. The angels are mourning. The Son of God hangs on the cross. The men who followed him are struck with terror. The women who loved him are gripped by grief. They don’t know that Sunday’s coming.”
While his heart thudded in his chest, Tic could almost hear the cries of Mary and the disciples at the foot of the cross. His memories flashed back to Dani, buried in their backyard. The night of Dick’s attack, the hunting knife slicing open his leg. The fear for Cammy, for his father, for his sister. The guilt for not sparing his mother from terror, for not being able to wipe the worry and the heaviness and the depression away for her.
The hunger pangs that had recently crescendoed to a persistent howl in the background of each and every day.
He turned the page in his notebook. This was where he and AJ had deviated the most from the original. He hoped nobody thought it was too sacrilegious. Maybe they shouldn’t have changed it so much.
“It’s Friday.” His voice was quieter now. More uncertain. He glanced up at AJ, who gave him an encouraging nod.
“The grid is fallen. The power is off. The people are hungry and scared. Some have died. Some are far away from home.”
He heard a sniffle but didn’t look up from the page. If he saw Mom crying, he wouldn’t be able to finish.
“It’s Friday,” he repeated. “We’re cold and we’re scared, and there’s a lot about the future we don’t know yet. But there’s one thing we do know.” A smile spread across his face. Strength surged through his entire body. He looked up at everyone around him, Mom included, and couldn’t keep the hope from pouring out of his soul and into his words.
“We don’t know when the power will be restored. We don’t know where my sister is. We don’t know when my dad will be returning home. But there’s one thing that we do know. Sunday’s coming.”

To Be Continued 

 


NOTE FROM THE AUTHORS


We’re so glad you joined us on the Winters’ adventure of faith and survival. This is a story the two of us started brainstorming at least a decade ago when we’d drive our family from Glennallen to Anchorage and think of all the dangers that lay on the big highway between home and the nearest city.
Leviathan Rising is the very first book we’ve written together, which was a really fun project to take on. We hope to see you back for book 2!
 


DISCUSSION QUESTIONS


The following questions can be for personal reflection or for use with a book club.

Ice Breaker Questions
1.	Have you ever been to Alaska? Could you ever see yourself living as remotely as the Winters do?
2.	Assuming you weren’t in mortal danger and your needs were met, what would you miss most if electronics, cell phones, and Internet all went out?
3.	If you had to pick 2-3 other families to all move in together and share resources, who would you pick and why?
4.	Have you ever had to live through a power outage, home displacement, or anything similar to what the Winters have gone through?
5.	What are your general thoughts about artificial intelligence and its impact on society today and in the future?

Story-Related Questions
1.	Did you have a favorite character in Leviathan Rising? Who did you find yourself relating to most? Any you disagreed with significantly?
2.	What do you think about Yaya, her faith journey, her relationship with Boone, and your expectations for her future?
3.	Do you think Miss Cecelia was too harsh with Rachel about spending so much time in bed, or was that the message Rachel needed to hear?
4.	Leviathan Rising is book one in a series. What did you think of the ending? What do you hope to see in the next novel?
5.	If power and order are restored overnight, Caleb and Griz bring Yaya home, and life goes back to normal, in what ways will the characters in this book still be changed by what they’ve gone through?

Issue-Based Questions
1.	What are some of the ways the people living in the Winters’ home continue to encourage each other in the faith? Does this feel genuine to you or more like a pretense?
2.	What’s your opinion about Rachel and Cecelia going to the food bank armed with a rifle?
3.	How did you feel when Rachel’s mama bear came out during the night of Tic’s attack? Do you agree with her reactions or not?
4.	How do you think the Christians in your community would react in a global catastrophe like this?
5.	How do Jesus’s teachings about the Golden Rule and turning the other cheek play into issues of self-preservation, defending a loved one, or protecting your home, especially in the face of a total global catastrophe of this magnitude?
